The Copyright Law Review Committee was established in 1983 to consider and
report to the Attorney-General on specific copyright matters
referred to it from
time to time. Since March 1993 the Minister for Justice has had primary
responsibility for copyright law and
policy development and consequently this
report, and future reports of the Committee, will be presented to the Minister
for Justice.
At the date of this Report, the Committee comprises the following
Mr Justice I F Sheppard, AO
Chairman, Justice of the Federal Court of Australia and President of the
Mr Peter Banki
Solicitor, Phillips Fox, Sydney and Chairman of the Australian Copyright
Ms Lyndsey Cattermole
Managing Director, Aspect Computing Pty Ltd, Melbourne.
Mr Brett Cottle#
Chief Executive, Australasian Performing Right Association.
Mr Chris Creswell
Assistant Secretary, Attorney-General’s Department.
Mr Derek Fielding
Pro-Vice-Chancellor (Academic Services), University of Queensland,
Mr Michael Frankel
Solicitor, Michael Frankel and Co Solicitors, Sydney.
Mr Patrick Gallagher†
Managing Director, Allen and Unwin (Aust) Pty Ltd, Sydney.
Ms Janice Luck†
Consultant, Davies Ryan De Boos Solicitors, Melbourne.
Professor Dennis Pearce
Professor of Law, Faculty of Law, Australian National University,
Mr Bob Rodgers
Director, Terrace Counsellors Pty Ltd, Perth.
Professor Ron Sacks–Davis#
Research Director, Collaborative Information Technology Research Institute,
(RMIT), Melbourne.
† These Committee members were not appointed to take part in this
# The terms of appointment of these Comittee members expired on 30 June 1994
but the members kindly agreed to participate in the finalisation
Since 1983 the Committee has been asked to consider eight references and has
now completed and forwarded to the Attorney-General reports
on all of these. The
references completed by the Committee are those on the meaning of publication in
, the use of copyright materials by churches,
performers’ protection, moral rights, the importation provisions of the
, conversion damages and journalists’ copyright.
The completion of this reference on computer software leaves the Committee
no outstanding references.
Enquiries concerning the Committee and its work should be directed to:
Mr Peter Treyde

Robert Garran Offices
National Circuit
BARTON ACT 2600

The Committee sought further comment on particular issues identified in its
Draft Report
on Computer Software Protection
(paragraphs 9.42,
9.79, 14.43. 16.10 and 16.24) and in response to this request it received a
further 42 submissions prior to the
preparation of this final report. As
mentioned in Chapters 1 and 3, the Committee is appreciative of the submissions
made to it and
where possible has endeavoured to acknowledge in this report, the
source of opinions and comments made to it.
Report on Computer Software Protection
represents an
amended version of the
Draft Report on Computer Software Protection
released in June 1993
It is the Committee’s intention that readers
be able to study the
Final Report
without the need to refer to the
Draft Report

Note: In this report the Committee has referred to Directives of the
European Union, as “EC Directives” given that the
dates of the
Directives referred to in the report precede the formation of the European Union
(EU), as it is now known.
Glossary of Terms
A finite set of well defined rules for the solution of a problem in a finite
number of steps.
Applications program
A program written to perform a specific task for the user. It does not
contribute to the effective functioning of the computer.
Assembler code
A type of programming language used to represent programs in machine code in
a convenient and readable notation.
Compilation
The process whereby a program written in a high level language is converted
into equivalent instructions in machine code or object
Decompilation
The working back from the object code of a computer program to a version of
the source code. This process may involve a substantial
recreation or
reproduction of the source code of the original program. Decompilation is
achieved using a computer program called
a decompiler.
Disassembly
The working back from object code to assembler code ie, a special case of
decompilation. Disassembly is achieved using a computer
program called a
disassembler.
Interface specifications
The description of the logical and physical interconnection and interaction
which allows a hardware or computer program product to
interact as intended with
other hardware, computer program(s) or users.
Interoperability
The ability of computer systems to exchange information and mutually to use
the information which has been exchanged.
Interpretation
The process whereby a source code program is translated “on the
run” one instruction at a time into machine code or object
Machine code
The class of computer languages which can be interpreted directly by
computers (machines) without a translation process.
A very low level machine code used to write programs that define machine
instructions to perform specific machine operational functions
eg. moving or
examining data.
Micro computer
A computer system that uses a microprocessor as its processing unit.
Microprocessor
An integrated circuit that performs the function of the central processor of
a computer. It carries out the arithmetic, logic, and
control functions.
Object code
The class of computer programs expressed in machine code which contain
additional information necessary to ensure correct loading
of the program.
Operating systems program
A program or collection of programs used to manage hardware and software
resources on a computer for the user.
Personal computer
A general purpose computer designed for operation and use by one person at a
Making the arrangements necessary so that a computer program that runs on one
computer is able to run on a differently configured
computer. This generally
involves modification of the original program.
Public domain computer programs
Programs which the copyright owner has granted the world at large a licence
to reproduce. While copyright subsists in such programs
the copyright owner is,
however, precluded from claiming damages from anyone who reproduces the
Reverse engineering
The study or analysis of a computer product (including a computer program) in
order to reveal the underlying idea or principle on
which it operates. This
analysis may include an examination of relevant published documentation, study
of the operation of the product
and, in the case of a computer programs, their
decompilation. Studying the operation of a program would involve reproduction of
program in the same way as normal use.
Programs which have been made available for users to reproduce and use free
of charge subject to certain conditions. Typical conditions
that apply to
shareware are that it is freely transferable to other users provided that after
a specified period the user(s) cease
to use it or make a payment to the
Source code
The class of computer languages expressed in human readable form and which
allow a human programmer to work with instructions rather
than binary or
hexadecimal numbers.
Terms of Reference
1.01 On 19 October 1988 the then acting Attorney-General, Senator Michael
Tate, announced the inquiry by the Copyright Law Review
Committee into the
reference were:
Whether the
, as amended by the
Amendment Act 1984
, adequately and appropriately protects computer programs
in human and machine readable forms, works created by or with the assistance
computer programs, and works stored in computer memory.
, which expressly extended
the first judicial consideration
of the application of the
(the Act), as it previously stood, to computer programs. It may be
useful if, at the outset, attention is drawn to the definition
“computer program” which was introduced into the Act by the 1984
amendments and was central to the Committee’s
deliberations:
“computer program” means an expression, in any language, code or
notation, of a set of instructions (whether with or without
related information)
intended, either directly or after either or both of the following:
(a) conversion to another language, code or notation;
(b) reproduction in a different material form;
to cause a device having digital information processing capabilities to
perform a particular function.
1.03 The Committee received two additions to its terms of reference. By
letter dated 5 January 1989, the then Attorney-General, Mr
Bowen, asked the
Committee to review its 1988
Report on the Importation Provisions of the
as it applies to computer programs. On 18 January 1991
the acting Attorney-General, Senator Michael Tate, asked the Committee to
Whether there is any need to amend
provide expressly that the copyright in a published edition extends beyond
reproduction by a means that includes a photographic
process to reproduction
from a database where entry of the work was effected by purely electronic or
mechanical means.
SCOPE OF THE TERMS OF REFERENCE
1.04 In the period that the Committee has had the reference, an enormous
amount of relevant literature has been published. Much of
this material is
important and a deal of it is comparatively recent. It originates principally
from the academic and legal communities
and the industry. It is to be found in a
variety of textbooks, academic, legal and computer industry journals, and other
publications. The Committee has attempted to keep abreast of this
material but does not pretend that it is familiar with it all.
The task of
endeavouring to keep up with it has been a continuing one, made the more arduous
by the rate of development of the technology
with which it is dealing, and the
local and international events to which reference has been made.
1.05 The Committee is extremely grateful for the assistance it has received.
Many of the written submissions, both prior to the release
of the Draft Report
and later in response to it, have been painstakingly prepared and are of a most
elaborate kind. In addition,
the Committee greatly benefited from the open
discussion which took place at the public hearings and, despite the fact that
were held in camera, the demonstrations conducted by IBM Australia Ltd, the
Supporters of Interoperable Systems in Australia and
Alcatel Australia Ltd. The
Committee has also benefited from its informal discussions, which one or more of
its members and its Secretary
have had, with various persons, particularly those
from the academic and legal communities. The Chairman is particularly grateful
for the helpful discussions he had with representatives of a number of United
States companies when he was in the United States in
1.06 The Committee’s task has been a most onerous one. It has involved
the Committee in a substantial number of long meetings.
Since 1989 it has met,
mainly in connection with this reference, on more than 20 occasions, each time
for a full day or more. It
has had a voluminous reading task and it has had the
difficulty of reaching conclusions on a large number of issues about many of
which there is considerable disagreement in academic and industry circles both
in Australia and overseas.
1.07 It should be clear that the Committee has endeavoured to confine its
report to the issues which appear to it to be the most significant
recognises that it may have omitted some matters which some may think are
important, but it believes that it has covered
the critical ones. A further
point that needs to be made is that the Committee has thought it unwise to write
an elaborate treatise
on computers and computer programs or on the law of this
and other countries relating to them. The Committee has endeavoured to produce
report which contains sufficient reference to the technology and the applicable
law, both statutory and judge-made, and to concentrate
on the principal
considerations relevant to the determination of each issue.
1.08 This report is to the Minister for Justice, but it is clear that it will
be widely read and considered by relevant interests
both here and overseas. An
attempt has been made to write a report which will be able to be comprehended,
not only by persons well
familiar with the technology and the issues which are
in question, but also by others with less familiarity with what is involved
members of Parliament) who will nevertheless have the task of considering the
report and deciding whether or not its recommendations
implemented.
Summary of Recommendations
2.01 Before stating its recommendations, the Committee wishes to emphasise
that none of its recommendations is intended to undermine
or weaken the quality
of the protection of the rights which owners of computer programs should
reasonably have. The Committee is
aware that the Australian Government is
committed to the proper protection of intellectual property rights of all kinds.
The Committee
is strongly supportive of the Government’s commitment in
that regard. Nevertheless, in the creation and protection of any property
rights, an attempt must be made to strike the right balance between adequate
protection and the need to provide the community with
reasonable access to
intellectual property and the benefits which it confers. That there should be
such access is important to the
owners of intellectual property themselves as
well as to potential users of it and the public generally. The striking of the
is something which must be attempted in the public interest. The task
has not been an easy one.
2.02 The Committee’s Draft Report on Computer Software Protection was
released for public comment in June 1993 and 42 responses
were received by the
Committee in response to its draft recommendations. The Committee has considered
all of those submissions in
revising its Draft Report and some have led the
Committee to change its draft recommendations. Of most significance are the
Committee’s
changed recommendations in relation to the definition of
reproduction in the Act (Chapter 6), s.35 of the Act concerning ownership
(Chapter 7) and the parallel importation provisions (Chapter 11). The Committee
has also revised its recommendations in relation
to computer-generated materials
(Chapter 13), published edition copyright (Chapter 15) and some exceptions to
exclusive rights in
Chapter 10, such as back-up copying, decompilation for error
correction and the overlap between the
(the Act) and
Circuit Layouts Act 1989
. The recommendations from each chapter of
the report are set out below. References to paragraph numbers in brackets are to
paragraphs in which the recommendation is made in the respective
CHAPTER 4 — FORM OF PROTECTION
2.03 The Committee confirms its draft recommendation that computer programs
(see paragraph 2.04(c) for the recommended definition
of this expression) should
continue to be protected under the Act as “literary works”(paragraph
CHAPTER 6 — THE 1984 AMENDMENTS
2.04 The Committee has amended the recommendations it made in the Draft
Report and now recommends that:
(a) The words “whether or not in a visible form” be deleted from
the definition of “literary work” in s.10(1)
of the Act (paragraph
(b) A definition of computer program be retained in the Act (paragraph
(c) The definition of “computer program” in s.10(1) of the Act be
deleted and the following definition substituted (paragraph
A “computer program” is a set of statements or instructions to be
used directly or indirectly in a computer in order to
bring about a certain
(d) No change be made to the Act to expressly state that algorithms are not
protected (paragraph 6.36)
(e) A clarifying definition of “reproduction” be added to the Act
in relation to:
(i) Works stored electronically—The Act should deem that the mere
conversion of a work or an adaptation of a work from its hard
readable form to an electronic form of storage, such as digital, which is
machine readable and which, when printed out
is unintelligible by reason of
consisting of machine readable symbols to be a reproduction of the work or
the adaptation. Similarly the instance of converting a work or an adaptation
from an electronic form to a hard copy, such as making
a print out of a work
stored electronically should be encompassed in the deeming definition (paragraph
(ii) Computer programs in source code and object code—Given the close
relationship between source code and object code versions
of a program, a
majority of the Committee recommend that the Act should be amended by the
addition of a deeming provision which provides
that, in relation to a computer
program, a reproduction includes (but is not limited to) (paragraph 6.76):
) an object code version of the program that has been derived from
the program in source code by compilation; and
) a source code version of the program that has been derived from
the program in object code by decompilation.
The Chairman is of the opinion that there should be no definition of
“reproduction” and that any problem can be overcome
change to the definition of “adaptation”.
(f) No change be made to the definition of “adaptation” in
s.10(1) of the Act (paragraph 6.77)
(g) No change be made to the definition of “material form” in
s.10(1) of the Act (paragraph 6.87).
CHAPTER 7 — OWNERSHIP
2.05 In the Draft Report the Committee recommended that s.35 of the Act be
amended (draft recommendation 2.04). The Committee now
recommends that no change
be made to the provisions of s.35(6) of the Act in its application to computer
programs (paragraphs 7.05
CHAPTER 8 — DURATION
2.06 The Committee affirms its draft recommendation that:
(a) The existing term of protection consisting of life of the author plus 50
years should apply to all computer programs, whether
published or unpublished
(paragraph 8.03)
(b) No other changes should be made to the existing provisions of the Act
concerning duration of protection of computer programs (paragraph
CHAPTER 9 — EXCLUSIVE RIGHTS
Economic rights
2.07 The Committee affirms its recommendation made in the Draft Report that
the owner of copyright in a computer program should have
the same exclusive
rights as are provided for in s.31 of the Act in relation to literary works.
The right to reproduce the work in a material
2.08 The Committee recommends that the author of a computer program should
have the exclusive right to control the reproduction of
his or her work
(paragraph 9.08).
Look and feel
2.09 The Committee recommends that no amendments should be made to the Act to
establish additional protection for the “look
and feel” of computer
programs (paragraph 9.42).
Screen displays
2.10 In the Draft Report the Committee sought submissions on the need for
additional or alternative protection for screen displays
(paragraph 9.42). After
considering the submissions received following the Draft Report, the Committee
has decided not to recommend
any additional form of protection for screen
displays (paragraph 9.48).
The other rights in section 31
2.11 The unauthorised exercise of these rights should continue to constitute
an infringement of copyright subject only to the existing
exceptions in the Act,
as modified by the Committee’s recommendations concerning normal use,
back-up copying, the making of
adaptations to improve or facilitate use, error
correction, and decompilation for interoperability. The rights which the
owner should have are:
(a) the right to make an adaptation of the work; (paragraph 9.53)
(b) the right to publish the work; (paragraph 9.58)
(c) the right to perform the work in public, to the extent that it is
relevant to computer programs; (paragraph 9.64)
(d) the right to broadcast the work, taking account of the recommendations in
the Copyright Convergence Group’s (CCG) report;
and (paragraph 9.68)
(e) the right to cause the work to be transmitted to subscribers to a
diffusion service, subject to the recommendation of the CCG
that this right be
encompassed within a new transmission right (paragraph 9.74).
A right to control rental of computer
2.12 The Committee affirms its draft recommendation that a rental right be
introduced for copyright owners of computer programs, noting
recommendation has now been fully endorsed by the TRIPS Agreement. The right
should be subject to the limitation that it
does not extend to copies of
computer programs incorporated in utilitarian articles or other articles where
the program cannot be
copied during the ordinary use of those articles
(paragraph 9.85).
A right to control public lending of computer
2.13 In the Draft Report the Committee expressed the view that a public
lending right with respect to computer programs should not
be introduced, but
invited further comment on this issue. Following its consideration of the five
submissions that it received commenting
on the possible introduction of such a
right, the Committee still inclines to the view that an exclusive right to
authorise or prohibit
public lending of computer programs by copyright owners
should not be introduced at this time. However, the Committee considers
this issue could be reviewed at a future date as part of the Committee’s
review of the Act (paragraph 9.92).
Moral rights
2.14 The Committee is of the view that authors of computer programs should
not be in a worse position than authors of other literary
works. However, the
Committee remains of the view, that it expressed in its 1988
Report on Moral
that there is no need for the enactment of specific new legislation
for the protection of moral rights of authors, including authors
of computer
programs. But, the Committee does endorse the approach proposed in the
Discussion Paper,
Proposed Moral Rights Legislation for Copyright
, as a general test to determine whether exercising a moral right is
reasonable in any particular case (paragraph 9.99).
CHAPTER 10 — EXCEPTIONS TO EXCLUSIVE RIGHTS
Copying for normal use
2.15 The Committee affirms its draft recommendation that the Act should be
amended to expressly provide that the copyright in a computer
program is not
infringed by a person who copies that program where the making of that copy is
reasonable or necessary for the normal
use of the program (paragraphs 10.12).
Back-up copying
2.16 The Committee recommends that s.43A(1)(b) of the
Act be amended
so that it applies to licensees (paragraph 10.14).
2.17 The Committee affirms it draft recommendations that s.43A be amended
(paragraph 10.15):
(a) to enable a person who has made a back-up copy of a computer program to
use that back-up copy and store the original copy ;
(b) to provide that, if the original copy or a back-up copy is destroyed or
damaged, the surviving original or back-up copy, as the
case may be, may be used
to make another back-up copy; and
(c) to provide that there should be no limitation on the number of times that
the making of a copy in accordance with (b) may be done.
2.18 The Committee reverses its draft recommendation that the amendments to
s.43A should be made subject to s.43A(2)(b) and now recommends
that s.43A(2)(b)
be repealed (paragraph 10.16).
2.19 The Committee recommends that the right to make a back-up copy should
not extend to copies of computer programs that have been
“locked” by
the copyright owner against the making of copies (paragraph 10.17)
Ephemeral back-up copying by businesses
2.20 The Committee recommends that the Act be amended to provide that the
making of an ephemeral back-up copy of a computer program
that is incidental to
the normal back-up copying of business data for security purposes is not an
infringement of copyright (paragraph
Third party maintainers
2.21 The Committee recommends that the copying of computer programs by third
party maintainers should remain a matter for negotiation
between the third party
maintainer and the copyright owner (paragraph 10.20).
Reverse engineering and decompilation
2.22 The Committee recommends that reverse engineering that includes
decompilation should be prohibited except to the extent that
it is required for
interoperability and error correction (paragraph 10.25).
Interoperability with other programs and
2.23 The Committee recommends that decompilation of a computer program should
be allowed where it is necessary to achieve the interoperability
independently created computer program or hardware device with other programs or
hardware devices
provided (paragraph 10.58):
(a) decompilation is performed by the owner of a lawfully acquired copy of
the program or another person having a right to use the
copy or on their behalf
by a person authorised to do so; and
(b) the information necessary
to achieve interoperability has not
previously been readily available; and
(c) the acts are confined to those
necessary to achieve
interoperability.
The following limitations should apply:
(i) the decompilation should only be used to achieve interoperability;
(ii) the information obtained should only be given to others when necessary
for the interoperability of the independently created
computer program or
hardware device
Modification for enhanced performance
2.24 The Committee recommends that the right to modify computer programs for
enhanced performance should remain a matter for negotiation
between the
purchaser and the copyright owner (paragraph 10.59).
Modification for networking
2.25 The Committee recommends that the right to modify computer programs to
run on networks should be left to negotiation between
the purchaser and the
2.26 The Committee recommends that (paragraph 10.68):
(a) that the right to decompile for such purposes should continue to be a
matter for negotiation with the copyright owner; and
(b) no change should be made to the Act to allow porting.
Decompilation for error correction
2.27 The Committee has reconsidered its draft recommendations in relation to
decompilation for error correction and now recommends
(a) the Act should be amended to provide that decompilation for error
correction should not be an infringement of copyright where
an error free
version of the program cannot be obtained within a reasonable time at a normal
commercial price (paragraphs 10.75 and
10.85); and
(b) decompilation for error correction be allowed to ensure the correct
operation of a program with another program(s) or hardware
device(s) provided
(paragraph 10.84):
(i) decompilation is performed by the owner of a lawfully acquired copy of
the program or another person having a right to use the
copy or on their behalf
by a person authorised to do so;
(ii) a version of the computer program free of the error has not previously
been readily available;
(iii) the acts are confined to those necessary to correct the error; and
(iv) a version of the program free of the error is not available within a
reasonable time at a normal commercial price.
The following limitations should apply:
(i) the decompilation should only be used to achieve error correction;
(ii) the information obtained should only be given to others for the purpose
of correcting errors.
Decompilation to understand techniques
2.28 The Committee has reconsidered its draft recommendation that
decompilation to understand techniques should be governed by the
fair dealing
provisions of the Act and adds the qualification to this recommendation that the
fair dealing provisions of the Act
should only apply in respect of
“non-commercial” activities (paragraph 10.89).
Decompilation to defeat program
2.29 Subject to the Committee’s recommendations concerning copying for
back-up copying, interoperability and error correction,
the modification of a
locked computer program for the purpose of circumventing the lock should be
prohibited as should subsequent
copying of such modified programs (paragraph
Reproduction in the course of studying the operation
of a program: Article 5(3) of the EC Directive on Computer Programs
2.30 The Committee recommends that the Act be amended to allow the
reproduction and study of computer programs in the circumstances
contemplated in
article 5(3) of the EC
Directive on the Legal Protection of Computer
(paragraph 10.96).
Contracting out
2.31 The Committee recommends that the Act be amended to ensure that the
Committee’s recommendations concerning decompilation,
back-up copying and
reproduction in the course of studying the operation of a program cannot be
avoided by contractual means (paragraph
The onus of proof
2.32 The Committee affirms its draft recommendation that the Act should
provide that a person who copies another’s program without
a licence and
who seeks to justify it under an exception requiring the copying to have had a
certain purpose should have the onus
of satisfying the court that he or she had
that purpose. By way of further qualification, the Committee recommends that a
who is relying on an exception to infringement should have to show that
it was not possible to rely on any warranty in the time available
or required to
fix the software (paragraph 10.108).
Crown use of computer programs
2.33 The Committee affirms its draft
recommendation that s.183 be
amended to provide that copying of computer programs for the services of the
Commonwealth, State and
Territory Governments should be restricted to those
cases where a copy of the program cannot be obtained from the copyright owner
his or her agent within a reasonable time at a normal commercial price
(paragraphs 10.119 and 10.122).
2.34 The Committee affirms its draft recommendation that no change be made to
the Act in respect of unauthorised home copying of computer
programs (paragraph
Shareware and public domain computer
2.35 The Committee affirms its draft recommendation that no amendments be
made to the Act to define the terms “shareware”
and “public
domain computer programs” or to make special provision for these two
categories of computer programs (paragraph
Overlap of the
Circuit Layouts Act 1989

draft recommendation, the Committee recommends that appropriate

made to the Act and the
Circuit Layouts Act 1989
so that (paragraph

CHAPTER 11 — PARALLEL IMPORTATION
Decriminalisation of parallel importation
2.38 The Committee’s 1988 recommendation with respect to
decriminalisation was made in the context of a limited right to allow
importation to meet specific orders where a product was not available. However,
as the Committee is of the view that the
parallel importation provisions, as
they apply to computer programs, should be maintained and that availability of
computer programs
is not a pressing problem, the Committee recommends that
criminal sanctions in respect of parallel importation should remain (paragraph
The importation provisions
The Committee’s recommendations as revised are that:
(a) the restrictions on parallel importation of computer programs remain
unchanged (paragraph 11.96);
(b) the position be reviewed in three years time (one member recommends in
two years time) (paragraph 11.96);
(c) computer manuals that are available separately from computer programs
should be able to be imported in the same way as other books
(paragraph 11.103);
(d) its recommendation concerning the importation provisions should apply
equally to second-hand computer programs (paragraph 11.105).
CHAPTER 12 — EDUCATIONAL USE OF COMPUTER
2.40 The Committee has not changed its draft recommendations that (paragraph
(a) in accordance with the expressed preference of most educational
institutions and makers of computer programs that a statutory
licence scheme for
the educational use of computer programs should not be established; and
(b) the Government review the situation in three years time to determine
whether an optional statutory licence scheme should be introduced
to encourage
voluntary licensing.
2.41 In relation to the issue of authorisation of copyright infringement by
educational institutions, the Committee recommends that
no change be made to the
Act in this respect (paragraph 12.21).
CHAPTER 13 — WORKS MADE WITH THE ASSISTANCE OF
COMPUTER PROGRAMS AND COMPUTER-GENERATED MATERIALS
2.42 The Committee has revised its draft recommendations and now recommends
(a) protection be afforded to “computer-generated material” as a
new class of subject matter other than works in the Act
(paragraph 13.17).
(b) a definition of the term “computer-generated” be added to the
Act that provides (paragraph 13.18):
“computer-generated”, in relation to computer-generated material
means that the material is generated by computer in
circumstances such that
there is no human author of the material.
(c) the Act be amended to provide that with respect to authorship of
computer-generated material, the author shall be taken to be
the person by whom
the arrangements necessary for the creation of the material are undertaken
(paragraph 13.21).
(d) the Act be amended to provide that the term of protection of
“computer-generated” material be 25 years from the end
of the year
in which the material is made (paragraph 13.23).
CHAPTER 14 — WORKS STORED IN COMPUTER MEMORY:
A use right
2.43 The Committee considers that the introduction of a use right for
into and from
computer memory is not justified or necessary and does not recommend any
further consideration of the introduction
of such an additional right (paragraph
Electrocopying
2.44 The Committee does not recommend the introduction of a statutory licence
in relation to the electrocopying of works (paragraph
2.45 The Committee recommends that the Act be amended to extend the

(paragraph 14.16).
Liability of database operators

to include a provision to the effect that where the

the database to subscribers would not, of
itself, amount to authorisation of the making of such copies if the operator

Copying by libraries and educational
institutions

(including electronic

applicable, as now apply to the making of hard copies (paragraph 14.25).

(paragraph 14.27):
(a) the reference to “reprographic” copies is deleted; and

Section 40—fair dealing
2.50 The Committee does not consider that a sufficient case has been made out
for recommending explicit exclusion of electrocopying
from the concept of
“copying” in s.40 and therefore recommends that no change be made to
the section (paragraph 14.29).
Screen displays
2.51 The Committee does not make any recommendation regarding the
transmission right proposed by the Copyright Convergence Group in
Highways to Change—Copyright in the New Communications Environment
but it supports in principle its introduction and its application to screen
displays (paragraph 14.40).
The Committee affirms its draft recommendation that the Act be
amended to make it clear that screen displays do not constitute a reproduction
in a material form of works stored in computer memory (paragraph 14.42).
2.53 The Committee affirms the view expressed in the Draft Report that it
should not recommend the introduction of a “display
right” for works
stored in computer memory (paragraph 14.43).
2.54 The Committee also affirms its draft recommendation that, if its
recommendation in regard to the jurisdiction of the Copyright
Tribunal is
accepted then, an amendment be made to the Act requiring the Copyright Tribunal
to take into account a proposal by a
display access when arbitrating a dispute under Part VI of the Act (paragraph
2.55 The Committee recommends, as it did in the Draft Report, that the Act be
amended to provide that the screen display of a work
stored in computer memory
does not, by itself, constitute a public performance of that work (paragraph
The Committee recommends no change to the diffusion right, while
recognising that the recommendations of the Copyright Convergence
implemented, will impact on this right (paragraph 14.50).
Definition of literary work
2.57 The Committee adheres to the recommendation it made in the Draft Report
that no amendment is necessary to the definition of literary
work either to
specifically include a reference to computer databases or to define compilation
(paragraph 14.64).
Term of protection
2.58 The Committee remains of the view that databases should be protected for
the same period as other literary works and, accordingly,
does not recommend any
change to the Act (paragraph 14.65).
2.59 As the Committee is limited by its terms of reference, the Committee
does not recommend any change in the law regarding the authorship
of works which
are created and stored in computer memory (paragraph 14.72).
2.60 The Committee does not recommend any change to the law regarding the
ownership of copyright in respect of works stored or created
in computer memory
being of the view that the mere fact that works are in this form of storage does
not of itself warrant or require
any change in the Act (paragraph 14.73).
Protection of non-original databases
2.61 The Committee is of the view that a decision that Australia adopt a
right of unfair extraction should be deferred until consideration
has been given
to the final form of the EC
Amended Proposal for a Council Directive on the
Legal Protection of Databases
and accordingly the Committee recommends
further review of this issue bearing in mind developments in relation to this
(paragraph 14.80).
2.62 In its Draft Report, the Committee invited comment on the form of
protection, if any, that should be afforded hypertexts. Having
reconsidered the
issue in the light of only one comment on protection of hypertexts, the
Committee makes no recommendation on additional
or alternative protection for
hypertexts (paragraph 14.82).
Multi-media
2.63 Having given further consideration to the nature of multi-media
materials and the issue of copyright protection for multi-media,
the Committee
(paragraph 14.87):
(a) does not recommend the creation of a new category of protection for
multi-media; and
(b) does accept that affording protection to multi-media within the category
of “cinematograph film” stretches the generally
understood meaning
of that term and therefore recommends consideration of a more appropriate term,
such as “audiovisual work”
which could encompass cinematograph films
and multi-media materials.
Section 183—government use of copyright
materials held on database
2.64 As the Committee understands that the Government has made a decision to
enact reforms to s.183 which are currently being drafted
for presentation to
Parliament, the Committee does not make any recommendation in relation to s.183
as it applies to government use
of copyright materials held on database
(paragraph 14.91).
CHAPTER 15 — PUBLISHED EDITION COPYRIGHT
2.65 The Committee has revised its draft recommendations and now recommends
(a) the words “by a means that includes a photographic process, a
reproduction” in s.88 of the Act be deleted and the
facsimile copy” be inserted as their replacement (paragraph 15.14);
(b) with the exception of one member, the Act be amended to provide that
published edition copyright be extended to include publication
in computer or
machine readable format (paragraph 15.22).

Privilege against self-incrimination

Possession in the course of a business
2.71 In the Draft Report the Committee sought further comments on the
creation of a civil right of action by a copyright owner against
a person for
possession, for commercial purposes, of infringing copies of his or her works
known to be such, ie, a counterpart action
to the offence found in s.132(2A) of
the Act and whether the wider infringing activity of "possession in the course
of business"
(as in the UK) would be more appropriate. The Committee now
recommends that (paragraph 16.37):
(a) the Act be amended so that an employer will be guilty of an offence where
he or she is found in possession of an article which
he or she knew, or ought to
have reasonably known, is an infringing copy of a work;
(b) consistent with the Committee’s recommendation that software
penalties be brought into line with those applying in respect
of unauthorised
commercial copying of cinematographic films:
(i) a first offence be punishable with a fine not exceeding $1,500 for the
article, or for each article, to which the offence relates
or imprisonment for a
period not exceeding 2 years, or both; or if the person is a body
corporate—a fine not exceeding $7,500
for the article, or for each
article, to which the offence relates; and
(ii) for any subsequent offence a penalty not exceeding $50,000 for an
individual, or 5 years imprisonment, or both; or if the person
corporate—a fine not exceeding $250,000.

COURSE OF THE INQUIRY
3.01 The Committee advertised for submissions in February 1989. In April 1990
the Committee released an Issues Paper based on the
submissions received. A list
of the parties who made submissions appears in Appendix A. About 400 copies of
the Issues Paper were
distributed. In early July 1990 the Committee wrote to all
organisations and individuals who had made submissions, and other interested
parties, announcing its intention to hold public hearings in Sydney on 26 and 27
July 1990. The hearings were conducted in the Ground
Floor Theatrette, State
Office Block, 74-96 Phillip Street, Sydney. A list of those who made submissions
to the Committee at the
public hearings is to be found in Appendix B. At the
public hearings the Committee invited further submissions concerning issues
raised during the course of the hearings. A list of those organisations and
individuals that made submissions following the hearings
is contained in
Appendix C.
3.02 At the public hearings, IBM Australia Limited (IBM) offered to conduct a
demonstration of decompilation for the Committee. This
took place in Melbourne
on 22 November 1990. IBM insisted on the demonstration being conducted in
camera. The Committee agreed to
this request on the grounds that it should have
the benefit of the demonstration and that it has always observed requests for
submissions
to it to be treated on a confidential basis. Nevertheless, it would
have preferred to have been able to give other interests an opportunity
present, particularly those who advocate the introduction of a qualified right
to engage in reverse engineering and decompilation
of computer programs for
certain limited purposes. Having said this, the Committee acknowledges that IBM,
along with numbers of other
computer manufacturers and suppliers, has a
continuing anxiety about piracy. Any step at all which tends to facilitate
accessibility
to information about how a particular program operates is seen as
a threat to the program’s integrity. Experience shows that
these fears are
not without foundation.
3.03 On 7 February 1991, in response to IBM’s demonstration to the
Committee, a group of companies operating under the title
“Supporters of
Interoperable Products in Australia” (SISA) (listed in Appendix E) made a
presentation to the Committee.
They sought to demonstrate the importance of the
development of interoperable computer products and the concept of open systems.
The Committee was also requested to treat this demonstration on a confidential
3.04 Following this demonstration, the Committee visited Alcatel–STC
Australia Limited’s (Alcatel) electronic switching
design section in the
Sydney suburb of Alexandria. During this visit, Mr P Conrick, Manager of
Alcatel’s Patent Department,
made a short presentation to the Committee
concerning the industrial applications of computer programs in which he outlined
concerns over the current scope of protection afforded to
computer programs. Mr Conrick explained the operation of modern telephone
switching and communications equipment.

of the efforts of those responsible for the submissions, which have

3.10 Since the Committee received its reference, several important

Directive on
Rental right and lending right and on certain rights

. The implications

Uruguay Round of GATT
3.12 On 15 December 1993 in Geneva representatives of the contracting states
party to the General Agreement on Tariffs and Trade (GATT)
1947 agreed on the
Final Act embodying the results of the Uruguay Round of Multilateral Trade
Negotiations, which included an Agreement
on Trade-Related Aspects of
Intellectual Property Rights, commonly referred to as the TRIPS Agreement. On 15
April 1994 at Marrakech,
Ministers representing the governments of those
countries formally signed the Final Act, which is expected to enter into force,
ratification, on 1 January 1995 or, failing that, 1 July 1995.
3.13 The TRIPS Agreement imposes obligations on more than 115 member
countries, including Australia, in relation to "Copyright and
Related Rights,
Trademarks, Geographical Indications, Industrial Designs, Patents,
Layout-Designs (Topographies) of Integrated Circuits,
Protection of Undisclosed
Information and Control of Anti-Competitive Practices in Contractual Licences"
(Part II). The Agreement
also imposes very extensive and detailed obligations in
relation to "Enforcement of Intellectual Property Rights", including "General
Obligations, Civil and Administrative Procedures and Remedies, Provisional
Measures, Special Requirements Related to Border Measures
and Criminal
Procedures" (Part III).
3.14For the purposes of this report, the section of the TRIPS Agreement on
"Copyright and Related Rights" includes the requirement
that members comply with
articles 1–21 (except art.6
on moral rights) of the latest
(1971) Act of the Berne Convention for the Protection of Literary and Artistic
Works (Berne Convention)
(art.9(1)—Appendix O). Article 10(1) requires
Computer programs, whether in source or object code, shall be protected as
literary works under the Berne Convention (1971).
Article 10(2) requires in part that:
Compilations of data or other material, whether in machine readable or other
form, which by reason of the selection or arrangement
of their contents
constitute intellectual creations shall be protected as such . . .
Article 11 requires members to provide authors and their successors in title
. . . the right to authorise or prohibit the commercial rental to the public
of originals or copies of their copyright works.
which includes computer programs. Article 11 further provides that:
. . . this obligation does not apply to rentals where the program itself is
not the essential object of the rental.
Article 13 provides that:
Members shall confine limitations or exceptions to exclusive rights to
certain special cases which do not conflict with a normal exploitation
work and do not unreasonably prejudice the legitimate interests of the right
3.15Under the transitional arrangements provided for in the Agreement, member
countries have one year from the date of entry into
force of the Agreement
establishing the World Trade Organisation, which was also annexed to the Uruguay
Round Final Act, to apply
the obligations of the TRIPS Agreement. However, up to
an additional four years is allowed to members that are "developing countries"
and those that are (art.65(3)):
. . . in the process of transformation from a centrally-planned into a
market, free-enterprise economy and which [are] undertaking
structural reform of
[their] intellectual property system and facing special problems in the
preparation and implementation of intellectual
property laws.
3.16The TRIPS Agreement has been dealt with at some length here because of
the decisive impact it has on the range of options that
may be considered for
the protection of computer programs and computerised databases. This is apparent
from the very clear import
of articles 9 and 10. The full text of relevant
articles of the TRIPS Agreement is at Appendix O.
WIPO Committee of Experts on a Possible Protocol to
the Berne Convention
3.17 The World Intellectual Property Organisation (WIPO), which is
responsible for the administration of the Berne Convention, has
established a
Committee of Experts on a Possible Protocol to the Convention. Thus far, the
Committee has met three times in Geneva,
on 4–8 November 1991, 10–17
February 1992 and 21–25 June 1993. These three sessions have considered,
amongst other
matters, a draft proposal developed by WIPO for expressly
affirming that protection under the Convention extends to computer programs,
for extending protection to databases and other computer-stored works, and
expert systems. Following the second session, the
Assembly of the Berne Union
(the governing body established by the Convention), at a meeting in September
1992, resolved to omit
computer-produced works and expert systems from the terms
of reference of the Committee of Experts, evidently because of lack of
for their inclusion in the proposed Protocol. Following the third session of the
Committee, and the subsequent conclusion
of the TRIPS Agreement as part of the
Uruguay Round outcome, WIPO prepared a revised proposal for the Protocol for the
consideration
of the Committee at a session scheduled for June 1994. At an
extraordinary meeting on 28–29 April 1994, the Berne Union Assembly
resolved that the session should be deferred till December 1994. At the time of
presenting this report, the Australian Government
is understood to be engaged in
consultations with affected interests on the attitude to be adopted to the WIPO
"second generation"
proposal for the Protocol.
Washington Treaty on integrated circuits
3.18 On 26 May 1989, the Treaty on Intellectual Property in Respect of
Integrated Circuits (Washington Treaty) opened for signature
in Washington.
Although the Treaty was adopted by a substantial majority, as at 1 January 1994
it had been ratified by only one state
(Egypt) and therefore is not yet in
force. According to WIPO, there are interests in the states that produce the
majority of integrated
circuits that oppose the ratification of or accession to
the Treaty. This may explain the reluctance of other states to ratify a
which they see as having very little practical value without the membership of
those major producer states. According to representatives
Attorney-General’s Department, Australia has taken no action towards
accession pending the outcome of the GATT TRIPs
negotiations.
3.19As already mentioned (paragraphs 3.13–3.14 above), the TRIPS
Agreement imposes obligations on member countries in respect
“Layout-Designs (Topographies) of Integrated Circuits”. Article 35
of TRIPS requires members to provide protection
for layout-designs in accordance
with articles 2–7 of the Washington Treaty (except for articles 6(3)), 12
and 16(3)) of the
Washington Treaty), and articles 36–38 make further
provision regarding the scope of protection, acts not requiring the
authorisation
of the right holder and the term of protection.
3.20 Prior to the conclusion of the Washington Treaty, Australia enacted the
Circuit Layouts Act 1989
which provides protection for integrated
circuits in similar measure to that provided for under the Treaty. This
enactment secured
temporary protection for Australian integrated circuit layouts
under the United States
Semiconductor Chip Protection Act 1984.
Committee understands that the Attorney-General’s Department is of the
view that the
Circuit Layouts Act
satisfies the obligations
under articles 35–38 of TRIPS. On that basis, it seems reasonable to
expect that protection of Australian
layouts will be made permanent under the US
National developments in Australia’s trading
3.21 Several of Australia’s major trading partners have amended their

to comply with its requirements (
Berne Convention

(PL 100–568)). In the same year, the United

, which made

amongst other matters, creates criminal penalties where there has been an

, so as to implement the requirements of TRIPS,
