Threatened unilateral action by the United
3.22Under the US
Trade Act 1974
, as amended by the
Omnibus Trade
and Competitiveness Act 1988
, the US Trade Representative must annually
identify countries that deny “adequate and effective” intellectual
protection and “fair and equitable” market access to US
businesses that depend on intellectual property protection. Countries
have the most unacceptable standards are designated “priority foreign
countries” and investigated for possible
trade sanctions under
“special” s.301 of the
Trade Act 1974
. A middle tier, the
“priority watch list”, is reserved for countries whose regimes need
careful monitoring. Those countries
presenting the least concern are cited as
“watch list” countries.
3.23On 30 April 1994, the US Trade Representative announced the listings
following the latest annual review. Australia, which had
previously been on the
“priority watch list”, was transferred to the watch list. This
evidently reflected satisfaction
by the US Government with the making of
regulations in April 1994 extending performers’ protection under the
Australian Copyright
Act to US nationals and residents. The Committee understood
that one of the reasons for Australia’s previous inclusion in the
“priority watch list” was the concern of the US Government that the
Committee might recommend weakening protection for
computer software. The
Committee notes that the decision to include Australia in the “priority
watch list” in 1993, announced
in April 1993, preceded the release of the
Committee’s Draft Report in June 1993. The Committee would like to think
Draft Report, providing authoritative documentation of the
Committee’s views, served to reassure the US Government and remove
misapprehension that it might have previously had about the Committee’s
intentions. In any case, as the Committee understands
has been made clear to the
US Government, the Committee’s views will not, by themselves, bring about
any change in the law:
implementation of any of the Committee’s
recommendations would require a Government decision to adopt them and then
of implementing legislation through the Parliament.
3.24The Committee notes a report in
(23 May 1994) that Japan,
which was included in the “priority watch list” announced by the US
Trade Representative on
30 April 1994, was so listed because of, inter alia,
“possible weakening of copyright protection for computer programs”.
The attention of the Committee had been drawn to press reports to the effect
that an advisory council established in July 1993 by
the Japanese Cultural
Affairs Agency was understood to have been inclined towards recommending
adoption of a broad right to decompile
computer programs, and that officials of
the US Trade Representative had expressed great concern at this possibility.
3.25In June 1994, a representative of the Copyright Division of the Japanese
Agency for Cultural Affairs provided the Australian Government
with a copy of a
summary of the “Report of the Consultative Committee on Copyright Issues
Relating to Computer Programs”
dated 30 May 1994. On the matter of
“Research and analysis of existing computer programs”, the summary
records that “the
Committee could not reach a conclusion proposing a
specific revision of the Copyright Law at this stage”. It went on:
The Committee considers it appropriate to trust in the developments of case
law and legal theory on the interpretation of the present
provisions, and to
reexamine this issue in the light of future national and international
developments.
The Form of Protection and the Subject Matter to be
CURRENT FORM OF PROTECTION
4.01 One of the fundamental questions which needs to be answered by the
Committee is the form of protection of computer programs which
is to be adopted.
At the moment the principal existing protection which is conferred is to be
found in the
particularly since the
coming into force of the amendments made to the Act by the
Amendment Act 1984
. However, the Act is not the only relevant statute. Some
reference needs also to be made to the
Patents Act 1990
Layouts Act 1989
Designs Act 1906
. Of relevance also is the
law relating to confidential information and passing off and, in that latter
respect, Part V of the
Trade Practices Act 1974
However, the Committee
is focusing its attention on the
4.02 A question which is raised for discussion from time to time in the
relevant literature is whether copyright protection is appropriate.
protection which is afforded by the Act is protection which is based upon
computer programs being, or being treated as, literary
works. It would be
possible to devise a form of protection which would treat computer programs as
if they were appropriately the
subject of protection by copyright legislation
but, instead of treating them as works, treating them as subject matter in the
way as are films, sound recordings, sound broadcasts and published
editions; see Part IV of the Act. It would also be possible to
devise a form of
protection which was not based on any existing regime but was special to the
nature of computer programs. In the
language of some of the commentators it
sui generis
form of protection, such as has been afforded to
integrated circuit layout-designs under the
Circuit Layouts Act 1989
1984 Amendments to the Act
4.03 The 1984 amendments to the Act extended the definition of
“literary work” so as to include within it computer programs
(s.10(1)). Amendments were made to s.10(1) adding new definitions or modifying
existing definitions designed to ensure that there
was a comprehensive set of
provisions having the effect of protecting computer programs as literary works.
These amendments are discussed
in detail in Chapter 6.
4.04 Part III of the
Act, which deals with works (literary, dramatic,
musical and artistic) and so much of Part IV of the Act as deals with films,
with some other provisions of the Act, give effect to Australia’s
obligations as a member of the Berne Convention for the Protection
of Literary
and Artistic Works (Paris Act, 1971) (Berne Convention), to earlier Acts of
which Australia has been a party in its own
right since 1928. In its Draft
Report the Committee noted that if computer programs were literary works within
the Berne Convention,
Australia had an obligation to protect them as such and to
ensure that that protection was in accordance with the requirements of
Convention. The Draft Report further noted that if computer programs were not in
truth literary works but were simply treated
as if they were (in other words
deemed to be literary works), then there was no obligation to give them the
protection for which
the Berne Convention provided. An analysis of the decisions
of the Australian courts and a consideration of the relevant technology
suggested to the Committee that, whilst some computer programs were in truth
literary works, some might not be. More specifically,
the majority of the
Committee concluded that computer programs were literary works in the
conventional sense when they were expressed
in some forms but might not be
literary works when they were expressed in others. The Draft Report then set out
(paragraphs 4.05–4.20)
the Committee’s analysis of the applicability
of the Berne Convention to computer programs in their various forms.
TRIPS Agreement
4.05 As noted in Chapter 3, since the release of the Draft Report, the TRIPS
Agreement has been concluded as part of the Final Act
of the Uruguay Round of
multilateral trade negotiations. The Final Act was agreed on in December 1993 by
more than 115 countries,
including Australia, and signed by ministers of these
governments in April 1994. The Committee understands that Australia will ratify
the Uruguay Round Final Act, of which the Agreement Establishing the World Trade
Organisation will, on entering into force, trigger
the commencement of the
period (of 1 year in the case of Australia and other developed countries) within
which the obligations under
the TRIPS Agreement will have to be implemented.
Article 10(1) of TRIPS, as noted in paragraph 3.14, provides:
Computer programs, whether in source or object code, shall be protected as
literary works under the Berne Convention (1971).
It seems to the Committee that, whatever might have been the extent of the
applicability of the Berne Convention as a result of its
analysis in the Draft
Report, the obligations being undertaken by Australia under the TRIPS Agreement
put an end to all argument
by emphatically requiring all forms of computer
programs to be protected as literary works. With very few notable exceptions the
industrialised and very many of the developing countries of the world will take
on the same obligations (the Committee understands
that China, Russia and
Taiwan, being 3 conspicuous non-members of the GATT, are actively negotiating
admission to the GATT with a
view to becoming original adherents to the Uruguay
Round and, thus, TRIPS).
4.06 In its discussion in the Draft Report (paragraphs 4.21–4.27) of
alternative forms of protection of computer programs to
treatment as a literary
work, the Committee expressed a preference for introduction of
went on to conclude that such a change would risk contravention
of existing
obligations under the Berne Convention, inconsistency with the approach of
Australia's major trading partners and possible
damage to trading relationships
that the change might cause. Comments made on the Draft Report characterised the
Committee’s
conclusion as "an unfortunate failure of conviction". The
matter is foreclosed by the subsequent conclusion of the TRIPS Agreement.
Nevertheless, in deference to those who made submissions querying the
suitability of copyright as the most appropriate form of protection,
Committee has retained in this report, in the paragraphs immediately following,
the analysis that it made and the conclusions
it reached regarding the case for
patent protection as well as for a
sui generis
form of protection for
computer programs.
POSSIBLE FORMS OF PROTECTION OTHER THAN
4.07 In earlier years it was not uncommon to find support for a regime of
protection which, if not pure patent protection, was a patent-type
protection.
So far as the Committee was able to ascertain there was in the submissions made
to it very little, if any, support for
such an approach. If one were to
contemplate it, it would seem that protection would be afforded only where there
was an invention.
For a program to be protected in this way, it, or at least
some substantial features of it, would need to amount to an invention.
particular it would be necessary to show that it was novel, not obvious and
involved an inventive step (
Patents Act 1990
Most computer
programs would not meet these criteria, and yet much skill and effort may go
into the writing of programs with the
consequence that they will reflect a high
degree of originality. Patent-type protection would have the important public
of requiring disclosure in return for the monopoly which would be
granted, but it would have a number of disadvantages not the least
would be the time taken to prepare and lodge for registration an appropriate
specification and to have it examined by the
Patent Office. Copyright or
There is not the uncertainty
which prevails in relation to patents particularly
between the date of the invention and the date when the application is lodged
Report of the Office of Technology Assessment of the United States
(1992), pp. 30–31).
4.08 The Committee did not therefore regard as adequate, or support, the
development of patent or patent-type protection. It should,
however, be
mentioned in passing that patent protection is available in Australia in some
cases. In the Draft Report the Committee
noted that patent offices both here and
overseas have approved specifications in which the invention is a concept or an
idea. Indeed
the Committee notes a recent review of software protection in the
US that states that between 1972–1992 over 23 000 patents
for software
were issued (
Computer Law and Practice
, Vol 10, No. 4, 1994, p.133). In
Australia the present position is that copyright protection is available to
protect original forms
of expression and patent protection to protect an idea or
concept which has been found to be sufficiently inventive. An example is
provided by the decision of Burchett J of the Federal Court in
International
Business Machines Corporation
Commissioner of Patents
[1991] FCA 625
(1991) 33 FCR
). An appeal against the decision was
4.09 A review of the history of possible patent protection for computer
programs was recently made by the full Federal Court in
CCOM Pty Ltd &
Jiejing Pty Ltd & Ors (
[1994] FCA 1168
1994) 122 ALR 417
28 IPR 481.
court referred to the position in the UK and in Australia as a result of the
decision in the
, referred to in paragraph 4.08. The significant
aspect of this later decision is the statement by the Court that computer
are not expressly excluded from patent protection by the
Patents Act
(or the 1952 Act) and that computer programs can be patentable
inventions if they disclose “a manner of manufacture” as
required by
Patents Act 1990
. In the course of their joint
judgement, Spender, Gummow and Heerey JJ noted that, unlike s.1(2)(c) of the UK
Patents Act 1977
, which expressly states that a program for a computer is
not an invention and therefore not patentable, the Australian
Patents Act
does not expressly exclude computer programs from qualifying for
protection. In the particular case before them, concerning a word
program that assembled text in Chinese language characters, the Court found that
it should not have been denied patent
protection on the ground that it was not a
“manner of manufacture”.
Sui generis
4.10 In 1977 the World Intellectual Property Organisation (WIPO), evidently
concluding that copyright protection was inappropriate
for computer software,
Model Provisions on the Protection of Computer Software
Industrial Property
265) which proposed a
sui generis
of protection. The model provisions contained definitions of “computer
program”, “program description”,
“supporting
material”, “computer software” and “proprietor”.
“Computer program” was
defined to mean:
a set of instructions capable, when incorporated in a machine-readable
medium, of causing a machine having information-processing
capabilities to
indicate, perform or achieve a particular function, task or result.
“Computer software” was defined to mean any or several of the
items “computer program”, “program description”
“supporting material”.
Section 2(1)
provided that the rights which
were conferred in respect of computer software should belong to the person who
created it except where
it was created by an employee in the course of his
employment, in which case the rights, subject to a contrary agreement, were to
belong to the employer.
provided for originality and s.4 said that the
rights conferred should not extend to the concepts on which the software was
dealt with the rights of the proprietor,
with infringement
with the duration of the rights which would be conferred.
provided that, subject to paragraph (b), the rights should expire at the
end of a period of 20 years calculated from the earlier
of two dates, namely,
the date when the program was first used and the date when the software was
first sold, leased or licensed.
Section 7(2)(b)
provided that the rights
conferred should in no case extend beyond 25 years from the time that the
software was created. Section
8 dealt with relief—injunction and
damages—and
dealt with the application of other laws. The purpose of
was to make it clear that it would be permissible for members of the Union
to confer additional protection on computer programs by
the application of the
general principles of law or of any other law such as patent law, copyright law
or the law on unfair competition.
4.11 The Model Provisions failed to gain acceptance as countries increasingly
turned to copyright as the preferred form of protection
for software. It is
clear that, if they had been adopted, it would have been possible for there to
be much more flexibility in the
protection of computer programs than is
presently the case. Laws could have been made with special regard to the nature
of computer
programs. They would not have been lumped in with other literary
works and thus governed by general provisions which, in many respects,
appropriate.
4.12 The foregoing discussion would suggest that, if one were to devise a
sui generis
regime of protection, a copyright-type approach would seem to
be the preferable option. Copyright protects forms of expression, but
It is clear that the essence of a computer program in whatever code it is
expressed is its form. That is where its originality
is to be found. It may
be—indeed it will usually be the case—that the form of expression
will have been devised in order
to give effect to an original idea or concept,
perhaps an algorithm, which the author had. But it is the form of expression
will enable that idea or concept to be realised. However, it is not the
idea or concept which ought to be the subject of protection
unless it meets the
criteria which must be present for the grant of letters patent for an invention.
To protect an idea or concept
which does not meet these criteria would amount to
conferring on the author a monopoly, which would prevent others having a similar
idea or concept from developing a program to achieve it. It would be manifestly
contrary to the public interest to confer such wide
protection. An
author’s creativity will be sufficiently protected and rewarded by
ensuring that the form of expression which
has been used is not infringed by
another program which is in whole or in substantial measure a reproduction of
that of the author.
PREFERENCE FOR COPYRIGHT PROTECTION FOR COMPUTER
4.13 Treating computer programs as literary works under copyright law does
not appear to the Committee to give rise to any matter
of serious contention, at
least in the industry. That approach found broad agreement and support in the
various submissions which
the Committee received. In summary those submissions
completion of formalities or a high degree of novelty or originality (obtaining
protection is therefore inexpensive);
there is a substantial body of case law relating to works
which can appropriately be applied to resolve issues relating to computer
is expressed. Accordingly, copyright protection does not prevent the
independent
development of other forms of expression of the same idea; and
programs overseas. It is important that Australia, from a trade perspective,
a protection regime for programs which is compatible with those of its major
trading partners.
The entry into force of the TRIPS Agreement, with more than
115 adherents, will put this beyond debate.
The bulk of submissions received by the Committee advocated or assumed that
the protection afforded computer programs would be a copyright
protection. But they were not, at least in most cases, concerned whether
programs were to be protected as literary works
or as some other form of subject
matter. What most, but not all, contended was that the form of protection should
be a copyright
form of protection rather than a patent-like form of protection
or some other and new form of protection. The industry has become
would be likely to create a good deal of uncertainty
when, despite difficulties
here and there, there is a common understanding and approach not only in
Australia but in other countries
as well. All these considerations led the
Committee to conclude, in its Draft Report, that the optimum form of protection
was copyright
and, further, the removal of computer programs from
and their placement in
as a separate category of
subject matter. Because of the imperative of art.10 of TRIPS, the last mentioned
conclusion preferring
sui generis
literary works is no longer sustainable.
4.14 In its Draft Report, even though it was before TRIPS had been finalised,
the Committee concluded that it should not recommend
sui generis
protection, despite its evident advantages compared with literary work
protection. Because of the comments made suggesting that
sui generis
protection should not have been rejected, the Committee thinks that it should
recall the reasons given in the Draft Report for doing
so. First and foremost,
for reasons given in paragraphs 4.05–4.20 in the Draft Report, adoption of
sui generis
protection would place Australia in breach of its obligations
under the Berne Convention. Equally importantly it would involve
Australia’s
form of protection being out of step with that conferred in a
number of major countries which include those with whom Australia has
substantial trading links. There were (and still are) very strong views held in
the United States and the European Union that protection
of computer programs as
literary works is the appropriate form of protection. It seemed to the Committee
that this view was entrenched
and, at least in the medium term, was unlikely to
change. The conclusion of TRIPS seems to have reinforced that prediction. The
thought that adoption of its preferred option would not have been
welcomed in a number of other countries important to Australia’s
and trading interests and might, however erroneously, have caused some to have
misgivings about Australia’s commitment
to the proper protection of
computer programs. Australia has a computer program industry of its own but it
is still developing and
it is not particularly substantial, notwithstanding the
promise which it has. Australia is a net importer of computers and computer
programs. That situation will continue for the foreseeable future. What needs to
be stressed is that Australia needs technology of
all kinds for its continuing
development. It must have access to overseas sources for this, not only for the
purposes of its own
computer industry, but also for a vast number of other
purposes, many of them far more important than the support and development
that industry. Governments, government agencies, educational institutions and
the broad range of commercial and industrial undertakings
all require access to
the latest computer technology for their efficient operation and development.
These considerations suggested
to the Committee that Australia should retain,
although with some amendments, the form of protection which it adopted in 1984.
Committee remains of this view and recommends that computer programs
continue to be protected by the Act as literary works (Recommendation
4.15 While the Committee has now concluded that there is an unambiguous
requirement under art.10 of TRIPS to protect computer programs
as literary works
under the Berne Convention, that does not dispose of the need to consider the
most appropriate way of implementing
this obligation in legislation, as is
evident from the discussion in succeeding chapters. On the one hand, the US, so
the Committee
understands, strongly favours leaving the application of literary
work protection to computer programs to the courts, ie. only minimal
legislative
adaptation of literary work protection to the unique features of computer
programs. On the other hand, art.1(1) of the
Directive on the Legal
Protection of Computer Programs
, which requires their protection as literary
works within the meaning of the Berne Convention, includes a number of
provisions specifying
the manner of protection and exceptions to such
protection. The application of the Berne Convention itself to computer programs
currently one of the items on the agenda of the Committee of Experts
considering a possible protocol to the Convention. So while
the conclusion of
the TRIPS Agreement and its intended adoption by Australia may have determined
the issue of the basic form of protection
of computer programs in favour of
resolution of
many issues—of which some have considerable commercial
implications—attributable to the distinctive nature of computer
compared with what may be called conventional literary works.
PROTECTION OF “LOOK AND FEEL” OF
COMPUTER PROGRAMS
4.16 It remains to mention two questions which relate to the subsistence of
in the computer
jargon “look and feel” of a program. Both these matters will be
dealt with in Chapters 14 and 6 respectively,
but in relation to “look and
feel” the following points may be noted. “Look and feel”
involves two matters
which may relate to each other or overlap. First there is
the user interface. This will usually, but not always, concern screen displays.
There is a view that some screen displays or some parts of them may be
registrable as an industrial design. An objection to this
is that what appears
on the screen is transitory or ephemeral so that it does not take a permanent
form. Such an objection was upheld
by a delegate of the Registrar of Designs in
Re Applications by Comshare Inc.
[1991] ADO 2
(1991) 23 IPR 145.
Secondly, there is
what has become described as the “sequence, structure and
organisation” of a program. This relates
to the way in which a program is
organised and structured. The question is whether the author of a program should
have an exclusive
right in relation to its organisation and structure. This may
involve more extensive protection than would be conferred by protecting
form in which a program is expressed. The competing views which are in play are
well discussed in two decisions of the United
States Court of Appeals, namely,
the decision of the Third Circuit in
Whelan Associates
Jaslow Dental
[1986] USCA3 976
797 F.2d 1222
(3rd Cir. 1986) and the decision of the Second
Computer Associates International Inc.
23 IPR 385.
4.17 The United States decisions were given in the context of a statute which
confers conventional copyright protection in respect
of computer programs. The
Committee needs to make recommendations, firstly on the question of what
protection ought to be conferred
in relation to screen displays and the
sequence, structure and organisation of programs, and then whether the measure
of protection
which it recommends needs to be conferred by special provisions of
the legislation additional to those which will protect the form
of the language
or code in which programs are expressed.
Recent History of Australian Law
5.01 Computer programs are currently protected in Australia as literary works
(the Act). In 1984 the Act was amended to
make express provision for the protection of computer programs. The
Amendment Act 1984
amended the definitions in s.10(1) of “literary
work” and “adaptation” and added new definitions for
program” and “material form” as follows:
literary work
” includes:
(a) a table, or compilation, expressed in words, figures or symbols (whether
or not in a visible form); and
(b) a computer program or compilation of computer programs.
(ba) in relation to a literary work being a computer program—a version
of the work (whether or not in the language, code or
notation in which the work
was originally expressed) not being a reproduction of the work.
computer program
” means an expression, in
any language, code or notation, of a set of instructions (whether with or
without related information)
intended, either directly or after either or both
of the following:
(a) conversion to another language, code or notation;
(b) reproduction in a different material form,
to cause a device having digital information processing capabilities to
perform a particular function.
material form
”, in relation to a work or
an adaptation of a work, includes any form (whether visible or not) of storage
from which the work
or adaptation, or a substantial part of the work or
adaptation, can be reproduced.
THE POSITION BEFORE THE 1984 AMENDMENTS
The Apple case
5.02 The 1984 amendments, set out above, were enacted following the first
instance decision in the Federal Court in
Apple Computer Inc
Computer Edge Pty Ltd
(1983) 50 ALR 581
The intent of the
legislature in passing the amendments was to make it clear that computer
programs, whether in source or object
code, are literary works within the
meaning of the Act. The case went on appeal to the full Federal Court and
subsequently to the
High Court. Although the High Court handed down its decision
Computer Edge Pty Ltd
Apple Computer Inc
[1986] HCA 19
(1986) 161 CLR 171
) after the amendments, it interpreted the Act in
its form prior to the 1984 amendments. The substance of the High Court’s
summarised in the Committee’s Issues Paper (April 1990), is as
5.03 The US company, Apple, marketed computers in Australia. Their computers
were operated by an object code program stored in a ROM.
The program was a
systems program which, when the computer was switched on, caused it to take the
action that the program was designed
to achieve. The systems program was derived
from a program in source code. However, before the source program could be used
computer it had to be converted into a program in object code by another
5.04. The defendant, an Australian company, imported Wombat computers. These
contained a program in object code, stored in a ROM,
which had been copied from
the Apple programs in object code. The making of these object code programs did
not necessarily involve
converting the Apple object code back to its source code
5.05 The majority of the High Court, in ruling in favour of the defendant,
(a) programs written in source code were literary works for the purposes of
(b) programs in object code, in that they consisted of electrical impulses
which could not be perceived by the senses and were not
intended to convey any
message to a human being and did not represent words, letters, figures or
symbols, were not literary works;
(c) programs in object code were not translations or other adaptations of the
programs in source code for the following differing
an adaptation must be a “work” and the object code
programs were not;
• object code programs were not a reproduction or a
“translation” of the source codes within paragraph (c)(i) of
definition of “adaptation” in s.10(1), but rather the outcome of the
action taken in compliance with information
conveyed by the source programs;
• “translation” in that definition meant the action or
process of turning from one language into another and not
the expression or
rendering of something in another medium or form;
• reproduction requires two elements:
(i) the infringing work must sufficiently resemble the copyright
work—this element requires a degree of objective similarity
between the
two works; and
(ii) the infringing work must be produced by the use of the copyright work;
The Committee notes that the majority (3:2) decision of the High Court,
namely, that copyright did not protect an operating system
program in object
code stored in ROM chip, is limited in its practical application to programs
copied prior to the 1984 amendments.
provides, in effect, that where, by virtue of amendments made by that
Act, copyright subsists in a computer program made before its
commencement,
nothing done before its commencement in relation to the computer program shall

THE POSITION SINCE THE 1984 AMENDMENTS
The Autodesk case
5.06 The scope of copyright protection for computer programs since the 1984
amendments has received extensive judicial consideration
in the case of
Autodesk Inc
Martin Dyason and others
. The decision at first
instance ((1989)
[1989] FCA 295
went on appeal to the full Federal Court ((1990)
[1990] FCA 469
18 IPR 109)
and subsequently to the High Court. The High Court’s
decision was handed down on 12 February 1992 ((No 1)
[1992] HCA 2
(1992) 173 CLR 330
Autodesk case
). An application to the High Court to review its
decision was refused (
Autodesk Inc
Martin Dyason and others
[1993] HCA 6
(1993) 176 CLR 300
25 IPR 33).
5.07 The facts of the
Autodesk case
can be briefly stated as follows.
Autodesk produced and marketed a computer package called AutoCAD, designed to
assist in architectural
and engineering drafting. Supplied to purchasers with
each copy of the program was a hardware lock. Purchasers of the program were
permitted by Autodesk to make additional copies of the program if they wished to
do so. However, a sub-program in the program called
would, while the program was running, send a series of impulses to the lock. If
no response was received to
these impulses the program would cease to run.
Widget C operated by comparing the responses it received from the AutoCAD lock
a table of 127 responses stored in memory (termed a “look-up
table”). This meant that the AutoCAD program could only
run on machines
fitted with the AutoCAD lock.
5.08 Mr Kelly, an electronics designer, studied the operation of the AutoCAD
lock using an oscilloscope and, with the other defendants,
produced and marketed
a device called the Autokey lock. The Autokey lock enabled a purchaser to use
the AutoCAD program or a copy
of it on a computer without an AutoCAD lock.
Autodesk sought to restrain the defendants from producing and marketing the
locks by alleging, amongst other things, that the Autokey lock infringed
their copyright in the AutoCAD program and the AutoCAD lock.
5.09 At first instance the Federal Court (Northrop J) found that both the
AutoCAD lock and the Autokey lock were computer programs
within the meaning of
the Act. His Honour also found that both locks performed the same function.
Having regard to the word “function”
in the definition of computer
program, his Honour concluded that, in determining whether there was a
reproduction of the AutoCAD
lock by the Autokey lock, the function of the locks
should be taken into consideration. Accordingly, his Honour held that the
lock reproduced the AutoCAD lock.
5.10 On appeal, the full Federal Court found that the combination of the
Widget C program and the AutoCAD lock could together be regarded
as a computer
program. The Court also noted that the only similarity between the Autokey lock
and the Widget C–AutoCAD lock
was their function. However, the Court
rejected the approach adopted by Northrop J, in determining whether there had
been a reproduction
of the Widget C–AutoCAD lock. The functional
similarity between Widget C–AutoCAD and the Autokey lock was achieved by
using two completely different algorithms. Accordingly, the Court held that the
Autokey lock was neither a reproduction nor an adaptation
of the Widget
C–AutoCAD lock program, reversing Northrop J’s decision.
5.11 The decision of the full Federal Court was overturned by the High Court.
The High Court held that the AutoCAD program was a compilation
of computer
programs which included Widget C. The “look-up table” of 127
responses stored in the Widget C program was
regarded by the High Court as a
substantial part of the Widget C program. The Court held that Mr Kelly had
indirectly copied the
look-up table, and therefore that the 127 responses stored
in the Autokey lock infringed copyright in the Widget C program.
5.12 Following the High Court’s decision in the
Autodesk case
the respondent, Dyason, sought to have the judgment vacated on the basis that he
had not been heard on the following issues:
(a) whether the 127-bit series embedded in the EPROM in the Autokey lock
constituted a reproduction of a substantial part of Widget
C, namely, the
look-up table stored in Widget C;
(b) whether the third respondent indirectly copied the look-up table in
Widget C by copying the sequence of numbers generated by the
AutoCAD lock;
(c) whether copying data contained in a computer program can constitute a
reproduction of a substantial part of that computer program.
In essence the court was being asked to decide whether they had heard
arguments to the effect that the look-up table in Widget C did
not amount to a
set of instructions (ie, a computer program) but was simply data and that the
look-up table was not a substantial
part of the Widget C program. A majority of
the court (Brennan, Dawson and Gaudron JJ; Mason CJ and Deane J dissenting)
no rehearing should be allowed because they were satisfied that
arguments on these matters had been made. Both the judgements of
Gaudron JJ refer to the arguments made in the lower courts and in the High Court
which support this conclusion.
5.13 The High Court’s decision in the
since been applied by the Federal Court in
Accounting Systems 2000
(Developments) Pty Ltd
CCH Australia Ltd
(1993) 114 ALR 355
relation to the fundamental issues of copyright protection for computer
programs. The
remains the leading case in Australia
in relation to copyright protection of computer programs and some of the issues
raised by the
decisions of the High Court and the Federal Court are discussed
The scope of protection of computer
5.14 The issue of whether the AutoCAD program in source code or object code
was a computer program under the Act and protected by
contested before the High Court. However, Mason CJ, Brennan and Dean JJ did
discuss the scope of protection afforded
to computer programs by the definition
of “computer program” introduced by the 1984 amendments. They
referred to the
possibility that a narrow literal construction could be given to
the definition of computer program, with the result that copyright
protect a computer program unless it was in some form of written expression.
They rejected that construction however, stating
[1992] HCA 2
(173 CLR 330
[1992] HCA 2
That narrow literal construction would, however, partly frustrate the obvious
legislative intent to confer real protection upon the
actual set of instructions
regardless of whether they be actually expressed in written form or merely
embedded or stored in a non-sensate
form such as electrical impulses on a disk,
ROM or EPROM.
Their Honours continued
[1992] HCA 2
(173 CLR 330
[1992] HCA 2
. . . the reference to “an expression, in any language, code or
notation” should not be understood as referring only to
an actual written
expression or representation of a set of instructions with the result that
such a written expression actually exists
or has existed . . .
Their Honours indicated that the protection afforded to the set of
instructions under the s.10(1) definition is done in a way that
is adapted to
the nature of copyright. Accordingly, they held that
[1992] HCA 2
(173 CLR 330
[1992] HCA 2
. . . the stored set of instructions in a non-sensate form such as electrical
impulses is itself protected on the basis that copyright
actually subsists in
any expression or description of it which can theoretically be made in language,
code or notation.
It is clear from this discussion that a majority of the Court was of the view
that the definition of computer program in s.10(1) itself
extends protection to
computer programs in both source and object code.
5.15 In the
Autodesk case
(No 2) Mason CJ referred to the two
fundamental principles which the
Autodesk case
(No 1) had confirmed. The
first of those being that the definition of “computer program”
confers protection on the set
of instructions itself, the program, and not just
the method of storage of those instructions chosen by the author.
The definition of “computer
5.16 The full Federal Court in the
Autodesk case
considered the
meaning of the definition of computer program in s.10(1) of the Act.
Sheppard J, with whose reasoning Lockhart J agreed,
expressed the view that
the governing word in the definition of computer program is the word
“expression”. His Honour
noted that the definition includes a
reference to “reproduction in a different material form” and that
form” is defined in s.10(1) of the Act as including:
any form (whether visible or not) of storage from which the work or
adaptation can be reproduced.
Having regard to this definition Sheppard J considered that a set of
instructions would be a computer program within the meaning of
the Act if:
the set of instructions is reproduced in a form of storage in
a device having digital information processing capabilities; and
it causes the device to perform a particular function.
This reasoning was applied by Davies J in
Star Micronics Pty Ltd
Five Star Computers Pty Ltd
(1990) 18 IPR 225
, in which he held that a
computer program embodied in a ROM was a computer program for the purposes of
5.17 In the High Court’s decision in the
Autodesk case
indicated there was no need for the Court to suggest a particular test for
determining what is a computer program within
the meaning of the definition.
The meaning of “reproduction”
5.18 The meaning of reproduction is not defined in the Act. As discussed in
paragraphs 5.10 to 5.11, Northrop J’s findings in
Autodesk case
that both the AutoCAD lock and the Autokey lock were computer programs and
that the Autokey lock reproduced the AutoCAD lock were
rejected by the full
Federal Court and the High Court.
5.19 In the decision of the full Federal Court, Lockhart J, when considering
whether the Autokey lock reproduced the Widget C–AutoCAD
lock program, had
regard to the essential ingredients of reproduction identified by the High Court
the High Court held that the
Wombat object programs were not reproductions of the Apple source programs.
Gibbs CJ and Brennan in
their judgements referred to the requirement that
the infringing work sufficiently resemble the copyright work. Brennan J stated
[1986] HCA 19
(161 CLR 171
[1986] HCA 19
The notion of reproduction still connotes a resemblance between the work in
which copyright subsists and the work which is copied
from. The material form to
which the respective works are reduced need not be the same, but if the forms
are so dissimilar as to
deny resemblance between the works, one cannot be said
to be a reproduction of the other.
5.20 Applying the
Apple case,
Lockhart J concluded that there was a
fundamental difficulty in finding reproduction on the facts of the case. His
Honour held that,
in the absence of a sufficient degree of objective similarity
between the copyright work and the infringing work, the conclusion
had been a reproduction could not be sustained.
5.21 Sheppard J considered the significance of the words “to perform a
particular function” in the definition in s.10(1)
and whether, as Northrop
J had concluded, function should be taken into consideration in determining if
there was a sufficient degree
of similarly between the two locks. His Honour
concluded that the drafter of the definition of “computer program”
concerned to make it clear that the initial words “expression... of a
set of instructions” meant that there had to be
the expression of a set of
instructions which caused a device having digital information processing
capabilities to perform a particular
function. The reference in the definition
to causing a device having digital information processing capabilities to
perform a particular
function was intended to qualify only the words, “set
of instructions”, and did no more than set the limits of what it
the set of instructions applied to. For this reason his Honour declined to
follow the approach adopted by Northrop J.
5.22 In the High Court, Dawson J (with whom the other Judges agreed) held
that the fact both locks produced the same output in response
to a challenge
(from the Widget C program) and performed the same function did not mean that
there was any similarity between the
two sets of instructions. His Honour also
held that placing such significance on the function of the two locks, as
Northrop J had,
disregarded the traditional dichotomy in the law of copyright
between an idea and the expression of an idea.
5.23 In determining whether or not there had been a reproduction of the
AutoCAD lock, Dawson J made the following observation
[1992] HCA 2
(173 CLR 330
[1992] HCA 2
In the present case there can be no doubt about the originality of authorship
of the look-up table expressed as it is in Widget C.
But no reliance was placed
upon it as a literary work in itself. It is enough that it forms a substantial
part of a computer program
which is a literary work in itself . . .
This was one of the reasons given by Dawson J for concluding that the
production of the Autokey lock constituted an infringement of
the copyright in
the AutoCAD program, in particular Widget C.
5.24 If the reasoning applied by Dawson J is of general application and not
confined to the facts of the
Autodesk case
(the Committee is not aware of
any reason why it should be so confined), it follows that other things which may
not qualify for separate
protection under the Act as computer programs may
nonetheless be protected if they can properly be characterised as a substantial
part of a computer program. For example a screen display that is the user
interface of an otherwise original program, but which has
been designed to
resemble the screen display of another program, may infringe copyright in that
other computer program even though
the actual instructions of the
“look-alike” program that generate the particular display are
5.25 The conclusion that the look-up table was a substantial part of the
Widget C program, with the result that copying of the look-up
table constituted
copying of that program, causes the Committee some concern. Further, the
conclusion that the creation of the look-up
table in Autokey from observation of
the operation of Widget C and the AutoCAD lock was a copying of the look-up
table in Widget
C, even though the maker of Autokey (Mr Kelly) never saw that
table, is also viewed by the Committee as a far-reaching and questionable
extension of copyright. The Committee notes that Mason CJ as part of the
minority in the
Autodesk case
(No 2) was prepared to reopen judgement,
because he thought that it was arguable that the look-up table may have been
simply data
and not a substantial part of the program, and that these issues had
not been raised in argument. As already noted (paragraphs 3.08
and 5.06), the
majority of the High Court rejected an application that the decision should be
reviewed. In framing its recommendations
that cover matters affected by the
Autodesk case
decision, the Committee has kept in view its mandate to
recommend what the law should be.
The meaning of “adaptation”
5.26 The issue of adaptation was not considered by the High Court. However,
the full Federal Court, in determining whether the Autokey
lock was an
adaptation of the AutoCAD lock, considered the definition of
“adaptation” in s.10(1). It concluded that an
adaptation was a
version of the work which embodies or preserves the original qualities of the
to the word “version” used in
the definition was its ordinary meaning, ie, “a special form or variant of
something”.
In this regard Lockhart J commented that there were indicia in
the definition of adaptation which demonstrated that a work may be
an adaptation
of another work notwithstanding considerable differences between them. However,
the Court’s conclusion was that,
as the only element that the Widget
C–AutoCAD lock program had in common with the Autokey lock was their
function, the Court
was precluded from finding that the Autokey lock was an
adaptation by reason of being a version of the AutoCAD lock within the
of adaptation.
The 1984 Amendments
6.01 The amendments made to the
(the Act) in 1984
to afford protection to computer programs were clearly skilfully conceived and
drafted and appear to have served
adequately the interests of the computer
industry for the past ten years. Since their introduction in 1984, the
amendments have given
rise to only two significant cases:
[1992] HCA 2
(1992) 173 CLR 330
Autodesk case)
Star Micronics Pty Ltd
Five Star Computers Pty Ltd
This fact in itself suggests that the amendments are working with a
good degree of success. In essence, the Committee acknowledges
that the 1984
amendments provide a fundamentally sound framework for the protection of
computer programs. Moreover, the type and
extent of protection for computer
programs adopted in Australia in 1984 have now been given international
endorsement by the TRIPS
agreement which states in article 10(1) that computer
programs are to be protected by copyright as literary works under the Berne
Convention for the Protection of Literary and Artistic Works (Berne
Convention). Viewed from this perspective, Australia stands
out as a leading
country in the protection of computer programs and has certainly not lagged
behind other countries in protecting
computer programs.
6.02 Nonetheless, the Committee notes that the Act has not been without its
critics, even though most of those critics have come from
within the legal
community and not those most affected by the operation of the
provisions—the computer industry. Additionally,
mostly as a result of this
reference, a number of suggestions have been made, both directly to the
Committee and more generally in
the community, to improve the 1984 amendments.
The Committee has formulated its recommendations taking account of both these
recommendations
and its terms of reference which required it to address the
issue of whether there was adequate protection of computer programs,
stored in computer memory and works created by or with the assistance of
computer programs. The short answer to the terms of
reference in relation to
computer programs is that “yes” the Act as drafted does adequately
protect computer programs.
However, the Committee adds this qualification to its
short answer—the Act should be amended to clarify some remaining
uncertainties
and to ensure more comprehensive protection for computer programs.
This is the underlying reason for, and the essence of, the Committee’s
recommendations.
SUMMARY OF RESPONSES TO THE DRAFT REPORT AND
RECOMMENDATIONS
6.03 The Committee received a number of submissions following the release of
its Draft Report which commented on aspects of this chapter
in that Draft. The
strongest criticisms made in relation to the Committee’s recommendations
in its Draft Report concerned the
definition of “computer program”
suggested by the Committee and the failure of the Committee to recommend the
of a definition of “computer”. The Committee’s
response to these submissions and an outline of the criticisms made
included at paragraph 6.18 and following. Other relevant comments have been
noted and discussed elsewhere in the chapter.
6.04 Some submitters also chose to express, once again, their opposition to
the protection of computer programs as “literary
works” and the
Committee’s recommendation that they be protected as such under the Act.
The Committee’s main discussion
of this issue is contained in Chapter 4.
It is sufficient to recall here that with the conclusion of the TRIPS agreement,
Draft Report was released, Australia is now bound to protect computer
programs as literary works under the Berne Convention.
6.05 In summary, the Committee’s recommendations have remained the same
since the Draft Report, except in relation to the definition
of reproduction.
Accordingly, the Committee’s recommendations are as follows:
(a) The words “whether or not in a visible form” be deleted from
the definition of “literary work” in s.10(1)
of the Act (paragraph
(b) A definition of “computer program” be retained in the Act
(paragraph 6.25).
(c) The current definition of “computer program” in s.10(1) of
the Act be deleted and the following definition substituted
“computer program” means a set of statements or instructions to
be used directly or indirectly in a computer in order
to bring about a certain
(d) No change be made to the Act to expressly provide that algorithms are not
protected by the Act (paragraph 6.36).
(e) A clarifying definition of “reproduction” with respect to
works stored electronically and in relation to computer
programs in source code
and object code be added to the Act. (paragraphs 6.55 and 6.76).
(f) The definition of “adaptation” in s.10(1) of the Act remain
unchanged (paragraph 6.77).
(g) No change be made to the definition of “material form” in
s.10(1) of the Act (paragraph 6.87).
DEFINITION OF “LITERARY WORK”
6.06 In 1984, s.10(1) of the Act was amended to provide that:
“literary work” includes:
(a) a table, or compilation, expressed in words, figures or symbols (whether
or not in a visible form); and
(b) a computer program or compilation of computer programs.
One of the features of this definition is that the words in brackets
“whether or not in a visible form” occurring in paragraph
relation to tables and compilations are not included in paragraph (b) which
deals with computer programs and compilations
of computer programs. It is in
relation to this aspect of the definition of computer program that the Committee
received comment
on the Draft Report from a number of submitters.
6.07 Prior to the Draft Report, the Committee received submissions from Apple
Computer Australia Pty Limited (Apple) and IBM Australia
Limited (IBM) that the
definition of “literary work” in s.10(1) of the
Act should be
amended. In its submission IBM suggested that the words in brackets were
superfluous. IBM was of the view that the definition
may be open to a
construction that a computer program must be in a visible form if it is to be
regarded as a literary work. Accordingly,
IBM recommended to the Committee that
the words in brackets in paragraph (a) of the definition should be added to
paragraph (b) of
the definition. The submission from Apple similarly queried the
effect of the words in brackets, but contrary to IBM, Apple suggested
words in brackets be removed from the definition altogether.
6.08 In response to these suggestions the Committee recommended that the
definition of “literary work” be amended and
the words in brackets
in paragraph (a) “whether or not in a visible form” be deleted
(Draft Report paragraph 6.04). The
Committee reached this view after considering
the submissions made by IBM and Apple and the other provisions of the Act which
with the subsistence of copyright in “works”, including
literary works.
6.09 Section 32(1) provides that copyright subsists in an original
unpublished work of which the author was a qualified person “at
when the work was made”. Section 22 provides that a reference to when a
work was “made” is to be read
as a reference to when the work
“was first reduced to writing or to some other material form”. Under
s.10(1) “material
form” is defined as including:
any form (whether visible or not) of storage from which the work
. . . can be reproduced.
Section 32(2) provides for the continuation of subsistence of copyright in
unpublished works after their publication, if criteria
concerning place of
publication and qualification of the author at the time of publication are
6.10 Having regard to s.22 of the Act and the definition of “material
form” the Committee was of the view that, since
the Act already provides
that copyright will subsist in works stored invisibly, there seemed no need for
the words in brackets in
paragraph (a) of the definition of “literary
work”. For this reason the Committee recommended that the words
or not in a visible form” in brackets in paragraph (a) of
the definition of “literary work” should be deleted.
6.11 The Committee received some comment from the Australian Copyright
Council (ACC) with respect to this recommendation. In its submission
stated that it was not certain whether it was necessary to remove the words from
the definition of “literary work”
as recommended by the Committee.
The Committee takes the ACC’s comments to mean, in effect, that the words
“whether or
not in a visible form” are not words of limitation, and
that it is possible that the current definition extends to literary
listed in the definition in (a)) or computer programs in a non-visible form.
Although the Committee has noted the ACC’s
comment, it is the
Committee’s view that given that both the ACC and IBM appear to be in
agreement that the additional words
“whether or not in a visible
form” do not really add anything to the definition of literary work, the
best course is
to remove them. Therefore the Committee abides by its previous
recommendation. (Recommendation 2.04(a)).
6.12 A related proposal was put forward by Mr. M Paterson of
Picton–Warlow & Co Solicitors who submitted that the definition
“work” should be amended so as to include the visible and the
invisible. Mr Paterson made this suggestion, in the
belief that it would
overcome any lingering doubts, as a result of the decision of Gibbs CJ and Deane
Computer Edge
Apple Computer
[1986] HCA 19
(1986) 161 CLR 171
), that works stored in a non-visible form are not
6.13 The Committee has considered this proposition, but notes as did Mr
Paterson that the decision of Pincus J of the Federal Court
Corporation
Lorenzo and Sons Pty Ltd
[1991] FCA 617
(1991) 22 IPR 245
), that a manual, a type of literary work, embodied in a computer disk
was a “work” eliminates some of this doubt. The

that if the reasoning of the High Court in relation to computer programs in
Autodesk Inc
[1992] HCA 2
(1992) 173 CLR 330
Autodesk case
) was applied in relation to other types of

Protection of object code
6.14 In its submission Microsoft Pty Limited (Microsoft) suggested that a
provision should be inserted which deems a computer program
in object code to be
a literary work. The Committee notes that a majority of the High Court in the
were of the view that, prior to the 1984 amendments, copyright
protection as a literary work extended only to computer programs written
source code. In the
Autodesk case
the High Court was not asked to
consider whether a computer program in either source code or object code was a
literary work. However,
Mason CJ, Brennan and Deane JJ considered the effect of
the 1984 amendments on the definition of “computer program”.
Honours noted that to give the amendments a narrow, literal construction would
partly frustrate the obvious legislative intent
to confer real protection on
computer programs. In interpreting the definition of “computer
program”, their Honours said
that the definition should be understood as
conferring protection in a way that is adapted to copyright and found that it
to both visible and non-sensate (ie, invisible) forms of computer
programs. The storage of computer programs expressed in either
object or source
code in an electronic form will necessarily mean they are stored in a
non-sensate form. Accordingly, the Committee
takes their Honours to be
concluding that the definition applies to computer programs in both source or
object code, whether stored
electronically or not.
6.15 In the Draft Report, by way of preliminary discussion, the Committee
stated its understanding of the general classification of
computer programs
(paragraphs 6.06–6.09). This discussion now appears in Appendix N.
However, in the course of that discussion
the Committee did reach a number of
conclusions, set out below, which it confirms.
6.16 In relation to operating systems programs and applications programs the
Committee was of the view that as the distinctions between
them were no longer
clear cut, they should both be protected by copyright and in the same way.
6.17 The Committee also recommended that any definition of “computer
program” should clearly and unambiguously apply to
programs expressed in
source code, programs expressed in object code (compiled from source code) and
to microcode. The Committee
notes that this recommendation has received support
from those members of the Law Council of Australia (LCA) who agree that computer
programs should be protected by copyright as literary works (see further
discussion of this point at paragraph 6.19 and 6.21).
DEFINITION OF “COMPUTER
6.18 Following the 1984 amendments, “computer program” is defined
in the Act in s.10(1). The Act states that:
“computer program” means an expression, in any language, code or
notation, of a set of instructions (whether with or without
related information)
intended, either directly or after either or both of the following:
(a) conversion to another language, code or notation;
(b) reproduction in a different material form,
to cause a device having digital information processing capabilities to
perform a particular function.
The recent judicial interpretation of this definition is referred to in
paragraphs 5.16–5.17.
Comments received on the current definition in the
Protection of object code
6.19 The Committee received submissions from the Australian
Vice-Chancellors’ Committee (AVCC), Lotus Development Pty Ltd (Lotus),
Microsoft and the LCA which suggested that the current definition of
“computer program” does not clarify whether copyright
subsists in
computer programs written in object code. The Committee notes that these
concerns are wholly or partly based on the reasoning
of the High Court in the
(see paragraph 5.05). Having regard to the High Court’s
reasoning, Lotus and Microsoft submitted that the words in the definition,
“in any language, code or notation”, did not cover a sequence of
electrical impulses and could only be read as applying
to human readable source
code programs. Lotus suggested that the words in brackets in the definition
should be amended to read:
(whether with or without related information, whether visible or not and
whether in human or machine readable form).
In the Draft Report the Committee noted that the inclusion of the words
“in any language, code or notation” in the definition
“computer program” was intended to make it clear that copyright
protection extends to computer programs written (expressed)
in source or object
code (Copyright Amendment Bill 1984: Explanatory Memorandum, p.6). Having regard
to this and the High Court’s
decision in the
Autodesk case
paragraph 5.14 ), the Committee is of the view that it is now clear that
programs in object code fall within the definition
of “computer
program” and accordingly makes no recommendation in relation to this
Protection of programs written in declarative
6.20 The Draft Report also referred to other concerns raised by the AVCC
regarding the definition. The AVCC submitted that the words
instructions” may not apply to declarative programming languages, that is,
so-called fourth-generation languages,
which consist of a series of declarative
statements rather than instructions. The Committee recognises there is an
argument that
a strict interpretation of the definition might exclude programs
written in “non-procedural” languages. While the Committee
agree that such a restrictive interpretation would necessarily be adopted by the
courts, it does accept this as a reason
to consider changing the definition of
“computer program” in the Act.
6.21 The AVCC submission also suggested that the current definition may
exclude programs which are executed by interpretation as well
as those intended
to run on non-digital machines. In respect of the first point the Committee
notes that because a program written
in a high-level language cannot be executed
directly on a computer, two alternative techniques, namely compilation and
interpretation,
are used to execute a program written in a high level language,
(see Appendix N paragraph N.05 ). When a program is executed by interpretation
it is, in effect, treated as data by the interpreter program, which performs in
accordance with the instructions specified by the
program being interpreted. In
the Committee’s view, the way in which a program is executed (either by
compilation or interpretation)
is not relevant to the issue of determining
whether the set of instructions is a computer program. Accordingly, it does not
that programs executed by interpretation do not come within the definition
of “computer program” in the Act. The Committee
also considers that
the definition of “computer program” should, and does, cover
programs written in microcode, ie, microprograms
Use of the word “digital”
6.22 The final issue raised regarding the definition of “computer
program” concerns the word “digital”. In
its Issues Paper, the
Committee asked whether the reference to digital should remain in the definition
of computer program as it
precludes programs written for non-digital (analogue)
computers from coming within the definition. The observation by the AVCC that
the current definition may exclude programs written in code to run on
non-digital computers is the only substantial comment the Committee
received on
this issue. The Committee is uncertain as to the practical significance that
such an interpretation may have. However,
the Committee believes that the
definition of a computer program should have as broad an application as
possible. Accordingly, it
recommended in the Draft Report that the reference to
digital should be removed from the definition (Draft recommendation 2.03(3)).
This recommendation was supported in submissions from the CSIRO Institute of
Information Science and Engineering, Oracle Systems
(Australia) Pty Limited
(Oracle) and the LCA.
International approaches to defining
“computer program”
6.23 The Committee notes that, internationally, different approaches have
been adopted on the question whether to define the expression
program” or not. The approach taken by the European Union, including the
United Kingdom, is different from
that adopted by the United States and Japan.
Directive on the Legal Protection of Computer Programs
art.1(1) (Appendix G) (EC Directive on Computer Programs) the European Union has
not defined “computer program” but
expressly states that computer
programs are to be protected as “literary works”. The benefits of
this approach are that
any legislation implementing this Directive will be
sufficiently flexible to accommodate new developments in technology,
particularly
in this rapidly changing field, and many of the pitfalls of
interpretation, including some of the problems outlined above, are avoided.
6.24 The approach adopted in the United States and Japan has been, like that
in Australia, to define “computer program.”
Section 101 of the US
(US Act) defines a “computer program”
. . . a set of statements or instructions to be used directly or indirectly
in a computer in order to bring about a certain result.
The Japanese
(Law No. 48) provides in art.2(1)(x
) that “program” means:
an expression of combined instructions given to a computer so as to make it
function and obtain a certain result.
The principal benefit of defining “computer program” is that it
as the technology
and concepts involved in computer programs become increasingly familiar, the
need for such definitions decreases.
6.25 The Committee acknowledges that the approach of the European Union,
including the United Kingdom (
s.3(1)(b)) is attractive in that it allows for greater flexibility. However, the
Committee is of the view that to remove the definition
of “computer
program” from the Act may create uncertainty. The Committee therefore
recommends that a definition of “computer
program” be retained in
the Act (Recommendation 2.04(b)).
6.26 In the Draft Report the Committee recommended that the US definition of
“computer program” be substituted for the
definition—this recommendation attracted most comment from submitters. The
LCA did not support the adoption of the
definition, but did not elaborate on its
reasons for this view. The Australian Information Industry Association (AIIA)
the recommendation of the Committee. So did Dr Christie (Senior
Lecturer in Law, University of Melbourne) in a paper presented at
jointly organised by the Centre for International Research on Communication and
Information Technologies (CIRCIT) and the
University of Melbourne Law School in
September 1993, subject to his view that the approach adopted by the European
Union is preferable.
The ACC suggested that the effect of the adoption of the US
definition may be that American case law may become more relevant in
In a similar vein Mr Muratore (Partner, Freehill, Hollingdale and Page
Solicitors) expressed the cautionary view that
Australia should not borrow too
heavily from American precedents. It would be a matter for courts to determine
what weight will be
given American authorities. It is not to be assumed that
they will necessarily be given preemptive weight just because the Australian
definition of computer program is the same as the US definition. There are many
provisions in the Act governing the protection of
literary works that are not
the same as corresponding provisions in the US law. The American authorities
will be relevant for courts
to consider, as they are now. It does not follow
that they will be decisive of the outcome of any given case.
6.27 Messrs Houghton and Reid–Smith of CIRCIT submitted that the
reference to “a set of statements or instructions”
in the proposed
definition did not offer any certainty in determining the bounds of a relevant
computer program. The consequence
that they envisaged was that it would be
difficult, if not impossible, to determine if there had been a substantial
reproduction
of a computer program if there was no way of knowing how many
instructions or statements make up a set and especially if there were
criteria for establishing the “in-the-set” to the
“out-of-the-set” boundaries. Their submission suggests
a resolution
of this problem by focusing on the function of a particular statement or
instruction. In their view the appropriate
test would be whether a statement or
instruction “contributes to, and is an elemental part of the process of
performing a specific
function” in which case it would be part of the
“in-the-set” statements.
6.28 In the Committee’s view this “test” does not
necessarily need to form part of the definition of “computer
program” in the Act, especially as it relates to the issue of reproduction
more than to the issue of what is being protected.
It is a test that the courts
could apply when dealing with cases of alleged reproduction and which in any
event they must already
apply in determining the subject matter in question.
There are two other reasons for leaving to the courts the determination of what
is and what is not part of a computer program. The definition of “literary
work” gives no guidance such as is proposed
on what forms part of or does
not form part of works other than computer programs. This could be an issue in
relation to, eg, unpublished
notes for a draft of a novel. Second, that
definition refers to “a computer program or compilation of computer
acknowledging, so it seems, that what is under consideration
may constitute a single program or two or more programs. The facts of
Autodesk case
illustrate that possibility.
6.29 The Supporters of Interoperable Systems in Australia (SISA) submitted
that in addition to recommending the adoption of the definition
of computer
program in s.101 of the US Act the Committee should also recommend the adoption
of a form of exclusion such as occurs
in s.102(b) of the US Act. SISA’s
view was that this should be done in order to set more precise boundaries on
what elements
of computer programs are protected. SISA indicated that this
approach has been adopted by Japan in its Copyright Act (art.10(3) and
Directive on Computer Programs (art.1(2)). Section 102(b) of the US Act
In no case does copyright protection for an original work of authorship
extend to any idea, procedure, process, system, method of
operation, concept,
principle, or discovery, regardless of the form in which it is described,
explained, illustrated, or embodied
in such work.
6.30 The question of whether algorithms are protected as a result of
in this chapter at
paragraph 6.33. The Committee is of the view that its conclusions in regard to
algorithms apply equally to the
other items listed in s.10(2)(b). As the
Committee notes, with respect to algorithms, the fundamental nature of copyright
it does not protect ideas as such. For this reason, the Committee is of
the opinion that there is no need for an explicit statement
to this effect to be
included the Act. In reaching this conclusion the Committee has also taken note
of the views of the Computer
and Business Equipment Manufacturers Association
(CBEMA) who agreed that principles of copyright should apply to determine
specific elements of a computer program are copyrightable and the strong
opposition of IBM to SISA’s submission.
6.31 One final concern with the US definition, raised by the ACC, was that it
may not extend to include a defective computer program
which failed to
“bring about a certain result”. The Committee notes that this
concern could be addressed by inserting
the words “that is intended”
in place of the words “in order” in the US definition. However, the
considers that this would also have the undesirable effect of
protecting fragments of computer programs. The Committee also notes
present many computer programs are written with “bugs” or errors and
need to be corrected in order to operate
correctly. Makers of computer programs
such as the major software houses employ teams of people for this purpose.
Programs are continually
modified and updated with errors being corrected as
they are discovered. The Committee believes that the circumstances of the
and supply of a defective program would lead a court to determine
that protection should not be withheld because the defect prevented
performing as intended. The Committee is not aware that the US definition has
been shown to be deficient in ensuring protection
for these programs. For these
reasons the Committee is of the view that the definition, adopted in the US
legislation, is both adequate
and appropriate to describe the subject matter for
protection.
Conclusion and recommendation
6.32 The Committee is of the view that any definition of “computer
program” should not be unduly restrictive of future
developments in
programming languages and should be clearly comprehensive in its coverage. The
Committee is mindful that the position
adopted in Australia in relation to the
extent of the protection afforded to computer programs should, as far as
possible, be consistent
with that adopted by our major trading partners. The
Committee believes that the US definition, by using the words “to be used
directly or indirectly in a computer”, covers programs written in source
code, object code and microcode. Accordingly, it disposes
of any uncertainty
whether protection is limited to when the program code is visible. Also, the
inclusion of the word “statements”
clearly extends the scope of the
definition to cover programs written in declarative programming languages. The
definition omits
any requirement that the computer be digital. Finally, while
the definition does not include a reference to “expression of”
preceding “a set of statements or instructions”, the Committee
believes that the requirement of originality for copyright
to subsist in a work
in s.32(1)of the Act, would result in the expression–idea dichotomy being
applied to computer programs
thus defined, as it is to any other work.
Furthermore, the Committee notes that the definitions of other items
constituting works,
eg, “drawing” which is a form of artistic work
and which includes “diagram, map, chart or plan” does not
“expression of”. Accordingly, the Committee recommends that the
definition of “computer program”
in the Act be replaced by the
definition used in the US Act which provides in s.101 (Recommendation
A “computer program” is a set of statements or instructions to be
used directly or indirectly in a computer in order to
bring about a certain
Should algorithms be protected ?
6.33 In their supplementary submission to the Committee, Gilbert & Tobin
Solicitors raised concerns whether copyright protection
under the Act extends to
the algorithms employed in a program. The Committee has adopted the following
definition of an algorithm
for the purposes of this report (
Processing-Vocabulary, ISO Standards Handbook 10
, 1982, p.16):
An algorithm is a finite set of well defined rules for the solution of a
problem in a finite number of steps.
Gilbert & Tobin Solicitors argued that if protection is extended to
algorithms, then the line between protecting the expression
of an idea and the
idea itself becomes almost impossible to draw. The Committee acknowledges this
to be a real concern.
6.34 The Explanatory Memorandum to the
stated in relation to the definition of “computer program” that
(p.6, paragraph 16):
The phrase “expression . . . of a set of instructions” is
intended to make it clear that it is not an abstract idea, algorithm
mathematical principle which is protected but rather a particular expression of
that abstraction.
This is consistent with the position taken in the EC Directive
Preamble to the Directive, Appendix G), the Japanese Copyright Act and with
directives from the US Copyright Office which state
that copyright protection
does not extend to algorithms. (US Copyright Office Circular R61:
Protection for Computer Programs
—quoted in 30 October 1990 submission
from Gilbert and Tobin Solicitors, p.4, fn.13).
6.35 In the
Autodesk case
, Dawson J, with whom the rest of the Court
agreed, reiterated and reaffirmed the fundamental nature of copyright
protection. His
honour said

(173 CLR 330

true that it is often

and the decision of the High Court in the

, the Committee
does not recommend any amendment to the Act expressly stating that copyright
