DEFINITION OF “COMPUTER”
6.37 In response to the Draft Report, Dr Christie indicated his support for
the decision of the Committee to recommend that the US
definition of
“computer program” be adopted subject to one reservation—that
the word “computer” should
be defined in the Act as well. Messrs
Houghton and Reid–Smith raised a similar point, submitting that if the
Committee was
assuming that a “computer” was “a device having
information processing capabilities” (which forms part of
the current
definition of “computer program”) then this may include (p.4):
. . . class[es] of devices with information processing capabilities . . . far
larger than the class of devices we usually call computers”.
6.38 The Committee recognises that Dr Christie and Messrs Houghton and
Reid–Smith are raising an important issue and the Committee
has considered
their suggestions. However, the Committee does not propose to recommend that a
definition of “computer”
be included in the Act. The Committee is
conscious that in recommending the replacement of the existing definition of
program” with the US definition, it is effectively
recommending removing a definition of “computer”. It has made
recommendation recognising the need for a technology neutral definition which is
not tied to the concept of digital computers
unlike the existing definition of
computer program. However, the Committee is not aware of a satisfactory
“technology-neutral”
definition of “computer” and
therefore prefers to leave it to the courts to apply a normal understanding of
what constitutes
a “computer”, in cases as they come to be decided.
The Committee has chosen to recommend the retention of the definition
“computer program” for the reasons already discussed in this
chapter, in particular, to create a greater degree of
certainty over what is
being protected and in order to grant limited exceptions to the rights of
THE MEANING OF “REPRODUCTION IN A MATERIAL
Reproduction in relation to works in
6.39 One of the rights of the copyright owner of a work is the right to
“reproduce the work in a material form” (s.31(1)(a)(i)).
“Material form” is the subject of an inclusive definition inserted
in the Act by the 1984 amendments. Section 10(1) provides:
“material form”, in relation to a work or an adaptation of a
work, includes any form (whether visible or not) of storage
from which the work
or adaptation, or a substantial part of the work or adaptation, can be
reproduced.
As to the meaning of “reproduction”, it is not exhaustively
defined in the Act. Apart from s.21, which deems sound recordings
and films of
literary, dramatic and musical works to be reproductions of the works recorded
or filmed, it has been left to the courts
to ascertain what constitutes a
reproduction. Reproduction has been held by the courts to encompass more than
literal copying of
a work; there must be objective similarity between the
original work and the alleged copy and there must be a causal connection between
the two (ie, there must be some evidence of copying)
Francis Day and Hunter
[1963] Ch 587
Ladbroke (Football) Ltd
Hill (Football) Ltd
[1964] 1 WLR 273.
The requirement for objective
similarity between a work and a copy, in order to establish reproduction, poses
some difficulties where
the works are stored electronically. This difficulty is
discussed below from paragraph 6.46
6.40 The Committee received comments on the meaning of reproduction in a
material form from Apple, IBM and the LCA. In their submissions,
Apple and the
LCA took the view that the definition of material form does not answer the
argument put by the High Court in the
that programs in object
code are not a “reproduction” of the source code but, rather, the
result of action taken in compliance
with directions in the source code. (Put
another way, the source code program is the recipe and the object code program
ROM is the “rabbit pie” made in accordance with the
recipe). The Committee acknowledges that the LCA has raised legitimate
in relation to the consequences of the High Court’s decision in the
. The Committee’s following recommendations are made
having regard to the High Court’s reasoning and the various submissions
just referred to. However, the Committee feels it should make it clear that it
does not consider the High Court’s analysis
of the relationship between
source code and object code to be correct. Object code is created by the
translation of source code into
a machine readable form and not as a result of
action taken in compliance with directions in the source code. The source code
instructions
are translated into lower level object code instructions. The LCA
also submitted that the definition did not overcome the High Court’s
finding in that case that for there to be a reproduction of source code it must
be in a human readable form.
6.41 Concerns by Apple and Microsoft about the possible effect of the High
Court’s decision in the
regarding the meaning of
“reproduction” led them to suggest that a number of clarifying
definitions be added to the Act.
Apple submitted that a definition of
reproduction should be added to s.10(1) as follows:
“Reproduction” means: a material object in which a work is fixed
by any means from which the work can be perceived, produced,
or otherwise
communicated, either directly or with the aid of a machine or device.
6.42 Microsoft also submitted that reproduction should be defined. It
suggested that reproduction should include:
The copying or duplication of a work howsoever recorded, stored, or embodied
and whether visible or invisible and whether in human
or machine readable
Microsoft further suggested the recording, storage or embodiment of a work
should be deemed to include any media from which the work
can be retrieved by
electronic or other means.
6.43 As a consequence of the definition of reproduction it proposed, Apple
suggested further amendments to s.21 and to s.22 (which
determines when a work
or other subject-matter is made). Apple suggested adding the following
sub-section to s.21:
(5) For the purposes of the Act, a literary work shall be deemed not to have
been reproduced where a copy of the whole or any part
thereof is made in the
course of use on a computer in accordance with conditions (if any) imposed by
the copyright owner. The conditions
of any licence or other document
accompanying the work at the time of its original acquisition or of which the
acquirer was aware
prior to such acquisition shall be deemed to be conditions
imposed by the copyright owner.
6.44 Apple also suggested that the term “copy” should be defined
to include:
The material object in which the work is first fixed and that
“reproduce” should mean:
The making of a reproduction including storage of the work in any medium by
electronic means, or producing the work from such medium
of storage and that
s.22(1) should provide:
until it is recorded, in writing or otherwise, including
storage of the work in
any medium by electronic means; and references in this Act to the time at which
such a work is made are to
the time at which it is so recorded.
6.45 In its Draft Report the Committee stated that it was not persuaded that
there was a need for a general definition of “reproduction”
Act. The Committee’s exact words were (paragraph 6.27):
The Committee has given careful consideration to these suggestions. It notes
that the proposed definitions of the term “reproduction”
would apply
to all works protected under the Act. It could be expected that if the absence
of a definition of “reproduction”
caused copyright owners
difficulties in enforcing their rights, representations would be made by the
various organisations representing
their interests to the Government seeking
statutory clarification of the meaning of the term. The Committee understands
has not been the case. Accordingly, the Committee is not persuaded
that there is a need for a general definition of “reproduction”
6.46 Since the Committee made its recommendation in the Draft Report it has
had further opportunity to consider whether there is a
need for a definition of
reproduction in relation to works, and in particular in relation to works stored
in computer memory after
their initial creation in some other format. The
Committee is concerned that at present the concept of reproduction may not
electronically stored or “digitised” works. To be more
precise, the Committee is uncertain whether once a work in a more
traditional
form of expression is stored electronically (usually in a digital format) it can
then be said to be “objectively
similar” to the original work. It
should be noted that the Committee’s following comments will apply in
relation to its
discussion of databases in Chapter 14. It should also be noted
that the Committee is here concerned with the question whether digital
of a work in a printed form is a reproduction of that work and not whether the
conversion of a computer program in source
code and in printed form to object
code in digital form is a reproduction of the source code. That last mentioned
issue will be addressed
later in this chapter (paragraph 6.58
following).
Objective similarity
6.47 As indicated above at paragraph 6.39 the requirement for objective
similarity is one of the factors in determining a “reproduction”
a work. If the digitisation of a work is not regarded as an act of reproduction,
then the rights of copyright owners are, in the
Committee’s view, clearly
deficient. The possibility of this deficiency has been noted and debated by
various commentators
and was addressed, although briefly, in a number of
submissions made to the Committee following the Draft Report. The following
represent the Committee’s attempt to summarise and present this
6.48 In 1990 Professor Ricketson said (
Essays on Computer Law
‘Copyright and Databases’, 1990, 91 at 98):
. . . the storage of a written literary work in machine-readable form will
not be a reproduction of that work as there will be no
objective resemblance
between them . . . A similar conclusion would also be likely where the work
which is stored is not embodied
in writing but exists in some other material
form, such as a sound recording or a cinematograph film.
Professor Ricketson was discussing the law as it stood after the
and prior to the High Court decision in the
Autodesk case.
6.49 More recently Ms Ann Monotti has argued that the
Autodesk case
can be interpreted to overcome this problem in relation to computer programs
and other types of works, such as electronic databases
(Ann Monotti,
‘Copyright Protection of Computerised Databases’, AIPJ, (1992) 3,
135). In Ms Monotti’s view the
judgement of Mason CJ, Brennan and Deane JJ
Autodesk case
suggests that it is permissible in determining
whether there is objective similarity, and hence a “reproduction”,
an intangible reproduction (such as a scanned work) to a written
expression in order to make a valid comparison. In a commentary
on this aspect
of the judgement, Ms Ann Monotti says (at 146):
. . . the comparison for the purposes of reproduction would be between the
alleged reproduction and some form of written expression
of the set of
instructions, rather than with the electrical impulses. In fact, this is the
comparison which was made.
She then quotes from the
Autodesk case
The 127-bit series embedded in the EPROM in the Auto Key lock constituted a
reproduction in a material form . . . of a substantial
part of any actual or
theoretical “expression” in binary “notation” of the
“set of instructions“
constituting Widget C.
Ms Monotti’s discussion continues, with reference to the passage just
quoted from the
Autodesk case
(at 146–147):
This is a comparison between electrical impulses and written binary notation.
As the requirement for objective similarity is not mentioned,
their approach [ie
the High Court’s] could either result in removal of the need for objective
similarity in works of this kind
or in the acceptance that objective similarity
between works may be analysed by comparing the electrical impulses in the EPROM
expressed in binary notation with the work expressed in that form . . . One
could therefore argue that reproduction is not limited
to duplication of a work
in the same material form . . . Technical means, . . . could be used to
establish that the invisible works
are objectively similar . . . Objective
similarity could also be determined after “reading out” the data in
a human readable
works which are expressed in different material
forms, one of which is not visible to the human eye, may also be considered to
objectively similar if that resemblance could be assessed by converting the
invisible work into the same material form
(emphasis added).
6.50 Mr David Lindsay has also recently commented on the meaning of
reproduction and suggested the addition of a deeming provision
to s.21 of the
Journal of Law and Information Science,
Vol 4(2), 1993, 287) (His
article is based on a paper presented to the CIRCIT–University of
Melbourne seminar referred to at
paragraph 6.25). Mr Lindsay says
I do not think that anyone could disagree with the fact that there remain
important uncertainties about the application of the current
exclusive rights to
works incorporated in a computer database. There is, at the outset, considerable
uncertainty over the application
of the reproduction right. On the one hand,
even given some of the comments in the Autodesk decision, the status of the
requirement
of “objective similarity” between a work and a copy,
before there can be infringement, remains unclear. At a very basic
therefore, it is unclear whether a work stored in electronic form can be a
reproduction of a work in a written form . . .
While there are obvious dangers
involved in attempting to clarify something as basic to copyright protection as
the reproduction
right, I feel that the addition of some new deeming provision
in Section 21 of the Act, which deals with reproduction in different
dimensions,
should be looked at again; at least as a means for clarifying the conditions
under which the electronic storage, or downloading,
will constitute a
reproduction.
6.51 The Committee is of the view that the reasoning of the Federal Court in
Roland case
, already referred to at paragraph 6.13, lends support to
the views of Ms Monotti that a comparison to determine objective similarity
transforming a work stored in a particular form of storage to another can be
justified in order to make a visual comparison. In
that case Pincus J held that
the copying of a print-out of the contents of a disk reproduced the contents of
the disk. In reaching
this decision Pincus J recognised that the words and
symbols in the print-out and as stored in the disk were dissimilar, but he said
Reading s 31(1)(a)(i) with the definition of “material form”, one
gets the result that copyright in the content of the
disk includes the right to
reproduce the content of the disk in any form of storage from which the work
reproduced . . . Even if one regards the expression “the
work” as to be identified with the particular embodiment of
it on the disk
— rather than the words or other symbols so embodied — no doubt one
can, by typing the verbal and symbolic
content back onto an appropriate word
processor, reproduce the contents of the disk. Further, I think that to read the
“form . . . of storage” as excluding ordinary language
would be too narrow. The intention of the definition appears to
be to focus
attention upon the content of that which is copyright, rather than the
particular form in which it is embodied. A mere
change in form (for example from
digital to alphabetic expression) without any change in content gives a
reproduction, not an original
6.52 Comparable comments have been made in the United Kingdom. In
Richardson Computers Ltd
Flanders and Anor
[1993] FSR 497
, Ferris J referred to the earlier case of
Thrustcode Ltd
W W Computing Ltd
[1983] FSR 502
(which concerned a motion to obain an
interlocutory injunction to restrain alleged infringement of copyright in a
computer program)
in which Sir Robert Megarry V-C said (at 505–506):
In the normal case in which an infringement of literary copyright is alleged,
it is possible to put side by side the written, typed
or printed words in which
the plaintiff claims copyright and the corresponding words which are said to
infringe that copyright. The
words can be seen and compared and discussed. . .
For computers [and for works stored in computer memory] . . . what must be
are the things said to have been copied and the thing said to be an
infringing copy. If these two things are invisible, then normally
they must be
reproduced in visible form, or in a form that is in some way perceptible, before
it can be determined whether one infringes
6.53 Nonetheless, submissions to the Committee following the Draft Report
have highlighted the difficulty with the notion of reproduction
application to works stored electronically. Oracle posed a series of questions
concerning the conversion of works from non-digital
storage to digital storage
and vice versa and sought appropriate amendments to the Act to recognise such
changes in form. Also submissions
from the CBEMA, De Data and the ACC urged the
Committee to reconsider its recommendation. Both the ACC and Oracle raised an
concerning the digitisation of a work and then its alteration. According
to their submissions, the altered work may bear little resemblance
original work and so it would be hard to establish that there had been a
reproduction at any point, either in scanning the
works to digital storage or in
any alteration of the stored work.
Proposed deeming of digitisation to be
reproduction
6.54 Having reconsidered this issue, the Committee accepts the reasoning of
Ms Monotti that the Act as it stands may in fact offer
protection to copyright
owners whose works are being reproduced in different material forms.
Nonetheless, the Committee acknowledges
that proving reproduction by relying on
the “Monotti interpretation” is likely to be a difficult
interpretational task
of uncertain outcome. The Committee has had regard to the
concerns of those making submissions to it that the present legal situation
uncertain and would benefit from clarification. In accordance with the
Committee‘s terms of reference the Committee has
considered whether the
Act adequately and appropriately protects works stored in computer memory, and
has now formed the view that
the Act does not necessarily achieve this with
clarity with respect to the reproduction right. The Committee notes that the
Convergence Group, which was required by its terms of reference to
consider the adequacy of current copyright protection for broadcasts
transmissions) and underlying materials used in those transmissions, in its
Highways to Change—Copyright in the New Communications
Environment
, indicated that concern had been expressed to it about the
definition of “copy” in relation to subject matter other than
and that this may need to be addressed at a future time (p.64, paragraph 7.6).
6.55 Therefore the Committee recommends that a clarifying definition of
reproduction with respect to works stored electronically should
be added to the
Act (Recommendation 2.04(e)(i)). It may be appropriate to add such a provision
to s.21 of the Act which deems certain
dealings with works to be reproductions.
Such an amendment should be drafted so as to deem the mere act of conversion of
an adaptation of a work from its hard copy human readable form to an
electronic form of storage, such as digital, which is machine
readable and
which, when printed out is unintelligible by reason of consisting of machine
readable symbols to be a reproduction of
the work or the adaptation. Such
definition, or more accurately, interpretive provision would be non-exhaustive,
like for example
the existing definition of “literary work” which
only defines a fraction of the full possible range of such works. Similarly
instance of converting a work or an adaptation from an electronic form to a hard
copy, such as making a print out of a work stored
electronically should, in the
Committee’s opinion, be encompassed in the deeming definition.
6.56 Any new deeming provision should not extend to cover instances where a
work is converted from one visible material form to another
or from one
non-visible material form to another. In these instances it is the
Committee’s understanding that the issue of
objective similarity will not
arise and, in any event, the Committee wishes to ensure that the ordinary tests
of reproduction are
still applied where works are reproduced in such a way that
a comparison is possible based on objective similarity. It is also the
Committee’s intention that its recommendation not apply to screen
displays, which the Committee has recommended should not
be regarded as
constituting a reproduction in a material form ( See recommendation 2.52).
As far as published edition copyright is concerned, the Committee
considers that the definition of reproduction proposed in relation
should not apply to published editions. As the Committee’s discussion in
Chapter 15 indicates, the extent of the reproduction
right in relation to
published editions is necessarily confined to facsimile reproduction and the
bare act of scanning onto a database
would not constitute such a reproduction
(see paragraphs 15.11 and 15.13). The exclusion of the proposed definition of
“reproduction”
would be accomplished simply by the limiting of the
published edition right to facsimile reproduction.
Reproduction in relation to computer
6.58 The Committee’s recommendation that a new deeming provision in
relation to the reproduction of works and adaptations by
the acts of electronic
storage and retrieval be adopted will only have a limited impact in relation to
computer programs. It will
ensure that source code that is first written and
then stored electronically or created electronically and subsequently printed
will be protected by copyright against such storage and printing out without
authority, as each version will be a reproduction of
the other. Similarly a
print-out of the object code will be a reproduction.
6.59 In the Draft Report the Committee examined the lingering concerns as a
result of the High Court’s conclusion that, under
the Act before the 1984
amendments, a program in object code would not be considered to be a
reproduction of the source code of that
program. The Committee’s
discussion was as follows:
6.29 In the
Autodesk case
, the High Court clearly indicated that the
protection afforded under the Act to computer programs extended beyond programs
in source code to those which existed in a non-sensate form such as
electrical impulses (ie, programs in object code). Because of
their importance
to this issue, the comments of Mason CJ, Brennan and Deane JJ that have already
been discussed at paragraph 5.13
are recalled:
It is arguable that the effect of the 1984 amendments is merely to recognise
or description of
the relevant “set of instructions” in some “language code or
notation”. That narrow literal
construction would, however, partly
frustrate the obvious legislative intent to confer real protection upon the
actual set of instructions
regardless of whether they be actually expressed in
written form ... or stored in a non-sensate form such as electrical impulses
a disk, ROM or EPROM....
[T]he stored set of instructions in a non-sensate form such as electrical
impulses is itself protected on the basis that copyright
actually subsists in
any expression or description of it which can theoretically be made in language,
code or notation. On that basis,
the test of originality is satisfied by the
originality of the set of instructions
and any unauthorised expression
of it in language, code or notation will infringe the copyright in the computer
(emphasis added)
6.30 The judgment quoted in the last paragraph does not discuss why an
unauthorised object code version of a program would infringe
source code version of the program (or vice versa). However, given the nature of
the protection afforded to computer
programs, namely copyright, the reason can
only be that an object code version of a program is either a reproduction or an
of the program in source code.
6.31 In the decision of the Full Federal court in the
Autodesk case
Lockhart J considered the meaning of reproduction. His Honour commented:
The element which was held in the
Apple Computer case
essential ingredient of the notion of reproduction is that there should be
“a sufficient degree of objective similarity
between the two works”
Francis Day & Hunter Ltd
) or to use the language of
Gibbs CJ: “The notion of reproduction still connotes a resemblance between
the work in which copyright
subsists and the work which is copied from it. The
material form to which the respective works are reduced need not be the same,
but if the forms are so dissimilar as to deny resemblance between the works, one
cannot be said to be a reproduction of the other”.
In the light of these comments, the Committee thinks that it must remain
doubtful whether a computer program in object code is a reproduction
of the same
program written in source code.
The Committee does not, however, believe
that this question is of great consequence, now that the High Court has affirmed
that protection
extends to programs in object code. It understands that in the
usual case of alleged infringement of a program, the program allegedly
will have existed in object code. However, there may be the occasional case of
alleged infringement of a source code, eg,
where the source code allegedly
developed to object code by the defendant was not so developed by the plaintiff.
The Committee concludes
that if an unauthorised object code version made of a
program in source code infringes copyright in the source code and yet is not
reproduction of the source code, the basis of infringement must necessarily be
that it is an adaptation of the source code (emphasis
added — Final
6.60 In paragraph 6.31 of the Draft Report the Committee summarised the issue
of concern—that it is not clear whether it is
a reproduction of a computer
program in source code to render that same program in object code (see sentence
with emphasis added).
The Committee examined the amendment to the adaptation
right that was introduced in 1984 and said:
6.32 The meaning of adaptation was considered by Sheppard J in the
Autodesk case
His Honour noted that the word “version” which
appears in the paragraphs (a), (b), (ba) and (c) of the definition of
is not defined in the Act. His Honour concluded that the word version should
bear its ordinary meaning as “a special
form or variant of
something”. Lockhart J noted that paragraph (c)(ii) of the definition of
“adaptation” deemed
it to include a version of a literary work in
which the story or action is conveyed solely or principally by means of
pictures. He
took this to illustrate the “wide connotation to be
attributed to the word “version”” in the definition.
to paragraph (ba), which defines adaptations of computer programs and which he
said was “designed to cover many possibilities”,
and added that:
A clear case is where an adaptation is made from one code to another; for
example, from source code to object code.
6.33 On the basis of the High Court’s interpretation of copyright
protection for computer programs against unauthorised copying
augmentation of that protection that appears available via the adaptation right,
the Committee is of the view that a definition
of reproduction in respect of
computer programs is not required. Accordingly, it recommends that no change be
made to the Act in
this regard.
6.61 Having regard to the preceding discussion, the High Court’s
comments in the
Autodesk case
Committee’s discussion in Appendix N (concerning the relationship between
programs in source code and object code)
the Committee is of the view that a
computer program in object code is properly characterised as an adaptation of
the source code
program. For this reason, the need for a definition of

6.62 It remains to be noted that the issue of reproduction also raises the
question whether a program that replicates the “look

DEFINITION OF “ADAPTATION” AND
“REPRODUCTION”
6.63 The Committee received submissions from the AVCC, Apple, IBM, the LCA,
Lotus and Microsoft concerning the definition of “adaptation”
amended in 1984. IBM submitted that traditionally an “adaptation”
was regarded as a reproduction of the original work.
However, IBM noted that the
1984 amendments provided that, in relation to computer programs, an
“adaptation” is a version
“not being a reproduction of the
work” (emphasis added). IBM queried the need for this distinction in the
definition,
as in its view an “adaptation” of a computer program was
something “not altogether different from a reproduction”.
6.64 The Committee notes that, prior to the 1984 amendments, the adaptation
right in respect of literary works included the right
“dramatisations, novelisations, translations and picturisations” of
such works; these provisions were not altered
by those amendments, which added
paragraph (ba) defining “adaptation” in relation to computer
programs. Each of these
treatments of such works can be regarded as a type of
reproduction in a material form (Ricketson,
The Law of Intellectual
, 1984, paragraph 9.39). This accords with IBM’s contention
that traditionally an adaptation was regarded as a reproduction
of the original
6.65 Apple in its submission also regarded the words “not being a
reproduction of the work” as adding an unnecessary complication
definition. In Apple’s view, the words preclude the possibility (19 May
1989, p.16, paragraph 1.2.4.9):
. . . that object code is an adaptation of the source code, since it can also
be a reproduction of the source code.
Apple agreed with IBM’s submission to the extent that there is no
reason why an adaptation should not be a reproduction of the
original work
translated. By excluding reproductions from the definition Apple took the view
that only “look and feel”
versions of programs fell within the
definition. Accordingly, Apple suggested that the definition of
“adaptation” should
be worded as follows (19 May 1989, p.55,
paragraph 3.12.1.1):
(ba) in relation to a literary work being a computer program – a
version of the work in which it is converted into or out of
a computer language
or code into a different computer language or code, or any other form in which
the work may be recast, transferred
or adapted, otherwise than incidentally in
the course of running the program.
6.66 Submissions from the AVCC, the LCA and Lotus all queried the effect of
the definition in light of the High Court’s decision
. In its submission the AVCC noted that the majority of the High Court
held there was no relationship between source code and object
code. As mentioned
in paragraph 5.05, the High Court found that object code is the result of
following instructions inherent in the
source code and in no way expresses or
renders the object code an adaptation or reproduction. As already indicated in
paragraph 6.40
the Committee does not agree with the High Court’s analysis
of the relationship between source code and object code. The LCA
also expressed
doubt that object code is an adaptation of source code because the High Court
did not regard a program in object code
as being a literary work. Lotus
suggested that object code embodied in ROM may not be a version of the source
code because Deane
J took the view that object code in ROM is not a version but
a functional article made according to a description or specification.
6.67 The submissions from Lotus and Microsoft also argued that object code
should be protected whether or not it is capable of being
regarded as a work in
its own right. Accordingly, Lotus suggested that s.31(1)(a)(vii) which deals
with the rights subsisting in
an adaptation should be amended to read as
(vii) to do, in relation to an adaptation of the work any of the acts
specified in relation to the work in subparagraphs (i) to (v),
Microsoft submitted that, consistent with its view, the definition of
adaptation should be amended by adding the words:
and whether or not the version, translation, arrangement or transcription as
the case may be is itself a work.
Microsoft also suggested that the definition of an adaptation should be
expanded to include derivative works by adding the words “a
program which is a derivative of the first mentioned computer program”. In
its first submission in response to the
Draft Report Oracle supported
Microsoft’s view that adaptation should be defined to include derivative
6.68 The Committee understands that the concern of computer program
manufacturers is that the unauthorised rendering of a program
in another code,
and in particular the rendering of a source code in object code, may not be
indisputably subject to the control
of the program copyright owner. This concern
is prompted by lingering concerns that object code may not be a reproduction of
source code (see paragraph 6.60), and doubts that a program in object code
is a work and, as such, able to be regarded as an adaptation
of the source code.
As to the eligibility of a program in object code for protection, the joint
judgment of Mason CJ, Brennan and
Deane JJ in the
Autodesk case
to the Committee to have removed those doubts, as noted in paragraph 6.29 of the
Draft Report (see paragraph 6.59).
6.69 In its consideration of the definition of “adaptation” the
Committee has also had regard to the reasons for granting
the owners of
regards it as essential that the copyright
owner should have the exclusive right
to make versions of his or her programs which embody or preserve the original
qualities of
the program. Of course, the scope of this right should not extend
to the protection of underlying ideas. The types of acts recognised
Committee as coming within the term “adaptation” include any form of
translation or compilation of a computer
program, including the conversion or
translation of a program in source code from one language to another language
and compilation
of a program in source code into object code. The definition of
“adaptation” contemplates such conversions by the inclusion
words “whether or not in the language, code or notation in which the work
was originally expressed”.
6.70 In his second reading speech on the
the then Attorney-General (Senator Gareth Evans) said:
One of the important rights in literary works is the right of adaptation,
which includes translation. Although computer scientists
speak of translation of
a program from one computer language to another, this may not presently be taken
by the courts to be translation
in the sense in which it appears in the present
definition of adaptation in relation to a computer program is intended to
make clear that it covers translation between computer
languages of different
levels such as happens when a program is being compiled or assembled.
The Explanatory Memorandum to the Act included the following comment on the
amendment to the definition of “adaptation”
(paragraph 14):
It is also possible for a program to be converted from object code into
source code, or between different languages of similar level.
circumstances this process will result largely in substantial reproduction of
the original program. In other cases, however,
such as compilation followed by
decompilation, the differences may be so substantial that one cannot speak of a
reproduction although
the final product is clearly derived from the original.
The new definition of adaptation is intended to cover such situations.
6.71 These quotations make it clear that the amendment was intended to cover
the case where rendering a program in source code in
object code or vice versa
would not normally be considered a reproduction. Accordingly, it appears to the
Committee that the intended
scope of paragraph (ba) of the definition of
“adaptation” as conveyed by the extrinsic materials quoted in
6.70, is based on the understanding by the draftsman that a computer
program in object code (created by the compilation of a source
code program) is
not a reproduction of the source code but an adaptation. This accords with the
Committee’s view expressed
in paragraph 6.61.
6.72 A result of an object code version of a program being characterised as
an “adaptation”, of the source code instead
“reproduction” of the source code program would be that the owner of
lose control over adaptations made
from the adaptation (object code program). This is because under s.31(1)(a)(vii)
the rights of
the owner of copyright in a work in relation to adaptations of the
work do not include the right to control adaptations
adaptations—
that is, where that owner is not also the owner of
limitations
of the adaptation right is contained in Chapter 9 from paragraph
9.51 onwards.
6.73 The Committee believes that it is particularly important that the right
of owners of copyright in computer programs to control
whatever may properly be
regarded as reproduction should not be curtailed by the intrusion of the
adaptation right with its limitation
just referred to. The Committee also
understands that in most cases object code programs will be derived from source
code by compilation
and that for practical purposes the industry regards the
object code version of a program as a mechanical derivative of the source
version, in other words as effectively a reproduction of the source code.
6.74 Despite this understanding in the computer industry, it is evident from
the preceding discussion that the law does not presently
interpret the
relationship between the two forms of code in this way. Because of the
requirement for objective similarity between
original works and reproductions,
the law views the relationship between source code and object code as analogous
to that of a musical
work and a sound recording, a book and a film based on it
or a plan and a building constructed from it. In all of these instances,
physical form of the sound recording, the film and the building could not be
viewed as being objectively similar to the originating
works, even though by
playing the sound recording or viewing the film or examining the building the
derivation from the original
work may be ascertainable. For this reason the Act
deems these representations of the works and other subject matter to be
reproductions.
6.75 Section 21(1) of the Act, which gives effect to articles 9(3) and 14(1)
of the Berne Convention, provides that a sound recording
or a cinematograph film
of a literary, dramatic or musical work is deemed to be a reproduction. Artistic
works are dealt with in
s.21(3). It provides that the conversion of an artistic
work from two-dimensional form to three-dimensional form and vice versa is
to be regarded as a reproduction. In the Committee’s view these provisions
serve the function of clarifying the scope
of the reproduction right where the
test of objective similarity cannot be easily satisfied.
6.76 For the same reason and given the close relationship between source code
and object code versions of a program the Act should
be amended by the addition
of a deeming provision which provides that, in relation to a computer program, a
reproduction includes
(but is not limited to) (Recommendation 2.04(e)(ii)):
(a) an object code version of the program that has been derived from the
program in source code by compilation; and
(b) a source code version of the program that has been derived from the
program in object code by decompilation.
Such an amendment will provide effective protection of copyright
owners’ property. The impact of this recommendation on the
scope of the
exclusive right of authors of computer programs to reproduce them is discussed
in Chapter 9, from paragraph 9.05 and
6.77 If this recommendation is adopted the Committee notes that there will be
no need to alter the wording of the definition of an
adaptation in s.10(1) and
accordingly recommends that no change be made to the definition of adaptation in
the Act (Recommendation
2.04(f)). A consequence of deeming both object compiled
from source code and source code decompiled from object code to be a
reproduction
would be that the scope of the definition of adaptation will in
effect be narrowed to cover only different source code versions of
written in source code, ie, translations other than compilations or
decompilations, eg new versions of a program obtained
after porting or error
correction. Chapter 9 discusses the scope of the adaptation right in the light
of this recommended change
in paragraph 9.51 and following.
6.78 The Chairman has misgivings about the recommendation in paragraph 6.76.
On balance, he would prefer that no definition of “reproduction”
recommended so that it will be for the courts to give the word content upon the
basis of the facts and circumstances of each particular
case and the
developments in technology current at the time of the particular decision. That
is the present position. It is to be
observed that there is no definition of
“reproduction” in any of the more recent legislation of the common
law countries.
6.79 The concern of those who recommend a definition appears to be based on
the fact that s.31 of the Act does not confer upon the
owner of copyright in
works the exclusive right to make an adaptation of an adaptation of a work.
Section 31(1)(a)(vii) provides
that copyright, in relation to a work, is the
exclusive right to do, in relation to a work that is an adaptation of another
any of the acts specified in relation to the first-mentioned work in
subsections (i) to (v) inclusive. Subsection (1)(a)(vi), which
confers the
exclusive right to make an adaptation of a work, is omitted from subsection
(vii). The reason for this would seem to
be that the making of an adaptation of
anything will usually involve both an element of copying and an element of
original work.
An adaptation may be distinguished from a reproduction which is
the literal copying of something and thus involves no original work
6.80 The present problem stems from the likelihood that all or part of the
source code of a program which is expressed in object code
or which is in
machine readable form may be retrieved without the authority of the copyright
owner. This may amount to an adaptation
of something which is itself already an
adaptation. That will not universally be the case, but it may be true in some
6.81 There is no problem about the unauthorised conversion of source code to
object code. That will either be a reproduction of the
source code or an
adaptation of it; see subsection (ba) of the definition of
“adaptation” in s.10(1) which is to be retained.
6.82 For present purposes the relevant part of the proposed definition of
“reproduction” in paragraph 6.76 is paragraph
(b). It provides that
a reproduction includes (but is not limited to) a source code version of the
program that has been derived
from the program in object code by decompilation.
It is the words “by decompilation” which concern the Chairman. This
is because, in his view, they are too restrictive. It is likely that, in the
future, even if not now, source code may be retrieved
from object code by a
means other than decompilation. The Chairman appreciates that the definition is
not exhaustive but his fear
is that there will be unauthorised retrievals of
source code from object code which will not be unlawful because the retrieval
not been achieved by decompilation. The fact that the definition is not
comprehensive may not overcome the problem. A court may well
say that the
legislature has expressly indicated its intention in relation to the
circumstances in which unauthorised retrieval from
object code will be
6.83 An earlier draft of paragraph (b) of the definition of
“reproduction” circulated to members of the Committee used
“by any means including decompilation”. The Chairman, despite his
preference for no definition at all, was
prepared to accept this because the
words covered the field. The reason for the change stems from a concern that the
retention of
the words “by any means” would produce an inconsistency
or unevenness between the recommendations in Chapter 10 concerning
exceptions to
exclusive rights and the definition of “reproduction” in this
chapter. Some of the recommendations in Chapter
10 recommend allowing
decompilation for certain specified purposes. In no case, however, is there to
be a general right of decompilation
nor is there to be a right to engage in
reverse engineering which extends beyond endeavouring to understand the way in
which a program
works and the underlying idea of it by operating the program,
studying its operation and studying any published literature about
Chairman fails to understand why these matters give rise to any problem
concerning the meaning of reproduction. In numerous
situations, the law, whether
statutory or judge-made, provides for a general rule and specified exceptions to
that rule. The situation
is common. The general rule will be stated widely; the
exception will be stated narrowly and precisely. Here the definition of
“reproduction”
needs to be expressed in wide terms; otherwise
activity which ought to be unlawful will not be caught. The exceptions provided
in Chapter 10 can be stated specifically and precisely. The terms in which
they are expressed will show quite plainly what is excepted.
All else will be
within the general rule and thus unlawful if unauthorised.
6.84 It follows that, in the Chairman’s opinion, if there is to be a
definition of “reproduction” it should contain
some such words as
“by any means” which were originally included. An alternative course
would be to amend s.10(1)(ba)
of the Act so as to apply it expressly to the case
of the retrieval of source codes from other forms of expression of computer
This has two advantages. It leaves the problem to be dealt with as one
concerning adaptation and not reproduction, something which
the industry, the
legal profession and the courts have been used to since the 1984 amendments.
Secondly, it avoids the danger that
defining “reproduction”, albeit
inclusively rather than exhaustively, will tend to restrict or limit the reach
of “adaptation”
in this area. Indeed, the insertion of the
definition of “reproduction” may turn an area which has been certain
which people are used to working into one that is quite unclear and
needs recharting. There is no reason for an excursion into unchartered
6.85 Finally, the problem raised by these various considerations should be
recognised for what it is. It is a drafting problem. The
Committee is not
attempting to draft legislation. Its task is to consider and make
recommendations about matters of substance. In
the Chairman’s opinion, the
legislation should ensure that computer programs in whatever form they are
expressed are safe from
piracy. At the same time certain specific situations in
which a person is allowed to reproduce or adapt a program without the consent
the owner of the copyright need to be provided for. Perhaps that is all that
needs to be said about this whole problem.
The definition of “material
6.86 In relation to the exclusive right to reproduce a literary work
(computer program) “in a material form” (s.31(1)(a)(i)),
submitted that the definition of “material form” in s.10(1) is
circular, because (1989, p.13):
. . . whether a particular form is a material form in the expanded sense is
determined by its having the quality that “the work”
can therefrom
be reproduced in a form of the same type [ie, any form from which a reproduction
can be made in a similar form to the
original work]. However, what type of form
that is, is not resolved.
The Committee considers that this analysis of the definition of
“material form”, which is set out in paragraph 5.01, pays
insufficient attention to the words “of storage” immediately
following “(whether visible or not)” in the
definition. It is
apparent from these words that the intention of the definition is to cover any
way now known or hereafter devised
for capturing a work so that it can be
reproduced. As noted earlier in the chapter, “reproduced” could
cover substantial
similarity as well as an identical copy.
6.87 The Committee also notes that the definition of material form is an
inclusive, not exhaustive, definition. The sense described
in an inclusive
definition serves to extend the normal meaning not to overtake it (cf
Local Board of Barton–Eccles
(1883) 8 App Cas
Lord Selborne LC at 801. This sense identifies a distinct test against which a
form can be measured and any further
resolution (suggested by IBM) would
sacrifice the generality to this test. Accordingly, in the Committee’s
view, “material
form” should be construed as meaning not only
something of corporeal form, composed of matter, but as including those forms
storage where a work or an adaptation of a work exists in a form that would not
normally be regarded as material, such as electronic
and magnetic forms of
storage, but which are amenable to reproduction. For this reason it does not
agree with IBM’s conclusion
that the definition is necessarily circular.
The Committee therefore recommends that no change be made to the definition of
form in s.10(1) of the Act (Recommendation 2.04(g)).
Ownership of Copyright in Computer Programs
Section 35(2)
(the Act) provides that,
subject to the section, the author of a literary work is the owner of any
Subject to what is later to be mentioned in
relation to employees and some other classes of persons, it follows that at the
time the owner of the copyright in a computer program is the author.
JOINT AUTHORS
7.02 Very often more than one author will be involved, with the consequence
that the provisions dealing with works of joint authorship
will apply. In this
respect s.78 of the Act provides that a reference in the Act to the author of a
work shall, unless otherwise
expressly provided by the Act, be read, in relation
to a work of joint authorship, as a reference to all the authors of the work.
Section 79 provides that references in s.32 (original works in which copyright
subsists) and in s.34(2) (ascertainment of authors
of anonymous and pseudonymous
works) to the author of a work shall, in relation to a work of joint authorship,
be read as references
to any one or more of the authors of the work.
7.03 On the basis that all computer programs in whatever form they are
expressed are required to be protected as literary works (see
Chapter 4), the
Committee is of the opinion that these general provisions should continue to
apply to computer programs. Many computer
programs will be written or compiled,
not by one person, but by many. This circumstance may not infrequently give rise
to difficulty
in ascertaining the persons, or the totality of the persons, who
are joint authors. The position is likely to be more difficult than
is in relation to conventional works because the authors of computer programs
are likely to be substantially more numerous
than is the case in respect of
other works.
7.04 A further problem may arise in relation to programs which are the
subject of the work of numerous authors but where the authors
are not joint
authors. Such a situation could occur where a program is the result of the
putting together of several separate and
distinct sections each written or
compiled separately from the others. In such cases the reality may be that there
are a number of
separate and distinct works which have been joined together to
constitute one program. The entire program may not be a work of joint
authorship. Each separate section will be itself a work in which copyright will
subsist and there may be a separate copyright in
the compilation of those
7.05 Despite these various problems, the Committee sees no alternative to
leaving the present provisions in place. If disputes occur,
courts may need to
determine complex questions of fact and law which may arise in a given case. But
it is not a matter which is appropriate
for legislation. It would be undesirable
and impractical to endeavour to provide comprehensively for the numerous
possibilities which
there might be as a consequence of these difficulties.
Individual treatment is necessary and this can only be afforded by the
determination
of disputes by courts (See Recommendation 2.05 at paragraph 7.09).
EMPLOYED OR COMMISSIONED AUTHORS
7.06 The present position is ameliorated to a degree by the provisions of
s.35(6) which provides that where a literary work, to which
neither of the last
two preceding subsections (subsections (4) and (5)) applies, is made by the
author in pursuance of the terms
of his employment by another person under a
contract of service or apprenticeship, that other person, ie, the employer, is
of any copyright subsisting in the work. Reference will be made to
s.35(4) and (5) a little later. The present position then is that
author of a program is an employee and it is made in pursuance of the terms of
his/her employment, it will be the employer
who is the owner of the copyright
and not the employee. The Committee is of the opinion that this provision should
be retained in
relation to computer programs.
7.07 In its Draft Report the Committee advanced the view that, so far as
computer programs were concerned, it would take the position
somewhat further.
It took the view that no distinction should be made between employees and
persons (not employees) who were retained,
for example as consultants or
independent contractors, to create a program. The Committee saw this as avoiding
unnecessary inquiries
into whether particular relationships were employment
relationships or contractual relationships of a different kind. More
importantly,
it seemed to the Committee to be more sensible and more equitable
to confer ownership on the person who had commissioned the writing
of a program
rather than upon those, whether employees or not, who were the authors of it. In
so saying, the Committee noted that
it would, of course, be possible for the
parties to any arrangement to reach an agreement providing expressly for the
ownership of
the copyright. It would be open to them to agree that,
notwithstanding the provisions of the legislation, the copyright was to be
by one or more specified persons, whether the authors, the commissioner or some
other person or persons or by persons falling
into all or some of these
categories. It seemed to the Committee that, if the present s.35(6), insofar as
it applies to computer
programs, were amended along the lines favoured, a great
many of the potential problems which may exist in relation to works of joint
authorship would tend to disappear. In the Committee’s view, there would
be more certainty and a fairer regime.
7.08 At least eight submissions commented on the view of the Committee, in
its Draft Report, that ownership of copyright in commissioned
computer programs
should reside with the commissioning party. All of the comments opposed that
view. However, reports of public discussion
include comments supporting the
Committee’s view, as did at least one journal article ([1994]
European Intellectual Property Review
Also, the Committee did not
attach a lot of weight to one of the criticisms of its view, namely, that
adoption of original copyright
ownership by commissioning parties would set us
apart from, and complicate cross-border transactions with parties in, other
Recommendation regarding all authors
7.09 The Committee has the impression that there is already a variety of
approaches around the world to ownership of copyright in
works by employed and
commissioned authors. Nevertheless, after reviewing the various comments on its
Draft Report, the Committee
accepted that the task of identifying the author(s)
of computer program was not so much more difficult—if indeed more
at all—than in the case of some other types of copyright works
as to justify a different allocation of copyright ownership
from that applying
to other works. Accordingly the Committee now recommends against any change to
the provisions of s.35(6) in its
application to computer programs
(Recommendation 2.05).
EMPLOYEES OF NEWSPAPERS, MAGAZINES AND SIMILAR
PERIODICALS

no relevance to the present problem because it applies
to artistic works.

or similar periodicals under contracts of

publication
in newspapers, magazines or similar periodicals, the relevant

periodical, broadcasting the work, or reproduction
of the work for the purpose
of its being so published or broadcast, but not otherwise. This provision is
unlikely to be of any relevance
in relation to the making of computer programs.
They will not be published in newspapers, and journalists will be unlikely to
computer programs at least in the ordinary course of their employment as
