COMPUTER PROGRAMS PRODUCED IN EDUCATIONAL
INSTITUTIONS
7.11 It remains to say something of computer programs produced by students of
educational institutions. In submissions made to the
Committee by the Australian
Vice-Chancellors’ Committee and Australian Committee of Directors and
Principals Limited (AVCC–ACDP)
it is said that there is an issue as to who
should own computer programs produced by staff and/or students, particularly
are produced by the use of equipment and other resources provided by
the institution. Reference was made to s.35(6) of the Act and
acknowledged that, in relation to a staff member, ownership of copyright would
depend upon whether or not the work was created
in pursuance of the terms of
his/her employment. In the case of students it was acknowledged that ownership
resided with the student.
Paragraph 29 of the submission (July 1989) was as
Whilst the existing scheme may or may not be particularly clear or desirable,
it would seem that it may not be something that is best
dealt with in the
probably best left for agreement between
higher education institutions, and
staff and students by way of contractual provision in employment contracts and
institutional rules
and statutes.
In the light of that submission, the Committee does not recommend the making
of any special provision for the ownership of copyright
in computer programs
produced by either staff or students of educational institutions.
COMPUTER PROGRAMS PRODUCED BY OR FOR
GOVERNMENTS
7.12 In its very well considered and detailed comments on the Committee's
Draft Report, the Australian Copyright Council (ACC) invited
the Committee to
comment on the appropriateness of ss.176–178 in their application to
computer programs. Sections 176 and 178
vest in the Commonwealth or a State or
Territory the copyright in original works, sound recordings and films made by or
direction or control, where that would not result under other
provisions of the Act. Section 177 vests in the Commonwealth or a State
Territory the copyright in works first published in Australia under its
direction or control. The ACC expressed doubt about the
justification for the
provisions, which are based on provisions in the UK
that were not carried over into the
. The ACC questioned the justification for ss.176–178 particularly
in relation to material that it referred to as not "governmental"
by nature. The
ACC envisaged—but without providing recorded instances of—cases
where copyright would vest in a government
under those sections but would not do
so otherwise. In inviting the Committee to appraise the justification for these
sections, the
ACC noted that the Committee had made a recommendation for
amendment to s.183 which provides for a general licence to governments
the copyrights of others, subject to notifying such use and terms for the use
(Draft Report—paragraph 10.83, Final Report—paragraph
Recommendation 2.33).
7.13 As to the last mentioned comment by the ACC, the Committee addressed the
merits of s.183 in relation to government use of computer
programs because the
issue had been raised in submissions received prior to preparation of the
Committee's Draft Report. Also, it
was made aware of allegations of the
widespread unauthorised use by government agencies of computer programs.
However, while not
denying that there may be a good case for reviewing
ss.176–178, the Committee does not feel able to embark on that exercise
the context and circumstances of this reference. As already noted, the Committee
has not been provided with instances of the oppressive
consequences of
ss.176–178, although this is not to deny the possibility that instances
have occurred. If the Committee had
been persuaded that changes to the
provisions might be desirable, it would have preferred to give governments the
opportunity to
respond to such a conclusion, as it did in the case of s.183 by
including its recommendation in its Draft Report. The Committee understands
governments are substantial creators of computer programs, and would have a
clear interest in considering any suggested changes
to the provisions concerning
government ownership of copyright. For these reasons, the Committee concludes
that it should not make
any recommendation regarding ss.176–178, and
simply draws attention to the ACC’s concern for whatever further
consideration
the Government may care to give to it.
Duration of Protection of Computer Programs
8.01 The duration of protection of computer programs is a difficult area.
Article 7 of the Berne Convention for the Protection of
Literary and Artistic
Works (Berne Convention) provides that the period of protection for literary and
artistic works is the life
of the author and a further period of 50 years. That
provision is given effect to in Australia by
(the Act). Article 7(6) of the Berne Convention provides that member
countries may grant a longer term of protection. As noted in
Chapter 3, the
TRIPS Agreement adopted as part of Final Act of the Uruguay Round of
multilateral trade negotiations concluded in
December 1993, ie, after release of
the Committee’s Draft Report, requires member countries to comply with
art.7 of the Berne
Convention, amongst others, and to protect computer programs
as literary works under the Berne Convention. Article 12 of TRIPS further
Whenever the term of protection of a work, other than a photographic work or
a work of applied art, is calculated on a basis other
than the life of a natural
person, such term shall be no less than 50 years from the end of the calendar
year of authorised publication,
or, failing such authorised publication within
50 years from the making of the work, 50 years from the end of the calendar year
COMMITTEE’S
VIEW IN DRAFT
Term of protection
8.02 In its Draft Report, the Committee expressed the view that, in relation
to computer programs, the period of life of the author
plus 50 years was not
only too long but also likely to be productive of substantial uncertainty and
difficulty. The Committee’s
view was that the desirable period of
protection should be no more than 50 years from the end of the year in which the
program was
made. Some members of the Committee considered that a period of 25
years would be sufficient. It would be a matter for the court
in each case to
determine the date or year of making. This was something which it was accustomed
to doing in relation to all types
of works, by reason of s.22 of the Act. That
section provides, inter alia, that a reference to the time when a work was made
be read as a reference to the time when the work was first reduced to
writing or some other material form. It will be recalled that
the definition of
“material form” was inserted in 1984. It includes any form (whether
visible or not) of storage from
which a work or adaptation can be produced.
Published and unpublished works
8.03 The Act presently draws a distinction between the rights conferred in
respect of an unpublished work and those conferred in respect
of a work that has
been published. In its Draft Report, the Committee also expressed the view that
no distinction should be drawn
in relation to the length of the period of
protection in respect of unpublished and published computer programs. Presently
of the Act provides that if, before the death of the author of a work,
the work was not published (or publicly performed, broadcast
or released on
record) copyright in the work continues to subsist until 50 years after the
expiration of the year in which the work
is first published, etc. If the work is
never published (or publicly performed, broadcast or released on record),
indefinitely. Many programs will be published in the sense
that they are sold or licensed on wholesale or retail markets, but many
not. However the Committee saw no reason to distinguish between the two. There
may be good reason for allowing copyright to
subsist in conventional literary
works for however long they remain unpublished, performed, etc; for example,
private letters not
intended for publication when written. However, all computer
programs have a utilitarian purpose and, whether published or not, will
limited useful life because of constant development of computer technology.
Accordingly, the Committee affirms its draft recommendation
that the existing
term of protection consisting of life of the author plus 50 years should apply
to all computer programs, whether
published or unpublished (Recommendation
8.04 Although the Committee considered, in its Draft Report, that the period
of protection should be reduced to not more than 50 years
from the year of
making, it concluded that there were reasons stemming from Australia’s
membership of the Berne Convention
which made it difficult for the provisions of
s.33 of the Act to be amended to give effect to the Committee’s
preference.
REASONS FOR COMMITTEE’S VIEW IN DRAFT
8.05 In its Draft Report, ie, before the conclusion of TRIPS, the Committee
alluded to what it understood was a possibility that
the protocol being
considered to the Berne Convention might include specific and more appropriate
protection for computer programs,
which might be of shorter duration than that
for literary works. On that basis, the Committee felt that it was worthwhile
the reasons why the Committee was of the view that protection for the
life of the author plus a further period of 50 years was too
long. Among those
who made comments on the issue, Professor Cornish, Professor-Elect of Law,
Magdalene College, University of Cambridge
(UK) urged the Committee to recommend
its preferred term of protection, ie, 50 years, on the basis that it was more
appropriate than
life of the author plus 50 years and that Berne was of doubtful
application to computer programs. Of the others to comment on this
part of the
Draft Report, most favoured the existing term of protection for literary works.
8.06 The Committee’s reasons for its view that a shorter term of
• A period of life plus 50 years is intrinsically too long a period of
protection for computer programs. By their very nature
they do not require
protection for so long. This view is widely held both in Australia and overseas.
It was reflected in many, but
by no means all, of the submissions received by
the Committee before release of its Draft Report and half of those received on
Draft Report that commented on this issue . Overseas examples of it are to
be found in the 1977 World Intellectual Property Organisation
Provisions on the Protection of
Computer Software
and in the law of
France. The Committee has considered carefully those submissions which advocate
the retention of the conventional
period of life of the author plus 50 years but
remains of the opinion that this is not only too long in an intrinsic sense but
impractical. A majority of the Committee was persuaded, notwithstanding
submissions to the contrary, that a period of 25 years from
making might be too
short and concluded that a period of 50 years from the end of the year of making
would have been a satisfactory
compromise.
• There is a particular difficulty in the area of computer programs in
defining the period of protection by reference to the
life of the author. This
arises because programs will very often be written or compiled by numerous
authors. The Act, like its overseas
counterparts, makes provision for the period
of protection of works of joint authorship; see ss.78, 79 and 80. The period is
after the death of the last surviving author; s.80. These provisions
were enacted without the problems associated with computers
and computer
programs in mind. They have usually been applied in relation to conventional
literary, musical or dramatic works. In
relation to these, they occasion little
difficulty. But many computer programs have a multiplicity of authors whose
numbers may be
measured in tens or even scores. The exercise of writing a
particular program may be spread over many months and sometimes longer.
of programmers may not remain the same throughout the entirety of the period.
Members who commenced may leave; others may
come in part way through and remain
either for the balance of the period or for part of it. Some may be employed or
retained only
to help with certain parts of the work; yet their contribution may
be of substantial importance. A joint author must have taken a
substantial part
in the creation of the work; see
Copinger and Skone James on Copyright
(12th ed., London, Sweet and Maxwell,
1980, Ch. 10
, pp.141 et seq). In some
cases a program which has been written by a number of people may not be a work
of joint authorship. Rather,
the position may be that each author (or group of
authors) has contributed a distinct part of the whole. In such a case there will
be more than one work making up the composite. Each case will depend on its own
circumstances. For present purposes it is enough
to make the point that the fact
that a work may be one of joint authorship or a composite of two or more
separate and distinct works,
demonstrates how complex the task may be of
identifying the author or authors of a program. These various considerations
that the life of the author is not a useful or an apposite guide to the
desirable period of protection. No doubt those who were responsible
earlier copyright legislation were concerned that termination of the copyright
owner’s rights on death (which was the
original law) might operate most
unjustly. That led to the addition of the 50 year period. But the technology
with which this reference
is concerned is so vastly different from what is
involved in relation to conventional works that it is essential that protection
be for a finite period not related to the lifetime of any person.
• There is a reason, stemming from the public interest, why the same
finite period of protection should be conferred in respect
of all computer
programs. The market for computer programs is a most competitive one. It is not
in the public interest that computer
programs be protected for longer than is
reasonably necessary. And it is certainly not in the public interest that
periods of protection
for different programs, perhaps competing programs, differ
according to the length of the lives of the respective authors.
• Furthermore, it is important that competitors be able to ascertain
with as much certainty as possible what the actual period
of protection in a
given case is. This is one of the benefits of the patent law in relation to
patents. There is no system of registration
of copyright either in Australia or
in most other countries. The system of registration in the United States is now
optional unless
the American owner of the copyright wishes to enforce its
rights. It will not always be an easy task to ascertain when a program
But it will be more difficult for a competitor, assuming it can identify the
author or authors of a program (itself an
almost insuperable task in this area)
to set about the task of finding out whether some or all of the authors are
alive and then,
as the decades go by, of finding out when the death of the last
survivor occurred so that the beginning of the 50 year period can
Those engaged in this task would of course come from later generations of people
than those who wrote the program. In practical
terms protection for the life of
the last surviving author plus a further period of 50 years is protection, in
the area of computer
programs, in perpetuity. In every conceivable case the
period will continue long after the program has been superseded and has become
valueless and irrelevant.
• In commenting on the Draft Report the Australian Copyright Council
(ACC) stated that they were (p.13, paragraph 62):
. . . not convinced that the existing periods (sic) of protection necessarily
inhibit the development of computer programs.
The ACC further noted that:
The Committee's remarks seem to be premised on an assumption that the
inability to copy another person's program creates an impediment
to the creation
of new programs.
The Committee acknowledges that to be the case, but points out that the
purpose of copying may be, as it is given to understand,
to study the code of
the program with a view to developing a competing product, such as can be done
with other copyright works without
fear of infringement. As will be seen in
Chapter 10, the Committee's recommendation regarding permitted decompilation of
in copyright may not extend to all possible cases of decompilation for
that purpose. If a program is out of copyright, decompilation
can be undertaken
without any possibility of infringement.
• The need to ascertain the identity of the author or authors of the
program and the date of the death of the last surviving
author may also provide
problems for copyright owners seeking to enforce their rights. If a person
claiming to be the copyright owner
of a computer program sues for infringement,
the defendant may put the plaintiff to proof of his or her title. In many cases
onus which the copyright owner has may be discharged by showing that one or
more of the authors of the program is alive. It will
not be relevant to know any
more because the period of 50 years will not have commenced to run and the
period of protection will
not have expired. But as the years go by and members
of staff leave their employment and consultants who may have taken part in the
writing of programs change their occupations and addresses, the difficulty for
some plaintiffs will increase. In an area as dynamic
as computer programs, this
may not often create a problem but it may do so in some cases and it seems to
the Committee to be very
much in the interests of copyright owners to have a
situation in which all they need prove is that the program was made in a
year and that a period of 50 years has not elapsed since that
United States—term of protection for
”works for hire”
8.07 In the course of its deliberations, the Committee also considered the
“work for hire” provisions of the United States
legislation. Section
(US) provides that, in general, copyright
in a work created on or after 1 January 1978 subsists from its creation and,
the later provisions of the section, endures for a term consisting of
the life of the author and 50 years after the author’s
death. In the case
of a joint work by authors who “did not work for hire”, the
of the life of the last surviving author
and 50 years after such last surviving author’s death. Section 302(c)
deals with anonymous
works, pseudonymous works, and works made “for
hire”. It is unnecessary to refer to the provisions concerning anonymous
and pseudonymous works but the provisions in relation to those are in many
respects similar to those which affect works “for
hire”. In relation
to such works, the copyright endures for a term of 75 years from the year of its
first publication, or a
term of 100 years from the year of its creation,
whichever expires first. Works made “for hire” are dealt with in
It provides that, in the case of a work made “for hire”,
the employer or other person for whom the work was prepared
is considered the
author and, unless the parties have expressly agreed otherwise in writing signed
by them, owns all of the rights
comprised in the copyright. A work made
“for hire” is defined in s.101 of the Act. It is to be noted that
not all commissioned
works are considered to be works made “for
Nimmer On Copyright
(1992, pp.5.13 to 5.30, paragraph
5.03[B]); but it would seem from the analysis which appears there that most
commissioned programs
would be works made “for hire”.
8.08 The United States position is interesting but is not an example which it
is helpful to follow. The fixed term is applied only
in relation to “works
for hire”. Computer programs will very often be “works for
hire” but that will not
always be so. Furthermore, even if a program is a
work for hire, the periods of protection of 75 and 100 years which may then be
applicable, may not always accord with the Berne Convention. Many of the most
successful program writers have been extremely young.
Many have been in their
early twenties when the work was created. If one assumes a life expectancy of,
say, 50 years and adds a further
period of 50 years, the result substantially
exceeds the period of 75 years provided for in the legislation. 100 years may be
the mark but even it may be exceeded in some cases.
8.09 The very mention of periods of 75 and 100 years in this context lends
emphasis to the Committee’s view that a period of
the life of the author
and a further period of 50 years are just too long. Even the 50 years from the
year of making favoured by
the Committee is at the upper limit of what is
reasonable. As noted earlier, once one contemplates periods of this length for
protection of a right in property which is so dynamic, one is,
realistically, talking in terms of a period which lacks any relevant
ending. It is truly protection in perpetuity. A minority of the Committee have a
similar view about the period of protection
of 50 years. In the minority
opinion, that also is a very long time. It may not amount to protection in
perpetuity but, from a practical
point of view, it will in most cases amount to
the same thing.
8.10 Whilst all these considerations are relevant and important, the
Committee cannot put forward a recommendation that the conventional
protection for computer programs be shortened. Adherence by Australia to the
TRIPS agreement, as is the Government’s
stated intention, would prevent
the implementation of any such recommendation. For reasons already alluded to in
paragraph 8.01 and
fully elaborated in Chapter 4, the obligations to be taken on
by Australia under TRIPS will preclude the further contemplation of
of protection of anything short of life/lives of the author/s plus 50 years.
Accordingly, the Committee has reluctantly
concluded that the provisions of s.33
of the Act, subject to what has been said about unpublished works, must remain
as they are
(Recommendation 2.06(b)).
8.11 In its Draft Report, the Committee expressed the hope that within Berne
and at other international meetings and conventions,
Australia would seek to
achieve agreement on a much shorter period of protection than that which is
presently provided for, and agreement
with the submission dated 14 July 1992 by
the Australian Government to WIPO on the possible protocol to Berne in so far as
it included
the proposal of a 50 years’ fixed term of protection for
computer programs. However with the conclusion of TRIPS, that seems
a vain hope,
however compelling the Committee believes the case to be.
Exclusive Rights
ECONOMIC RIGHTS
9.01 The exclusive rights of the owner of copyright in a literary work are
(the Act). In the case of
a literary work, those rights are the exclusive right to:
(a) reproduce the work in a material form;
(b) publish the work;
(c) perform the work in public;
(d) broadcast the work;
(e) cause the work to be transmitted to subscribers to a diffusion
(f) make an adaptation of the work; and
(g) do any of acts (a) to (e) in relation to an adaptation of the work.
Section 31(2) provides that the generality of the right of reproduction is
not affected by the express right to make an adaptation.
9.02 Submissions to the Committee made prior to the Draft Report did not
specify the appropriate bundle of rights that should be afforded
to the owner of
accepted that the owner of copyright
in a computer program should enjoy all the
rights that subsist in literary works. Submissions made following the Draft
Report mostly
endorsed the recommendations of the Committee in relation to the
exclusive rights of copyright owners of computer programs. A number
submissions did suggest that the owner of copyright in a computer program should
also have the additional right to control the
rental of his or her computer
programs. The requirement of the Agreement on Trade Related Aspects of
Intellectual Property Rights
(TRIPS) embodied in the General Agreement on
Tariffs and Trade (GATT) that a rental right be introduced is discussed at
9.77–9.85 (See Chapter 3 for full discussion of the TRIPS
Agreement).
9.03 As noted in Chapter 4 (paragraph 4.04), the Committee reached the
conclusion in the Draft Report that computer programs in source
not also in object code—were required to be protected as literary works
under the Berne Convention for the Protection
of Literary and Artistic Works
(Berne Convention). As made clear by the discussion in Chapter 4, the
finalisation of the TRIPS Agreement
now puts an end to all speculation on this
matter by “emphatically requiring all forms of computer programs to be
as literary works under the Berne Convention” by virtue of
art.10(1) (paragraph 4.05).
9.04 The Berne Convention prescribes certain minimum standards of protection
as well as the various exclusive rights authors enjoy
in respect of their works.
The need to have regard to the requirements of the Berne Convention and now the
TRIPS Agreement in relation
to, in particular, the exclusive rights, has
precluded the Committee from recommending against certain of those rights in
of computer programs.
9.05 The Committee also notes that the unique nature of computer programs as
perform certain functions,
means that not all aspects of computer programs will readily come within the
scope of copyright protection.
In particular, this is evident when consideration
is given to the scope of the reproduction right and its application in respect
of the “look and feel”, “user interface”,
“non-literal elements” and the “structure sequence
organisation” of computer programs. It is clear from a consideration of
these aspects of computer programs that some may
be regarded as commercially
valuable and deserving of protection. However, it can be argued that some of
them fall outside the proper
scope of copyright protection. The Committee does
not regard this fact as a consideration which necessarily warrants amendments to
the Act. It notes that the scope of protection afforded under each of the
various statutory intellectual and industrial property
regimes has its own
limitations. This simply reflects the nature of that protection.
THE RIGHT TO REPRODUCE A COMPUTER PROGRAM IN A
MATERIAL FORM
9.06 The right to reproduce a work in a material form is fundamental to the
protection of authors. Without this right authors would
be unable to effectively
exploit their works. The Committee received the clear impression from all that
it heard and read that the
right to control reproduction was the pre-eminent
concern—indeed almost the only concern—of computer program
However, the determination of whether a program has been copied by
another program is often a highly technical and more complex exercise
determining whether a book or song has been plagiarised. This is not to suggest
that copyright infringement concerning traditional
forms of works is not
difficult; the Committee simply wishes to emphasise just how difficult the issue
of copyright infringement
concerning computer programs can be by any standards.
This is inevitable given the extremely technical and complex nature of programs.
The facts of
Autodesk Inc
Martin Dyason and others
(High Court decision: (No 1)
[1992] HCA 2
(1992) 173 CLR
22 IPR 163)
already discussed from paragraph 5.06 are by no means
exceptional amongst computer program cases in their complexity; they are fairly
9.07 The Committee notes that the intense focus of industry and users on the
reproduction right arises from several considerations.
First and most obvious is
that the ubiquity of and investment in computers mean that the scale of
investment in development and marketing
of programs is vast, with the result
that the proprietors of successful programs stand to suffer great loss if not
protected from
competition from unauthorised copies. More importantly, from a
social perspective, large scale unauthorised copying will stifle development
new programs because program writers will be unable to reap just rewards for
their labours. Next, the technology of computers
means that, in most cases,
copying of programs can be accomplished effortlessly and instantaneously.
Indeed, copying is part of the
normal use of most, if not all, programs. The
competition amongst program producers is vigorous: whenever that is the case
producers of intellectual property, the field of competitors is bound to
include imitators and copyists. Because of the functional
purpose of computer
programs, once a particular feature becomes standardised by popularity amongst
users, eg, the graphic user interface,
the competition to win market share will
inevitably mean a convergence between rival products and a consequent increase
in the incentive
for copying. Finally, because of the nature of computer
programs—the Committee has in mind the evolution from source code to
finished product, and the fact that many programs in fact comprise collections
of modules which are themselves programs—the
possibilities of copying,
inadvertent or deliberate, by a competitor seem to be very great.
9.08 Having regard to the negative effects uncontrolled copying of computer
programs would have on the incentive to develop new and
improved versions, and
the losses that would be sustained by authors, the Committee recommends that the
author of a computer program
should have the exclusive right to control the
reproduction of his or her work (Recommendation 2.08). The Committee believes
its recommendation in relation to the definition of reproduction, set out
in Chapter 6 in paragraphs 6.55 and 6.76 will considerably
strengthen the rights
of the owners of copyright in computer programs, as well as authors and owners
of copyright in other types
of works. However, that is not the end, but only the
beginning, of consideration of this right as it applies to computer programs.
The following discussion sets out, in simple terms, the Committee’s
understanding of the nature of computer programs. It is
the unique nature of
computer programs, amongst other works protected by copyright, that gives rise
to particular difficulties in
determining the appropriate scope of the
reproduction right for programs.
The nature of computer programs
9.09 In March 1991 the Committee’s Secretary attended a World
Intellectual Property Organisation (WIPO) symposium in San Francisco
examined intellectual property issues raised by artificial intelligence. At that
symposium Professor Randall Davis, of the Massachusetts
Institute of Technology,
delivered a paper entitled “Intellectual Property and Software: the
assumptions are broken”
(WIPO Worldwide Symposium on the Intellectual
Property Aspects of Artificial Intelligence
, WIPO, Geneva, 1991). In his
paper Professor Davis makes the point that not only are computer programs text
(embodied in code) but
that they also behave (p.110):
. . . [the] text-like aspects [of the program] can be accommodated by
traditional copyright concepts and vocabulary. [But] a second,
important, thing about a program is its behaviour: a program exists in order to
get the computer to
something (‘bring about a certain
result’). A word processing program can for instance, insert text, delete
it, move it, align the margins, etc.
9.10 Professor Davis noted that both the “literal” (ie, textual)
elements of a program and its behavioural elements possess
their own structure,
sequence and organisation, and that each class of elements is, to the user,
independent of the other. He illustrated
this fact by using the example of a
word processing program. Two of the behaviours (or functions) of a typical word
processing program
are deleting words and deleting paragraphs. If the text of
such a program were written out in source code one might find that the
the “delete word” function precedes the code for the “delete
paragraph” function. However, the sequence
of those two routines, ie, the
order in which they appear in the source code, can be altered without changing
the behaviour of the
program in any way. Accordingly, a user of two such
programs would be unable to tell which of the two programs he or she was using
because, although the structure, sequence and organisation of the
differed, the structure, sequence, and organisation of the
would remain the same.
9.11 To further illustrate the degree of independence of these two aspects of
computer programs, Professor Davis made the following
observation. A user may
become very familiar with the structure, sequence and organisation of a
program’s behaviour to the
extent that he or she might claim to know very
well what the program can do. However, this in itself will not give the user any
of what the program code looks like. In other words the program’s
behaviour tells a user nothing about the text of the program.
9.12 The Committee accepts Professor Davis’ analysis of computer
programs. From that analysis it is clear that computer programs
features in common with traditional literary works, and others that are not.
Those features of a computer program that
comprise its text or code (whether
source or object code) lend themselves to comparison with elements of
traditional literary works,
while those features that can be described as its
behaviour do not.
The meaning of “look and feel”,
“user interface”, “non-literal elements” and
”structure,
sequence and organisation”
9.13 From submissions made to the Committee and from the reasoning adopted in
several judicial decisions overseas, there appears to
be some confusion as to
what features of a computer program can be considered for the purpose of
establishing whether or not it has
been infringed by the creation of another
computer program. The owners of copyright in computer programs have sought to
protect such
things as the “look and feel”, “user
interface”, “non-literal elements” and “structure,
sequence and organisation” of computer programs from being copied. In its
1990 submission Sun Microsystems Australia Pty Ltd
(Sun Microsystems) noted that
It is not clear if the concept of ‘look and feel’ of software is
the idea or the expression of the idea.
Before considering whether copyright protection should extend to any of these
features of a computer program, the Committee will express
its understanding of
each of them for the purposes of this report.
“Look and feel” and the “user
9.14 The concepts of “look and feel” and the “user
interface” are peculiar to computer software. The “look
feel” or “user interface” of a traditional literary work is
not, so far as the Committee is aware, normally
a matter for consideration in
actions for infringement of copyright in such works. However, it has been made
very clear to the Committee
that the “look and feel” or “user
interface” of a computer program is the distinguishing feature as far as
most, if not all, users are concerned. This reflects the functional nature of
computer programs.
9.15 Unlike even the most utilitarian traditional literary work, eg, an
instruction manual for an electrical appliance, a computer
program is not bought
to be read, but to drive a machine or, more precisely, to cause it to perform
specified functions. Indeed,
if one viewed in hardcopy or on the screen the
object code of a computer program, it would necessarily be a low level
representation
of the electrical impulses comprising the program and, as such,
would be meaningless to the average computer user. It would be a
representation
of “on” and “off” commands, comparable to the
“long” and “short” signals
of morse code transmission,
except that the latter when decoded comprises words conveying a message
comprehensible to and intended
for humans, whereas the former does not. This
serves to emphasise that programs are normally acquired for what they can make a
do, and not for any information they contain (the Committee here
distinguishes the program itself from any data accompanying it).
So, as between
competing programs that cause a computer to perform similar functions, selection
is often made according to how easy,
or “user friendly”, each one
looks and feels to use.
9.16 In some submissions to the Committee, the terms “look and
feel” and the “user interface” were both used
to describe what
were referred to as the “non-literal elements” of a computer
program, which the Committee took to be
a reference to elements other than the
words, digits or symbols comprising the text or code of the program. In its
submission to
the Committee, Lotus submitted that the “look and
feel” of a program includes such things as the menu, command structure,
including the choice of command terms, the structure and order of those terms,
their presentation on the screen and the long prompts
(these features seem to
encompass aspects of the structure, sequence and organisation of the behaviour
of a program but not the code
itself). However, Lotus Development Pty Ltd
(Lotus) suggested that the term “look and feel” was not entirely
appropriate
to describe the “non-literal elements” of computer
programs and that they were better described by the term “user
interface”.
9.17 The meaning of “user interface” is defined, in simple terms,
in a position paper prepared by the League for Programming
Freedom (LPF) and
submitted to the Committee by Associate Professor G Doherty of the Department of
Computing Science of the University
of Wollongong. However, the Committee has
adopted the definition used in the IEEE Standard Glossary of Software
Engineering Terminology
(Std 610.12–1990), now Australian Standard
3611—Glossary of Software Engineering 1993) which provides that a
interface” is (AS 3611–p. 78):
An interface that enables information to be passed between a human user and
hardware or software components of a computer system.
“Structure, sequence and organisation of
computer programs” and “non-literal elements”
9.18 The Committee notes that American cases concerning the copying of
computer programs that discuss the issue of “look and
often referred to the “structure, sequence and organisation” of
programs. It is clear that the text
(code) and the behaviour (function) of a
computer program will each have their own separate “structure, sequence
and organisation”.
In relation to the code, the Committee understands that
“structure, sequence and organisation” can be roughly equated
organisation and layout of chapters, sections, index and accompanying
illustrations, maps and diagrams in a book of a technical
nature. In this
regard, the Committee notes the view of Mr Daniel Hunter in his article
“‘Look and feel’ Computer
Australian Intellectual Property Journal
at 76 that:
Creating the structure, interaction of modules, and organisation of the
program forms one of the most time-consuming and expensive
components of
programming. By adopting structured programming methods, actual coding is almost
trivial since the structure determines
which instructions to use and where they
should be placed.
The submission of IBM Australia Limited (IBM) referred to at paragraph 9.34
also argues this point.
9.19 On the other hand, the “structure sequence and organisation”
of the behaviour of the program are those features which
give it its user appeal
or “friendliness” and are most easily identified with its
“look and feel”. As noted
above in paragraph 9.16 this includes such
things as individual screen icons, screen displays and the sequence of screen
a user accesses when using the computer. The Committee regards these
behavioural aspects of a computer program as its non-code or
non-textual
(“non-literal”) elements.
9.20 As mentioned in paragraph 9.16 Lotus submitted that the term “look
and feel” was not entirely appropriate to describe
“non-literal” aspects of a computer program. The non-literal
elements of a computer program are understood by the
Committee to be those
elements of a program that are independent of, or exist at a higher level of
abstraction than, the actual program
code (either source or object). As such,
the “non-literal elements” of a program will include both the
structure, sequence
and organisation of a program’s behaviour and the
structure, sequence and organisation of its underlying code as separate
To the extent that the term “non-literal elements”
includes aspects of a program’s behavioural structure, sequence
organisation, it is clear that it overlaps with the concepts of “look and
feel” and the “user interface”.
On the other hand those
aspects such as the “structure sequence and organisation” of the
program’s code do not.
9.21 Some comment was made in submissions on the Committee’s discussion
and use of the expression “structure, sequence
and organisation” in
the Draft Report. Of these Mr Hunter asserted that the Committee “seemed
unable to make a clear
distinction between program structure and look and
feel” (p.5) and the Supporters of Interoperable Systems in Australia
recommended that the Committee should avoid the use of the expression
“structure, sequence and organisation” in reference
program’s code or behaviour and should instead refer to “non-literal
elements” which they asserted encompassed
the internal structure of a
program, its interface specifications and the non-literal aspects of a
program’s user interface
(paragraph 2.20, p. 5). SISA did however also
refer to paragraph 9.19 of the Draft Report in which the Committee recognised
very point. Despite the concerns expressed in these submissions,
submissions from De Data Pty Ltd, the Australian Copyright Council
Alcatel Australia Ltd and the Computer and Business Equipment Manufacturers
Association (CBEMA) supported the Committee’s
recommendation in the Draft
Report that “look and feel” not be given express copyright
protection.
Definition and interpretation of terms
9.22 The Committee notes that at the date of this report the courts in
Australia have not had occasion to consider the meaning of
any of these terms.
Indeed, having regard to the wide range of applications each of the terms has in
common usage, the task of defining
each term is particularly difficult and any
definitions are likely to be of only limited utility. The Committee notes that
the terms encompasses the “behavioural manifestations” of a
computer program. It appears to the Committee that it is
the protection of these
“behavioural manifestations” of a computer program that has been the
main concern of copyright
owners who have sought to protect the “look and
feel”, “user interface”, “non-literal elements”
“structure sequence and organisation” of their programs.
Protection beyond the code
9.23 Applying the analysis of computer programs set out in paragraphs
9.09–9.12 to what is encompassed by “look and feel”
clearer picture of those aspects of computer programs which are currently
protected and those features that are not. It also
raises the question whether
those features not protected by copyright might nonetheless require protection.
The Committee notes that
the “non-literal elements” of computer
programs comprising the “structure sequence and organisation” of the
program code (see paragraph 9.20) as distinguished from the structure, sequence
and organisation of the program’s behaviour
lend themselves to protection
by copyright in the same manner and to the same extent as the structure,
sequence and organisation
of traditional literary works. For this reason it does
not consider it necessary to recommend any change to the Act in this respect.
already noted, computer programs provide particular challenges to courts seeking
to determining questions of infringement. However,
the Committee is of the view
that it is undesirable and unnecessary to include specific provisions in the Act
codifying either tests
or standards for courts to apply in determining whether
Protection of “look and feel”
Protection of “look and feel”
internationally
9.24 The Committee is aware of recent developments in the USA concerning the
protection of “look and feel” and the “non-literal”
copying of computer programs. A useful summary and analysis of most of the
recent cases is contained in the two-part article by Mr
Hunter, already referred
to at paragraph 9.18. The Committee does not propose to analyse in detail the
particular lines of cases
that have been decided in the US. It makes reference
to some of them here only to note those decisions that have been helpful and
instructive in its consideration of the issues of “look and feel”
and the protection of “non-literal elements”
of computer
9.25 The scope of copyright protection of the “structure sequence and
organisation” of the code of a computer program
(its “non-literal
elements”) was considered in the US by the Court of Appeals for the Second
Circuit in the case of
Computer Associates
International, Inc
982 F. 2d 693
1992); 23 IPR 385
Associates case)
9.26 In its decision, the Second Circuit propounded a three-step
abstraction–filtration–comparison test for determining
substantial similarity existed between the original and the allegedly infringing
programs. This test can be summarised
as follows:
Abstraction.
The program is broken down into various levels
of abstraction or layers. At the lowest level is the actual program code; higher
include submodules and modules up to components, and finally higher level
functional design. The higher one goes up through the level
of abstraction, the
more ideas are found and the less expression of ideas.
Filtration.
The second stage of the process is to remove the
non-protectable material from protectable expression. In its decision the court
various unprotectable elements. These included:
(a) elements dictated by external factors, such as the mechanical
specifications of the computer on which the program was to run,
compatibility
requirements with other programs, design standards and industry demands; and
(b) elements dictated by efficiency. In this regard the court found that
some elements, not constrained by external considerations,
were dictated by
efficiency considerations. These included the design choices available to
programmers notwithstanding the fact that
hypothetically there might be a myriad
of ways in which a certain set of functions could be given effect within a
program. In other
words, where a particular implementation is driven by
efficiency concerns that narrow the range of choice so as to make only one
two forms of expression workable options, the doctrine of merger
applied—the idea has merged with its expression.
Comparison.
The final step of the test is to compare what remains
at each level of abstraction to determine what, if any, of the protectable
has been copied.
9.27 The Committee has considered the approach adopted by the Court in the
Computer Associates case
. It regards the test set out by the court in
that case as a very practical and useful guide for determining infringement of
programs and supports the approach it adopted. The Committee also notes
that the analysis undertaken by the court at first instance
relied heavily on

Protection of “look and feel”:
consideration of the arguments
9.37 Regarding IBM’s contention that the adaptation right may afford
this protection, it is the Committee’s opinion that
a program code that
produces a similar “look and feel” or “user interface”
to that generated by another program
code cannot be regarded as an adaptation of
the original code, where the later code is original, ie, has not copied or
the earlier code. If the non-code elements of a program are truly to
be characterised as behaviour, the Committee has strong reservations
desirability of seeking to use the adaptation right as a means of protecting the
behaviour of a computer program. In the
Committee’s view there is an
insufficient nexus between the source code or object code on the one hand and
the program’s
behaviour on the other to justify protection of the former
extending to the latter. As stated earlier, observing the behaviour or
of a computer program does not reveal anything about either the object code or
the source code.
9.38 Copyright protection should be confined to the expression of ideas in a
material form and not extend to protection of behavioural
aspects of programs.
This is not to deny that any case can be made out for the protection of the
behavioural aspect of computer programs—protection
of screen displays is
further considered below (paragraph 9.43). However, it seems to the Committee
that the true goal of those seeking
to protect “non-code”
behavioural aspects of computer programs is to prevent the development of
competitive functional
equivalents. The making of functional equivalents by
cloning or reproducing substantial parts of the source or object code of a
can be properly prevented by the copyright owner using his or her
exclusive right to reproduce or make an adaptation of the program.
However, the
Committee questions whether the author of a computer program should be able to
prevent the development of functional
equivalents which are independently
created in circumstances where neither the source or object code has been
copied. The protection
of function is not and should not be an object of
9.39 The Committee is also persuaded of the desirability for standard
“look and feel” or “user interface”
features to promote
consistency and ease of use in a rapidly developing area. In particular, the
Committee notes the dangers to the
industry as a whole in encouraging diversity
for diversity’s sake, where the resulting variations do not result in the
efficient and acceptable “user interface” features being used
or developed, simply because the most efficient and user
friendly features have
determining protection of
other categories of works, that it is in the case of
computer programs serves to mark these out on account of their functional
9.40 Determining the scope of rights that should be afforded to copyright
owners is a question of balancing the competing interests
of the copyright owner
with those of society as a whole. As regards balancing the needs of the public
against those of the software
industry and other author interests, the
Committee, while acknowledging the creativity that can be devoted to developing
new standards
in user interfaces, notes the behavioural nature of “look
and feel” and considers that it is inappropriate for copyright
to extend beyond expression of an idea to the behavioural aspects of computer
programs. If a book that taught a method
of doing something, such as
constructing a particular piece of furniture, enabled the reader to achieve the
same effective result
as an earlier book that taught a different method of
construction, the fact that use of the later book enabled the same finished
product to be built would not (and should not) make it an infringement of the
earlier one. Further, where such protection, if it
did extend to behaviour,
would impede or cause a developing industry to become less efficient,
consideration must also be given to
the underlying goals and fundamental
principles of copyright. As noted by the US Court of Appeals in the
Associates case
982 F. 2d 693
The interest of the copyright law is not in simply conferring a monopoly on
industrious persons, but in advancing the public welfare
through rewarding
artistic creativity, in a manner that permits the free use and development of
non-protectable ideas and processes.
Thus, where copyright protection would not advance the overriding
“public welfare” it should not be granted.
9.41 If a new method or device for improving the ease of using a programmed
computer is introduced, eg, the mouse for facilitating
the movement of the
cursor on the screen, then the Committee believes that the law of patents is the
appropriate recourse for protection
of such inventive effort. This does not
conflict with the view just expressed because the term of a patent is much
shorter than that
for copyright, reflecting the policy that the term of the
monopoly conferred on the inventor by a patent should not deprive others
chance of manufacturing a competing product, or the public from benefiting from
such competition, for an unduly long period—
certainly nothing like the
period of copyright protection for literary works.
Recommendation: protection of “look and
9.42 In the Committee’s view the need for standardisation and the need
for efficient user interfaces to be used and developed
outweighs the need to
grant authors express copyright protection in the “look and feel” of
their programs’ behaviour.
For the reasons given above the Committee is of
the view that no amendments should be made to the Act to establish additional
for the “look and feel” of computer programs
(Recommendation 2.09).
Protection of screen displays
9.43 The Committee’s conclusion that the “look and feel” of
computer programs amounts to behaviour and as such should
not be protected by
the “look and feel”,
does not dispose of the question of what
protection, if any, should be afforded to screen displays.
9.44 Submissions received by the Committee prior to the Draft Report
concerning look and feel did not canvass the issue of the protection
displays in any detail. Nor did they propose any special form of protection. In
the Draft Report the Committee commented
that it understood that most screen
displays are not stored in computer memory. Screen displays may be generated
either by retrieving
data stored in some form of machine memory or generated
using a computer program. (The Committee has noted the subsequent submission
IBM that screen displays are stored in computer memory). Which of either of
these two categories a particular screen display falls
into will depend on the
nature of what is being displayed, and in some cases, the nature of the program
being used. Screen displays
generated by a computer program can be characterised
as part of the program’s behaviour. The question which arises in this
context is whether this particular form of program behaviour should be
protected. The issues concerning the protection of these types
displays are different to those that apply where the screen display is generated
by the retrieval of a work that is stored
in computer memory. In respect of such
displays the issue of protection is relatively straight forward. Where copyright
in the work that is being displayed, the unauthorised copying of the
screen display of the work will infringe copyright in the work.
9.45 However, the protection of screen displays that are generated by
computer programs is less certain. There is some doubt as to
whether screen
displays generated by a computer program would fall within the definition of an
artistic work in s.10(1) of the Act.
It is arguable that they do not come within
the ordinary meaning of the words “painting” or
“drawing” used
in the definition. There must also be doubt that
artistic copyright could subsist in such display alone, for it to do so the
must have been “made” (s.32) and making requires reduction
to a material form (s.22(1)). As has already been noted, the
definition of
“material form” in s.10(1) which is inclusive not exhaustive,
requires a form of storage, and the ephemeral
nature of computer-generated
screen displays would appear to fail to satisfy this requirement. Furthermore,
it is unlikely that such
screen displays are protected as cinematograph films
because the definition of a cinematograph film in s.10(1) provides, in part,
“cinematograph film” means the aggregate of the visual images
embodied in an article or thing . . .
Accordingly, where the visual images that make up the screen display are not
stored in computer memory, the requirement under the
definition that visual
in an article or thing would not appear to be met.
9.46 The Committee notes that in the US, under its
limited recognition of the special nature of screen displays appears to have
been afforded by their treatment as “audio–visual
works” which
are entitled to copyright protection (s.102(a)(6)). Nonetheless, protection of
screen displays in the US is not
automatic and as IBM indicated in its October
1993 submission, the courts in the US have not been unanimous in their response
whether screen displays are a separate work entitled to protection. IBM noted
that the US Register of Copyright’s view in his
June 1988 decision was
that a computer program together, with its screen display, was a single work and
should therefore be given
a single registration (Registration and Deposit of
Computer Screen Displays, 53 Fed. Reg. 21 817, 21 819 (1988)). This decision is
reflected in the US Copyright Office’s Circular No 61 (last revised in
November 1993) which sets out the requirements for registration
of computer
programs and indicates that a single registration application for a computer
program will “cover any copyrightable
authorship contained in the computer
program and screen displays”.
9.47 In the Draft Report the Committee invited submissions on the need for a
similar form of protection for screen displays in Australia.
The Committee
received 12 submissions that addressed this issue but the views were divided in
their response on this issue, though
those in favour of some form of protection
were in the majority. However, Oracle Systems (Australia) Pty Ltd (Oracle) and
Council of Australia (LCA) were clearly opposed to a form of protection
arising independently of protection for the computer program.
The LCA submitted
that copyright protection of a screen display would give de facto protection to
“look and feel”. This
coincides with the Committee’s view that
some displays are an aspect of the program’s behaviour. Two concerns
in other submissions were that any independent protection for a screen
display not be greater than for the underlying program (SISA)
and that new areas
of artistic expression not be excluded arbitrarily from copyright protection,
such as multimedia screen displays
(ACC and Australian Pay TV Pty Limited
9.48 Having considered these submissions and in light of the discussion in
paragraphs 9.44–9.45 the Committee has decided not
to recommend any
additional form of protection for screen displays (Recommendation 2.10).
Design protection for screen displays
9.49 It remains to be noted that Sun Microsystems suggested that, to avoid
blatant copying, protection should be afforded under the
law of registered
designs to the screen displays generated by programs. However, as mentioned in
paragraph 4.16, a recent application
for the registration of an icon as a design
was rejected by the Registrar of Designs, on the grounds that the icon was not
of distinguishing the computer screen because the image was transitory
Re Applications by Comshare Inc.
[1991] ADO 2
(1991) 23 IPR 145).
A majority of the
Committee shares the view that it is reasonable to reject such an application
because an icon or small picture
on the screen does not distinguish a physical
object like a computer screen as it is not always displayed on the computer
However, a majority of the Committee notes that such an icon or even a
complete screen display can adequately distinguish a computer
program because
the program can be so written that the icon is always on the screen when the
program is in use. A majority of the
Committee assumes that insofar as an icon
produced by a program is a registered trade mark, appropriate protection as such
be provided for under the
Trade Marks Act 1955
9.50 The Committee notes that the decision of the Registrar of Designs brings
into question whether computer generated screen displays
can ever qualify for
registration under the
Designs Act 1906
. In the Draft Report the
Committee also noted that the
Designs Act 1906
is under wholesale review
by the Law Reform Commission. Since the Committee released its Draft Report, the
Law Reform Commission
has released a Discussion Paper on Designs which proposes
some changes to the existing legislation, including the definition of
(Discussion Paper 58, August 1994). The issue of possible
design protection for screen displays is not mentioned in the Discussion
However, the Discussion Paper proposes a new definition of “design”
which removes the reference to “applicable
to an article” and is
instead more broadly defined as:
The appearance of a product [being any manufactured thing], as perceived
visually, constitutes a design and a new definition of manufactured
A thing is a manufactured thing whether it has been made wholly by the use of
machinery, partly by the use of machinery and partly
by hand, or wholly by
It would appear from the above definitions that a computer program would fall
within the broad definition of a manufactured thing.
However, it is questionable
whether a screen display would correctly be regarded as the appearance of the
computer program. As stated
above it is a manifestation of the program’s
behaviour. (If a computer program has any appearance it would seem to be the
code and that is already protected by copyright.) Regardless of how the
above definitions may be interpreted the Committee is of
the view that designs
protection is an inappropriate way to protect screen displays.
THE RIGHT TO MAKE AN ADAPTATION OF A COMPUTER
9.51 The scope of the adaptation right under the Act and its application to
computer programs has already been discussed in Chapter
6 in relation to the
1984 amendment to the definition of adaptation (See paragraphs 6.63–6.85).
It is sufficient to note here
that, since releasing the Draft Report, the
Committee has reconsidered the question whether any change should be made to the
definitions of “reproduction” and “adaptation”
in the Act. In Chapter 6 the Committee has recommended (Recommendation
2.04(e)(ii)) that the Act be amended by the addition of a deeming provision to
provide that, in relation to a computer program, a
reproduction includes (but is
not limited to) (paragraph 6.76):
(a) an object code version of the program that has been derived from the
program in source code by compilation; and
(b) a source code version of the program that has been derived from the
program in object code by decompilation.
9.52 The consequence of introducing this provision is, as noted in paragraph
6.77, that the scope of the adaptation right in relation
to computer programs
will be narrowed and apply only in specific instances to certain translations of
computer programs, eg, from
one source code language to another and new versions
of a program obtained after porting or error correction.
9.53 The Committee recommends that copyright owners should have the right to
control the adaptation of their computer programs (Recommendation
2.11(a)). The
Committee recognises that computer programs can be “translated”
relatively easily from one language to
another (in either source or object
code), and that to the extent that they are not reproductions, such translations
constitute adaptations
of the original program. The unauthorised appropriation
of the skill and labour of the author of the original program in this manner
should be an activity which the author can control. To the extent that such
activity does not amount to reproducing the programs,
the Committee regards an
adaptation right as an important supplement to the reproduction right. The
Committee’s recommendation
for preservation of the existing definition of
“adaptation” and to add a definition of “reproduction”
intended to clarify those activities which come within these exclusive rights
of the copyright owner (See recommendations 2.04(e)
and 2.04(f)).
Rights in respect of an adaptation
9.54 It remains to be recalled that, as noted in paragraph 6.72, under
s.31(1)(a)(vii) of the Act the adaptation right is limited
to the right to make
an adaptation of the original work. The adaptation right does not extend to
preventing the making of adaptations
of adaptations. In other words the author
of a book in English may exercise his or her exclusive right to make an
adaptation by translating
the book into another language. If the author permits
another person to translate his book into German, and a third person translates
the German version into Spanish, or writes a play in German based on the German
translation, the author would be unable to prevent
these acts.
9.55 The Committee notes that s.31(2) of the Act provides that the adaptation
right is not intended to reduce the scope of the reproduction
Accordingly, in the example given above where the third person mentioned makes
an English translation of the German translation
(of the original English book),
that translation may amount to a reproduction of the original work
notwithstanding the fact that
it is also an adaptation of the German
translation. In such circumstances the question whether the English translation
of the German
translation amounted to an infringing copy of the original English
book would depend upon the author of the original work being able
to establish
that unauthorised copying (direct or indirect) occurred in the making of the
English translation. The Committee queries
whether, in the absence of any
evidence of actual copying, indirect copying should be inferred.
9.56 It was suggested to the Committee by the ACC in their response to the
Draft Report that (p.15, paragraph 72):
. . . it would be open to argue that copyright is infringed by an indirect
adaptation — that is, an adaptation made from an
intermediate item. The
proposition is that an infringing adaptation may be made without direct access
to the work, in a similar way
that an infringing reproduction may be made
without direct access to the work provided a causal connection can be shown.
If the Committee’s recommendation regarding the definition of
“reproduction” in relation to computer programs were
adopted, the
owner of copyright in a computer program would have clear control over the
making of adaptations of either the source
code or object code version of their
programs and it would be unnecessary to argue infringement based on the making
of an indirect
adaptation, as suggested by the ACC.
9.57 In the Draft Report, the Committee considered how the analogy of
translation in paragraphs 9.54–9.55 reproduced above might
computer programs. The Committee then referred to the operation of s.31(2) of
the Act which provides, in effect, that the
generality of the reproduction right
is not affected by the adaptation right. Applying this section the Committee
concluded in the
Draft Report (paragraph 9.48):
Accordingly, all versions of a program in object code which reproduce or
substantially reproduce that object code version (adaptation)
of the source code
will amount to reproduction of the adaptation (as distinguished from an
adaptation of the adaptation) the making
of which if unauthorised will infringe
the exclusive rights of the owner of copyright in the source code.
While the Committee still regards this analysis as correct, it notes that the
suggested additions to the definition of reproduction
will clarify the
protection of computer programs in either source or object code.
THE RIGHT TO PUBLISH A COMPUTER PROGRAM
9.58 Like the reproduction right, the right of authors to control the
publication of their works, ie, supply of reproductions of their
works to the
public (s.31(1)(a)(ii) of the Act) is essential for the commercial exploitation
of their works. In this regard the Committee
notes that the meaning of
‘publish’ was considered by the High Court in
Avel Pty Ltd
Multicoin Amusements Pty Ltd
[1990] HCA 58
(1990) 171 CLR 88
18 IPR 443.
case the court held that the right to publish should be read as meaning a right
to make public that which has not been previously
made public in a copyright
territory. The Committee is of the view that the copyright subsisting in
computer programs should continue
to include a publication right. Accordingly,
the Committee recommends that the owner of copyright in a computer program
should have
the exclusive right to publish the program (Recommendation
9.59 In so recommending, the Committee draws attention to a consequence for
computer programs of s.29(1)(a), insofar as it deems a
literary work to have
been published:
. . . if, but only if,
reproductions
of the work . . . have been
supplied . . . to the public (emphasis added).
The Committee understands that the most common or usual modes of supply to
the public of computer programs are in object code either
in the form of a
floppy disc or loaded directly onto the hard disc of a computer. As discussed in
paragraph 6.68, the object code
version of a computer program may not be a
reproduction, but rather an adaptation, of the source code version. A
consequence of this
is that supply of a program in the ways mentioned above
would, under s.29(1)(a), constitute publication of the object code, but not
the source code. In noting this, the Committee does not understand that this
would cause problems for program copyright owners,
who may indeed desire just
that result. If the Committee’s recommendation that a deeming provision
regarding reproduction of
computer programs is adopted this issue should not
arise (See paragraphs 6.63–6.85).
9.60 Microsoft Pty Limited suggested that the definition of publication
should include rental and lending and that the right of publication
extend beyond the right of first publication. It argued that this was necessary
because of the ready availability of technology
which enables the copying of
rented or loaned computer programs. The need for a separate right to enable
computer copyright owners
to control rental and lending of their programs is
discussed in paragraph 9.77 and following. The Australian Copyright Council
suggested, in response to the Draft Report, that a right of distribution
be created under the Act which would encompass the right
of publication. This
issue affects all copyright owners, not just the creators of computer programs,
and any decision regarding the
desirability of such a broad right needs to be
considered in this wider context. The Committee notes that this issue remains
discussion at the international level (WIPO Committee of Experts on the
Possible Protocol to the Berne Convention). For these reasons
the Committee
considers it would be premature for Australia to introduce such a right at this
stage, especially as it appears to
the Committee that at present the publication
right serves the needs of authors of computer programs adequately. Accordingly,
makes no recommendation on this issue other than that it be pursued in the
relevant international fora. The Committee does note,
however, that in its
recent Cultural Policy Statement,
Creative Nation
(18 October 1994), the
Government gave its in principle support to a “broad-based distribution
right” which, it noted,
was being refined by WIPO’s discussions. The
Government also indicated that this Committee would be given a new reference to
conduct a review of the Act to simplify it and would be asked specifically to
examine the implementation of a distribution right
for tangible copyright
THE RIGHT TO PERFORM A COMPUTER PROGRAM IN
9.61 What constitutes a “performance” under the Act is defined in
s.27(1)(a) as including:
. . . any mode of visual or aural presentation, whether the presentation is
by the operation of wireless telegraphy apparatus, by
the exhibition of a
cinematograph film, by the use of a record or by any other means . . . and a
reference . . . to performing a
work or an adaptation of a work has a
corresponding meaning.
The relevant exclusive right in works is, under s.31(1)(a)(iii), to perform
the work concerned “in public”. Public is
not defined in the Act,
but has been construed in a number of cases as involving the presence of persons
in other than domestic circumstances.
This has been most recently considered in
the decision of Gummow J of the Federal Court of Australia in
Commonwealth Bank of Australia
[1992] FCA 609
(1992) 40 FCR 59
25 IPR 157.
Computer Edge Pty Ltd
Apple Computer Inc and Anor
[1986] HCA 19
(1986) 161 CLR 171
the High Court observed that computer programs
are not literary in the sense that they are written to inform or entertain. As
already been noted more than once, computer programs, even in source code,
are intended not to entertain or inform humans but to
be acted on by machines
when rendered in machine code. On that basis the Committee thinks it most
unlikely that a source code program
would be performed in public in the same
ways that other literary works, such as stories and poems, or musical and
dramatic works,
are performed. About the only way in which the Committee can
conceive that a source code might be “performed” is if the
projected, eg, via an overhead slide, for viewing by participants in a seminar
(such a use would be a performance within
the meaning of s.27 of the Act).
9.63 The concept of a performance may have even less relevance in the running
of an object code program on a computer. The object
code which causes the
computer to function is invisible and imperceptible to the human senses. For
this reason, unlike source code
and traditional literary works, it cannot be
performed. However, representations of the object code could be generated and
on a computer screen. Those representations on a computer screen would
appear to be a performance of the program under s.27(1) (if
a substantial amount
is displayed). If the screen display was projected to persons other than in a
domestic situation, the Committee
understands that this would be a public
performance of the materials displayed. As an example of what is possible, at
the demonstration
to the Committee by IBM Australia Limited, referred to in
paragraph 3.02, the screen display of a computer program in assembler code
projected onto a large screen for all members to see.
9.64 To the extent that it is relevant, the performance right in works
should, in the Committee’s view, extend to computer programs
does to other literary works and other types of works such as musical and
dramatic works (Recommendation 2.11(c)).
9.65 The Committee received two comments on its recommendation in relation to
the performance right. The Australian Information Industry
Association Limited
(AIAA) endorsed the Committee’s position, while the Copyright Agency
Limited submitted that screen displays
should be considered to be a public
performance and noted that a screen display right may become more relevant to
multimedia material.
Given the insubstantial comments made on this issue the
Committee has decided not to amend its recommendation. Other comments received
by the Committee that pertain to public performance of material stored in
THE RIGHT TO BROADCAST A COMPUTER PROGRAM
9.66 The Committee did not receive any submissions regarding the broadcasting
of computer programs prior to the Draft Report, but
did receive a number of
submissions following its release. These are discussed below. In s.10(1) of the
Act “broadcasting”
is defined as meaning to “transmit by
wireless telegraphy to the public”. Wireless telegraphy is defined in
s.10(1) as:
the emitting or receiving, otherwise than over a path that is provided by a
material substance, of electromagnetic energy.
9.67 While literary, musical or dramatic works may be broadcast to inform or
entertain, if one has regard to the functional nature
of computer programs it
appears unlikely that they would be broadcast for those purposes. The Committee
acknowledges that in some
instances users may wish to transmit computer programs
from one location to another and in circumstances where the transmission could
not be received by persons other than the person to whom it was transmitted.
This can be done by wire (telephone), optical cable,
microwave or satellite
link. However, none of these acts is likely to be directed to the public such as
to attract the operation
of “broadcast” as defined. The control of
these activities by the copyright owner could be exercised through the
reproduction
9.68 As far as the Committee is aware there is no technology currently being
used commercially for the broadcasting and reception
of computer programs in
object code in Australia. However, the Committee is aware that digital
broadcasting is being developed in
the United States, Japan, the European Union
and elsewhere. While lacking in submissions on the matter, the Committee accepts
possibility that there might be some interest in being able to include
program code in, for example, educational broadcasts. Accordingly,
the Committee
recommends that the owners of copyright in computer programs should retain the
right to control the broadcast of their
programs (Recommendation 2.11(d)).
9.69 As mentioned above, the Committee received a number of submissions
following the release of the Draft Report. The submission
from APTV indicated a
number of present difficulties with the definition of “broadcast”.
In its submission, Oracle stated
that the definition of “broadcast”
would need to be flexible given new technology, and in a later submission (23
1994), submitted that an exclusive “transmission right” to
cover transmission of a work (or other subject matter) by
any means to any
person should be introduced. The ACC, in a similar vein, suggested that the
present “broadcast right”
and the “diffusion right”
should be replaced with a single right “to communicate the work to the
The ACC cited, in support of its argument, articles of the Berne
Convention which refer to this right (Articles 11(1)(i) and (ii)
(1)(ii)) and the model proposed by WIPO at the second meeting on the
Possible Protocol to the Berne Convention in 1992.
9.70 Since the Committee released its Draft Report and received these
submissions the Copyright Convergence Group (CCG) has given
consideration to the
specific issues arising for copyright as a result of new methods of
communication and has released its report,
Highways to
Change—Copyright in the New Communications Environment
1994). The CCG’s terms of reference required it to consider the position
of both “works” and “subject
matter other than works”
and the operation of the existing broadcast and diffusion rights. Its
recommendations in relation
to the “broadcast” right were as follows
(pp. 25–28):
(a) a new right to authorise transmissions to the public be introduced into
the Act which would cover the transmission of copyright
material in intangible
form to the public by any means or combination of means which is capable of
being made perceivable or used
by a receiving device
(b) the right to broadcast should be retained in the Act but as a sub-set of
the new transmission right and should be redefined to
complement the meaning of
broadcast as used in the
Broadcasting Services Act 1992
so that broadcast
could be defined as the transmission of television or radio programmes by any
means or combination of means whatsoever
to persons having the equipment
appropriate for receiving such transmissions. A “broadcast” would
not include a service
which provides no more than data or text, makes copyright
material available on demand on a point-to-point basis or is outside the
of broadcast in the
Broadcasting Services Act
. (However, these activities
would be included in the new transmission right).
9.71 Although the Committee has noted here briefly the recommendations of the
CCG, it has not had the opportunity to consider the
impact of the CCG’s
recommendations on computer programs or databases, given that the report of the
CCG was not released until
30 August 1994, ie, after the Committee had concluded
its deliberations on this issue. The Committee notes that in the
Government’s
recent Cultural Policy Statement (already referred to at
paragraph 9.60) the Government announced that it would implement the
recommendations
of the CCG to remove the anomalies identified in the Act.
THE RIGHT TO TRANSMIT A COMPUTER PROGRAM TO
SUBSCRIBERS TO A DIFFUSION SERVICE
9.72 Owners of copyright in literary and other works enjoy a right to control
transmission of their works by wire to the premises
of “subscribers to a
diffusion service”(s.31(1)(a)(v)). This is typically understood to refer
to transmission by subscription
cable radio or television services, although
