under s.26 it could apparently include telephonic or telegraphic diffusion of
The Committee notes that, under s.31(1)(b)(iv), the diffusion right in
artistic works is more limited than for literary, musical
and dramatic
9.73 As already noted (paragraph 9.67), transmission of computer programs
from one location to another has been possible for some
time using the existing
telephone network. Similarly, other literary works may be transmitted over the
telephone network using facsimile
transmission machines. The Committee notes
that such uses would usually not come within the meaning of a diffusion service
of the Act because they are not provided to subscribers to a particular
service; rather they tend to be used by organisations and
individuals for their
own purposes or the distribution of the material would not be regarded as a
“service” as required
by s.26(1) (See
Australasian Performing
Right Association
Telstra Corporation Ltd
[1993] FCA 542
(1993) 27 IPR 357).
However, assuming the purpose of transmission is to provide the recipient with a
copy of the program transmitted, the copyright owner
can assert control by the
reproduction right, as was noted in paragraph 9.67.
9.74 However, the Committee is also aware that services, such as AARNET
connected to Internet, and AppleLink, are available which
have a wide and
growing public subscription. Such services may come within the meaning of
diffusion service in s.26 of the Act. Having
regard to this and the increasing
introduction of optical fibre and cable technology in Australia, the Committee
recommended in its
Draft Report that the owners of copyright in computer
programs should retain the right currently enjoyed to control the transmission
of their programs to subscribers to a diffusion service. The Committee sees no
reason to change this recommendation (Recommendations
9.75 Since the Committee considered this issue and made its recommendation,
the CCG has, as indicated above, specifically examined
both the broadcast and
diffusion rights and made recommendations in relation to each. The CCG
recommended that, as regards the existing
diffusion right (p.28, paragraph
(a) the right to transmit to subscribers to a diffusion service should be
removed from the Act and this right should be encompassed
within the new
transmission right (outlined in paragraph 9.71 above) and s.26 of the Act should
be repealed.
In making this recommendation the CCG noted that some activities which would
previously have been regarded as transmissions to subscribers
to a diffusion
service would now qualify as broadcasts or fall into the proposed transmission
right. The Committee notes that the
CCG’s report addressed the question of
what transmissions should be regarded as being “to the public” and
subject to that right, and has specifically adverted to diffusion via
in-house communications systems operated by businesses. The
CCG thought that
reliance could be placed on the reproduction right to control this type of use
of copyright materials which accords
with the Committee’s own view stated
a paragraphs 9.67 and 9.73. But bearing in mind the extensive scope of the right
of “public”
performance as interpreted in
Australasian Performing
Right Association v
Commonwealth Bank of Australia
[1992] FCA 609
(1992) 25 IPR 157
the Committee suggests that careful consideration will have to be given to
determining the application of the proposed new transmission
right to situations
such as in-house communications networks.
9.76 As mentioned at paragraph 9.71, the Committee has not fully considered
the CCG’s recommendations in any detail. However,
the submissions made to
the Committee in relation to this right indirectly queried its usefulness, given
its technological limitations
and hence its limited application. The content of
these submissions have already been referred to at paragraph 9.69 in relation to
the broadcast right, but the concerns raised in relation to the scope of the
broadcast right apply generally to the diffusion right
as well. Having regard to
the submissions made to the Committee and the CCG’s recent inquiry, the
Committee supports in principle
the CCG’s recommendation outlined above,
if those acts encompassed by the present diffusion right would become subject to
broadcast/transmission right.
A RIGHT TO CONTROL RENTAL OF COMPUTER
Submissions
9.77 The need for a rental right was raised in submissions to the Committee
prior to the Draft Report from the Australian Copyright
Council (ACC), the
Australian Information Industry Association (AIIA), the Australian Software
Distributors Association Inc (ASDA),
Autodesk Australia Pty Ltd, Curtin
University of Technology (WA) (Mr John Newman), Dataflow Computer Services Pty
Ltd, Gilbert and
Tobin Solicitors, ICL Australia Pty Limited, Lotus Development
Pty Limited, Microsoft Pty Limited, Mindscape International Pty Limited
Micronics Pty Ltd. Following the Draft Report a further seven submissions were
received, six of which supported the Committee’
recommendation to
introduce a rental right (Oracle, US Government, CBEMA, ACC, AIAA and
Hydrographic Service, Royal Australian Navy).
9.78 The main reason put forward for giving copyright owners a right to
control rental was that it was necessary if copyright owners
were to have any
chance of acting effectively against the unauthorised copying of their computer
programs. In their submission, Gilbert
and Tobin Solicitors noted that it was
unlikely that a computer program would be rented to perform a specific task.
They were usually
required on an ongoing basis to perform a continuing task.
Obvious examples of this are word processing and spread sheet programs
required by businesses on a day-to-day basis, not on a one-off basis. So rental
for use of the rented copy would appear
to be far less likely than rental for
making an own-use copy. Nevertheless, there may be some circumstances in which
the rental of
a program may be more desirable than its purchase—computer
games are one example.
9.79 The Committee also received two submissions prior to the Draft Report
and one in response to the Draft Report, from Mr Michael
Barnes, which did not
support the introduction of a rental right. In its submission, the LCA commented
that it was not persuaded
that there was a demonstrated need to include a rental
right among the exclusive rights of the owner of copyright in computer programs.
It observed that this was not the only form of subject matter that may form the
subject of a profitable rental business. The LCA
concluded that at this stage
neither the need for, nor the consequences of, a rental right had been
established. In its submission
the Department of State Services (WA) (WADSS)
noted that a rental right in itself would not prevent illegal copying. It urged
if a rental right were introduced, it should not apply to rental for study
or research purposes and that “rental” for
non-profit purposes, ie,
loans by libraries and educational institutions, should be allowed. The
Committee notes that this use of
“public lending” and is discussed later from paragraph 9.86.
The GATT TRIPS Agreement
9.80 As mentioned in paragraph 9.02, the finalisation of the Uruguay Round of
the GATT means that Australia must now grant authors
a rental right in respect
of computer programs. Article 11 of TRIPS provides, in relation to computer
In respect of at least computer programs . . . a member shall provide authors
and their successors in title the right to authorise
or to prohibit the
commercial rental to the public of originals or copies of their copyright works
. . . In respect of computer programs,
this obligation does not apply to rentals
where the program itself is not the essential object of the rental.
Implementation of TRIPS
9.81 The Committee notes that the Government has moved to introduce
legislation that will enable Australia to meet its international
obligations as
a result of TRIPS. The
was introduced into Parliament on 21 September 1994 and was debated in October
1994. This Bill proposes, among other things, amendments
to the Act to introduce
a rental right for copyright owners of computer programs. The Explanatory
Memorandum to the Bill explains
the scope of the new right being introduced
The new rental rights will apply only to a commercial rental arrangements
(sic). Public lending and other non-commercial lending arrangements
explicitly excluded. Also excluded from the right is rental of computer programs
where the rental of the program is not the essential
object of the rental. This
includes where it is not possible to copy the program in the normal use of the
rented object provided
the object is something other than a mere carrier, such
as a CD or circuit chip.
Page 5 of the Explanatory Memorandum further states:
. . . the copyright owner does not have the right to control rental of a copy
of a computer program embodied in a machine or device
if the program is not able
to be copied in the course of the ordinary use of the machine or device. This
would mean, for instance,
that the copyright owner could not control the rental
of a motor vehicle where a computer program is embodied in its engine control
Blank disc levy
9.82 The Committee notes that the main reason, or one of the main reasons,
why copyright owners of works and other subject matter
have been seeking a right
to control the rental of their works and other materials is to prevent
unauthorised copying. This was also
recognised by the European Commission in its
Explanatory Memorandum for its draft directive on rental and lending rights.
from rental, and the capacity to prohibit it outright are two ways of
enabling copyright owners to take action against loss of sales
unauthorised copying.
9.83 It has been argued that copyright owners could be compensated for this
copying by the introduction of a levy on blank recording
or storage media and
hardware. However, the Committee is of the view that computer programs are
different from, for example, musical
works and sound recordings for which a
blank tape royalty was introduced in Part VC of the Act. (The Committee notes
that this scheme
was held to be invalid by the High Court in
Australian Blank
Tape Manufacturers Association Ltd
Commonwealth of Australia
[1993] HCA 10
Like sound recordings computer programs vary enormously in their
production costs but, unlike sound recordings, their market price
also varies in
a manner which reflects, not so much the development or production cost, but the
function or purpose of the program.
It is the functional nature of computer
programs which distinguishes them from other copyright works and subject matter
such as books
and CDs whose function is to inform or entertain. While some
computer programs are the main component of computer games and, as such,
bought to entertain, a large portion of the market is for business computer
programs and programs with industrial applications.
The price of these reflects
the nature of the task they perform. The result is, not surprisingly, a huge
range in prices. For this
reason it would be difficult, if not impossible, to
set a blanket royalty rate on blank storage media that would adequately and
compensate all computer program copyright owners. Even if it were
possible, the range of prices of programs, as disclosed for example
interim report of the PSA on computer software prices (October 1992), would be
likely to argue in favour of a level of royalty
that was so high in relation to
the price of discs that it would be unacceptable to buyers and resisted by disc
manufacturers and
Recommendation
9.84 In the Draft Report the Committee said (paragraph 9.73):
The Committee accepts the view that uncontrolled rental of computer programs
is likely to lead to an increase in unauthorised copying.
For this reason
their computer programs. Accordingly,
the Committee recommends the introduction
of a rental right for computer programs. That right should be subject to the
that it does not extend to copies of computer programs incorporated
in utilitarian articles or other articles in which the computer
embodied and cannot be copied during the ordinary operation or use of that
article. In making this recommendation the
Committee is conscious that this
would be the first extension of the copyright owner’s right in Australia
to the control of
rental. The Committee is of the view that the particular
circumstances of rental of computer programs are such that this recommendation
should not automatically carry over to other categories of copyright works or
subject matter and that the case for a similar right
in respect of those other
works or subject matter would have to be separately made out.
9.85 The Committee reaffirms this recommendation noting that it has now been
fully endorsed by the TRIPS Agreement and that a rental
right will be introduced
not only for authors of computer programs but also for producers of phonograms
(articles 11 and 14(4) TRIPS)
(Recommendation 2.12).
A RIGHT TO CONTROL PUBLIC LENDING OF COMPUTER
9.86 Like rental, lending of works and other subject matter is not, under the
Act, among the exclusive rights of owners of copyright
in those materials.
However, the need to clarify the law in respect of the lending of computer
programs was raised by the Australian
Vice-Chancellors’ Committee (AVCC).
The AVCC submitted that university libraries, in particular, needed clear
guidelines on
the lending of computer programs to staff and students (including
external students).
International position
European Union
9.87 The EC Directive on
Rental and lending rights and on certain rights
relating to copyright
provides that Member States shall afford authors a
right to authorise or prohibit the lending of originals and copies of copyright
works and other subject matter (art.1). Article 1(3) of the Directive defines
lending as:
making available for use, for a limited period of time and not for direct or
indirect economic or commercial advantage, when it is
made through
establishments which are accessible to the public.
Article 2(1) of the Directive provides that (in the case of works) the right
to authorise or prohibit lending shall belong to the
author in respect of the
original or copies of his or her work.
9.88 Under the heading, “Rental of computer programs”, art.3 of
the Directive provides in effect that the Directive as
a whole is intended to be
without prejudice to art.4(c) of the EC Directive on Computer Programs (Appendix
G). While art.4(c) deals
with rental of computer programs, the Committee also
notes that the preamble to the Directive on Computer Programs includes the
“Rental” means the making available for use, for a limited period
of time and for profit-making purposes, of a computer
program or a copy
thereof;. . . this term does not include public lending, which, accordingly,
remains outside the scope of this
Accordingly, the EC Directive on Rental
alone appears to apply to the
lending of computer programs.
9.89 Article 5(1) of the Directive allows Members States to:
derogate from the exclusive right provided for in Article 1 in respect of
public lending provided that at least authors obtain remuneration
lending and to exempt certain categories of establishments from the payment of
such remuneration.
Possible Protocol to the Berne Convention
9.90 The Committee also notes that the provisional documents,
Concerning a Possible Protocol to the Berne Convention,
dated 29 April 1994,
prepared by the International Bureau of WIPO notes that there is little support
for the inclusion of a public
lending right in the Possible Protocol to the
Berne Convention. Accordingly, the proposal for a public lending right has been
from the Berne Protocol agenda and will not be discussed at the
forthcoming Committee of Experts meeting on the Berne Protocol in
December 1994.
Submissions
9.91 As noted in the Draft Report, the need for a public lending right was
not discussed in any detail in submissions to the Committee
and so the Committee
invited further submissions on the need for, and the scope of, (or any
exceptions to) a public lending right
for computer programs (paragraphs
9.78–9.79). Five submissions were received following the Draft Report. The
submissions were
divided in the views expressed about a public lending right.
The Australian Council of Libraries and Information Services (ACLIS)
CBEMA both agreed with the Committee’s recommendation in the Draft Report
not to introduce a public lending right.
On the other hand the AIAA and Oracle
did not agree with the recommendation. It should be noted that none of the
submissions provided
any substantial reasons for the views expressed. Mr Barnes
who was proposing to establish a club or society-run lending scheme was
opposed to the introduction of a public lending right and/or a rental right. The
Committee notes that if such a club or society
was not operated for profit, then
it would be unlikely to affected by the introduction of a rental right and if
the Committee’s
recommendation on public lending is accepted then such
clubs would similarly not be affected.
Recommendation
9.92 The Committee has considered these submissions and has decided to
confirm its draft recommendation. As the Committee stated in
the Draft Report,
the rationale for the introduction of a lending right in respect of computer
programs is similar to that for a
rental right. However, the Committee notes
that, unlike rental, there is no obligation under TRIPS for a public lending
be introduced and that internationally there is little support for such
a right. In the Draft Report the Committee also referred
to the public benefit
in allowing lending by educational institutions and institutions assisting the
handicapped, which it could
be argued outweighs the interests of copyright
owners. Although the Committee acknowledges that the potential exists for
owners to suffer some detriment as the result of the public lending of
computer programs, it is not persuaded by the submissions
it received that such
a right should be introduced at this time. In other words, the owner of
not have the exclusive right to authorise
or prohibit the public lending of his or her computer program (Recommendation
2.13). However,
the Committee considers that this issue could be reviewed at a
future date as part of the Committee’s inquiry into simplification
MORAL RIGHTS
9.93 The moral rights of authors are set out in art.6
of the Berne
Convention. It provides:
(1) Independently of the author’s economic rights, and even after the
transfer of the said rights, the author shall have the
right to claim authorship
of the work and to object to any distortion, mutilation or other modification
of, or other derogatory action
in relation to, the said work, which would be
prejudicial to his honour or reputation.
(2) The rights granted to the author in accordance with the preceding
paragraph shall, after his death, be maintained, at least until
the expiry of
the economic rights, and shall be exercisable by the persons or institutions
authorised by the legislation of the country
where protection is claimed.
However, those countries whose legislation, at the moment of their ratification
of or accession to this
Act, does not provide for the protection after the death
of the author of all the rights set out in the preceding paragraph may provide
that some of these rights may, after his death, cease to be maintained.
(3) The means of redress for safeguarding the rights granted by this Article
shall be governed by the legislation of the country where
protection is
9.94 Part IX of the Act which deals with false attribution of authorship can
be considered as implementing some of the obligations
under art.6
Other provisions in the Act supporting attribution of authorship of works are
enforceable by the copyright owner rather than the
author as such. To the extent
that the Act does not provide comprehensively for the rights established by
, it is in line with what the WIPO
Guide to the Berne
or the Protection of Literary and Artistic Works
Geneva, 1978) refers to as (p.44):
. . . the practice of member countries with an Anglo–Saxon legal
tradition, according to which the protection of the moral right
is mainly a
matter for the common law, and, in particular, the law of defamation.
9.95 The Committee is of the view that the authors of computer programs are
not in a different position to that of authors of other
particular, programs that have been produced by large teams of what would be
regarded as joint authors are not
essentially different to works comprising
substantial compilations, such as encyclopedias, directories and like
publications. In
those cases, the problem of dealing with a large number of
joint authors for the purposes of attributing authorship or securing consent
alteration of the work would be the same, so it seems to the Committee. While
the Committee has elsewhere in this report stressed
the functional nature of
programs, the Committee believes that some traditional literary works, such as
information directories,
timetables and the like are not greatly distinguishable
from computer programs in that regard. Accordingly, the Committee is of the
that the authors of computer programs should not be in a worse position than
authors of other literary works.
9.96 There were only five submissions following the Draft Report that
commented on the issue of moral rights and none of these was
particularly
critical of the Committee’s recommendation. Of the submitters, the CBEMA
and Oracle, while not vehemently opposed
to moral rights, did not see the need
for specific moral rights legislation for computer programs. Oracle’s 1994
did however appear to take a contrary position by raising concerns
submitted that it was not necessarily inappropriate that moral rights apply to
authors of computer programs.
Discussion Paper: Proposed Moral Rights Legislation
for Copyright Creators
9.97 The Committee notes that since the release of the Draft Report, the
Government has released a Discussion Paper on
Proposed Moral Rights
Legislation for Copyright Creators
(June 1994). Comment on the proposed
scheme was invited by 1 September 1994. The Introduction to the Discussion Paper
outlines the
history of the moral rights debate in Australia and the relevant
reports and inquiries in relation to this issue, including the Committee’s
Report on Moral Rights
. The introduction provides a summary of the
proposed scheme which is reproduced here (p.3):
In summary, the proposed scheme would involve an amendment to the Copyright
Act to accord relevant copyright creators the right of
attribution and the right
of integrity.
The right of attribution would involve the positive right to be made known to
the public as the creator of the work where reasonable
in all the circumstances,
the right to prevent others from wrongfully claiming authorship of a work and
the right to prevent others
from wrongfully attributing works to a creator.
The right of integrity would include the right to object to any material
alteration, distortion or other derogatory treatment of a
work. The right would
be limited by requiring that the action taken prejudicially affect the
author’s honour or reputation.
The legislation would provide that these rights apply to all works of
creators where reasonable in all the circumstances rather than
specific exceptions. The legislation would list a series of factors that would
be used as modifiers or guidelines for creators,
industries and organisations
that use copyright materials, and for the courts to follow in determining
whether the rights are applicable
in the circumstances. The intention is for the
legislation to strike a workable balance between the rights of copyright
the rights of users and producers of copyright material and the
community generally.
9.98 The proposed scheme is discussed in more detail in Part 3 of the
Discussion Paper. The proposed rights for authors, which the
understands are not intended to apply to authors of computer programs, are those
of attribution and integrity. The factors
to be taken into account to determine
whether exercising either of these rights is reasonable in all the circumstances
as follows (paragraphs 3.26 and 3.50):
(a) the nature of the work;
(b) the purpose or character of the use of the work;
(c) the context in which the work appears;
(d) any industry practice relevant to the work or the use of the work;
(e) any difficulty or expense to be incurred as a result of identifying the
(f) whether the work has been created in the course of employment; and
(g) whether the work of the author is identifiable or is part of a collective
9.99 As mentioned above, the Committee presented a
Report on Moral Rights
to the Government which was published in January 1988. In that report a
majority of the Committee expressed the view that it was inappropriate
introduce legislation to protect moral rights at that time. While the Committee
remains of the view that there is no need for
the enactment of specific new
legislation for the protection of the moral rights of the authors of computer
programs, it endorses
the approach proposed in the Discussion Paper as a general
test to determine whether exercising a moral right is reasonable in any
particular case (Recommendation 2.14).
Exceptions to Exclusive Rights
(the Act) provides for a number of
exceptions to the exclusive rights of copyright owners. These exceptions fall
into three broad
categories:
Nature of the use
: special characteristics of the use of the work
may render it in the public interest to allow such treatment. Current examples
the fair dealing provisions in ss.40–43 of the Act;
Nature of the user
: users who fall in to a particular class may be
given rights beyond those of other users. Examples of such users include Crown
educational
institutions, institutions assisting the handicapped and the Crown.
Copying of computer programs by the Crown under s.183 of the
Act is an area of
particular concern to producers of computer programs; and
Nature of the subject matter
: some subject matter which, although
aspects of it may come within the scope of copyright protection, is excluded
from copyright
protection because of its otherwise distinctive nature. Examples
include industrial designs and circuit layouts. Because of their
features care must be taken to ensure that any potential overlap between these
independently protected categories of subject
matter does not have unintended
Circuit Layouts Act 1989
(CL Act) is an
example of one such overlap with the Act.
The Berne Convention for the Protection of Literary and Artistic Works (Berne
Convention)
specifies in articles 9(2), 10 and 10
a number of
circumstances in which limitations are imposed upon the author’s exclusive
right to exploit his or her work in
recognition of the competing public need for
information. The provisions of the Act referred to at (A)
effect, allow free use of a copyright work by way of a fair dealing for research
or study, criticism or review, reporting
the news and judicial proceedings or
professional advice. Unlike dealings in category (A), those identified in
category (B) are permitted
under statutory licences which ensure that the
10.02 The breadth of the considerations with respect to educational use of
computer programs is such that a separate chapter has been
necessary to discuss
these matters (Chapter 12), but the remaining exceptions identified in (A) to
(C) are discussed in this chapter.
A. NATURE OF THE USE
INTRODUCTION
10.03 What is normally important to the purchaser or licensee of a computer
program is the right to use, ie, to execute or run the
program. The most common
form of commercial exploitation of copyright in a computer program is for the
or install a copy of the object code (a program which
can be loaded into a computer and run directly) and to grant a license to use
execute that program. The terms for use of the program are usually specified in
an agreement between the purchaser and the computer
program manufacturer or may
be implied. Where source code is distributed there are invariably complex
licensing arrangements.
10.04 Several submissions to the Committee contained assertions to the effect
that computer programs are often or mostly “licensed”
rather than
sold. When a copy of a copyright work published by or with the consent of the
the copyright passes with the copy. In
other words, what the purchaser of a book pursuant to a normal retail
transaction acquires
is ownership of the physical medium comprising a copy of
the work, but not of any copyright subsisting in the work. Thus the purchaser,
although owning the book, is not free to reproduce, perform or broadcast the
work, because these are the exclusive rights comprised
in the copyright, which
he or she has not acquired. To underline this analysis, the Committee notes that
the Act raises a contrary
presumption in the particular case of the disposition
by will of the manuscript of an unpublished work. Section 198 provides that
disposition is presumed to convey the copyright in the work unless there is a
contrary intention in the will.
10.05 Applying this analysis to computer programs as literary works, the
Committee does not believe that the owners of copyright in
published programs
intend, any more than owners of copyright in books, to part with their copyright
in selling or arranging to sell
copies of their programs. The distinction that
can be drawn between traditional literary works, such as books, and computer
is the nature of their use. Whereas books are purchased to be read, and
reading to oneself (or one’s family or friends) does
not involve the
exercise of any exclusive right, computer programs are bought to be used and use
necessitates reproduction which
is an exercise of an exclusive right of the
the normal use of the program. Thus in all circumstances in which copyright
is not conveyed to the purchaser of a copy of a program
and copying is required
for the normal use, the marketing of copies for normal use by the purchaser
necessarily involves “licensing”,
although such a licence might be
implied in the very act of marketing the program in the knowledge that its
normal use necessitates
copying. The licensing of computer programs seeks to
control that use typically by specifying the circumstances in which a computer
program can be reproduced for use by the purchaser (licensee).
The Committee also understands that copyright owners often seek
to impose restrictions on the use of copies of their programs over
and above the
restrictions flowing from their exclusive rights as owners of copyright in the
programs, and that they seek to do so
through the establishment of an effective
contract between the owner and the purchaser of the copy of the program binding
to observe those restrictions. For instance, as rental of copies of
owners, the
only way in which a copyright owner may restrain that use will be if there is a
contractual provision that empowers him
or her to do so. The Committee
understands that some copyright owners seek to impose restrictions of this
nature by so-called “shrink-wrap
licences”, viz, documents that are
enclosed in transparent wrapping around discs containing programs, and which
contain advice,
visible to the purchaser through the wrapping, that if he or she
opens the wrapping he or she thereby agrees to be bound by the additional
restrictions contained in the document. The Committee offers no comment on the
efficacy of the “shrink-wrap licence”
as a binding contract; this
depends on the application of the law of contract in each case. The purpose in
setting out the foregoing
analysis is to express the Committee’s concern
to distinguish between the licensing of a purchaser of a copy of a program to
copy it in the course of normal use, and restraining the purchaser from dealing
with the copy in ways that do not involve the copyright
owner’s exclusive
rights, eg, lending.
COPYING FOR NORMAL USE
10.07 As noted above, because most computer programs operate by reproduction
in whole or in part in the random access memory (RAM)
of the computer, each time
the purchaser of a copy of a computer program uses the program he or she
arguably exercises the copyright
owner’s right to reproduce the program in
material form. Unless the user has the permission of the copyright owner, this
constitute an infringement of copyright and, although permission may be
implied by the very act of marketing the program, the lack
of express statutory
sanction has been commented on.
10.08 Prior to the Draft Report the Australian Vice-Chancellors’
Committee and Australian Committee of Directors and Principals
(AVCC–ACDP) submitted
that the Act is silent about this and
other operations which may be necessary for the effective use of a program by
the purchaser.
The AVCC–ACDP commented that use of the program may require
(1990, pp. 1–2):
(a) copying the program into memory in order to execute it;
(b) copying the program onto a different floppy disk in order to use it on a
machine the floppy disk drives of which are incompatible
with the floppy disk on
which the software was supplied;
(c) copying the program onto a hard disk to permit efficient and convenient
(d) copying the program onto a different floppy disk or onto a hard disk to
enable it to be used in conjunction with other programs
supplied by a different
packages, grammar checkers, spreadsheets
etc. in conjunction with a word
(e) copying an application program (eg, a word processor) onto a new disk to
enable it to be used in conjunction with a later release
of operating system
software. The original operating system software may not support subsequent
upgrades to the computer or to other
(f) allowing a copy of a program resident on hard disk to be copied to
archival media (tapes or floppy disks etc.) as part of normal
system back-up
procedures. In a time-sharing or network environment, it is virtually universal
that the entire contents of all hard
disks, including copyright software, are
copied to a series of back-up tapes on a regular basis (daily or weekly). The
back-up tapes
are kept for the purpose of quickly restoring system operation in
the event of a disk failure.
10.09 The Committee has given careful consideration to the various examples
of uses mentioned in the AVCC–ACDP submission. In
the Draft Report
the Committee also noted that copyright owners often produce programs for
different computer formats in recognition of the potential
extent of use and
number of uses possible with a particular configuration. As the disc
configuration (allowing faster or wider access)
and the operating system program
(allowing network or increased multi-user access) can have some impact on these
factors, the Committee
approaches paragraphs (b) and (e) with caution but the
remaining examples are considered by the Committee to represent normal uses
the sense that they are uses a copyright owner or licensor would ordinarily
expect to be made of his or her computer program.
10.10 In the Draft Report the Committee expressed the view that (excluding
examples (b) and (e) outlined above) such reproductions
of computer programs
should not constitute infringements of copyright when undertaken with a
legitimate copy, ie, one made with the
licence of the copyright owner. In other
words, copying of a computer program that is incidental to its normal use should
not require
the licence, express or implied, of the copyright owner.
Accordingly, the Committee recommended that the Act be amended to expressly
provide that the copyright in a computer program is not infringed by a person
who copies that program where the making of that copy
is reasonable or necessary
for the normal use of a copy of the program made with the licence of the
draft recommendation was supported in
submissions from Alcatel Australia Limited (Alcatel) and the Law Council of
Australia (LCA).
10.11 In its submission commenting on the Draft Report De Data Pty Ltd (De
Data) suggested that the Committee should reconsider its
position regarding
paragraph (b). De Data commented that the Act should allow copying of a computer
program by either the supplier
or the consumer of a computer program from an
incompatible format where the required format is out of stock. However, the
is not persuaded that the non-availability of a computer program in a
particular format is sufficient reason in itself for derogating
from a copyright
owner’s right to control the reproduction of his or her property. There
may, in many cases, be sound commercial
reasons why a program is unavailable in
a particular format. Accordingly, the Committee reaffirms the comments made in
10.12 The Australian Information Industry Association (AIIA) and Computer
Sciences of Australia Pty Ltd (CSA) both commented that
the Committee’s
choice of the words “reasonable”, “necessary”, and the
term “normal use”
lacked certainty. Both suggested that the
Committee should clarify its intentions in respect of this recommendation. While
the Committee
acknowledges the desirability of certainty it notes that the word
“reasonable” is used in various provisions in the Act
and that its
use does not appear to have given rise to any particular difficulties. Also the
Committee does not favour defining what
is “necessary” nor the
meaning of “normal use” as this will depend upon the circumstances
in each case. Following
the Committee’s consideration of these
submissions, the Committee affirms its draft recommendation (Recommendation
BACK-UP COPYING
10.13 Section 43A of the Act as currently drafted allows the owner of a copy
of a computer program to make a back-up copy in certain
circumstances. The
section provides:
(1) Subject to subsection (2), the copyright in a literary work being a
computer program is not infringed by the making of a reproduction
of the work,
or of a computer program being an adaptation of the work, if:
(a) the reproduction is made by, or on behalf of, the owner of the copy (in
this section referred to as the “original copy”)
from which the
reproduction is made; and
(b) the reproduction is made for the purpose only of being used, by or on
behalf of the owner of the original copy, in lieu of the
original copy in the
event that the original copy is lost, destroyed or rendered unusable.
(2) Subsection (1) does not apply to the making of a reproduction of a
computer program, or of an adaptation of a computer program:
(a) from an infringing copy of the computer program; or
(b) contrary to an express direction by or on behalf of the owner of the
original copy not
later than the time when the owner of the original copy acquired the original
(3) For the purposes of this section:
(a) a reference to a copy of a computer program or of an adaptation of a
computer program is a reference to any article in which the
computer program or
adaptation is reproduced in a material form; and
(b) a reference to an express direction, in relation to a copy of a computer
program or of an adaptation of a computer program, includes
a reference to a
clearly legible direction printed on the copy or on a package in which the copy
is supplied.”
10.14 Submissions from the AIIA, Lotus Development Pty Ltd
Microsoft Pty Limited (Microsoft)
indicated that current industry
practice is to “license” most computer programs (discussed in
paragraphs 10.04–10.06).
Having regard to s.43A(2)(b) the purchaser of a
computer program who makes a back-up copy of the program will infringe copyright
if the licence forbids the making of back-up copies. Lotus submitted that
s.43A(1)(b) should be amended to enable a licensee to make
a back-up copy unless
expressly prohibited (because in many cases a copyright owner does not sell the
copy of the program to the
purchaser but merely licenses the copy). The
Committee accepts this proposal and accordingly recommends that s.43A(1)(b) be
to apply to licensees (Recommendation 2.16). Lotus also suggested that
s.43A(2)(b) should be amended so that it applies to the licensor
of a computer
program. However, the Committee understands that in most cases the licensor in
Australia will be the owner of copyright
in the program. If this is the case the
Committee queries the need to amend s.43A(2)(b) to apply to licensees.
10.15 It was also submitted that s.43A is at variance with the normal
practice of users of computer programs who make a back-up copy
to use and store
the original copy to protect it against damage. Therefore, it was suggested, the
section should be amended to allow
the back-up copy to be used instead of the
original. In the Draft Report the Committee recommended that the Act should
amended to allow a back-up copy to be used instead of the original
copy. It also recommended the Act
provide that where an original copy or
back-up copy is destroyed or damaged the surviving original, or back-up, as the
case may be,
may be used to make another back-up copy. There should be no
limitation on the number of times this can be done. The Committee also
acknowledged that this last mentioned recommendation may be opposed by some
effect, to a perpetual
copy. However, a majority of the Committee remains of the view that the
recommendation is essential having
regard to the particular characteristics of
computer programs. In this regard it noted the utilitarian nature of programs
in most cases they are required to perform their functions over a long
period of time. Secondly, their medium of storage is such
that they are easily
corrupted or destroyed. Accordingly the Committee affirms its prior
recommendations (Recommendations 2.17(a),
2.17(b) and 2.17(c)).
10.16 Also in the Draft Report the Committee recommended that a purchaser or
licensee of a copy of a computer program should only
be allowed to make a
back-up copy of the program subject to the positive negation of that right in
accordance with s. 43A(2)(b). This
comment was supported by the AIIA, the LCA
and the Computer and Business Equipment Manufacturers Association (CBEMA). The
Council of Libraries and Information Services (ACLIS) also supported
this recommendation. However, it noted that there might be an
increase in the
number of express prohibitions by software providers. Only Alcatel in its
submission indicated that it did not favour
the retention of s.43A(2)(b). The
Committee discussed this issue at its meetings in December 1993 and March 1994.
It agreed that
s.43A(2)(b) should be repealed. The Committee’s reason for
so recommending, notwithstanding the submissions it received supporting
earlier recommendation, is based on a policy consideration. As noted in
paragraph 10.15 computer programs are easily corrupted
and are utilitarian in
nature. These two factors mitigate in favour of purchasers having the
unqualified right to copy the program
for the limited purpose of making a
back-up. For this reason the Committee now recommends that s.43A(2)(b) be
deleted (Recommendation
2.18 ). The issue of contracting out is further
discussed at paragraph 10.106.
10.17 However, the Committee does accept the following limitation on the
right to make a back-up copy.
The right to make a back-up copy should not
extend to copies of computer programs that have been “locked” by the
owner against the making of copies (Recommendation 2.19). The locking
of computer programs is one way in which the copyright owner
can signal a desire
to prevent the making of copies for other than normal use.
EPHEMERAL BACK-UP COPYING BY BUSINESSES
10.18 There is one other form of back-up copying of computer programs which
the Committee believes deserves special mention, namely
ephemeral back-up
copying by businesses. The Committee understands that it is common practice for
many businesses to back up their
work each day. This usually means that
everything stored on the business’ computer, including data and programs,
is down loaded
onto another form of storage such as magnetic tape or floppy
disc. This copy is then stored in a safe place as a precaution against
unforeseen disasters such as fire, earthquake or even terrorist attack. Such
back-up copies are usually retained only for a limited
period as their
usefulness is short lived. Nonetheless, it is likely that businesses may keep
copies for a number of days before
reusing the tape or disc on which the
material is stored. Such back-up copying, insofar as it includes computer
programs together
with business data, ought not be an infringement of the
making of an ephemeral back-up copy of a computer program that is incidental to
the normal back-up copying of business data for security
purposes should not be
an infringement of copyright (Recommendation 2.20).
Third party maintainers
10.19 In a submission received from Bull HN Information Systems Australia Pty
Ltd (Bull HN) the Committee was asked to consider whether
the scope of the
back-up copy provision should be broadened to include the services provided by
third party maintainers. As the Committee
understands the proposal, persons who
service computer equipment are often required to replace integrated circuits
which contain
computer programs. This can be done by copying the program from an
identical integrated circuit and storing it on another. Bull HN
currently this activity is outside the scope of s.43A because the copy is not
made by the purchaser nor is it made from
the copy of the program owned by the
purchaser. The copy would be made for the purchaser by the third party
maintainer to replace
the defective copy.
10.20 The Committee has considered this proposal but it does not support
extension of s.43A as suggested by Bull HN. In the Committee’s
position of third party maintainers is quite different from that of purchasers
of computer programs. A purchaser is free
to choose whomever he or she wishes to
maintain his or her equipment. If the carrying out of maintenance work involves
an infringement
of copyright because part of what is being maintained needs to
be replaced the maintenance work may not be able to be carried out.
regard to the commercial nature of their operations a right such as that
suggested by Bull HN would amount to a licence to
freely copy computer programs
for the purpose of providing spare parts for computer hardware. The Committee
can see no justification
for providing what would be, in effect, a statutory
licence to copy programs for a commercial purpose. In the Committee’s
the copying of computer programs for such purposes should remain a
matter for negotiation between the copyright owner and the third
maintainer (Recommendation 2.21).
REVERSE ENGINEERING AND DECOMPILATION
Definitions
10.21 At the Committee’s public hearings held in Sydney in
July 1990 and in subsequent submissions to the Committee there were
number of different definitions put forward to explain the meaning of the terms
“reverse engineering”, “decompilation”
“disassembly”. For the sake of clarity, before commencing an
examination of the issues arising in this area, the
Committee has set out below
the definitions it
has adopted for each of those terms, as well as the
terms “porting”, “interoperability”, and
specifications” which are used in the succeeding
paragraphs. Following the release of the Draft Report the Committee received
submissions from Professor W R Cornish and the Supporters of Interoperable
Systems in Australia (SISA) regarding the Committee’s
definition of
interoperability. SISA suggested the definition of interoperability adopted by
the Committee should be replaced with
the following (p.11, paragraph 3.21):
Interoperability is the functional interaction between hardware, computer
programs, and users which gives the ability to exchange
information and to
mutually use the information which has been exchanged.
10.22 The Committee has reconsidered and revised its definition of
interoperability having regard to those comments it has also revised
definitions of decompilation and disassembly:
Decompilation
: the working back from the object code of a computer
program to a version of the source code. This process may involve a substantial
recreation or reproduction of the source code of the original program.
Decompilation is achieved using a computer program called
a decompiler.
Disassembly
: the working back from object code to assembler code ie, a
special case of decompilation. Disassembly is achieved using a computer
called a disassembler.
Interface specifications
the description of the logical and
physical interconnection and interaction which allows a hardware or computer
program product to
interact as intended with other hardware, computer program(s)
Interoperability
: the ability of computer systems to exchange
information and mutually to use the information which has been exchanged.
making the arrangements necessary so that a computer
program that runs on one computer is able to run on a differently configured
computer. This generally involves modification of the original program.
Reverse engineering
: the study or analysis of a computer product
(including a computer program) in order to reveal the underlying idea or
principle on
which it operates. This analysis may include an examination of
relevant published documentation, study of the operation of the product
the case of computer programs, their decompilation. Studying of the operation of
a program would involve reproduction of
the program in the same way as normal
use (see paragraphs 10.07–10.12).
Introduction
10.23 It has long been a practice in the area of engineered products that
techniques are developed to examine a product in order to
create a new product
which performs a similar function. Those techniques are known as “reverse
engineering”. In the area
of patented inventions, usually it will be the
product developed using this practice, rather than the act of reverse
engineering,
that may (or may not) infringe the patent. In most cases reverse
engineering would not usually involve the making of a copy of a
patented product
as to do so would infringe the patent. With traditional literary works, eg, a
book, an author may decide to write
a definitive work on a subject and, to do
so, the author may study (read) a number of books. Such reading will not
infringe copyright
(although if the author’s work is found to reproduce
substantial parts of the books read, the reading of those books will be
that the reader had an opportunity to copy; without more, however, it will not
establish copying).
10.24 Computer programs are rarely distributed in a form that lends itself to
the ready ascertainment of the underlying ideas and
principles upon which the
program is based. Most programs are published in object code which is not
perceivable by human senses.
Accordingly, to determine the underlying codes and
principles of the program for whatever purpose — either to create a
product, an interoperable product or simply to correct an error, some
form of reverse engineering of the object code version of the
program will be
necessary. As recognised by the Committee’s definition of reverse
engineering in paragraph 10.22, that process
may involve not only the study of
relevant published documentation and the operation of the program (which may
include measuring
the various electrical signals that are generated while it is
operating, including those signals which pass between the computer
on which it
is running and other computers or devices), but also, decompilation of the
object code. Studying the operation of the
program and decompilation will
involve its reproduction. The need to decompile the object code of a program in
the course of reverse
engineering will depend upon whether or not sufficient
information can be obtained to understand the underlying ideas and principle,
from a study of the operation of the program and any published
documentation.
10.25 The Committee understands that a major concern of the owners of
to be undertaken
in the course of reverse engineering provides an opportunity for competitors to
clone their programs with relative
ease (this is discussed in more detail in
paragraphs 10.38—10.39). Once decompiled the source code can be
manipulated relatively
easily to hide any visual similarity while retaining the
same functionality. In other words decompilation can be used in this way
competitors to free ride on the efforts of the original creator. The Committee
accepts this as a real concern. Accordingly, it
recommends that reverse
engineering including decompilation should be prohibited except for the limited
circumstances required for
interoperability and error correction discussed later
in this chapter (Recommendation 2.22).
10.26 The Committee also notes that reproducing a computer program in the
course of running it for the purpose of reverse engineering
may not be a
licensed “normal use”(it is also an activity that cannot in practise
be controlled by the copyright owner).
Reverse engineering of a program
that extends to decompiling it is also an activity that is
unlikely to be
licensed by the copyright owner. Further, construction of a version of a program
that approximates its source code
may, in the light of the
Autodesk Inc
Martin Dyason and others (
[1992] HCA 2
(1992) 173 CLR 330
) also amount to a reproduction of the source code.
Accordingly, unless
reverse engineering and limited decompilation come
within the fair dealing exception in the Act if it is to be sanctioned it will
have to be made an exception to the exclusive reproduction right of the
5(3) and 6 of
Directive on the Legal Protection of Computer Programs
Directive on Computer Programs)
(Appendix G).
FAIR DEALING UNDER THE ACT
10.27 In the draft report the Committee considered whether the fair dealing
exception to copyright infringement under s.40 of the
Act offered substantial
scope for what might be regarded as justifiable reverse engineering. Section
40(1) provides that:
A fair dealing with a literary. . . work, or with an adaptation of a . . .
work, for the purposes of research or study does not constitute
an infringement
of the copyright in the work.
Section 40(2) provides that:
. . . the matters to which regard shall be had, in determining whether a
dealing with a . . . work or with an adaptation . . . being
a dealing by way of
copying the whole or a part of the work or adaptation, constitutes a fair
dealing . . . for the purpose of research
or study include:
(a) the purpose and character of the dealing;
(b) the nature of the work or adaptation;
(c) the possibility of obtaining the work or adaptation within a reasonable
time at an ordinary commercial price;
(d) the effect of the dealing upon the potential market for, or value of, the
work or adaptation . . .
10.28 In the Draft Report the Committee referred to a case in the USA,
the Court of Appeals (9th Circuit) held that reverse engineering,
including the
decompilation of a program to determine its unprotected
ideas and functional concepts was permissible as a “fair use”
s.107 of the US
Sega Enterprises Ltd
[1993] USCA9 19
977 F. 2d 1510
(9th Cir. 1992) (
That decision, to which further reference will be made hereafter, has attracted
considerable attention. However, the Committee
attaches importance to the
decision as a guide to what might be done by way of amendment to Australian law
to address the matter
of reverse engineering, rather than as a guide to the
interpretation of “fair dealing” under s.40. While the Committee
understands that the list of matters in s.40(2) quoted in the last paragraph was
drawn from a comparable list to the one used
in s.107 of the US
there are differences. Notably, and relevantly
for present purposes, s.40(2)(c) has no counterpart in the US provision.
the requirement in s.40 that a fair dealing must be for the purposes of
“research or study” is narrower than the US provision
which has an
inclusive, not exhaustive, list of purposes for which “fair use”
will not be an infringement. Thus in two
very relevant respects, “fair
use” under the US Act appears to be different to and broader than
“fair dealing”
under s.40.
10.29 The requirement that a fair dealing under s.40 be for the purposes of
“research or study” has been considered by
Denis Rose QC, Chief
General Counsel of the Attorney-General’s Department. Although Mr
Rose’s opinion was sought in relation
to a different activity to that of
reverse engineering, his conclusion nevertheless seems to be applicable to the
latter. It is,
in Mr Rose’s view:
likely that the courts would hold that ‘study’ is confined to
study by individuals for their own purposes, whether in
private or in some
institutional course or otherwise. Moreover, the courts could well confine
‘research’ to research
activities such as those in universities and
the CSIRO, for the purpose of increasing knowledge in the community as a
contrast for instance, with research in a Government Department
for the purpose of advising Ministers on proposed legislation, or
research by a
manufacturing company for the purpose of improving its products.
Should the courts adopt such an interpretation it would limit considerably
the extent to which commercial computer program producers
could rely on s.40 in
defence of reverse engineering undertaken by them in the course of their
commercial developmental activities.
10.30 There is a second matter to be addressed in invoking s.40 to justify
reverse engineering, namely, s.40(2)(c), which as already
noted has no
counterpart in the US “fair use” provision and which was included as
part of the implementation of the Franki
report on photocopying (
the Copyright Law Committee on Reprographic Reproduction
, AGPS, Canberra,
1976). The Committee understands that a typical commercial research and
development operation is where a commercial
program producer reverse engineers a
successful and therefore readily available program of a competitor. A normal
reading of s.40(2)(c)
would weigh against a conclusion that all types of copying
in the course of reverse engineering including that done during
decompilation,
could be justified as a fair dealing. The Committee notes
that s.40(2)(c) would apply only to, say, making a copy in the course of
the program preparatory to decompiling it. Section 40(2)(c) would presumably
have no application to the version produced
as a result of decompilation, which
would clearly not be commercially available.
10.31 In the light of these considerations, the Committee doubts that s.40 is
a satisfactory existing means of marking out the extent
of permissible reverse
engineering.
REVERSE ENGINEERING: COMPATIBILITY WITH THE BERNE
10.32 Allowing decompilation in the course of reverse engineering was alleged
by IBM Australia Limited (IBM)
to be “problematic” in view of
the international ramifications of such a right being granted. In its submission
1990 IBM stated that sanctioning
decompilation as part of reverse
engineering would, among other things (p. 8):
• place Australian developers at an advantage relative to competing
developers in other countries where such an exception does
conflict with the provision under the Berne Convention,
Article 9(2), since allowing some copying and adaptation
of a copyright work for commercial purposes—frequently to obtain an
advantage over the copyright owner of the subject program—would
‘conflict with the normal exploitation of the work’
‘unreasonably prejudice the legitimate interests of the author’;
— Article 10, which permits the utilisation of literary works for
teaching provided such utilisation is ‘compatible with
In the Draft Report the Committee considered the applicability of the Berne
Convention to the decompilation of computer programs in
object code (see
Chapter 4 of the Draft Report), and there the Committee (with one exception)
concluded that the Berne Convention does not require
the protection of programs
in object code. However, the Committee also noted that a consequence of the
Autodesk case
decision may be that the decompiled version of a program in
object code may reproduce a substantial part of the source code program
notwithstanding that the person doing the decompilation has, self-evidently, not
seen the source code (see paragraph 5.25). For this
reason the Committee decided
to treat the Berne Convention as applying to decompilation to create an
approximation of the
source code. Since the Draft Report was completed
negotiations in the Uruguay Round of the GATT were concluded. When that
comes into force Australia will be bound under art.10 of the TRIPS
text to protect computer programs, whether in source or object
code, as literary
works under the Berne Convention. This will remove any uncertainty as to the
applicability of the Berne Convention
to computer programs.
10.33 The Committee notes that art.9(2) of the Berne Convention provides:
(2) It shall be a matter for legislation in the countries of the Union to
permit the reproduction of such works in certain special
cases, provided that
such reproduction does not conflict with a normal exploitation of the work and
does not unreasonably prejudice
the legitimate interests of the author.
The provision contains two conditions which apply cumulatively, ie, permitted
reproduction must satisfy both conditions to be compatible
with art.9(2). In
relation to the first condition, viz, “provided that such reproduction
does not conflict with a normal exploitation
of the work” the
the Berne Convention
or the Protection of Literary and Artistic Works
(WIPO, Geneva, 1978) (WIPO Guide) states (p. 55, paragraph 9.7):
If the contemplated reproduction would be such as to conflict with the normal
exploitation of the work it is not permitted at all.
Novels, school books, etc.,
are normally exploited by being printed and sold to the public. This Article
does not permit member countries
to allow this eg., under compulsory licences,
even if payment is made to the copyright owners.
If the information gained from decompilation in the course of reverse
engineering
were used to make and market a similar program which amounted
to a copy and which failed to come within the permitted exception in
it would be the making of that copy, and not the reproduction for decompilation
undertaken in the course of reverse engineering
that would be the prohibited
10.34 If the first condition is met, so that the reproduction does not
conflict with the normal exploitation of the work, it is still
necessary to see
if there is unreasonable prejudice to the legitimate interests of the author. In
paragraph 9.8 of the WIPO Guide
the question of what is unreasonable
prejudice is examined in terms of lost sales. The making of a single copy of a
journal may result
in the loss of one sale but it is unlikely to unreasonably
prejudice the interests of the author. However, the making of many copies
limited edition publication and its subsequent distribution would. Again, in the
context of reverse engineering a computer program
by running it and observing
its behaviour to determine its underlying idea and
to enable the
independent creation of a similar program using that idea (or the development of
a program that will operate in conjunction
with the first), it is difficult to
see how reproduction for the purpose of these activities will unreasonably
prejudice the interests
of the author. On the other hand, a broadly expressed
right to reproduce for decompilation might not come within art.9(2) on the
that it facilitated unauthorised copying to such an extent as to conflict with
the normal sales of the work to the prejudice
of the interests of the owner of
10.35 IBM also asserted that allowing decompilation as part of the reverse
engineering process
would conflict with art.10 of the Berne Convention
or, more precisely, art.10(2)(use of works by way of illustration for teaching).
The Committee understands that in most cases decompilation is not used for
teaching purposes. Decompilation is used in the course
of reverse engineering by
competitors to make interoperable and competing products. The provisions of
art.10(2) do not appear to
be relevant in that context. The sanctioning
of decompilation in such cases
would fall to be considered under
art.9(2) already discussed.
10.36 Where a program is reverse engineered and decompiled for teaching
purposes, for example in a university course, the Committee
notes that the
article provides that such utilisation of the works must be “compatible
with fair practice”. In the WIPO
Guide at paragraph 10.4 the concept
of compatible with fair practice is discussed. According to the Guide (p.
It implies an objective appreciation of what is normally considered
admissible. The fairness or otherwise of what is done is ultimately
a matter for
the courts, who will no doubt consider such questions as the size of the extract
in proportion both to the work from
which it was taken and that in which it is
used, and, particularly the extent to which, if any, the new work, by competing
old, cuts in upon its sale and circulation, etc.
Having regard to these comments the Committee concludes that reverse
engineering including limited decompilation could be allowed
for teaching
purposes consistently with the provisions of art.10(2).
REASONS FOR REVERSE ENGINEERING
10.37 Reverse engineering of computer programs may be undertaken for a number
of reasons including the purposes of:
(a) creating clones (programs that look and perform like the program thus
(b) modification for interoperability and to determine interface
specifications;
(c) facilitating porting;
(d) facilitating error correction (debugging); and
(e) understanding techniques used in the program by its creators.
Creating clones
10.38 In its submission to the Committee, Lotus claimed that the main purpose
of reverse engineering is to create a clone of the original
program. IBM claimed
in its presentation to the Committee that computer programs are available which
decompile other programs very
rapidly making the task of cloning programs
relatively easy. IBM submitted that to allow decompilation as part of the
reverse engineering
would (July 1990, p.8):
benefit software copiers only—by providing an opportunity to create
successive adaptations of computer programs without liability
to the copyright
According to IBM
once a program is available in a high level language
(which is not limited to a particular machine), a competitor intent on cloning
the program can manipulate the code thus derived to hide any similarity to the
original program. IBM’s demonstration to the
Committee was held on 22
November 1990. At that presentation, only a simple form of decompilation was
demonstrated, namely the disassembly
of a relatively small program. No evidence
of generalised decompilation to high level computer languages was provided.
The Committee shares IBM’s concern that any new exception
to existing rights should not expand the opportunities for computer
copiers to create and market programs which are simply copies or adaptations of
the original program. However, it notes that
any new program that is produced by
reverse engineering an existing program and which is a copy or adaptation of the
latter program
is no less an infringement. Nevertheless, it accepts that any
such new exception to exclusive rights must be limited to particular
purposes. The Committee does not agree with IBM that only computer program
copiers will benefit from such a right. Submissions
from a number of interests,
including hardware vendors, indicated a need for such an exception for other
purposes, which have been
identified in paragraph 10.37 and which will be
examined in detail. The Committee also notes that the large number of ways and
number of steps that can be involved in the compilation of source code to
object code mean that it is highly unlikely that decompilation
will lead back to
a complete version of the original source code.
Rights in relation to modification
Interoperability and interface
specifications
10.40 The owner or user of a purchased computer program may wish to modify
that program to allow interoperability with:
(a) other programs on the same computer including those written by the user
(including modules added to extend the functionality of
the program);
(b) other hardware attached to the same computer;
(c) new operating system programs on the same computer;
(d) other computers accessed via a network;
10.41 There are two major ways in which programs can be made to interact with
each other and with hardware:
(a) existing programs can be modified to communicate directly with other
programs or newly acquired hardware; and
(b) additional programs may be written to provide a link between existing
programs or between an existing program and hardware.
There is no copyright basis for preventing creation of additional original
programs. Under the approach outlined in (b), the user
could either write the
linking program which provides the interoperability or obtain a linking program
from a third party. The creation
of programs capable of operating in conjunction
with existing programs and hardware is a common need of the vast majority of
of computer programs. Those who have the resources will wish to create
their own programs to allow existing hardware and computer
program products to
be used together and to pass information easily between multiple products.
Others will wish to purchase such
products written by third parties.
The process of writing a program to facilitate interoperability
involves examining the interfaces of existing products. Knowledge
of interfaces
to hardware may also be required. Often interface specifications will be
published or can be determined from observation
of the existing system in
operation. Sometimes an examination of the program with which the new program is
interoperating or of a
related program, is necessary to fully determine the
interface. Where a program is distributed as source code, this can simply be
examined. Where it is distributed in machine-readable form, some sort of reverse
engineering process may be necessary. That process
may include decompilation of
the object code. The Act does not currently prevent the independent creation of
new programs which interact
with an existing program and the Committee can see
no reason for changing this position. The only issue that remains is what
can be used to determine the interface specifications.
10.43 At present, modifying programs without licence would, in many cases,
constitute an infringement of copyright, as the process
of modification would
frequently include creating an unauthorised reproduction or adaptation of the
original program. Achieving interoperability
by modifying the existing program
would therefore require the user in such cases to negotiate a licence with the
to modify the program. Further exploitation of that adaptation,
or substantial reproduction,
such as making copies of, or publishing the
modified program would also be subject to the permission of the owner of
the original program.
10.44 In its submission to the Committee, Fujitsu Australia Ltd (Fujitsu)
stated that (p. 18):
The only way to make interoperable products is to determine the interfacing
rules that must be followed to allow product-to-product
communication or data
exchange. While many interface requirements are published, they usually are
described incompletely, inaccurately
or belatedly, necessitating the application
of other research techniques.
Fujitsu further submitted that reverse engineering for interoperability is
essentially reverse engineering to determine interface
specifications to
facilitate interoperability. This view is affirmed by submissions from SISA and
the EC Directive on Computer Programs.
10.45 Fujitsu identified two main situations where reverse engineering is
required. These were, first, in the development of interoperable
products, the
determination of interface specifications was said to be essential because the
. . . interfacing rules . . . must be followed to allow product-to-product
communication or data exchange
and second, where different programs are required to run on the same system.
In such cases it may be necessary to discover the interfaces
of each of the
programs, to each other and to the operating system, to determine (p. 17):
. . . how products from several vendors will best work together, with or
without additional software.
Both of these situations demonstrate that interoperability and interfaces are
inextricably linked.
10.46 Bull HN submitted that only in the most basic situations can reverse
engineering be effectively conducted by monitoring the
external behaviour of a
system. In most cases access to the code of the program is necessary. The
submission further described a
procedure used in the US and Japan, similar to
the “clean room” technique, to make interoperable products. The
program is decompiled and analysed by a team of programmers which
supplies details of those elements of the program necessary for
interoperability. A second team, without reference to the program thus analysed,
creates another program using only the information
supplied and its own
creativity. Having regard to this procedure, Bull HN submitted that reverse
engineering is not a short cut,
the analysis of the decompiled program is
time-consuming and this time must be added to the time taken to create the
compatible program.
10.47 The Committee notes that in the circumstances outlined by Bull HN, the
“reverse engineer” is seeking to create an
interoperable product
without copying the original program. One team of programmers determines the
underlying ideas of the program,
the other team uses that information to
independently create an interoperable program. Those programmers do not copy or
original program; they independently write an interoperable program.
However, where the process of
decompilation and disassembly of the
original program involves adaptation of that program, the general use of these
techniques is
at present prohibited without the licence of the copyright
10.48 In its submission made prior to the Draft Report
IBM rejected
the argument that determination of interfaces should be the basis for a reverse
engineering exception to facilitate and
thus to encourage interoperability. In
that submission IBM stated (July 1990, p. 9):
To a certain extent, the international software market has witnessed
significant industry initiatives to standardise interconnections
facilitate this, copyright owners have taken steps to make their
‘interface’ information available. Some of these
initiatives include:
Open Systems Interconnection (‘OSI’), a set of
standards for transaction processing, electronic data interchange, remote
Organisation for Standardisation, the ‘ISO’).
In telecommunications, recommendations by CCITT, a committee
of the International Telecommunications Union (‘ITU’), a
organisation, for interconnection to the Integrated Services Digital Network
Several industry organisations have been formed recently to
promote greater availability of information about interfaces, including
X–OPEN and the Open Software Foundation (‘OSF’), INIX
International and the Extended Industry Standard Architecture
(‘EISA’) consortium.
In the United States, primarily in response to consumer demand, software and
hardware suppliers are already voluntarily moving towards
open systems, and we
would argue, that without strong copyright protection, this trend which is
proceeding satisfactorily throughout
the world, is unlikely to have
publish, interfaces to encourage others to write applications
programs for their
systems. IBM, for example, has published 300–400 interfaces (described in
many volumes) specifically to
allow users to write programs to run with the IBM
System 370 operating system MVS/XA.
10.49 The Committee acknowledges the extensive work being done
internationally to encourage open systems and the publication of standard
interface specifications to promote interoperability between products from
different vendors. The Committee also notes that the product
which IBM cites as
an example of the extensive publication of interfaces is an operating system and
an operating system is rarely
marketable unless a number of manufacturers are
creating programs for it, hence the need to extensively publish the interfaces.
products are rarely as extensively documented, and the Committee is not
convinced that the makers would always be willing to make
the information
available if users had to rely on them, ie, if decompilation was not permitted
for the purpose. The Committee also
notes that reverse engineering is usually a
time-consuming and costly exercise and if reliable interface specifications were
users would be less likely to go to the trouble of decompiling the
product in question. If users had a right to decompile a product
and vendors
wished to discourage users from doing so, they would be likely to publish
detailed and accurate interface specifications.
Therefore, the Committee
contends that the move towards open systems will be promoted rather than
retarded by allowing users to decompile
products to determine the interfaces,
particularly if this right is confined to situations where the information is
otherwise not
available. In the draft report the Committee recommended that the
decompilation of computer programs for the limited purposes of
interoperability with other computer programs should not constitute an
infringement of copyright where the information
necessary to achieve this is
otherwise not available.
For interoperability with other programs
10.50 The Committee received nine submissions commenting on its draft
recommendation regarding decompilation for interoperability.
The AIIA advised
the Committee that as there was no consensus within its membership it was unable
to offer a specific recommendation
on this issue. The submissions from IBM, the
CBEMA and CSC/CSA made the following points, which the Committee notes largely
comments received prior to the Draft Report:
• an exception for decompilation was not supported by any legitimate
need in the market place;
• a consequence of the introduction of a decompilation right will be an
increased risk of piracy;
• the decompilation provisions were unjustified given customer demand
for interoperable systems, new standards and the increasing
move to “open
• the relevant information is usually available free or subject to a
licence; and
• the “fair dealing” provisions of the Act should apply in
these circumstances (this point was also supported by
Mr Muratore).
However, IBM also submitted that if the Committee remained inclined towards
recommending an exception for decompilation it should
recommend that any
Australian legislation follow the precise wording of the EC Directive on
Computer Programs.
10.51 The Committee received a submission from the American Committee for
Interoperable Systems (ACIS) whose members support the introduction
of a limited
right to allow decompilation for interoperability. ACIS submitted that:
• market forces alone would not solve the “imperative of
interoperability”;
• decompilation is a technical necessity to an adequate understanding
of the ideas and principles embodied in a computer program;
• decompilation is not a tool for pirates. At most it reveals a
“distant shadow” of the high level source code in
which the program
was written.
The ACIS submission also noted that the industry is divided on this
10.52 In his submission Mr Muratore observed that allowing decompilation for
interoperability has consequences for the scope of copyright
protection in the
original program. In the case of applications programs he noted there are at
least two possibilities. One is that
small parts of the original program may
have to be embodied in the other to make it functional. The other is that the
interoperable
program may operate only if it can be attached
“Siamese-like” to the original. In both cases he noted that there
a derogation of the original program owner’s rights. The Committee is
