divided on the question of whether allowing decompilation
in such circumstances
derogates from the copyright owner’s rights. However, the Committee is of
the view that the public interest
is better served by allowing the developers of
interoperable products to develop interoperable products in this way. It also
that the development and marketing of an interoperable program that
attaches “Siamese-like” to another would likely advantage
of copyright in the original program.
10.53 Alcatel, SISA, and the LCA all supported the Committee’s draft
recommendation. The LCA commented that the exception should
not be limited to
decompilation as defined by the Committee. It proposed that it should extend to
(p. 4, paragraph 4.7):
reproduction or adaptation of the program necessary to
achieve interoperability, not just reproduction in the course of working back
from object code to a higher level language
so long as that reproduction or adaptation was confined to the parts of the
computer program necessary to achieve interoperability
and was only for the
purpose of achieving that interoperability. The LCA noted that art.6 of the EC
Directive on Computer Programs
includes reproduction or translation of program
code. (The Committee has adopted this suggestion—see paragraph 10.58).
The LCA commented that if its proposal was adopted it would permit
incorporation of protected program code in a new computer program
(or device)
where otherwise the incorporation (reproduction) of a small piece of code may be
an infringement (in light of the High
Court’s decision in the
). The LCA’s comments were echoed in the submission from SISA.
10.54 Alcatel queried the application of paragraph (c) of draft
recommendation 2.22, which provides that decompilation for interoperability
should be allowed where the acts are confined to the parts of the program
necessary to achieve interoperability, on the basis that
it is impossible to
know beforehand how much of a program needs to be decompiled. SISA also
commented that the use of the phrase
“parts of the program” in
paragraph (c) of the draft recommendation is based on the assumption that a
developer can identify
in advance those parts of the program that relate to
interoperability. SISA submitted that this will rarely be the case and suggested
that paragraph (c) should read:
(c) the acts are confined to those necessary to achieve interoperability
to ensure that the intention of the provision is not defeated. The Committee
has considered these comments. It agrees, having regard
to the limitations that
apply ie. the decompilation should only be used to achieve interoperability; and
the information obtained
should only be given to others when necessary for the
interoperability of the independently created program, that the scope of
(c) should be amended as suggested by SISA. Accordingly, the Committee
has decided that the draft recommendation should be amended
decompilation for interoperability provided, any acts of decompilation are
confined to those necessary to achieve interoperability
(see paragraph
10.55 SISA also made the following comments in addition to those referred to
above (p. 11, paragraph 3.11)
the use of the phrase “has not previously been readily available”
in paragraph (b) of the draft recommendation may require
developers to request
interface information first thereby disclosing their development plans. It may
also encourage some copyright
owners to place onerous pricing or licensing
regimes on interface specifications.
To avoid this SISA suggested paragraph (b) of draft recommendation 2.22
should be modified to read:
(b) the information to achieve interoperability has not previously been
published and is not at the time of decompilation readily
available on
reasonable terms.
10.56 The Committee notes these concerns but does not consider that they
justify an amendment to paragraph (b) such as suggested by
SISA. SISA also
suggested that there may be some doubt whether the words “owner” and
“or another person having
a right to use the copy” used by the
Committee in paragraph (a) of the draft recommendation would include a licensee.
suggested that the term “lawful possessor” should be
substituted in place of the word “owner”. The Committee
share SISA’s concerns. A purchaser will in most cases own the physical
copy although he or she may only have a licence
to use the program. Furthermore,
in its view a licensee would clearly be a person having a right to use the
For interoperability with hardware
10.57 The LCA also submitted that the exception should not be limited to
decompilation to achieve interoperability of an independently
created program
other programs
. The exception should be extended to include
decompilation to achieve the interoperability of an independently created
program with
(ie. a hardware item). Similarly, in its submission
SISA stated (p.10, paragraph 3.5):
There are occasions where the developer of new hardware, particularly a CPU,
may be required to decompile another company’s
computer program, usually
an operating system program, to ensure that new hardware will interoperate
successfully with the computer
SISA submitted that there was (p.10, paragraph 3.6):
. . . no justifiable copyright law, technology or public policy reason not to
extend . . .
the Committee’s recommendation to the creation of interoperable
hardware. In the submission the Committee received from SISA’s
equivalent, the European Committee for Interoperable Systems (ECIS), ECIS stated
that although controversy exists about
the permissibility of decompilation for
the purpose of achieving hardware interoperability nothing in the EC Directive
on Computer
Programs explicitly states that decompilation is not permitted for
that purpose. ECIS drew to the Committee’s attention Recital
Directive which states that an objective of art.6 is:
to make it possible to connect all components of a computer system, including
those of different manufacturers, so they can work together.
ECIS also pointed to Recital 10 which states that:
interconnection and interaction is required to permit all elements of
software and hardware to work with other software and hardware.
10.58 The Committee has considered these submissions. It notes that to the
extent that hardware devices embody computer programs and
those programs provide
the means by which interoperability is achieved the Committee’s draft
recommendation would appear to
already apply to those cases. However, the
Committee acknowledges that there may be cases where the interoperability of
and hardware devices may not depend on the interaction of two programs.
The Committee’s recommendation concerning decompilation
interoperability recognises the need to encourage competition as well as the
fact that in some circumstances decompilation may
be the only means available to
readily determine the underlying ideas and principles to enable the development
of an interoperable
program. These same considerations apply in respect of the
development of interoperable hardware. For these reasons the Committee
with the suggestion made by SISA. Accordingly, the Committee recommends that
decompilation of a computer program should be
allowed where it is necessary to
achieve the interoperability of an independently created computer program or
hardware device with
other programs or hardware devices provided (Recommendation
(a) decompilation is performed by the owner of a lawfully acquired copy of
the program or another person having a right to use the
copy or on their behalf
by a person authorised to do so; and
(b) the information necessary to achieve interoperability has not previously
been readily available; and
(c) the acts are confined to those necessary to achieve interoperability.
The following limitations should apply:
(i) the decompilation should only be used to achieve interoperability;
(ii) the information obtained should only be given to others when necessary
for the interoperability of the independently created
computer program or
hardware device.
For enhanced performance
10.59 Alcatel submitted that industrial programs are regarded by industry as
an essential tool. For this reason it is essential that
the purchasers have the
right to modify a program to meet their specific needs. Examples identified
included modifying a program
in circumstances where new equipment with different
operating parameters was installed. Alcatel also submitted this right should
be left to be determined by contract. In this regard the Committee recognises
that in certain circumstances timing may be an
important consideration and that
the need to modify or adapt a computer program may arise in a very tight
However, the Committee does not recommend any further
broadening of the limited circumstances in which decompilation should be allowed
beyond those discussed above in paragraphs 10.50 to 10.58. The Committee
considers that such needs should be covered by an agreement
between the
purchaser and the copyright owner (Recommendation 2.24).
For networking
10.60 Submissions to the Committee suggested that both the educational and
industrial fields may wish to modify computer programs
in order to facilitate
their use. Many of the examples amounted to modification to create interoperable
systems. One such example
is the suggestion by both the Australian Education
Council, Department of School Education (NSW)
and the Department of
Education, Queensland that users of programs which are not able to run on
networks be allowed to modify them
to do so. Such a modification would be a
modification to make the subject program interoperable with the network protocol
(ie, those programs controlling the exchange of information between
entities in a computer network) and the hardware connecting the
10.61 In respect of educational institutions, Microsoft submitted that the
adaptation of computer programs should not be allowed.
However if it were to
become permissible, adaptation should only be allowed along the lines of the
fair dealing exceptions that apply
in respect of other literary works. The
Committee notes that fair dealing would have little or no application to
networking.
10.62 In the Draft Report the Committee commented
that modification of
a program, so far as it amounts to making an adaptation of the program, would
involve the exercise of the adaptation
right—in addition to the exercise
of the reproduction right—if that was necessary to enable the adaptation
to be made.
While in the case of determining interface specifications there may
be wider developmental benefits to the industry and users generally,
particular user’s desire to modify a program for his or her particular
needs does not seem to justify an exception to the
rights. Both educational institutions and computer program manufacturers have
expressed the wish that they
should continue to negotiate licence fees for
educational copying of computer programs. The Committee concluded that the right
modify computer programs should be left to negotiation between the user and
the copyright owner or licensee. In their submissions
both Oracle Systems
(Australia) Pty Ltd (Oracle) and the LCA agreed with the Committee’s
recommendation that this matter should
be left to negotiation. Accordingly, the
Committee reaffirms the recommendation it made in the Draft Report
(Recommendation 2.25).
10.63 The use of reverse engineering to facilitate porting was partly raised
by IBM in their submission on cloning. A computer program
is said to be
“portable” if it can be readily transferred from one computer to
other computers. There are many reasons
why a user would want to take a computer
program written to run on one type of computer (type A) and modify it so it can
run on another
machine B include:
(a) type B is available to the user and type A is not;
(b) the program is required on a system which contains other programs
available only in a version for type B;
(c) type B is cheaper than type A;
(d) type B is faster than type A;
(e) type B allows more users to access its files either because it supports
more users than type A or because it has greater networking
capabilities.
Decompilation to a high level language which is substantially machine
independent (ie, to a language that can be compiled, with little
modification,
to operate on a number of different types of computers) would make porting for
these purposes considerably easier for
those users who only have access to
object code. Although there is no evidence that this is possible, disassembly
and other methods
of analysis may still be of some use in this regard.
10.64 The two major circumstances where the user might choose to modify the
program distributed for computer A are that a version
for B has not yet been
published, or that the version for A is much cheaper than that for B. Such price
differences are reasonable
as they may reflect the difference in cost associated
with development of programs on different machines, the different size of the
market for different machines, and also the number of users who might be able to
use the program simultaneously. A version for machine
A which only allows a
single user might be considerably cheaper than a version for machine B which
allows 40 users to access the
program either on one machine or through a network
connecting machines.
10.65 In some respects porting is analogous to the translation of traditional
literary works from one language to another. The publisher
in this situation is
permitted to consider the cost of translation together with the size of the new
market. The publisher can then
price versions in different languages
accordingly. Likewise, if a translation of a copyrighted literary work is
unavailable, one
must negotiate with the owner of the right to obtain a licence
to translate the work. The Committee can see no reason for distinguishing
computer programs from other literary works in this respect.
10.66 Subject to the Committee’s recommendation in chapter 6 and its
discussion of the reproduction and adaptation rights in
modification of a computer program to enable it to operate on different
computers may involve either reproducing the program or making
an adaptation of
the program. Modification of the source code to meet functional requirements of
another computer’s operating
system will involve reproduction of a
substantial part of the original program. Where the modifications are extensive
or a new version
(not being a reproduction of the program) has been written,
possibly in another language, that new version may be an adaptation of
original program. Compilation of a program in source code to object code will at
the very least amount to the making of an adaptation.
Similarly, where porting
is achieved by modifications made to the object code, it could be expected that
in most cases the modified
program will reproduce a substantial part of the
original program’s object code.
10.67 Where a computer program is published in source code (in particular in
a high level language) rather than as machine-specific
object code, the user,
without any great difficulty, can often make a running version of the program
that will operate a different
computer. This may sometimes be done by simply
compiling the source code for the other computer but more frequently by doing so
a few minor alterations to the source code. Whether the user has an
implied licence allowing them to compile the program for a wider
computers will depend on the terms of the agreement between the user and the
things, the licence agreement or,
in the absence of such an agreement, the particular facts and circumstances in
each case which
are the basis for any implied agreement. As porting a program
will involve exercising either the reproduction or adaptation right,
source code by contract. In the Draft Report
the Committee stated that it
did not see the need to recommend any changes to the Act to allow porting.
10.68The Committee received three submissions commenting on this
recommendation. Both the LCA and Oracle agreed with the position
adopted by the
Committee. In a submission from the CSIRO’s Institute of Information
Science and Engineering (CSIRO) it was
suggested that the copyright owner should
not have the right to prevent the making of changes to enable porting if the
owner was unable or unwilling to supply a suitably modified version
within a reasonable time. This submission in effect calls for
the establishment
of a statutory licence to allow the modification of computer programs in certain
circumstances. While the Committee
recognises the needs of users who require
versions of a program to run on a different type of computer to that for which
the program
was originally written the Committee considers that the right to
modify computer programs to allow them to run on other machines
should remain
the subject of negotiation with the copyright owner. The Committee also
recommends that to decompile for such purposes
should continue to be negotiated
with the copyright holder (Recommendation 2.26(a)). The Committee can see no
justification for a
scheme such as that suggested by the CSIRO (apart from the
convenience of the user). Nor does the Committee consider that such an
of the rights of the copyright owner is justified. Accordingly, it reaffirms its
draft recommendation that no change be made
to the Act to allow porting
(Recommendation 2.26(b)).
Error correction
10.69 Computer programs, even for quite small computers, are becoming
increasingly large and complex. Because they have to interact
with a wide
variety of configurations of hardware, large numbers of other programs and many
different versions of operating systems,
errors and unexpected behaviour can
occur. Debugging is a term applied to the process of identifying the source of
such errors and
rectifying them. The origins of the term go back to an era when
computers were large machines that consisted substantially of valves.
were attracted to the warmth of the valves and dead insects often came in
characteristics. Hence to
fix unexpected behaviour in such a machine, the operator had to remove all of
the dead bugs, ie, to debug
the machine. The term has since been generalised,
applying now to any source of unexpected behaviour. In its submission to the
Fujitsu suggested that a right to reverse engineer and decompile a
computer program is important (p. 17):
To ‘debug’ or solve problems in a program distributed in a
machine-readable format, an end-user or third-party maintenance
provider must be
able to disassemble portions of the program and examine the computer’s
memory values to find where the problem
10.70 The ways in which such bugs or errors
are rectified are many and
varied. Ideally the error is reported to the manufacturer, who often has
exclusive access to the source
code and hence is better equipped than most to
isolate and rectify the error. The source code is then appropriately modified
a number of consequential changes are included in the next release of the
object code.
10.71 Where the computer program is on a complex network or multi-user system
with many other interacting programs, the manufacturer
may not be able to
eliminate the error. Usually the users in such cases will have sufficient
expertise to isolate and correct the
errors themselves. The necessary action may
comprise any combination of direct modification of the source code, if the user
to it, direct modification of the object code or the creation of an
extra program or programs which are used in conjunction with
the original
program to avoid the situation which causes the behaviour. The modifications and
additional programs are often brought
together in a program which can be applied
to the original code to produce a modified version without the error. Such
programs are
appropriately labelled “patches”.
10.72 In most cases, application of the patches or the modification of source
code will amount to an adaptation or reproduction of
the program, which is an
exclusive right of the copyright owner. If these patches are supplied by the
owner, there will be an express
or implied licence to modify the program. Where
such patches are created by the user or supplied by a third party, some problems
arise. Licences which expressly grant a right to correct errors in programs are
usually limited to a small number of clients (usually
universities or research
institutions). Users of computer programs require them to operate on an ongoing
basis and where an error
is located, a solution is often required in a very
tight time frame. This means that it is often impractical to wait for the
manufacturer
to correct the error, either in their own patch or in a new
release, or to negotiate a
licence to modify the program at the time the
error becomes apparent.
10.73 Where there is an undertaking to maintain a system under a contractual
maintenance agreement, the maintenance provider may wish
to prevent the user
from modifying the code as such modifications may complicate and frustrate its
ability to ensure the system is
functional. Allowing users to modify code for
the purposes of error correction will not interfere with such agreements as they
and the Committee understands usually do, have explicit clauses excluding
liability of the manufacturer or third party maintenance
contractor if the
program is modified by the user. The rights and obligations of the praties will
depend upon the terms of the licence
or contract and the conduct of the user. It
may be that the user will lose rights because of a breach of contract.
10.74 Having regard to the functional nature of computer programs, the
Committee considers it essential that users should have the
right to correct
errors in programs where a corrected version of the program is unavailable. It
notes that in order to fix any errors
in a program it may be necessary for the
user to modify the source code or object code of the program. Such acts will
involve either
reproducing or adapting the computer program and, if not fully
within the terms of the licensed use of the program, will infringe
However, users of computer programs require them to operate on an ongoing basis.
Given the impracticality of fixing errors
in object code without making use of
decompilation, the Committee recommended in the Draft Report that reverse
engineering including
decompilation be permitted for the purposes of fixing
errors in computer programs.
Accordingly, the making by a lawful user of a computer
of a reproduction or adaptation of the program to restore its
intended functionality should not infringe copyright where a correctly
functioning version of the program is not available within a reasonable time at
a normal commercial price. The Committee notes that
what constitutes a
“reasonable time” and “normal commercial price” will
depend on the circumstances in each
case. The Committee further notes that this
recommendation, if implemented, would not prejudice any right that the purchaser
copy of the program might have against the supplier and/or producer for
damage suffered by the purchaser as a result of the error
(Recommendation
Decompilation for error correction
Consistently with the view expressed above the Committee
recommended in the Draft Report
that decompilation of a computer program
should be allowed where it is necessary to correct errors in the operation of
the program
provided (paragraph 10.72):
(a) decompilation is performed by the owner of a lawfully acquired copy of
the program or another person having a right to use the
copy or on their behalf
by a person authorised to do so;
(b) a version of the computer program free of the error has not previously
been readily available;
(c) the acts are confined to parts of the program necessary to correct the
(d) a correctly functioning version of the program is not available within a
reasonable time at a normal commercial price [and]
The following limitations should apply:
(i) the decompilation should only be used to achieve error correction;
(ii) the information obtained should only be given to others for the purpose
of correcting errors.
10.77 In relation to the making of “patches” the Committee
concluded that no special provisions were required in respect
of such programs.
However, insofar as it may be necessary to reproduce or adapt the source or
object code to ensure interoperability
of the patch program with the original
program the Committee recommended that the same limitations would apply as set
out in paragraph
10.72 of the Draft Report.
10.78 The Committee received seven submissions commenting on the draft
recommendation concerning decompilation for error correction.
Only the LCA
supported the Committee’s recommendation. It also suggested that the
exception should extend to “any reproduction
or adaptation of the program
necessary to correct the error.” In the submission received from the
United States Government
concern was expressed that the Committee’s
recommendation was too broad in that it may go too far in permitting the
dissemination
of the results of the decompilation to others for unrestricted
use. The Committee does not agree that this would be a consequence
of the draft
recommendation. It notes the relevant limitation in the recommendation is that
the information should only be “given
to others for the purpose of
correcting errors”. The US government further suggested that (p. 2):
Any information gained through reverse engineering/decompilation for the
purpose of error correction should be treated as the confidential
information of
the owner of the copyright in the program, and may not be released or otherwise
disseminated without the consent of
such owner.
The Committee does not accept that there is a justifiable need for a
limitation of the nature suggested by the US Government. Furthermore
it does not
consider it appropriate or consistent with the underlying principles of
in the manner suggested as to do
so would in effect provide defacto protection for ideas.
10.79 The CBEMA suggested that the Committee’s recommendation was
unnecessary on the grounds that it is highly unlikely that
the vendor of a
computer program would not take all steps necessary to correct the error, and
the developer of the product is in
a better place to cure flaws in the product
than any consumer. Similar comments were also made by IBM in its submission. In
regard the Committee notes that its recommendation was intended to operate
in those circumstances where the user of the program did
not have the time or
the supplier could not reasonably have been expected to be able to fix the bug
within the required time frame,
and the user has the necessary expertise. The
CBEMA also submitted that there should be a limitation on the dissemination of
products of the decompilation for use by others. The Committee notes that
the recommendation as worded in the Draft Report does not
contemplate permitting
any subsequent exploitation of either the corrected program or any information
obtained through the decompilation
10.80 IBM commented that the Committee’s recommendations regarding
decompilation in general (p. 9):
. . . unnecessarily increase the risks of piracy of the original program,
while providing users with benefits of questionable value.
In addition to its general concerns regarding piracy of software, IBM
submitted that any problems relating to the correction of errors
should be left
to the terms of the licence agreement between the supplier and the user. It also
cautioned that unauthorised attempts
to correct errors were likely to generate
their own problems.
10.81 In its submission Oracle stated that it would prefer existing copyright
principles to apply. However, it suggested that if a
limited right of
decompilation for error correction were to be permitted then the wording of the
Committee’s recommendation
should provide in sub-paragraph (b) that:
a version of the computer program free of the error has not been made
available within a reasonable time at a normal commercial price.
This in Oracle’s view would avoid what it saw as a suggestion in the
wording of the draft recommendation that the supplier had
an obligation to have
available a version of the program that did not include the error prior to the
date on which the error was
discovered by the user. Oracle also suggested that
the second limitation should be deleted as it implies that there is a right to
disclose programs to persons who are not lawful users of the particular copy of
software licensed. For the reasons given in paragraph
the Committee
does not agree with this suggestion.
10.82 SISA suggested that the Committee’s recommendation should be
expanded in scope to allow for error correction in a program
so that it will
function properly with a hardware device. The Committee can see no reason not to
allow decompilation for error correction
in these circumstances. Accordingly,
(and consistently with its recommendation concerning decompilation for
interoperability) it
recommends that decompilation for error correction be
allowed in these additional circumstances (see paragraph 10.84).
10.83 SISA noted that, as is the case where decompilation is undertaken for
interoperability, as a technical matter it would rarely
be the case that a
person trying to debug a program would be able to identify in advance the
relevant part of the programs object
code. Accordingly, SISA submitted that the
words “parts of the program” in the draft recommendation could be
thwart the intention of the recommendation. The Committee acknowledges
that in some cases this concern may be justified therefore,
it considers that
the draft recommendation should be amended to allow decompilation of those parts
of a computer program that are
reasonably necessary to identify and correct any
error (see paragraph 10.84).
10.84 The Committee has considered the submissions discussed above. It
recommends that decompilation for error correction be allowed
to ensure the
correct operation of a program with another program(s) or hardware device(s)
provided (Recommendation 2.27(b)):
(i) decompilation is performed by the owner of a lawfully acquired copy of
the program or another person having a right to use the
copy or on their behalf
by a person authorised to do so;
(ii) a version of the computer program free of the error has not previously
been readily available;
(iii) the acts are confined to those necessary to correct the error; and
(iv) a version of the program free of the error is not available within a
reasonable time at a normal commercial price.
The following limitations should apply:
(i) the decompilation should only be used to achieve error correction;
(ii) the information obtained should only be given to others for the purpose
of correcting errors.
10.85 The Committee also reaffirms that decompilation for error correction
should not be an infringement of copyright where an error
free version of the
program cannot be obtained within a reasonable time at a normal commercial price
(Recommendation 2.27(a)—see
previously paragraph 10.75). This would allow
the user of the program the opportunity to approach the copyright owner or his
agent to correct the error in accordance with any warranty agreement.
Where the manufacturer of the program is not given the opportunity
the error the onus should lie on the decompiler to show that the decompilation
was reasonably necessary to correct the
error and that it was not possible to
rely on the warranty in the time available to correct the error. Insofar as the
error correction
does not change the program, the user should be able to
continue to rely on his or her warranty.
Decompilation to understand techniques
10.86 Fujitsu’s submission to the Committee suggested that reverse
engineering and particularly decompilation of a computer
program might be needed
. . . simply to understand the ideas that underlie the current state of the
art and to improve upon them. Future generations of developers
must continue to
be able to stand on the shoulders of their predecessors.
10.87 As already noted (paragraph 10.27
above), s.40 of the Act allows
fair dealing with literary (and other) works for the purposes of research and
study and extends to
computer programs. In paragraph 10.29
the Committee
noted legal advice on what might be the scope of “research” as used
in s.40, which would appear to cover
reverse engineering and decompilation for
the purpose of, to use Fujitsu’s words, “simply understanding the
underlie the current state of the art.” In the Draft Report the
Committee commented that it saw
no need for an amendment to the Act to
address that concern. However, it also noted, research by a company for the
purpose of its
development aspirations, as distinct from that of general
community benefit, may not be within "research" as used in s.40.
10.88 The Committee received four submissions on this issue in response to
the Draft Report. Alcatel and the LCA both disagreed with
the Committee’s
recommendation that such activities remain governed by the fair dealing
provisions of the Act. Both submissions
commented that the fair dealing
provisions were too narrow in their scope as they arguably do not permit
decompilation to understand
techniques where that is done for a commercial
purpose. The LCA stated that the protection provided by copyright should not
the effect of restricting the development of knowledge (including the
understanding of the concepts and principles which underlie,
or are comprised in
a work). Given the nature of computer programs the LCA submitted that often a
general understanding of their
underlying principles will only be obtainable by
decompilation. In his submission Mr Muratore suggested that if decompilation
to be allowed then one option would be that it should (p. 9):
. . . be given upon [a] limited basis within the fair dealing provisions
thereby providing the copyright owner with the opportunity
of applying to the
Courts in appropriate cases.
10.89 Both the LCA and Mr Dan Hunter expressed the view that copyright law
should make the same concession in respect of understanding
techniques as is
made by s.23 of the CL Act for integrated circuits. Section 23 provides:
The EL rights in an eligible layout are not infringed:
(a) by making a copy or copies of the layout for the purpose of evaluating
the layout;
(b) by making an original circuit layout based on an evaluation or analysis
carried out with the use of a copy or copies referred
to in paragraph (a);
(c) by making an integrated circuit in accordance with an original circuit
layout referred to in paragraph (b); or
(d) by copying or commercially exploiting in Australia an original circuit
layout referred to in paragraph (b).”
The Committee has considered this provision of the CL Act
and decided
not to recommend the introduction of an analogous provision for computer
programs. Further it has decided that the wording
of its recommendation
regarding decompilation to understand techniques should state that the fair
dealing provisions would only apply
in respect of “non-commercial”
activities (Recommendation 2.28). In reaching this view the Committee has had
the inherently different nature of the subject-matter protected under
the CL Act. It does not accept that similar treatment of computer
programs is
appropriate in this case.
Decompilation to defeat program
10.90 Many products distributed for the personal computer market have some
form of protection to prevent copying of the program or,
, to prevent unauthorised use of the program. This may take the form of
a program lock (eg, a password) or a hardware lock (eg, the
AutoCAD lock). This
no doubt reflects the concern of the manufacturer that, while copying other than
in accordance with an express
or implied licence is an infringement, it is
impractical either to detect or to take infringement action in respect of such
Users often find these locks inconvenient and overcome them by
modifying the code to bypass the lock.
10.91 In the Draft Report the Committee said (paragraph 10.63):
Subject to the Committee’s recommendation concerning copying for
“normal use” (paragraph 10.10) and in the course
of debugging
(paragraph 10.58), the Committee is of the opinion that the modification of a
locked computer program for the purpose
of circumventing the lock should not be
permitted, nor should the subsequent copying of such modified computer programs.
Modification
of a computer program to defeat a copy protection lock will in most
cases involve either reproducing a substantial part of the original
making an adaptation of the program. These acts should continue to be prohibited
unless done with the licence of the copyright
owner. Any future amendments to
the Act should avoid eroding this protection for the copyright owner.
10.92 The Committee received five submissions on this issue. Alcatel
disagreed with the Committee’s recommendation on the ground
that (p. 5):
The ‘locking’ of programs is a form of protecting ideas and is
not within the scope of copyright. Any provision preventing
access to ideas
should be strictly limited to a period of less than five years.
The Committee accepts that the recommendation as worded in the Draft Report
would not permit decompilation for interoperability of
locked programs. The
submission from the CSIRO supported the Committee’s recommendation
provided “the locking is limited
to prevention of copying only”. The
Committee understands this to mean that it should be permissible to defeat locks
limited circumstances where a copy needs to be made for the purpose of
interoperability and error correction.
10.93 In its submission SISA, whose views were endorsed by the Computer and
Communications Industry Association (CCIA), commented
that any provision
relating to program locks should be carefully confined to ensure that such locks
could not be used to circumvent
exceptions to the rights of copyright owners. Mr
Muratore in his submission referred to the computer games market and noted that
some manufacturers included programs with their games that were recognised by
the game console. Unless a competing game contains
that program it will not be
recognised by the console and will not run. Mr Muratore commented that the
Committee’s recommendation
in the Draft Report would perpetuate this type
of monopoly by the computer game manufacturer. The Committee notes that an
allowing the decompilation of locked programs for the purpose of
interoperability would go some of the way to addressing this concern.
also submitted that copying or modification of a locked computer program should
be excepted from infringement where copying
is for the purposes of
interoperability, error correction or understanding techniques. The Committee
agrees with the thrust of these
10.94 Accordingly, the Committee now recommends, subject to the
Committee’s recommendations concerning copying for back-up copying,
interoperability, and error correction that the modification of a locked
computer program for the purpose of circumventing the lock
should be prohibited
as should subsequent copying of such modified programs (Recommendation 2.29).
Reproduction in the course of studying the
operation of a program: Article 5(3) of the EC Directive on Computer Programs
10.95 SISA submitted that so called “black box” reverse
engineering should be expressly provided for. The Committee understands
mean the study of the operation of a computer program without resorting to
decompilation of the object code. SISA claimed
that this is the most common form
of reverse engineering used in the computer industry. It further submitted that
techniques such
as line traces and memory dumps may involve a reproduction of
the program. Having regard to the
decision in the
Autodesk case
SISA commented that the need for such a provision was greater in Australia than
in the EU because the High Court in the
Autodesk case
held that the black
box reverse engineering engaged in by Mr Kelly was an infringing act.
Accordingly, it suggested the following
provision based on art.5(3) of the EC
Directive on Computer Programs be adopted.
A person having the right to use a computer program may, in the course of
that use and without the authorisation of the owner, observe,
study and test the
program to determine the underlying ideas and the other elements excluded from
protection pursuant to the definition
of computer program in s.10.
(The wording used in SISA’s definition assumes that the definition of
computer program is amended to expressly exclude “non-literal”
elements from protection.)
10.96 The Committee agrees that an exception of this nature is required. For
the reasons given in Chapter 6 the Committee has not
made any specific
recommendation in relation to the definition of a computer program expressly
excluding its behavioural aspects
from protection. However, the Committee has
argued in Chapter 9 that these non-literal elements are not presently protected
the Act. Accordingly, the Committee recommends that the Act be amended to
allow the reproduction and study of computer programs in
the circumstances
contemplated in art.5(3) of the EC Directive on Computer Programs
(Recommendation 2.30).
INTERNATIONAL DEVELOPMENTS
10.97 The issue of reverse engineering and decompilation was the topic of
intensive debate in the European Community for some time.
That debate was
concerned mainly with the issue of interoperability of computer products, in
particular the extent to which reverse
engineering should be permitted to
determine interface specifications. The EC Directive on Computer Programs as
drafted reflects
the outcome of that debate. ECIS, composed of systems
manufacturers including Unisys, Sun Microsystems, Amdahl, Bull, Olivetti and
Fujitsu, lobbied for the inclusion in the EC Directive of specific authority to
sanction reverse engineering of computer programs.
This was strongly opposed by
the Software Action Group for Europe (SAGE) which includes IBM, Digital,
Microsoft and Lotus.
10.98 The relevant article of the EC Directive on Computer Programs is art.6
(Decompilation). It provides that, in certain circumstances,
the exclusive
rights of the owner of copyright in a computer program are not infringed by a
licensee or other person entitled to
use the program who reproduces or adapts it
to obtain information necessary
to create or operate interoperable
programs. The conditions under which this may be done are:
(a) the acts are performed by the licensee or by another person having a
right to use a copy of the program;
(b) the information necessary to achieve interoperability has not been
readily available to the person;
(c) the acts are confined to the parts of the original program necessary to
achieve interoperability.
10.99 Further, the provision does not permit the information obtained:
(a) to be used otherwise than to achieve the interoperability of the
independently created computer program;
(b) to be given to others except where necessary to achieve interoperability;
(c) to be used for the development, production or marketing of a computer
program substantially similar in its expression or for any
other act which
infringes copyright.
The position in Japan and the United
10.100 From what it has read and heard at the various hearings and meetings
it conducted, the Committee understands that there appears
to be a general
consensus that reverse engineering of computer programs is carried out routinely
in the industry around the world.
Apart from the European Union, two of the most
important markets in the world for computer programs are the United States and
The Committee does not propose to analyse the various provisions of the
respective copyright laws in those two countries. However,
it notes that in
Japan the law, if literally interpreted, would prohibit the reproduction and
adaptation of computer programs for
the purpose of reverse engineering.
Similarly, in the United States reproduction and adaptation of
computer programs for the purpose of reverse engineering are prohibited
such acts come within the “fair use” exception under the US
. As already discussed (paragraph 10.28 above), the
sanctioning of reverse engineering as a fair use where it was undertaken to
interoperability has been endorsed in the decision of the US Court of
Appeals (9th Circuit)—the
The Court found that
Accolade’s disassembly of Sega’s computer code was a fair use
because it was the only way that Accolade
could gain access to the unprotected
aspects of Sega’s games, and that Accolade’s purpose was
[1993] USCA9 19
. . . simply to study the functional requirements for Genesis compatibility
so that it could modify existing games and make them useable
with the Genesis
The Committee also notes that care is needed in balancing any
rights given in respect of reverse engineering. Too narrow a right could
development and too broad a right could grant an unfair advantage to some
developers. A right to reproduce for reverse engineering
expressed in broad
terms, and not strictly circumscribed as in, eg, the EC Directive on Computer
Programs, could be open to abuse
and hamper legitimate efforts by copyright
owners to discourage wide scale own-use unauthorised reproduction.
REVERSE ENGINEERING: COMPATIBILITY WITH
10.103 With the conclusion of the GATT negotiations the Committee has also
considered how its recommendations concerning reverse engineering
decompilation sit with Australia’s obligations under TRIPS. Article 13 of
the TRIPS Agreement deals with limitations and
exceptions to exclusive rights.
It provides:
Members shall confine limitations or exceptions to exclusive rights to
certain special cases which do not conflict with a normal exploitation
work and do not unreasonably prejudice the legitimate interests of the right
The Committee considered whether reverse engineering including
decompilation in the circumstances and for the purposes discussed above
conflict with the “normal exploitation” of the
work. None of the
Committee’s recommendations authorise activities that enable others to
“free ride” on the work
of the copyright owner. For this reason, a
majority of the Committee is of the view that the position adopted in the EC
on Computer Programs, and the Committee’s own recommendations do
not conflict with the copyright owner’s normal exploitation
of his or her
10.104 The underlying argument in support of allowing reverse engineering is
that it encourages the free flow of non-protected ideas
and stimulates the
creation of new forms of expression of those ideas for the public benefit.
Sanctioning of reverse engineering
per se, as distinct from any unauthorised
commercial or own-use reproduction or adaptation resulting from that process,
make such infringing copying of programs any more difficult to prove.
Indeed if the maker of an allegedly infringing program acknowledged
reverse engineered a competitor’s program, it would make it impossible for
the maker to deny having had the opportunity
to copy if the court was disposed
to find that the reverse engineered program sufficiently resembled the
firstmentioned program to
amount to a copy. There are plenty of cases of actions
for infringement of traditional literary works where the defendant admittedly
based the alleged copy on a study of and the ideas in the plaintiff’s
work, and where the critical issue was whether the defendant’s
copied the plaintiff’s work or only the unprotectable ideas in that work.
So where a program was developed after reverse
engineering an earlier work,
proof of infringement of that earlier work would depend on determination that it
was copied by the later
work, not that the maker reverse engineered the earlier
work. Also if it is suggested that reverse engineering might be done secretly
for the purpose of making a disguised clone of the work, it is hard to see how
confirming reverse engineering to be an infringement
of copyright will prevent
such secret activity; indeed, to do so might serve only to encourage such
10.105 While reverse engineering to produce a clone program may involve
decompilation, reproduction of the “look and feel”
of a program can
also be achieved simply by consulting documentation or by running the program
and observing its behaviour. Whether
the replication in a program of the
“look and feel” of a pre-existing program amounts to reproduction of
the earlier
program is a contentious issue (see discussion in Chapter 9). To the
extent that the “look and feel” of a program is
unprotected, reverse engineering undertaken for the purpose of replicating such
features of a program cannot be simply
condemned as unacceptable.

Contracting out

of little practical effect if parties could rely on contractual provisions to

Computer Programs (Appendix G). Article 9(1) provides, in part, that:
Any contractual provisions contrary to Article 6 [
decompilation
the exceptions provided in Article 5(2) [
back-up copying)
observation and study
] shall be null and void.
The Committee recommends that the Act be amended to ensure that the
Committee’s recommendations concerning decompilation, back-up
copying, and
reproduction in the course of studying the operation of a program cannot be
avoided by contractual means (Recommendation
The onus of proof
10.107 In the Draft Report the Committee commented that
the suggested
additional exceptions to the copyright owner’s exclusive reproduction
right, discussed above, are of the same
nature as the fair dealing and other
exceptions in the Act, Part III, Division 3, headed “Acts not constituting
infringements
of copyright in works”, and should be provided for in that
Division. As such, the onus of showing that one of those proposed
applied would lie on the person invoking it (
McGraw–Hill Book Co (UK) Ltd
[1983] FSR 545
at 558, quoted in
Garis and Another
Neville Jeffress Pidler Pty Ltd
(1990) 37 FCR 99
18 IPR 292).
10.108 The Committee indicated that it
believed it appropriate that a
person who copies another’s program without a licence and who seeks to
justify it under an exception
requiring the copier to have had a certain purpose
in copying should have the onus of satisfying the court that he or she had that
purpose. In their submissions responding to the Draft Report, Oracle, the LCA
and the ACC all agreed with the Committee’s recommendation.
Accordingly
the Committee affirms that recommendation. By way of further qualification the
Committee also believes that a person
should have to show that it was not
possible to rely on any warranty in the time available or required to fix the
software (Recommendation
B. NATURE OF THE USER
CROWN USE OF COMPUTER PROGRAMS
10.109 Part VII of the Act deals generally with copyright and the Crown. It
contains provisions dealing with, among other things,
the use of literary works
by the Commonwealth and the States. Accordingly, the provisions of Part VII
apply to computer programs.
The provision of Part VII dealing with Crown use of
non-Crown copyright materials is s.183. Like the other provisions of Part VII,
s.183 applies to the Commonwealth, the States and the Territories. Section 183
of the Act provides a statutory licence for the Crown
to do acts in relation to
a range of copyright materials. This means that the Crown can do acts which
would otherwise infringe copyright
(such as copying) in relation to those
materials without infringing copyright. Section 7 of the Act further provides
. . . nothing in this Act renders the Crown liable to be prosecuted for an
10.110 The most important features of s.183 may be summarised as follows:
where the Crown does an act comprised in the copyright of
another person, or authorises the doing of such an act, and the act is
“for the services of” the Crown, it will not infringe that copyright
(s.183(1));
where the act is done, the Crown must as soon as possible
(unless contrary to the public interest) inform the owner of the copyright,
prescribed, of the doing of the act and furnish the owner with information which
the owner may reasonably require (s.183(4)) (It
is implicit in this subsection
that the Crown may carry out this obligation before or after the doing of the
the terms for the doing of the act are, whether before or
after the act is done, to be agreed between the Crown and the copyright
or, in default of agreement, fixed by the Copyright Tribunal (s.183(5)).
10.111 Prior to the Draft Report the Committee received submissions from
Lotus and Microsoft concerning the operation of s.183. In
its submission Lotus
argued that there was no reason why the Crown should be entitled to infringe
of an amount determined by the Copyright
Tribunal. It submitted that s.183 derived ultimately from provisions of 19th
century patents
legislation where the justification then was that the Crown
should not be hampered in the performance of its duties by the existence
intellectual property rights. Lotus claimed this justification can no longer be
sustained in view of the greatly expanded functions
of government in the present
10.112 Lotus also submitted that the
(UK) no longer contains a provision relating to the Crown use of
there should
be a substantial reduction in its scope. In this regard the Committee notes that
s.48 of the UK Act deals with material
communicated to the Crown. It applies to
literary, dramatic, musical and artistic works. Where that material is
communicated to the
Crown in the course of public business and with the licence
of the copyright owner, the Crown may under the following conditions
issue copies to the public. The conditions are:
(a) the copying or publication must be for the purpose for which the work was
communicated to the Crown or any related purpose which
could reasonably have
been anticipated by the copyright owner;
(b) the work may not be one which has previously been published except by
reason of s.48.
In its submission Lotus noted that the Crown use provisions in s.240 of
are limited in application to the Crown use of designs.
Those provisions restrict the Crown use of designs to defence purposes (defence
of the realm and foreign defence) and health service purposes.
10.113 Microsoft’s submission sought to distinguish computer programs
from other works on the grounds that they may be continually
copied or recopied
with each copy being as good as the original. Like Lotus, Microsoft expressed
concern at the potential for large
scale copying of computer programs by Crown
bodies. Microsoft noted that it had never received a notice under s.183 of the
suggested that this meant it was not required for the normal business
operation of Crown bodies. Lotus indicated it understood that
Departments had a policy not to use or rely on s.183 with respect to computer
programs. However, both Lotus and Microsoft
submitted that they had evidence of
unauthorised copying of computer programs by various government agencies.
10.114 Microsoft raised a number of other concerns in relation to the
operation of s.183 which can be summarised as follows:
s.183 limits the capacity of copyright owners to take legal
action against the Crown; injunctions are not available, redress must
before the Copyright Tribunal;
the Copyright Tribunal cannot award damages or costs (The
Committee notes s.174 of the Act provides that the Tribunal can order
the meaning of “the Commonwealth” and “a
State” is unclear. Accordingly, it is difficult for copyright owners
determine which bodies are amenable to civil actions for infringement and those
which are not;
s.183 conflicts with the “normal exploitation of the
work” and unreasonably prejudices the legitimate interests of the
Accordingly, s.183 is contrary to Article 9 of the Berne Convention.
Microsoft also noted that support for an amendment of s.183 could be gained
from noting that s.183(11) excludes from the application
of the section the
copying of works for educational purposes by Commonwealth and State educational
institutions, and that s. 135ZE
provides that nothing in Part VB of the Act
(copying of works under statutory licence by educational and other institutions)
in relation to a literary work being a computer program or a compilation
of computer programs. Accordingly, Microsoft submitted that
s.183 should be
amended by the addition of a subclause to subsection 12 worded as follows (1991,
nothing in this section applies in relation to a literary work, being a
computer program or a compilation of computer programs.
10.115 The Committee has considered the submissions by Lotus and Microsoft.
It does not accept Microsoft’s submission that computer
programs are
distinguishable from other works on the grounds that they may be continually
copied or recopied with each copy being
as good as the original. In this regard
it notes that digital storage of musical, artistic and other literary works is
quite commonplace.
All forms of digital storage lend themselves to high quality
copying. Further the Committee is not persuaded that much significance
attached to the fact that computer programs are usually digital. It notes that
high quality analogue copying of musical,
literary and artistic works is
available both commercially and domestically. Almost endless high quality
analogue copies can also
be made from a single analogue or digital original.
10.116 The Crown is an extremely large user of copyright materials. The
extensive range of functions discharged by various Government
departments at the
State and Federal levels means that there is a wide scope for the Crown to copy
a variety of materials in the
performance of these functions. From a
consideration of the provisions of s.183 it is apparent to the Committee that
the scheme established
the section, on the one hand, recognises the
needs of the Crown to make extensive use of copyright materials but, on the
other hand,
also seeks to strike a balance by ensuring that the exception does
not lead to abuse. This is evidenced by the notice requirements
some difficulties may be faced by copyright
owners under s.183 in determining
whether or not a body or organisation is the Crown. These problems are not faced
by the owners
of copyright in computer programs alone. They apply in relation to
all works and subject matter.
10.117 The Committee notes the allegations by both Microsoft and Lotus that
there appears to be some copying of computer programs
by the Crown and that, as
far as those two copyright owners are concerned, there is little if any
reporting of that copying in compliance
with the requirements of s.183. The
Committee understands that, at the federal level, the Attorney-General’s
Department has
adopted a policy of not copying programs and has advised this
approach to other departments that have consulted it. From the submissions
not possible for the Committee to gauge the size or seriousness for the owners
of copyright in computer programs of government
copying that has not been
notified as required. However, the Committee accepts that copyright owners have
a real basis for concern
if it can be shown that Government departments and
other bodies that are “the Commonwealth” and “a
are abusing the statutory licence provided under s.183. In
the Draft Report the Committee noted the finding of the Victorian
Auditor-General
that one in four software products in use in the Victorian
public sector were unlicensed.
10.118 In the case of, for instance, readily available packaged computer
programs, the substantial function for which they would be
put to normal use and
the substantial cost (compared with, say, newspapers, magazines and books) would
suggest that governments would
not have the same case for having the present
right under s.183 as they might in the case of newspapers, magazines and
Against this the Committee accepts that there may be circumstances
where the government should be able to copy computer programs.
In the Draft Report
the Committee recommended that s.183
be amended to provide that copying for the services of the Commonwealth and
States of computer
programs should be restricted to those cases where a copy of
the program cannot be obtained within a reasonable time at an ordinary
commercial price. In making this recommendation the Committee acknowledged that
the demands of government are such that the
proposed curtailment of the
operation of s.183 in relation to computer programs should not be presumed to be
adaptable to other works
or other subject matter without consideration of all
relevant circumstances.
10.120 In response to the Draft Report the Committee received five
submissions concerning Crown use of computer programs and the electronic
of works by the crown. Both the ACC and Copyright Agency Limited urged the
Committee to address the question of the entry
of works onto data bases by
government departments. As this issue relates to that aspect of the
Committee’s terms of reference
concerning the storage of works in computer
memory these submissions are dealt with in Chapter 14 of the report.
10.121 The LCA, ACC, AIIA, Oracle and De Data all supported the
Committee’s recommendation in the Draft Report. In its comments
suggested that the wording adopted by the Committee should be made “less
vague”. The Committee understands this
comment to be a reference to the
words “reasonable time” and “ordinary commercial price”.
The reason those
words were chosen is that they are used elsewhere in the Act,
in particular in the fair dealing provisions (see s.40). The Committee
aware that the use of those words in that context has given rise to any
particular difficulties in the application of those
provisions. In light of this
the Committee is disinclined to suggest a different test. De Data commented that
in addition to the
Committee’s recommendation, s.183 (p. 6):
. . . should be further amended to include, at least in the case of time
being a factor, that the copyright owner will be granted
appropriate
compensation before or after the act of copying the computer program(s).
The Committee notes here that s.183(5) provides a mechanism whereby copyright
owners are assured of receiving compensation.
10.122 The final additional comment the Committee received was from Oracle
suggesting that the Committee’s recommendation should
be further tightened
by requiring that notification be given within thirty days if the Crown sought
to rely on the statutory licence.
The Committee understands that the
notification provisions of s.183 are currently being reviewed. For this reason
it does not amend
its earlier recommendation in respect of these proposals (see
paragraph 10.119) (Recommendation 2.33).
10.123 The Committee did not receive a large number of submissions on this
particular issue (no submissions were received commenting
Committee’s draft recommendation). Those that it did receive were uniform
in their opposition to the introduction of
a scheme (analogous to the Blank Tape
Royalty Scheme in the now invalid Part VC of the Act) to impose a levy upon
floppy discs as
a means of compensating computer program manufacturers for home
copying of their programs. Perhaps surprisingly, this view was endorsed
by computer program manufacturers but also the Australian Federation of
Consumers Organisations and the PC Users Group.
10.124 The main reasons why such a scheme was regarded by these interests as
inappropriate were:
(a) the value of the levy would in no way represent the value of program
copied. (Unlike sound recordings the prices of which are
very similar, computer
program packages can vary in price from tens to thousands of dollars);
(b) the purchase of programs by organisations would not necessarily give an
accurate indication of the amount of copying of particular
(c) computer programs, unlike sound recordings, often require the provision
of support from suppliers. This needs to be financed through
(d) allowing copying for the payment of a fee would further encourage copying
and promote a view within the community that copying
was acceptable when in fact
it equates to stealing the intellectual property; and
(e) collecting societies are a costly and unsatisfactory mechanism for
recouping income lost to copying.
10.125 In the Draft Report the Committee noted that voluntary licensing of
copying of computer programs by individual copyright owners
or their licensees
may be a practical option
In contrast with other copyright works or
subject matter such as sound recordings
there is a relatively smaller
number of makers of computer programs and usually only one copyright to deal
with (compared with sound
recordings, for which there are copyrights in not only
the recording but also both the music and the words to consider as well as
enormous range of composers). The Committee also acknowledged arguments that
computer programs can be distinguished from sound
recordings on the basis of
price and the need for support service. Having regard to this and the fact that
both consumer organisations
and computer program manufacturers oppose the
introduction of such a levy or royalty scheme, the Committee reaffirms its draft
recommendation
that no change be made to the Act in respect of unauthorised home
copying of computer program
(Recommendation 2.34).
C. NATURE OF THE SUBJECT MATTER
SHAREWARE AND PUBLIC DOMAIN COMPUTER
10.126 At the public hearings and in a submission received following the
hearings, the concepts of “public domain” computer
programs and
“shareware” were discussed. From the submissions, the Committee
understands that those concepts are as follows:
(a) Public domain computer programs—programs which the copyright owner
has granted the world at large a licence to reproduce.
While copyright subsists
in such programs the copyright owner is, however, precluded from claiming
damages from anyone who reproduces
the program.
(b) Shareware—programs which have been made available for users to
reproduce and use free of charge subject to certain conditions.
conditions that apply to shareware are that it is freely transferable to other
users provided that after a specified period
the user(s) cease to use it or make
a payment to the copyright owner.
10.127 Discussion of these two terms at the public hearings indicated that
there was some confusion as to their meaning. There was
also concern expressed
by some users that in some cases it was not clear whether a program was in the
public domain. In the Draft
Report the Committee expressed
the view that
although there may be some public uncertainty as to the meaning of the terms and
into which category a particular program
may fall, these considerations alone
are not sufficient to necessitate legislative clarification of the terms. In the
any further submissions on this issue the Committee reaffirms its
recommendation in the Draft Report that no amendments be made to
define these terms (Recommendation 2.35).
OVERLAP OF THE COPYRIGHT ACT 1968 AND THE CIRCUIT
LAYOUTS ACT 1989
10.128 In the Draft Report the Committee referred to submissions it had
from Apple, Avel Pty Ltd (Avel) and Microsoft concerning the
operation of s.24(2) of the CL Act, which provides for the protection
intellectual property in the designs of integrated circuits, ie, computer chips.
Section 24 of the CL Act
concerns the commercial exploitation of original
designs for integrated circuits, called in the CL
Act “eligible
layouts”. Commercial exploitation is defined in s.8 of that Act to include
sale and other distribution by
way of trade and the importation for various
commercial purposes. In effect, if an eligible layout is commercially exploited
in Australia
or elsewhere by, or with the permission of, the owner of
intellectual property in the layout, s.24 provides that it is not an
infringement
of those rights if a person acquires a copy of the layout, or an
integrated circuit made from the layout, and commercially exploits
Australia. As can be seen, that would include importing the layout, or chips
lawfully made according to the layout, into Australia
10.129 Section 24(2) of the CL Act
In spite of
that Act to the extent that section 38 applies to imported articles, where the
commercial exploitation of an integrated circuit
containing a copy or adaptation
of a work (being an integrated circuit made in accordance with an eligible
layout) is not, under
this section, an infringement of the EL rights in the
layout, that commercial exploitation is not an infringement of the copyright
that work unless the making of that copy or adaptation was an infringement of
that copyright.
In its submission Microsoft noted that the explanatory memorandum and the
placement of this provision indicated that the intent of
the sub-section was to
prevent the owner of EL rights in a layout from including a trivial computer
program in an integrated circuit
made according to the layout and thus take
advantage of the
to prevent parallel importation of the
integrated circuit.
10.130 The Committee understands that the CL Act
was enacted to ensure
that Australian circuit layouts would be protected in the US under the US
Semiconductor Chip Protection Act 1984
(US SCP Act) and to enable
Australia to become a party to any future multilateral convention for the
protection of circuit layouts.
While, as noted by the Committee in Chapter 3
(paragraph 3.18), the Washington Treaty has not yet entered into force, s.24 of
nevertheless gives effect to art.6.5 of the Treaty, which
deals with the exhaustion of rights. Article 6.5 provides:
(5) [Exhaustion of Rights] Notwithstanding paragraph (1)(a)(ii), any
Contracting Party may consider lawful the performance, without
the authorisation
of the holder of the right, of any of the acts referred to in that paragraph
where the act is performed in respect
of a protected layout-design (topography)
or in respect of an integrated circuit in which such a layout design
(topography) is incorporated,
that has been put on the market by, or with the
consent of, the holder of the right.
Paragraph (1)(a) provides:
(1) [Acts Requiring the Authorisation of the Holder of the Right] (a) Any
Contracting Party shall consider unlawful the following
acts if performed
without the authorisation of the holder of the right:
(i) the act of reproducing, whether by incorporation in an integrated circuit
or otherwise, a protected layout-design (topography)
in its entirety or any part
thereof, except the act of reproducing any part that does not comply with the
requirement of originality
referred to in Article 3(2),
(ii) the act of importing, selling or otherwise distributing for commercial
purposes a protected layout-design (topography) or an
integrated circuit in
which a protected layout-design (topography) is incorporated.
10.131 A similar provision to art.6.5 is found in the US SCP Act. Section
906(b) of the US SCP Act provides:
Notwithstanding the provisions of section 905(2), [Exclusive rights in mask
works] the owner of a particular semiconductor chip product
made by the owner of
the mask work or by any person authorised by the owner of the mask work, may
import, distribute, or otherwise
dispose of or use, but not reproduce, the
particular semiconductor chip product without the authority of the owner of the
In the explanatory memorandum for the US SCP Act the paragraph dealing with
s.906(b) provides in part:
. . . section 906(b) . . . relates to application of the first sale doctrine
to mask works. It is made clear that a customer is free
to use a semiconductor
chip product unit as he chooses, after becoming its owner or buying it from the
mask work owner or its licensee.
However, the customer’s permissible use
does not include reproducing the semiconductor chip . . .
10.132 The Explanatory Memorandum for the CL Act
provides, in relation
to s.24(2) (p. 18, paragraph 51):
The intended effect of sub-clause (2) is that sub-clause (1) is not defeated
by the application of
sections 37
imported copies or adaptations of a work, made with the authority of the
10.133 When considering the issues raised by Apple, Avel and Microsoft, the
Committee noted that the US SCP Act does not have an equivalent
provision to
s.24(2). When preparing the Draft Report the Committee sought clarification of
the US position, in particular whether
any problems had arisen under the US SCP
Act in relation to the importation of semi-conductor chips, through the

Department’s Counsellor (Legal) in Washington. In

the US Customs Service, were not aware of any particular problems with the

where an attempt had been
made to prevent the importation of a computer product containing an integrated
circuit. The Committee notes
that the US SCP Act is expressed not to derogate
from the protection available under the US
Accordingly, it appears that “back door” copyright protection for
circuit layouts may be possible in the US by the inclusion
of copyright works in
integrated circuits.
10.134 The Committee is of the view that, having regard to art.6.5 of the
Washington Convention, s.906(b) of the US SCP Act and s.24(1)
of the CL Act, it
is clear that the policy underlying these provisions is that the owner of the
rights in a circuit layout should
not enjoy the right to control the parallel
importation of circuit layouts once they have been legitimately placed on the
It is also clear from the explanatory memorandum to the CL Act
that the purpose of s.24(2) is to ensure that the owners of rights in
circuit layouts are not able to achieve copyright protection
by the inclusion of
10.135 Apple, Avel and Microsoft all expressed concern that the effect of
s.24(2) is to deny parallel importation protection under
ss.37 and 38 of the Act
to all works stored in ROM. The submission from Avel noted that, in the case of
products sold by it, namely
printed circuit boards for video games, the programs
which operate the games are all encoded in integrated circuits. Two types of
integrated circuits are commonly used, the first type are EPROMs which are
purchased “off the shelf” and the programs
are loaded directly by
the games manufacturer (who also incorporates the programmed EPROM into the
printed circuit board). The second
Mask ROM. These are specially designed integrated circuits, where the computer
is incorporated by the circuit
manufacturer. Avel noted that
s.24(2) applied to both of these cases. It submitted that simply because the
author of a computer program
chose to store the program in an EPROM, he or she
should not lose copyright protection. Avel also noted that if the same program
were stored on a floppy disc, it would not attract the operation of s.24(2) and,
accordingly, the copyright owner would still be
able to exercise his or her
rights to control the importation of the program under the
In other words the effect of s.24(2) is that it discriminates against programs
embodied in integrated circuits.

sold by Avel was considered by the Full Court of the Federal

10.138 The Committee stated in the Draft Report that it believed that the use
of copyright in the packaging and labelling of non-copyright

appropriate analogy. The packaging and labelling of non-copyright articles was
considered in the Committee’s
1988 report on
The Importation Provisions
(pp. 223–226). The Committee there

and labelling

10.139 In the Draft Report the Committee stated that in its view the intended
effect of s.24(2) of the CL Act was clearly to prevent
a similar control over
importation of integrated circuits by storage on them of copyright computer
programs or other articles. The
Committee concluded that the policy behind the
drafting of s.24(2) could be stated as follows (paragraph 10.100):
Circuit layouts and integrated circuits are non-copyright utilitarian
articles. The nature of the intellectual property rights in
such articles does
not include the right to control the importation of legitimate copies of the
article. Furthermore the rights owner
should be prevented from obtaining
them. The Committee
is not persuaded that the apparent policy behind s.24(2)
should be altered.
Accordingly, the Committee recommended that no change
should be made
to s.24(2) of the
CL Act (paragraph 10.100).
10.140 The Committee received five submissions concerning this
recommendation. Only the submission from the CSIRO unreservedly supported
position adopted by the Committee. In a paper forwarded to the Committee by Mr
Yves Hazan, of Michell Sillar Australian and International
Attorneys, Mr Hazan
commented that the Committee’s recommendation had implications for the use
of copyright works in multimedia
products many of which would be stored on
integrated circuits. In its submission Oracle made a similar comment and
suggested that
it should be possible to maintain the purpose of s.24(2) while
not affecting the rights in works stored in integrated circuits. The
submissions
from Mr Hazan and Avel also commented that the scope of copyright protection for
works should not in this case depend
upon the nature of the media in which they
are stored. Mr Hazan cautioned that such an approach had the potential to (p.
. . . result in suppliers of technology not providing the best possible or
most desirable technology for a particular solution in
order to ensure
protection of their copyright.
10.141 The final submission on this issue came in a paper delivered by Mr Dan
Hunter of the Centre for International Research on Communication
and Information
Technologies (CIRCIT). In his paper Mr Hunter was critical of the Committee for
describing integrated circuits as
“utilitarian and somehow different from
between integrated circuits and copyright materials is recognised
internationally in the TRIPS Agreement and by the Berne and Washington
The distinction put simply is that the CL Act protects the designs for
integrated circuits and their embodiment in a chip
while copyright protects
works which may be stored in integrated circuits. The Committee acknowledges
that both may perform the same
function. However, it is not the function of the
computer program or the circuit layout that is the object of the protection. The
fact that these two distinct subject matters can be embodied in the one physical
object is precisely what gives rise to the difficulties
under s.24(2).
10.142 The Committee has considered the comments made in these submissions.
It agrees with the comment by Oracle that it should be
possible to give effect
to the intention of s.24(2) while not affecting the rights in works stored in
integrated circuits. Accordingly,
the Committee now recommends that where the
purpose of importing an integrated circuit is to import a copyright work
embodied in
that integrated circuit the owner of copyright in the embodied work
should be able to control that importation (ie the
apply) (Recommendation 2.36(a)). Where the purpose of the importation is to
import an article that is not the copyright work
then the CL Act should apply
(Recommendation 2.36(b)). In other words, where the work embodied in a chip is
the only or principal
reason of the importation, s.24(2) of the CL Act should
10.143 Apple and Microsoft also expressed concern at the operation of s.24(3)

Expressions used in subsection (2) that are used and defined in the
have the same respective meanings in that subsection

In their view,

means copyright

cannot be an infringement of copyright
under the Australian
and therefore
does not apply to works made outside Australia. To avoid

should be amended by the addition of the words “not being a computer
program” after the word “work” where
first appearing.

. . . any act
comprised in the copyright (emphasis added).
If, as a result, infringement of copyright in
was confined to
infringements in Australia—and the Committee adopts no concluded view on
the issue—the result could be
that the owner of copyright in a program
which, in another country, was copied without that owner’s consent onto
circuits would be powerless to prevent those unauthorised copies
being brought into Australia, if the integrated circuits could be
This would be undesirable, and the Committee recommends that the
concluding words of
