replaced with words to the effect that the making was done without the consent
the copyright owner in the place of manufacture (Recommendation 2.37). The
Committee therefore does not agree with the suggestion
of Apple simply to

Committee believes that this amendment would substantially defeat the purpose of
including the provision in the CL
Act. The Committee did not receive any

Parallel Importation
INTRODUCTION
11.01 In the Draft Report the Committee indicated that it was split fairly
evenly on the issue of whether to allow the parallel importation
of computer
programs, computer manuals and second-hand computer programs. The Draft Report
referred to a “bare” majority
being in favour of lifting the current
restriction on parallel importation. The members of the Committee who formed the
recommended that the parallel importation of computer programs,
computer manuals, whether or not sold together with computer programs,
second-hand computer programs should be allowed from those countries which
represent the main source of such goods currently
used in Australia (Draft
recommendations 2.31 and 2.33). A minority of the Committee remained opposed to
permitting the parallel
importation of computer programs.
11.02 The Committee has moved from the position it took in the Draft Report.
The Committee’s reasons and major recommendations
regarding parallel
importation are set out in paragraphs 11.93–11.96 following the discussion
below of the issues and submissions
it received.
TERMINOLOGY
11.03 In the Draft Report, the majority of the Committee in making its
recommendation regarding parallel importation referred to “computer
On the other hand, in the PSA’s final report its
recommendations were made in relation to “computer software”
Neither of these terms were defined or their use clarified either by the
Committee or the PSA. The implication of the Committee’s
use of the term
“computer program” is that if only “computer programs”
may be imported, the copyright owner
may nevertheless retain control over
parallel importation in relation to any other copyright material stored with the
program. Similarly,
in the Committee’s view, the use of the term
“computer software” leads to some uncertainty if undefined. It is
not clear, for example, whether the PSA intended “software” to mean
computer programs and ancillary data stored on the
same device, or whether the
PSA intended “software” to have a wider meaning (for example, to
include things such as databases
on CD–ROM, where the computer program is
arguably ancillary to the data.
11.04 The Committee has assumed, for the purposes of the discussion in this
chapter, that references to “software” in
the PSA report, responses
to it, and submissions to the Committee, are intended to refer to computer
programs and ancillary data
stored on the same carrier. The use of the term
“computer program” by the Committee should also be understood as
to a computer program and ancillary data but not including associated
1988—REPORT ON THE IMPORTATION PROVISIONS OF
THE COPYRIGHT ACT 1968
11.05 The Committee considered and reported on the importation provisions of
the Act in September 1988 as they affected a range of
industries, including the
book publishing, sound and record and computer software industries (
Importation Provisions of the
, AGPS, Canberra, 1988)
(1988 Report). The matters then considered by the Committee regarding computer
programs were the:
• possible repeal of ss.37 and 38 of the Act to meet concerns regarding
the price and availability of computer programs;
• extension of the scope of s.135 of the Act, that provides for customs
seizure of infringing imported (ie, unlicensed) copies
of print materials, to
include computer programs;
•possible decriminalisation of parallel importation; and
• the importation of non-copyright articles containing computer
Recommendations made in the 1988 Report
11.06 In its 1988 Report the Committee recommended that the parallel
importation provisions should continue to apply in relation to
computer software
and other copyright materials but that their application to such imports should
be relaxed in a number of respects
(paragraphs 2 and 198). In particular, the
Committee recommended that unlicensed importation by a person engaged in trade
or commerce
should be allowed either where a specific order for an article is
made by a customer or where the article sought to be imported is
unavailable in
Australia for longer than a reasonable period (see paragraphs 3(1) and 3(2)).The
Committee’s 1988 recommendations
in relation to the issue of availability
of copyright materials are referred to again in paragraphs 11.64 and 11.65.
11.07 The Committee also recommended that s.135 of the Act, which provides a
mechanism for copyright owners to restrict the importation
of printed copies of
works, should be amended to cover all works and subject matter other than works,
including computer programs
and to apply to exclusive licensees of copyright
material as well as to owners thereof (1988 Report—paragraph 3(6)). The
implementation
of this recommendation in the
Amendments) Bill 1994
is discussed later at paragraphs 11.89 and 11.90.
11.08 Further recommendations of the Committee concerned the
decriminalisation of parallel importation and the knowledge requirements
parallel importation provisions. First, in relation to the issue of
decriminalisation the Committee considered that a person
who imported a
non-pirated article ought not to be subject to criminal proceedings and
sanctions, provided that the onus, in civil
proceedings, was placed on the
importer to show that the imported articles were not pirate copies (1988
Report—paragraphs 3(5)
and 200). The Committee’s current view in
relation to this issue in the light of its recommendation in this report is
at paragraph 11.13. Second, in relation to the knowledge requirement
in the importation provisions the Committee recommended that
ss. 37, 38, 102 and
103 be amended to bring them into line with s.132 which only requires a
“knew or ought reasonably to
have known” that the imported article would have constituted an
infringement of copyright
if made by him/her in Australia (in their existing
form the parallel importation provisions required proof of actual knowledge on
the part of the importer). This recommendation was implemented by the
. In 1992 the Government introduced the
which proposed that the knowledge requirement be
removed altogether from ss. 37 and 102 of the Act. However, with the calling of
the March 1993 Federal election this bill lapsed and the Committee notes the
Bill has not yet been reintroduced.
11.09 Finally, the Committee noted in its 1988 Report that there was a
potential for abuse of the importation provisions of the Act,
by allowing
to be used to control importation of
those articles. However, it did not make a
specific recommendation on this issue. This issue is discussed in Chapter 10
(paragraphs
10.128–10.142).

DECRIMINALISATION OF PARALLEL IMPORTATION
11.13 As mentioned in paragraph 11.08 the Committee recommended in its 1988
Report that parallel importation should be decriminalised
provided that the
onus, in civil proceedings, was placed on the importer to show that the imported
articles were not pirate copies.
The Committee did not receive any further
comments on this recommendation in the submissions it received concerning this
The Committee’s 1988 recommendation was made in the context of
a limited right to allow parallel importation to meet specific
orders where a
product was not available. However, as the Committee is of the view that the
parallel importation provisions, as they
apply to computer programs should be
maintained and that availability of computer programs is not a pressing problem,
the Committee
recommends that criminal sanctions in respect of parallel
importation should remain. (Recommendation 2.38).

CONSIDERATION OF ISSUES
11.17 The benefits and possible adverse effects of the provisions which allow
owners of copyright to control parallel importation
have been the subject of
wide debate in Australia and in international arenas such as the World
Intellectual Property Organisation
(WIPO) and as part of the General Agreement
on Tariffs and Trade (GATT) negotiations. Despite the high level of debate, the
of whether to allow parallel importation remains contentious, largely
because the economic effects of allowing or restricting parallel
importation are
difficult, if not impossible, to measure. Those in favour of allowing parallel
importation point to other areas in
which the Government has recently
implemented policies designed to increase competition, such as telephone
services and air travel.
In the Committee’s view, it is questionable
whether these comparisons provide useful assistance. Set out below is a
of the reasons for the Committee’s view that parallel
importation in relation to computer programs should not be allowed
PURPOSE OF PARALLEL IMPORT PROVISIONS
11.18 Before considering the arguments in favour of restricting parallel
importation, the Committee thinks it is useful to acknowledge
the fundamental
purpose of parallel importation provisions. In the Committee’s opinion the
right to control parallel importation
of copyright materials serves the same
purpose as copyright laws in general—to encourage and reward investment
and creative
endeavour. The parallel importation provisions recognise that
result of various international treaties, vary from territory to territory. They
also recognise, as pointed out by the Law Council
of Australia (LCA), that there
is not a “single world market”. The parallel importation provisions
provide an incentive
for copyright owners in one territory to market (and
exclusively license others to market) their copyright, and articles
incorporating
their copyright, in other territories. This incentive is negated
if others may import those articles from other territories without
incurring the
marketing and other costs incurred by the owner of copyright.
11.19 As pointed out by the LCA in its submission, the exercise of copyright
rights necessarily has an effect on prices and short
term availability of
articles to the extent that it allows copyright owners to recoup their
investment and prevent others from free-riding
on that investment. The LCA
refers to the following comment by Professor Cornish in his text I
ntellectual
Property: Patents, Copyright, Trade Marks and Allied Rights
(2nd. ed, Sweet
and Maxwell, London, 1989, p.259, paragraph 9-024) which explains the impact of
the market:
That such practices [such as publishing hardback editions before paperbacks,
or releasing a film for cinema exhibition before showing
it on television]
should follow from the conferring of copyright is not of itself a ground for
criticism. The very purpose of the
protection is to allow recoupment for the
initiative of creating the material and the investment risked in producing and
it. In most instances a copyist could produce a directly competing
product at a much lower cost if copyright did not restrain him.
11.20 In the Committee’s opinion, it is essential to bear in mind the
underlying purpose and effect of parallel importation
controls (and copyright
generally) in considering the local Australian market and its operation and
impact on consumers. It should
also be remembered that the parallel importation
provisions complement the general bundle of rights which copyright owners are
and, as pointed out by the Australian Copyright Council (ACC), parallel
importation controls are intended to “ensure the effective
of the rights of copyright owners. The Committee is of the opinion that parallel
importation provisions must be maintained
if copyright owners are to have
effective control over their copyright materials.
ARGUMENTS CONCERNING PRICE AND COMPETITION
11.21 The principal criticism made about control of parallel importation of
computer programs is that such controls allegedly allow
unfairly, to charge higher prices in Australia than in other territories
(principally the United States). In a
broader sense, it is also argued that,
irrespective of demonstrated price differentials, the provisions are
anti-competitive, and
that this has an adverse effect in the long term on
employment and the economy generally. These criticisms are discussed in turn
below. In preparing the final report the Committee has had the opportunity to
consider papers prepared by the Department of Industry
Technology and Regional
Development (DITARD) and Treasury which commented on the Prices Surveillance
Authority’s (PSA) report
into prices of computer software and which were
made available to the Committee.
Price of computer programs
11.22 Those in favour of allowing parallel importation argue that parallel
importation will lead to lower prices in Australia for
computer programs. In the
Committee’s opinion this argument relies on the premise that prices of
software are presently unacceptably
high in Australia.
The PSA’s consideration of prices
11.23 In its interim report the PSA noted that at the commencement of its
inquiry the major industry associations conceded a price
differential “of
around 30 percent with USA software”. However, because of the numerous
price comparisons presented throughout
its inquiry, the PSA conducted its own
more comprehensive comparison of advertised prices. For this study it examined
of 33 of the highest selling PC business computer program packages
over a 37 month period from July 1989 to July 1992. The result
of this survey
was a finding that Australian prices of computer programs were consistently
higher than those in the USA. The average
price differential for that period was
11.24 In preparing its final report, the PSA gave the computer industry an
opportunity to respond to its findings in its interim report
and to comment on
its methodology. In response to disagreement over its methodology, the PSA
concluded that the principal conclusion
that Australian computer program prices
have been persistently and substantially higher than in the USA was not
dependent on the
methodology used to analyse the raw data. The PSA’s
report also addressed concerns expressed by the industry regarding the
variability of data, the value of spot or long term comparisons, the veracity of
its survey results, relative movements in nominal
prices and whether taxes
should be included in the surveyed prices.
Submissions to the Committee
11.25 As acknowledged by the Committee in the Draft Report, the evidence
presented to the Committee in relation to prices varied (paragraph
Dataflow maintained that computer program prices in Australia are lower than in
other countries but submissions from Apple,
Autodesk, Lotus, Microsoft and
Roland expressly or implicitly acknowledged that prices are higher in Australia
than in the United
11.26 In fact, Microsoft and Lotus presented reasons why prices were higher
in Australia. Microsoft identified the following factors
as contributing to
higher prices (p. 16, paragraph 5.6.8):
• freight and other charges represent a ten percent (10%) add on cost
to all three levels of the distributing channel in Australia—ie,
suppliers, distributors and outlets;
• cost of support per copy in Australia is higher than in the
USA—due to the greater geographic area of support to be provided;
• cost of hardware in Australia is more expensive than in the US;
• telephone charges and costs in Australia are again higher than in the
• the essential mobility of the industry necessitates the use of
company cars for sales representatives and executives in both
Australia. However again the costs of cars in general and on-road and running
costs is higher in Australia just as salary
and ‘salary-on’ costs
are lower in the US;
• office rent is higher in Australia;
• the size of the market is substantially smaller in Australia than in
the USA leading to an absence of economies of scale in
Australia; and
• warehousing is necessary in Australia to ensure continued
availability of stock with minimum delays.
11.27 The submission by Lotus attached a background paper prepared by the
Business Software Association of Australia (BSAA) entitled
Software Pricing
in Australia and Copyright Protection for Exclusive Importation
out many similar reasons to those given by Microsoft for higher computer program
prices in Australia, such as:
• Product adaptation and localisation, eg, modifications to spelling
check dictionaries, and changing US date/time systems to
• Add–Ins and specific changes—additional program utilities
or options which are included in the product for the
Australian market but not
in the product sold on the US market;
• The cost of doing business in Australia—costs of hardware,
airline tickets, phone calls and company cars are higher in
• Economies of scale—the US market is 250 million with 50 million
users of PCs, compared with the Australian market of
17 million with 2 million
•Supporting local distributors and dealers. Costs here include:
– Establishing local Australian offices with trained technical and
management staff;
– Production and distribution of “course ware” for training
– Distribution of free pre-launch copies for familiarisation and
–Employing specialist staff to support dealers; and
–Training trainers who train users.
•Indirect support is also provided by:
–demonstrating products at exhibitions;
–producing and distributing information brochures;
–publishing newsletters and technical bulletins;
–advertising and publicity campaigns;
–supporting user training; and
–supporting local user groups.
In addition, the BSAA submitted that comparisons between the recommended
retail price of computer programs in Australia and heavily
discounted prices in
the US are invalid, especially given that many of the low prices in the US are
of the “loss leader”
term, even in the US market. The comparison in the paper between Australian and
recommended retail prices suggests that while in some cases computer programs
may be marginally cheaper in Australia typically the
price differential is
between 20% and 30% (Australian prices being higher).
11.28 The BSAA paper concluded that computer program prices in Australia are
not substantially or inordinately higher than in other
countries, in particular
the US, when fair comparisons are made and that any price differentials that do
exist can be accounted for
in differences in product specifications including
adaptation to Australian conditions, add–ins (or additional features) and
differences in the market.
11.29 It is apparent to the Committee that even if it is accepted that prices
are higher in Australia than the US, this is a situation
which is not peculiar
to Australia alone. Prices are in fact higher outside the US in a number of
other countries, such as the UK.
Moreover, the submission from the AIAA to the
Committee following the Draft Report and the paper prepared by DITARD both
that prices of legitimately produced software are lower in Australia
than in many, or all, territories outside North America.
11.30 In addition, submissions following the Draft Report, from Dataflow and
the AIAA and the DITARD paper asserted that software
prices are subject to a
downward trend. According to DITARD and the AIIA, this trend was in train before
the PSA began its inquiry,
and is indicated by the PSA’s own data. A study
conducted by Factotum (May 1993) which was commissioned by the AIIA and which
aimed to replicate, for the March 1993 quarter, the methodology adopted by the
PSA for the 1992 March quarter found that (p. 2):
For the constant set of nineteen products tracked by the PSA over 37 months,
the street price differential in Australia at current
exchange rates is just 3.7
per cent above the US price for the same bundle; the UK price is 9.7 per cent
higher than the US.
— Removing the effect of the exchange rate movements means the
Australian differential is now 14.8 per cent higher than the
US; the UK price is
33 per cent higher than the US.
Factotum reached the conclusion that (p. 7):
. . . there is ample evidence that prices of computer software in Australia
have fallen dramatically over the past twelve months [ie.
1992–1993].
Heightened competition between suppliers means there are a wide variety of ways
in which consumers can acquire
software at prices substantially lower than those
which prevailed at the time the PSA conducted the bulk of its research.
short, through a variety of means, consumers are able to purchase a wide range
of software applications at prices that are at least
40 per cent
the current best street price
. Moreover, this trend is worldwide and, in the
absence of a dramatic change in industry concentration, seems likely to
Dataflow stated in their submission that the price of their three leading
software packages had fallen in price over 1991–August
1993 by 25%.
11.31 Of perhaps even more significance is that a fall in prices since the
PSA’s report seems to have been acknowledged by the
PSA itself. In
Treasury’s comments on the PSA’s final report reference is made to
the PSA’s “Price Probe”
Issue No. 16 of June 1993 (p. 11),
where the PSA stated that the price differential had narrowed and was currently
somewhere in the
order of 20%.
11.32 From the information available to the Committee, it seems that software
prices have been, and perhaps still are, subject to
a downward trend. Some of
the reasons given for this trend include that it has resulted from the
PSA’s inquiry, that the market
is becoming more competitive, that the
distribution mechanism has become more efficient which has led to lower prices
and that software
is now more widely available through large retail outlets such
as K–Mart, Myers, Harvey Norman and newsagents. The Committee
is not able
to weigh up the validity of these reasons but, whether or not they are valid, it
is clear to the Committee that the prices
of computer programs in Australia are
not in its view
unacceptably
high, even if they are higher than in the
11.33 A prediction that allowing parallel importation would necessarily
result in further lowering of prices of legitimate software
in Australia than in
other countries remains doubtful, given the existence of numerous other factors
which may affect prices such
as changing industry structures. This point was
made by the AIAA. A prediction about the extent to which prices would fall if
importation was allowed remains speculative, as acknowledged by the
then majority of the Committee in its Draft Report (paragraph
11.34 However, there are
other aspects of this trend in prices
which need to be considered. First, whether the trend indicates a competitive
environment despite
the existence of the parallel importation provisions and
second whether it affects the PSA’s predictions about the benefits
would arise if parallel importation were allowed.
Competition
11.35 Some of those in favour of allowing parallel importation (such as
Treasury) argue that the real issue is not whether a fall
in prices will result,
but whether allowing parallel importation will result in a more competitive
market environment. Those who
believe that a more competitive environment would
result assume that this would lead in turn to benefits for employment and the
generally. Again, such arguments rest on assumption—in this case
that the market is presently anti-competitive. In Treasury’s
The recent history of microeconomic reform in Australia clearly shows that
opening up the domestic market to increased competition,
particularly from
overseas, provides tangible and early benefits to consumers in terms of lower
prices and better quality and services
eg. telephone charges and air fares.
There is no reason to assume the computer software industry is so unique that
similar benefits
would not flow to the economy and consumers if the current
importation provisions were removed.
In the Committee’s opinion, Treasury’s assumption that the
deregulation of other areas such as telephone and airline services
useful guide for what may occur if parallel importation of computer programs was
allowed is questionable. It is interesting
to note that the PSA conducted
separate inquiries into the effect of parallel importation restrictions industry
by industry and reached
separate conclusions in respect of each industry The PSA
stressed this aspect of its inquiries in its final report commenting (p.
Some issues and related explanatory hypotheses may have been canvassed in
previous inquiries, [ie. inquiries into books and sound
recordings] but each is
considered in the context of the particular industry or market that is the
subject of the inquiry.
It appears to be recognised that the effects of such controls on each
industry are different and that common assumptions and conclusions
11.36 Treasury’s view that allowing parallel importation would result
in a more competitive environment and consequential benefits
for the economy has
also been questioned by others. DITARD argued that there is already a trend
towards a more competitive market,
which has occurred without allowing parallel
importation. In its paper DITARD states as follows (pp. 7–8):
Close sectoral examination reveals that the Australian distribution channels
for software are undergoing structural change, moving
towards a highly
competitive sector . . . Briefly, the . . . restructuring reflects the
availability of software via direct mail,
the availability of pre loaded
software/hardware through local retail outlets and established local computer
ware vendors, and the
availability of bulk software packages that provide
significant discounts to consumers compared to single unit purchasing.
Other submissions in response to the Draft Report, such as those from AIIA
and Oracle, similarly argued that the industry is already
very competitive.
11.37 To address the argument that the local industry is already competitive,
Treasury contended that if this is already the case
then parallel importation
would not be a significant threat and should be allowed anyway. While, the
Committee acknowledges that
in a competitive market allowing parallel
importation might not threaten the computer industry, it is apparent to the
Committee that
this view ignores the potential impact on copyright owners that
the importation of pirate copies would have. The view of the Committee
on balance this risk is not justifiable where the result would only confer a
minimum benefit on the consumer.
Direct mail order—importation for personal
11.38 The practice identified by DITARD where US mail order companies have
advertised in Australian and UK computer magazines the
availability of computer
software by direct mail not only indicates possible competition in the market,
but a trend of circumvention
of the parallel importation provisions. The current
operation of the Act allows private individuals to purchase software (ie
or manuals) directly through the mail without infringing ss.37 or 38
because such individuals could not be regarded as “importers”
persons dealing commercially in such articles). Equally, foreign mail order
companies would not appear to be caught by the operation
of s.38 which
contemplates an identifiable “importer”. The Draft Report (paragraph
11.10) noted Apple’s concern
that the provisions in enabling orders for
personal use could be abused by unscrupulous traders and that certain traders
might endorse
all their documentation in such a way so as to circumvent the
provisions. Apple argued that the likelihood of such practices being
pursued was
higher because of the higher unit price for most computer programs than for
books. The PSA noted the existence of such
operations in its interim report and
the Committee noted in the Draft Report the various submissions which urged
changes to the Act
to make such advertising illegal (paragraph 11.11). In its
submission Infomagic suggested a further extension to the operation of
(i) so that it becomes an offence to advertise the direct mail order of
products from overseas in magazines and newspapers published
in Australia;
(ii) so that a person cannot knowingly take delivery of imported software
packages which he or she does not intend either to use themselves
or deliver to
the authorised distributor.
Microsoft suggested that s.133A should be amended so that Australian
publications are forbidden to carry advertisements for direct
services for computer program sales by overseas companies. Microsoft also
provided the Committee, on an “in-confidence”
basis, with an outline
of a number of schemes and business practices which could be adopted to avoid
the operation of the parallel
importation provisions.
11.39 In the Draft Report the Committee noted these suggestions and indicated
they had previously been considered in the preparation
of the Committee’s
1988 report and recommendations. Since the Draft Report the Committee has also
considered the impact of
technology in the area of direct mail ordering. It
notes that increasingly consumers will have access to international electronic
means of communication, including electronic mail. Users of these services will
have access to both national and international electronic
bulletin board
services which may well carry advertisements for the sale of computer programs.
Domestic legislation to ban such advertising
in foreign services would be
ineffective.
11.40 It is conceivable that these electronic services could increasingly
become the preferred means for advertising and supplying
at least some types of
computer programs. Provided such purchases are for personal use and not for a
commercial purpose so as to
conflict with the importation provisions the
Committee sees no reason why the Act should be amended to make it more difficult
consumers to learn about and purchase new computer programs from any
legitimate source, wherever it may be located. However, the
acknowledges that if this form of “electronic shopping” does become
a significant way in which consumers obtain
their computer programs it could
have a considerable impact on the effectiveness of the importation provisions as
a means by which
computer programs. Nonetheless, having regard to the foregoing discussion
the views expressed in the submissions referred to above, the Committee is of
the view that it should not recommend any changes
to the Act to regulate the
advertising (electronic or otherwise) of direct mail order services.
Regulation of prices and competition by other
legislation
11.41 The Committee acknowledges that there may be circumstances in which
control over parallel importation could be exercised in
an anti-competitive
manner and which may breach the provisions of the
Trade Practices Act
. Indeed, the Trade Practices Commission’s background paper
Application of the
Trade Practices Act
to Intellectual Property
1991) states that there are many examples from Europe and the US which indicate
that the potential for anti-competitive conduct
in relation to intellectual
property exists (p. 3, paragraph 1.3). However, no evidence of this sort
has been presented to the Committee
in relation to computer software. In any
event, the Committee does not think that such a possibility would justify the
the control over parallel importation, which provides the basis of
international marketing of copyright-based goods. If there are
instances in
which this control is exercised anti-competitively, the remedies should be those
available in legislation which implements
competition policy.
11.42 One member of the Committee (previously a member of the majority in the
Draft Report) does not agree with the views contained
in paragraph 11.41 above.
That member believes that there are insufficient remedies available to prohibit
anti-competitive behaviour
by the exclusive licensee of copyright licences.
While parallel importation sanctions remain, the issue of addressing
anti-competitive
behaviour must be raised in the
Trade Practices Act
that protection of the exclusive licence granted in Australia does not have the
effect of granting that licensee any economic
rights over and above market based
performance criteria. The grant of an exclusive licence effectively prohibits
competition, therefore
incentives must apply to require that licensee to perform
adequately by way of service, availability of product, correction of faults
tailoring product to the needs of the local market.
11.43 The Committee considers that the mere existence of the control over
parallel importation, or any other property rights such
as those over real
property, does not of itself contravene competition policy. As pointed out by
the AIIA, abuse of market power
in relation to other property rights is dealt
with by deterrents such as fines, rather than by expropriation or forfeiture of
property. In relation to the control over parallel importation, the PSA has
proposed forfeiture of this control for
evidence of abuse of market power by any one of them.
11.44 There may also be other avenues of response to potentially
anti-competitive conduct, such as consumer/user demands. One example
cited by Treasury, was the decision by Microsoft after pressure from the PSA and
users, to unbundle the cost of technical
support and reduce the recommended
retail price of products to a level commensurate with the loss of technical
support. As reported
by Treasury, initially Microsoft was proposing to unbundle
technical support but maintain recommended retail prices at existing levels.
the Committee’s opinion, even if the PSA’s “pressure”
was enhanced by threat of repeal of the importation
provisions, the response by
Microsoft to pressure from users would seem to indicate a competitive
11.45 It should also be noted that procedures exist for the declaration of
companies under the
Prices Surveillance Act 1983
. Once a company is
declared by the Minister responsible,
has the effect that the company must
notify the PSA where it proposes to increase the price of goods or services
it provides. A penalty of $10 000 may be incurred for failure to comply with
this requirement. If the PSA is notified of such a proposal
it can make
recommendations agreeing or not agreeing with the proposal to increase price or
it can propose some variation of the
price increase proposed. However, the PSA
has no power to enforce its recommendations, though the Committee understands
is generally a 100% compliance rate with the PSA‘s
recommendations.
11.46 As the Committee was finalising this report, the Assistant Treasurer,
the Hon George Gear MP, announced on 8 November 1994,
that in 1995 the Prices
Surveillance Authority would be merged with the Trade Practices Commission to
form a new body, the Australian
Competition Commission (ACC). The Assistant
Treasurer’s press statement referred to the introduction of a new prices
regime to be administered by the ACC. This is a three-tiered approach
to prices surveillance including price surveillance, similar
to the existing
declaration system, formal price monitoring (where the Minister will have the
power to direct the ACC to declare
a firm for formal price monitoring and the
declared firm will then be required to provide the ACC with cost and price data
intervals) and informal price monitoring. As regards informal price
monitoring the Assistant Treasurer’s announcement indicated
that this may
apply to CDs and computer software. In the Committee’s view the
availability of such price monitoring mechanisms
will represent a viable
alternative option to use of the parallel importation provisions to control
competition and prices.
ARGUMENTS CONCERNING THE ECONOMY, EMPLOYMENT AND
LOCAL INDUSTRY
11.47 Those in favour of allowing parallel importation argue that greater
competition and/or lower software prices will result in
long term benefits to
employment and the economy. Treasury stated (pp. 1 and 3):
If the price of local software fell . . . the overall demand for computer
software is likely to grow, with consequent expansion of
production and
employment opportunities in local manufacturing and distribution . . . there is
likely to also be a change in the
structure of this sector, with new
distributors parallel importing and thereby expanding employment and
11.48 In its Draft Report, the then majority of the Committee said that it
regarded the effect of prices on both employment and Australia’s
international competitiveness as the two most important considerations to be
weighed against the copyright owners’ rights in
respect of parallel
importation (paragraph 11.40). The then majority of the Committee attached
“considerable importance”
to the findings of the National Institute
of Economics and Industry Research (NIEIR) engaged by the PSA to determine the
macro-economic
consequences of current pricing practices engaged in by the
computer industry and regarded the NIEIR finding that “current
impact negatively on Australia’s macro-economic performance” as
highly significant.
11.49 The predictions of the NIEIR report have been the subject of criticism,
and their accuracy and persuasiveness should be assessed
in the light of those
criticisms. The Committee think there is force in the arguments by the AIIA and
DITARD that the NIEIR findings
are flawed because they are based on unrealistic
assumptions, such as a one-off 20% reduction in the price of imported software,
and because they do not take into account factors outside the model (such as
business investment and employment and border control
costs associated with
parallel importation). The AIAA commissioned a report from Dr Larry Cook who
reviewed the NIEIR report and
analysed its results. Dr Cook examined the
calculations and modelling used in that report and indicated difficulty in using
adopted, concluding that (p. 5):
The IMP model [the model used by NIEIR] raises more questions than it
answers. . . Until better documentation and explanation is available
for the IMP
model, results and reports based on that model should be treated with extreme
11.50 In its most recent submission the AIAA repeated its criticism of the
NIEIR report and the PSA’s reliance on it which it
had made to the PSA
after the release of its interim report. The Committee is in agreement with the
AIAA’s view that (p. 7):
. . . [the] one thing that is clear from the PSA inquiry is the absence of
reliable data on the software industry, especially about
such factors such as
price and income elasticities of demand, and the significance of software as an
input to other industries.
It is these inadequacies which have led the Committee to doubt the validity
of the PSA’s findings and its recommendations and
not to rely on the NIEIR
report as a justification for allowing parallel importation.
11.51 As mentioned, DITARD and Treasury also indicated a common reservation
with the NIEIR report to the PSA on the basis that in
general economic models
used to estimate outcomes cannot be regarded as intrinsically reliable. DITARD
referred to (p. 2)
. . . common reservations about the ability of macro-economic models to match
estimates with actual outcomes . . .
11.52 Treasury, who supported removal of parallel importation controls, also
accepted that the NIEIR model may overstate the possible
benefits of parallel
importation and that (p. 3):
The initial fall in the price of packaged software may not be of the
magnitude modelled.
Treasury acknowledged that it is difficult to forecast the net employment
gain due to the two offsetting patterns in employment predicted
by the NIEIR,
and also acknowledged that initial employment losses would be likely if parallel
importation was allowed, but argued
that (p. 3):
. . . on a priori grounds, a positive longer term effect on employment is
likely to occur through productivity improvements.
DITARD, on the other hand, argued that net employment losses in the
distribution sector resulting from parallel importation would
substantial.
11.53 Treasury’s predictions appear to be based on the results of
implementing policies to increase competition in other areas
such as telephone
services and airfares. The Committee does not share Treasury’s confidence
that such comparisons are sound,
and that there are no relevant differences
between deregulation of other industries and allowing parallel importation of
11.54 Finally, the Committee notes that the LCA also expressed reservations
them. The LCA along with
Dr Cook noted the limited amount of detail published by the PSA about the NIEIR
report and that the findings
of the NIEIR were predicated on the PSA’s
conclusion that higher prices of software in Australia result, to a substantial
from the control on parallel importation.
11.55 While the Committee does not presume to have economic expertise in
relation to the possible effects of parallel importation
and relies on the
opinion of those more qualified in these matters, the Committee is concerned
that the evidence concerning prices
and effect on the economy if parallel
importation was allowed is not conclusive and that the principal report
commissioned by the
PSA has been subject to widespread criticism. These concerns
convince the Committee that the PSA’s findings, first released
interim report which is now over two years old, cannot be accepted and that
parallel importation should not be allowed on
the basis of that report.
11.56 As regards possible effects on employment, it is clear to the Committee
that even those in favour of allowing parallel importation
acknowledge that
there would be employment losses in the short term. Opinions differ as to the
extent and duration of these employment
losses, and the extent (if any) to which
they would be off-set by employment gains in the longer term. Given this. the
has decided not to recommend a change in the law which most
commentators seem to acknowledge would result in employment losses, with
compensating gains being speculative and disputed.
11.57 In considering a range of possible effects on the economy of allowing
parallel importation, the Committee’s attention
was also drawn to the
effect on the local industry. In the Draft Report the Committee referred to this
issue in paragraphs 11.14–11.15.
The Committee is of the view that as with
employment, it is difficult to predict the consequences for local industry.
Certainly unreserved
reliance cannot be placed on the PSA’s views that
local producers will face little direct competition from imported software.
Committee notes that the LCA took issue with the PSA’s description of the
local industry as a “niche market”
and questioned the ability of the
local industry to withstand parallel imports (where prices would be falling as
predicted by the
PSA). It was also suggested that if parallel importation were
allowed then many of the major suppliers of computer programs would
uneconomical to introduce and support the range of products that they presently
provide. This in turn would have an adverse
effect on those Australian
businesses that create interoperable programs. While the Committee accepts that
this is a possible effect
of allowing parallel importation it notes that in this
regard it is important to distinguish the market for the programs, particularly
in terms of the personal and small business market, mini and medium business
market, and large scale part of the market.
11.58 Finally, mention should be made of the effect allowing parallel
importation may have in discouraging new overseas producers
from establishing a
presence in Australia. Disincentive to invest in research and development in
Australia was a concern raised in
a number of submissions received by the
Committee following the Draft Report, including those from the AIIA, CBEMA and
the LCA. DITARD’s
paper also referred to this issue indicating that the
intellectual property regime of Australia (including controls on parallel
importation)
was a major factor in decisions taken by multinational companies to
locate software development projects in Australia. While the
recognises that Australia’s intellectual property regime is extremely
effective in protecting Australia’s creators
and investors and would
continue to offer protection even if controls over parallel importation were
relaxed, the Committee considers
that loss of control over parallel importation
would be a disincentive to investment by overseas producers in establishing a
in Australia. This would have flow-on effects on the local industry,
including less investment in research and development.
AVAILABILITY
11.59 The availability of software has been a peripheral issue in the debate
Draft Report were the
submissions from Autodesk, Dataflow, Lotus, Microsoft and Mindscape in which all
companies indicated that they
carried a full range of computer programs and that
availability was not a problem. Such delays as did occur from time to time, in
relation to new product coming on to the market, were attributed to the need to
test programs for bugs and to build up sufficient
stocks. The Committee was also
told that old computer programs were sometimes removed from the market in
anticipation of the arrival
of upgraded versions.
11.60 Microsoft’s submission made the Committee aware that many
computer companies market their computer programs on the basis
that, from time
to time, certain programs are unavailable. This not being due to a lack of
commitment to the Australian market, but
by a desire to provide the latest
versions to the market as soon as possible following their release overseas.
Accordingly, superseded
versions are removed from the market prior to the
release of the latest enhancement.
11.61 The Draft Report also referred to the PSA’s findings with respect
to availability in its interim and final reports. In
the interim report the PSA
noted that the initial complaints it received suggested there were availability
problems in respect of
both existing products and new releases of computer
programs. However, it concluded that the evidence presented to the inquiry
demonstrated
that overall availability was not a problem, although isolated
instances did arise. In response to the PSA’s observation concerning
availability, the AIIA submitted that repeal of the importation provisions would
threaten the viability of the industry with the
result that end users would
ultimately be worse off because of higher prices and reduced availability.
11.62 In Chapter 9 of its final report the PSA considered the effect of
repeal of the importation provisions on the domestic computer
program industry
and made reference to the issue of availability of software. The PSA stated (p.
During the preparation of the Interim Report the Authority believed that
availability of software was not generally a problem. However,
this view was
formed on the basis of evidence regarding the availability of PC software.
Subsequent evidence presented by the Australian
Share/Guide Ltd suggests that
product availability may be restricted for mid-range and mainframe software
The PSA concluded that unless Australian developers and business in general
had timely access to a wide range of new products and
releases, their cost
efficiency and hence Australia’s international competitiveness would fall.
The PSA believed that repeal
of the importation provisions should overcome this
11.63 As stated by the PSA, the evidence from the Australian Share/Guide Ltd
was suggestive only and, as far as the Committee is aware,
it was the only
evidence presented to the PSA on this issue following the interim report. For
these two reasons, the evidence concerning
availability seems of limited value
and tends to indicate more that availability is not a problem rather than the
reverse. By contrast,
the Committee views the argument, made by CBEMA in its
submission on the Draft Report, that allowing parallel importation may lead
diminished availability of products because of less incentive to create a market
for a new product in an environment where others
can free-ride on that
investment, as a far more weighty concern.
11.64 As already mentioned at the beginning of this Chapter at paragraph
11.08, the Committee considered the question of availability
of copyright
materials in 1988 and in order to address concerns voiced by software users
software products in Australia,
recommended that where such products were not available they be able to be
imported to without contravening
the parallel importation provisions. The
Committee noted the views of the PC Users Group Inc that products may be
unavailable in
Australia because for instance, the exclusive agent does not want
to market a new product released overseas in Australia and instead
stocks in Australia (1988 Report—paragraph 171(3)). In commenting on its
recommendation the Committee said (paragraph
. . . the Committee’s recommendations that importation be allowed where
there is either a specific order or where the article
sought to be imported is
unavailable in Australia . . . do offer solutions to the major problems raised
by the software users. The
range an quantity of imported software products
available to the Australian consumer will increase. On the other hand, the
and marketing activities of the licensed importers will not be
substantially affected because the demand for imported software products
need to be altered to meet Australian conditions will remain unaffected . . .
11.65 The Committee has recalled its discussion in the 1988 Report concerning
availability as it regards the comments it made then
as still being pertinent.
The Committee has not made any recommendation in this report to relax the
operation of the importation
provisions to allow limited instances of parallel
importation of computer programs to meet consumer demands, but it is of the view
that further consideration could be given to such an option. It notes that
following its 1988 Report and the PSA’s inquiry
into the prices of books
in 1989 the Government amended the importation provisions as they apply to books
(s.44A). The Committee understands
that these provisions are working well.
TECHNICAL SUPPORT AND CUSTOMER SERVICE
11.66 There were differing views in the submissions and papers presented to
the Committee about the desirability of bundling the cost
of technical support
with the cost of software. The Committee also received submissions concerning
the quality of existing customer
service from both exclusive licensees and
parallel importers. These submissions and views were outlined in the Draft
Report at paragraphs
11.45–11.53.
11.67 In their submissions to the Committee prior to the Draft Report,
Autodesk and Lotus both distinguished computer programs from
other copyright
materials, such as books and records, on the basis that additional expenditure
was required on an ongoing basis in
relation to computer programs to enable the
provision of training and support and educational services. In its submission to
Committee, Microsoft argued that the importation provisions were necessary
to enable distributors to provide users with essential
support and upgrade
paths. Sun Microsystems commented that the existing provisions helped prevent
the importation of pirate copies
of computer programs, ensured customers were
provided with a product support system, enabled the continuing provision of
avoided virus infection by computer programs from unauthorised sources
and enabled the provision of support for local organisations.
submitted that the provisions enabled it to provide user training, support,
distribution of its computer programs and
advertising.
11.68 While acknowledging that in some cases computer program prices would
fall in the short term, Microsoft submitted that if computer
programs were able
to be freely imported directly from the United States, in the long term support
for imported products would be
reduced. Microsoft also claimed that there would
be no opportunities for computer programs to be tailored in response to the
of the Australian consumer, there would be no technology transfer between
the USA and Australia and more directly the removal of
the provisions would
affect revenue generated in Australia as well as the employment and skill
development of Australians.
Australian Consumers’ Association
11.69 The submission from the ACA focused on the requirements of users of
computer programs.
The ACA’s submission classed consumers of
computer programs into two main categories—non-sophisticated beginners and
computer-literate
users. In respect of computer-literate users it argued that
these consumers did not benefit from the level of support currently
available. However, consumers in the non-sophisticated beginner category
depended to a large degree on the provision of back-up services
by experts.
11.70 The ACA noted that back-up service for computer programs could be
divided into two different categories; repair or debugging
and enhancements
(up-grading). The ACA submitted that repair or debugging service gave rise to
the same sorts of issues for consumers
of computer programs as those facing
purchasers of other consumer goods such as cars and electrical appliances and
that consumers
of computer programs are similarly protected as other consumers
Trade Practices Act
and other consumer legislation. On
this analysis the ACA submitted the issue unique to computer programs concerned
“enhancements”.
It claimed that in effect importers were using the
Act to enforce a built-in prepayment for after-sales services. The question for
consumers was what was the value of such a service.
11.71 The ACA’s submission identified the following inadequacies in the
service provided:
•suppliers’ lack of knowledge;
•lack of interest in providing assistance;
•inadequate demonstration at point of sale; and
•unhelpful manuals.
It also claimed that correspondence that it received indicated that rather
than sophisticated users subsidising entry level consumers,
consumers of varying
levels of sophistication were subsidising the Australian licensees.
11.72 Having regard to these considerations the ACA suggested that ideally
consumers should be able to tailor their level of support
to meet their needs
and their capacity to pay. Furthermore, consumers should have the option of
trading off lower purchase prices
by foregoing upgrading processes. Accordingly,
the ACA recommended that changes should be implemented to make after-sales
involvement
optional (p. 5):
. . . along lines already in place in other industries, such as extended
warranties. Risking expensive repairs could thus become a
regular decision with
renewable warranties being analogous to insurance.
On this approach enhancements would be provided on the basis of the
consumer’s needs.
11.73 The ACA commented that it was uncertain how many consumers benefit
from, or wish to stay at, the leading edge of available technology.
noted that such choices (computer programs at different prices with and without
support) were available in the US market.
This did not appear to have any side
effects in innovation and development. Accordingly, the ACA argued that the most
appropriate
structure of protection in the computer program industry was a
mixture of open competition between distributors and direct subsidies
computer program manufacturers and developers.
PSA’s consideration of the issue
11.74 In its interim report the PSA expressed concern at the efficiency
implications of including technical support as a component
of the purchase price
of packaged computer programs for personal computers. It noted that those users
who require technical support
infrequently or not at all are subsidising those
who frequently use technical support facilities. The efficiency effects
by the PSA were:
• there may be excess demand for technical support because it is
perceived to be free; and
•supply is likely to be excessive in response to excessive demands.
In its final report the PSA stated that, while it believed that
“bundling” (ie, the practice of the joint provision of
program with an offer of technical support) was undesirable, it also accepted
the AIIA’s contention that a decision
by a supplier to unbundle is a
commercial one based on the product, the experience of users and competitive
forces, rather than regulatory
mandate. For this reason it expressed a
preference that this should be encouraged by exposing the market to greater
competitive pressure
such as would occur by repealing the importation
provisions.
Product support by parallel importers
11.75 A number of advocates favouring the retention of ss.37 and 38 claimed
that parallel importers do not provide any support on
the products that they
sell. Computermate disputed this claim. Computermate noted that a seller’s
obligations to the buyer
are clearly defined in the
Trade Practices Act 1974
which was endorsed by the comments of the ACA. Computermate also submitted
that it was unaware of any complaints concerning warranty
matters as all
products sold by it are covered by a warranty from the overseas supplier through
Computermate. It was also claimed
that in some circumstances the warranty period
provided by the parallel importer was longer than that offered by the exclusive
Some of the views expressed by Computermate were echoed in a letter to
the Prime Minister from Comprepair which was referred to the
Comprepair claimed that the back-up service it had received from parallel
importers was superior to that provided by the
major companies.
Impact of “unbundling” support
11.76 The Committee has not formed any firm view on the merits or demerits of
bundling technical support with software sales. The
Committee notes that the PSA
found that the bundling of support with the purchase price was not a
“universal practice”
and that moves to unbundling were occurring.
Having a possible negative consequence was Dataflow’s assertion, in its
submission to the Committee after the Draft Report, that some software
publishers and distributors were (p. 2):
. . . imposing very significant charges on providing service to the consumer
as a result of unbundling service and software costs.
The Committee is not certain upon which consumers such costs are being
imposed, but the Committee is aware that licensed distributors
do charge for
service where a client had not made their initial purchase from them. This
situation should be contrasted with that
of a purchasing client. In any event,
it is the Committee’s view that the question of technical support is not a
matter which
is properly dealt with by the removal of parallel importation
provisions. If forms of marketing are occurring which contravene competition
policy, then the proper area of recourse is competition law.
11.77 One member of the Committee (previously a member of the majority in the
Draft Report) believes that the provision of technical
support, service and
backup should be a fundamental obligation of the vendor if the vendor has the
protection of an exclusive licence
protected by parallel importation sanctions.
Competition law in Australia has not, in this member’s opinion, yet proved
to address the requirements of such behaviour in the computer market
and as such, such issues should be addressed in competition
law to ensure full
service and technical support is available with the sale of the product.
Terminology
11.78 The issue of piracy was examined by the Committee in the Draft Report
in paragraphs 11.61–11.65. At the outset the Committee
clarified its use
of the term “piracy” by stating (paragraph 11.61):
Put simply, piracy is the copying of copyright materials for commercial
purposes knowing the copying to be an infringement, or the
commercial dealing
with such copies knowing them to be such. These are criminal offences.
Unauthorised copying by private individuals
or organisations for their own use
that amounts to an infringement is not, however, a criminal offence and cannot
be considered as
“piracy” in the sense of the offences just referred
to. That is, piracy does not extend to copying for internal use by
businesses or
copying by individuals for themselves, where there is no commercial element in
the activity in the sense that copies
are not sold or otherwise dealt with
commercially or otherwise distributed widely. Whether own-use copying is
authorised or, alternatively,
infringes copyright will of course depend on the
licence agreement between the user and the copyright owner or his or her
licensee. In this regard the Committee notes that its use of the term
“piracy” is somewhat different to that used by
the PSA in its
(The term piracy as used by the PSA was not limited to copying for commercial
purposes but included “user copying”—Interim
Report, p.29,
paragraph 2.12).
11.79 The Committee also defined “counterfeiting” stating
(paragraph 11.62):
The Committee understands that counterfeiting is the unauthorised manufacture
of copies of copyright materials which are made to look
like the originals as
far as is technically possible. This includes all aspects of presentation of the
product, manuals, packaging
etc. It is to be expected that counterfeiting
activities would be undertaken for commercial gain and, as such, would come
the concept of piracy as defined above. It is also conceivable that
individuals may make own-use counterfeit copies.
11.80 The use of these terms by the Committee in this final report has not
changed. However, it is worth noting, as did the PSA in
its interim and final
reports, that the sort of “piracy” that the Committee’s
discussion concerns is the sort that
can be controlled by the importation
provisions in ss.37, 38 or 135 of the Act. Obviously, unauthorised copies of
computer programs
made in Australia, even if dealt with commercially within
Australia, will not be affected by the importation provisions. It should
noted that the Committee’s discussion focuses on the importation for
commercial dealing of copies of computer programs
that have been reproduced
without the consent of the copyright owner in the country of origin. Copies of
computer programs made with
the consent of the copyright owner in the country of
manufacture and subsequently imported into Australia without the consent of
nominated local distributor do not fall within the accepted meaning of
“pirate goods” and are not the focus of the
Committee’s
discussion in this part of the chapter. The latter type of imported goods are
“parallel imports”, not
“pirate import goods”.
Importation of pirate products
11.81 It appears to the Committee that it is commonly accepted that the
repeal of the importation provisions, even if limited to certain
would increase the chance that some imported material would be pirated product.
It also appears to be agreed that detection
of this pirate product by customs
authorities and copyright owners and their distributors is already difficult and
could be exacerbated
if the parallel importation provisions were repealed.
Indeed the majority of the Committee in the Draft Report referred to piracy
“real threat” in paragraph 11.60. The PSA also identified piracy as
one of the major issues of its inquiry.
11.82 While the PSA’s discussion of piracy appeared to be primarily
concerned with the issue of copying of computer programs
by users in Australia
referred to as own-use copying (see discussion in Interim Report at p.29 and
p.103) and its impact on prices,
the PSA also identified the concern that
allowing the importation of computer programs, even legitimate product, may lead
to increased
problems with pirate imported product. In the Committee’s
view the PSA correctly identified the two key issues of concern with
imported pirate product—first, that “ . . . the sale of pirated
software directly correlates with lost revenue
and reduced economic
welfare” and second, that “. . . without protection from legitimate
parallel imports, detection
of pirate imports would be more difficult”
(Interim Report, p.155, paragraph 8.2.2). While noting these possible outcomes,
the PSA went on to conclude in its Interim Report that (p.156, paragraph
There seems little reason to expect that repeal of the importation provisions
will alter significantly the difficulty in detecting
individual pirated
Final Report
the PSA said, after acknowledging that
there were no direct remedies available to prevent the entry of pirated software
into Australia,
The Authority does not believe that repeal of the importation provisions will
lead to a flood of pirated or counterfeit products .
11.83 In contradiction to the PSA’s conclusion, the Committee’s
Draft Report referred to a number of submissions which
submitted that the repeal
of the parallel importation provisions would lead to an escalation of piracy in
Australia. Mindscape submitted
that the computer program industry is
particularly vulnerable to piracy. It noted that most of the copyright
“pirate havens”
are located in South-East Asia. Mindscape also
suggested that amendment of the importation provisions would increase the
of pirate product reaching the Australian market through mixed
shipments from overseas suppliers, thereby adding to the difficulty
of detecting
pirate product. Under the importation provisions as they presently stand, a
Australia need only show that a
particular article was not placed on the market with his or her permission to
establish that the
article is infringing. Commenting on the changes recommended
by the Committee in 1988 Mindscape said that proof would be required
imported articles were unauthorised copies. This, Mindscape submitted, may be
virtually impossible to establish in many cases.
Accordingly, Mindscape
submitted that any relaxation of the provisions would greatly increase the
likelihood of high quality pirate
products being imported into Australia.
11.84 Submissions in response to the Draft Report voiced similar concerns.
Dataflow submitted that (p. 2):
The removal of the parallel importation provisions of the
without doubt, decrease the fear that a potential pirate may have of
prosecution. Copyright owners based overseas, who look
to their exclusive
licensee to act on their behalf, will now have no-one who will be interested in
doing so if the parallel importation
provisions of the
are removed
. . . There is considerable fear of Australia being placed in the same category
as Thailand, Hong Kong and Indonesia,
in relation to the seriousness of piracy
in the eyes of an overseas publishers [sic].
11.85 A recent report in the
Australian Financial Review
(7 November
1994) echoes the fears of piracy referred to by Dataflow in its submission. The
article notes the views of Mr Ron Eckstrom
(Vice-President of the Business
Software Alliance) that (p.49):
. . . the biggest counterfeiting operations are centred in Taiwan and
mainland China, which arrange worldwide distribution for copied
manuals in packages almost identical to software companies’ own
11.86 The Committee recognises that one of the principal differences of
opinion between those in favour and those against allowing
parallel importation
is in their assessment of the level of piracy. In the Committee’s opinion
piracy should not be treated
dismissively and all possible measures should be
taken to reduce its incidence and discourage its growth. Submissions such as
from Dataflow lend considerable weight to the Committee’s view that
the threat of piracy is a principal reason why parallel
importation of computer
programs and associated manuals should not be allowed.
Border control provisions—section
11.87 Section 135 of the Act presently contains provisions that allow a
of copies of a work
where if the work had been made in Australia by the person importing it, it
would have constituted an infringement
of copyright in the work. The section
only applies to printed copies of a published literary, dramatic or musical
work. As discussed
in the Draft Report (paragraphs 11.16, 11.61–11.69) and
as mentioned above (paragraph 11.07), s.135 has been the subject of
inquiry and
recommendation by both this Committee and the PSA. The Committee recommended in
(i) section 135 be amended to cover copies, whether printed or not, of all
works and subject matter; and
(ii) section 135 be amended to empower the exclusive licensee, as well as the
owner of copyright to give a notice
11.88 Proposed amendments to s.135 have been included in the
Trade Organization Amendments) Bill 1994
, as a result of the conclusion of the
GATT TRIPS Agreement. These amendments are outlined in paragraphs
11.89–11.90. The Committee
is of the view that these amendments, if
passed, would not of themselves provide sufficient protection for copyright
owners against
the threat from imported pirate copies of their software.
Proposed amendments to section 135
implements the requirements of the TRIPS agreement in relation to border
enforcement provisions. Accordingly, as explained by the
Explanatory Memorandum,
this Bill will amend the Act to extend copyright-owner initiated Customs seizure
under s.135 of the Act from
application only to printed works to the
interception of unauthorised imports of all copyright materials including
artistic works,
sound recordings, computer disks, audio tapes, videotapes,
disks, films, CD ROMs and other forms of storage of copies of works, films
sound recordings. The Bill also makes it clear that the “owner” of
the copyright in copyright material includes an
exclusive licensee of the
possible for an exclusive
licensee to initiate seizure of copyright materials by
giving a notice to the customs authorities. The Committee notes that the Bill
effectively implements both of the Committee’s 1988 recommendations. The
Bill also introduces new provisions relating to seizure
11.90 The Explanatory Memorandum to the Bill explains the proposed changes as
follows (p. 7, paragraphs 28–30):
Article 51 of the TRIPS Agreement provides that Members shall extend border
enforcement measures to the full range of copyright material
covered by TRIPS.
There is no limitation in the TRIPS Agreement that the copyright material
referred to in Article 51 of TRIPS is
to be printed and published.
In accordance with the TRIPS provisions the amendments under clause 8 of the
Bill extend the border seizure provisions to all copyright
works whether
published or not but do so within the context of a new scheme which places the
onus on copyright owners to commence
proceedings in court for infringement of
By contrast with the existing provision under which seizure is determinative,
and which is understood to have never been utilised,
customs seizure under
amended s.135 will operate as merely a means of holding the copies pending a
decision of a court. If no proceedings
are instituted and there is no other
reason to hold the copies they will be released after the seizure period.
11.91 Although the Committee appreciates that the proposed amendments will
extend the border enforcement provisions to computer programs,
the Committee
believes that these provisions will not alter the fundamental difficulty for
product from legitimate product as it
enters Australia and notifying ACS accordingly. Reliance on s.135 if controls
over parallel
importation were to be removed would not offer a reasonable
alternative for copyright owners of computer programs. Identification
and counterfeit articles remains a very difficult practical problem which is
exacerbated if articles other than those
manufactured on behalf of Australian
were able to gather
reliable information concerning alleged importation of
pirate goods, s.135 will remain of little assistance to them. The
Committee’s
view is supported by DITARD’s paper which states (p.
The nature of the current importation provisions gives the software copyright
owners and their distribution channels control over
the delivery of their
intellectual property to the end-user. The distribution industry can verify
whether software is a legitimate
copy with relative ease . . . Parallel
importation has the potential to alter this situation significantly, as software
imported outside this informational network. Information on the
legitimacy of the imported product will no longer be contained within
distribution chain. Consequently, the commercial opportunities to sell pirated
product will expand as the risk of detection declines.
11.92 Finally the Committee notes, as did Dataflow in its submission (see
paragraph 11.84), that the removal of the parallel importation
provisions would
remove the incentive for an overseas copyright owner to appoint an exclusive
licensee in Australia. Without a local
presence such copyright owners may face
greater difficulties in detecting the importation of pirate copies of their
software and
enforcing their rights.
Conclusion and recommendations
11.93 As the preceding discussion in this chapter indicates, there are a
number of competing factors to be evaluated in any decision
concerning controls
over parallel importation. The Committee has been required to weigh up the
interests of and anticipate the future
effects on consumers of computer goods,
local producers of computer software and multinational producers of software and
their locally
appointed licensees/distributors. The broader issues of piracy of
computer software and the relationship between competition and
price within the
computer industry have also been key elements to be taken account of by the
Committee. One of the greatest difficulties
facing the Committee has been
anticipating what the future effects of allowing or not allowing parallel
importation may be and of
judging the merits of the arguments made to the
Committee as to possible consequences of adopting either course. In deciding
the restrictions on parallel importation should remain (thereby reversing
its recommendation in the Draft Report), the Committee
has taken most account of
the threat of an increase in the distribution of pirated software if parallel
importation was allowed and
the doubt cast by recent advice prepared by the
Attorney-General’s Department
on the compatibility of the
majority’s recommendation in the Draft Report (that parallel importation
be allowed from those countries
which are the main source of computer programs
used in Australia) with Australia’s obligations under the TRIPS Agreement.
downward trend in prices of computer software, which the Committee
understands had started before the PSA’s inquiry in 1992
and which is
continuing, is also a major factor that has led to the change in the
Committee’s recommendation.
11.94 On the whole the Committee does not think the justifications for
allowing parallel importation have been demonstrated with any
certainty. The claim by the PSA that prices of computer software are higher in
Australia than in the United States because
of control over parallel importation
is open to doubt, particularly given the evidence of a downward trend in prices
which has occurred
despite the provisions. Furthermore the economic predictions
in the PSA’s report have received no support in the submissions
to the Committee following the Draft Report; on the contrary its predictions
have been the subject of considerable criticism.
The Committee acknowledges that
parallel importation provisions may potentially be exercised in an
anti-competitive manner, but does
not think that this alone justifies their
repeal, particularly given the absence of evidence of instances of
anti-competitive exercise.
If there were such instances, it would be preferable
to deal with them under legislation which implements competition policy, such
Trade Practices Act 1974
. The Committee also recognises that the
benefits of allowing parallel importation may be impossible to measure in
economic terms
(and therefore are unable to be demonstrated in any conclusive
way) the Committee still holds the view that the possible detrimental
the computer software industry (ie, piracy) and the wider community (ie,
employment) of allowing parallel importation
are reason enough to retain
controls over parallel importation.
11.95 As regards the broad issue of the price of computer programs and
competition within the computer industry, one member of the
Committee is of the
view that pricing is not the major issue. That member is concerned that the
tension between competition policy
and the exclusive rights of the licensee must
be addressed so as to avoid anti-competitive conduct by the licensee, which in
view of that member of the Committee is not addressed sufficiently by the
existing provisions of the
Trade Practices Act 1974
and the various State
anti-competition laws. While agreeing that parallel importation remain
prohibited, that member of the Committee
believes that other mechanisms should
be put in place to ensure competitive performance within the market and to
ensure that licensees
adequately serve the interests of consumers by attending
to or providing, among other matters, the availability of product, a range
product, sufficient backup support, appropriate adjustments to programs to suit
the needs of the local market, regular updating
of product, reporting as to
availability and generally providing comprehensive service to customers. The
matters just listed are
indicative only of possible consumer demands and
concerns and in no way are intended to be an exhaustive list of matters that may
need to be addressed.
11.96 All members of the Committee now recommend that the restriction on
parallel importation of computer programs remain unchanged
(Recommendation
2.39(a)). The Committee also gave consideration to whether it should recommend a
further review of the parallel importation
provisions, bearing in mind any
changes in the local computer software market. Some members of the Committee
recommend such a review
in three years time, while one member (who was part of
the majority in the Draft Report ie. in favour of lifting the restrictions
parallel importation) recommends that the position be reviewed in two years
time. (Recommendation 2.39(b)).
Other issues
Computer manuals
11.97 In February and March 1990 Microsoft and Infomagic wrote to the
Attorney-General concerning possible implications of the then
Amendment Bill 1990
(the Books Bill) on the importation of computer manuals.
Copies of those letters were referred to the Committee. The Committee notes
the provisions of the Books Bill were given effect by the
which came into effect
on 23 December 1991.
11.98 It was submitted by Microsoft and Infomagic that most computer programs
are provided to users as part of a package which includes
a detailed manual
which explains to users how to get the most out of their programs and to help
them recover from errors. The manuals
are not usually sold separately from the
computer programs. Infomagic commented that one of the factors which prevents
the widespread
use of illegal copies of computer programs within Australia is
the fact that a user of an illegal copy will in most cases not have
access to a
11.99 The amendments to the Act introduced by the
provide in s.44A, among other things, that a copyright owner’s
control over importation is removed where:
(a) a book is first published outside Australia and not published in
Australia within 30 days;
(b) a book first published in Australia or published in Australia within 30
days of first publication overseas is not obtainable
from the copyright owner
for 90 days; and
(c)where a retailer receives a specific order for a single copy.
11.100 Infomagic and Microsoft submitted that:
(a) as it is the practice of some computer program distributors and
publishers not to sell manuals separately from the licence of
computer programs
it is arguable that they have never been supplied in Australia and therefore
suppliers would lose their rights
to control the importation of those
(b) the industry produces different versions of products for different
markets. Unless manuals were excluded from the amendments
various versions would
never be published in Australia, and could therefore be imported at any time;
(c) it is not possible to publish computer programs simultaneously around the
