world. The publication of computer programs involves
the progressive testing of
programs in different markets until any errors are found and removed.
Simultaneous publication could result
in the delivery of product to the
Australian market that was not entirely ready.
Accordingly, Microsoft and Infomagic urged in 1990 that manuals normally
provided as part of a computer program package be excluded
from the operation of
the proposals contained in the Books Bill.
11.101 The Committee notes that the amendments to the Act introduced by the
exclude computer manuals (see s.44A(9) of
the Act). The concerns expressed above are based on the assumption that there is
in the US and other markets that manuals for computer programs may be
purchased there separately from the computer program itself.
The submissions do
not make it clear whether this is the case or not and the Committee was not
otherwise informed in the matter.
(The Committee did not receive any specific
comments on its recommendation in the Draft Report regarding computer manuals or
other comments in relation to manuals). However, on the basis that such
manuals are available on the US market the question arises
why they are not
available for separate sale in Australia. Manuals are used by computer program
developers for the purpose of reverse
engineering and the development of
interoperable products. Accordingly, the Committee recognises that the
Australian computer program
industry needs access to up-to-date manuals so that
developers can react to trends in computer program design. The Committee does
not accept that manuals are used only by consumers who want to use them in
conjunction with unauthorised copies of computer programs.
11.102 In its interim report, the PSA noted that instruction manuals are
invariably included in the purchase price of packaged computer
(p. 153). Such manuals are essential to explain the installation and
operation of the computer program. As mentioned in
paragraph 11.101, the
relaxation of the importation provisions of the Act as they apply to books does
not extend to computer manuals.
In its final report the PSA recommended that
s.44A(9) of the Act be amended to include a manual sold with a computer program,
use in connection with that program in the definition of ‘book’
(p. 81). The Committee notes that such an amendment would
prevent the owner of
effect of the PSA’s recommendation
by prohibiting importation of computer
program packages that contain computer manuals by relying on his or her
manuals. A corresponding amendment would also have to be made
in respect to the published edition copyright in s.112A(9).
11.103 Having reconsidered the its position in relation to parallel
importation generally, the Committee still adheres to the view
it expressed in
the Draft Report and as stated above in paragraph 11.101 that the use made of
manuals is not just by consumers in
conjunction with unauthorised copies of
computer programs but rather by the industry for development reasons.
Accordingly, the Committee
recommends that computer manuals that are available
separately from computer programs, should be able to be imported in the same
as other books (Recommendation 2.39(c)).
Second-hand computer programs
11.104 The issue of the importation of second-hand computer programs was only
raised in a limited context to the Committee, namely,
coin-operated amusement
machines. The Committee received a number of submissions on this issue from the
coin-operated amusement machine
industry. Coin-operated amusement machines
provide games which are based on often highly sophisticated computer programs.
submitted that the local industry is totally dependent on overseas
manufacturers for product. Games often have only a short term
novelty value,
sometimes lasting no more than a few weeks. Following this period the games are
made available on the overseas market
second-hand. Often only the printed
circuit board with the necessary electronics is sold and prices are reduced to
reflect the lowered
earning potential of the game. In response to this practice,
a number of Australian importers of those products have established
distribution agreements with overseas manufacturers and have begun asserting
their exclusive distribution rights. This
has prevented other importers from
bringing into Australia second-hand games programs (incorporated in the printed
circuit boards)
in competition with the games controlled under the exclusive
distribution agreements.
11.105 These submissions raise two issues. First, should second-hand programs
be excluded from the operation of the importation provisions?
On this point, as
a matter of policy, the Committee is not aware of any reason for treating
second-hand computer programs any differently
to “new” computer
programs. Accordingly, the Committee contemplates that its recommendation
concerning the importation
provisions should apply equally to second-hand
computer programs (Recommendation 2.39(d)). The second issue is that of the
of the CL Act and the
. This arises because, as understood
by the Committee, most computer games are stored in integrated circuits which in
turn are attached
to printed circuit boards. Having regard to this the Committee
is of the view that in such cases second-hand computer programs should
with in accordance with its recommendation concerning the overlap of the CL Act
in Chapter 10 Importation of computer manuals
11.106 The PSA Report also raised the issue of penalties (p. 83). It
suggested that different penalties could be prescribed in respect
copying and piracy. The Committee has already noted that the Act distinguishes
between own-use copying, which is a civil
infringement of copyright, and piracy
which is a criminal offence (paragraph 11.78). In civil proceedings plaintiffs
are awarded
damages determined by the courts having regard to the damage done to
their intellectual property. In piracy prosecutions criminal
sanctions will
apply. In this sense different penalties already apply in respect of these
activities and no change to the present
situation appears to be needed.
Educational Use of Computer Programs
12.01 The Committee noted in its Issues Paper that the value of computer
programs in education is widely recognised (p. 21). This
in turn has led to an
increasing demand by educational institutions for more ready access to programs
suitable for their education
12.02 Submissions to the Committee in response to the Issues Paper, and some
of those following the public hearings, identified a
number of issues of
particular concern to both educational institutions, as users of computer
programs, and those computer program
manufacturers who supply educational
institutions. A number of submissions drew to the Committee’s attention
the fact that
in most cases schools use computer programs on personal computers
and do not rely on large mainframe systems for teaching purposes.
In this regard
school use of computer programs can be distinguished from some of the
applications in tertiary institutions. There
is also an increasing tendency for
tertiary institutions to write their own materials. As authors, they are keen to
ensure a balance
between reasonable access to information and fair remuneration
for the provision of it.
12.03 The requirements of schools, both primary and secondary, in respect of
the use of computer programs were concisely set out in
the Australian Education
Council’s (AEC) submission to the Committee (pp. 6–7). Those
requirements are:
(a) Education departments at system or local level need to be able to use
software at different locations for preview, evaluation,
trial and demonstration
(b) Education centres, which provide continuing education for teachers, and
consultants need to be able to use software to instruct
in-service teachers on
the range and use of software in schools.
(c) Schools do not always need multiple copies of software but they need to
be able to buy a program with a right to make a backup
copy or to be able to
make a backup for archival purposes in case of disk failure.
(d) Schools lend a variety of educational resources to each other and obtain
other resources from departmental libraries and education/resource
supplement their own resources. They need to be able to borrow computer software
in the same way. This is of crucial importance
to small schools who do not have
the financial resources to buy the large number of software packages needed and
often only require
them for one or two weeks a year.
(e) Teachers often need to preview software before purchasing it for a school
in order to see if it fits into the curriculum needs
of that school.
(f) Teachers need to preview/learn software before use in the classroom. This
sometimes necessitates using the package at home —
on a home computer.
(g) Primary schools frequently need multiple copies of curriculum software,
dependent upon the number of computers in a school, and
the size of the school.
Teachers may choose to use the software across the same year level or in the
case of word processing and
utility software across a number of year levels.
Even if only a single copy of software is required, it will almost certainly
to be used on different computers at different locations within the
(h) Secondary schools operate within a different environment. The computers
are often sited in a lab situation where there are a large
number of computers.
Teachers have classes for a limited time, and it would be an advantage to have
multiple copies available.
(i) There is a need on occasions to use software on a network where a number
of users at different computers can access, via the system,
the one piece of
software at the same time.
(j) Students need to copy software for research and study purposes.
12.04 The use of computer programs by higher education institutions was set
out in the submission to the Committee from the Australian
Vice-Chancellor’s Committee and the Australian Committee of Directors and
Principals Ltd (AVCC–ACDP) which made the following
comments (p. 1,
The use of computer software by higher education institutions has grown
markedly over the past few years. Courses involving computer-related
now extend to virtually all disciplines. Like most large organisations,
computer-based systems are heavily relied upon
for administration. Libraries in
higher education institutions use computer-based systems for library and
information storage and
retrieval and for computer assisted learning (CAL).
Library systems are generally very complex. Searching of remote and CD ROM
is now commonplace in most higher education institution libraries.
Many institutions have CAL networks with internally produced and
computer-assisted learning programs running from a hard disc. The role played by
computer software in the research activities
of higher education institutions
has also increased. Apart from providing a basis for software development
itself, commercial computer
programs are used for such tasks as the correlation
and analysis of data, simulation and modelling, and computer-aided design.
THE PRICE OF COMPUTER PROGRAMS
12.05 One of the issues raised before the Committee was that schools should
have access to computer programs for a “realistic”
fee which would
enable the school to run the computer programs on multiple computers. In
response to this claim Microsoft Pty Ltd
noted that no other copyright subject
matter supplied to schools is subject to a test of whether the price is fair.
Nor are other
essential services and materials supplied to educational
institutions at a special price. It was also submitted that all major computer
program suppliers already have arrangements whereby educational institutions,
including students and members of staff, are able to
obtain computer programs
more cheaply than others. In effect, access to computer programs should continue
to be determined by competitive
forces only.
12.06 One criticism that was levelled at schools in respect of the purchase
of computer programs was that in many instances they failed
appropriately for their computer program requirements. It was claimed that in
some cases schools purchased expensive hardware
only to find they had
insufficient funds to purchase the necessary computer programs to utilise the
initial purchase properly. One
submission to the Committee commented that it was
really a matter for the responsible State governments to ensure there was a
level of funding available for the purchase of computer programs by
12.07 The Committee notes the concerns of some schools and the computer
program producers on this issue. The Committee is not satisfied
that providing
computer programs at a cheaper rate for schools is justified. Given that no
other copyright subject matter is subject
to a “fair price” test, it
would be unfair to impose such a direction on computer program producers.
12.08 In the Draft Report, at paragraph 12.07, reference was made to the
decision of the majority of the Committee with respect to
parallel importation.
The Committee said:
The Committee’s majority recommendation concerning parallel importation
has regard to the PSA’s report on the prices of
computer programs.
Implementation of that recommendation, which accords with the PSA’s
recommendation, is expected by the majority
of the Committee to bring down the
price of computer programs.
12.09 The Committee has now formed the view that parallel importation should
not be allowed (see the discussion in Chapter 11). Whether
or not this
recommendation is followed, the Committee notes that submissions made to the
Committee following the Draft Report indicate
that the price of computer
programs has fallen (Submissions of AIAA and Discussion Papers of DITARD and
Treasury). As such, the Committee
recommends no change to the current
arrangements. The Committee hopes that this trend in the software industry will
continue so that
educational institutions are able to benefit from lower prices
of computer programs.
12.10 Submissions to the Committee concerning the terms of licensing
agreements concluded with computer program suppliers were generally
critical of
the scope and flexibility of the agreements and the willingness of suppliers to
meet the needs of the educational institutions.
In particular it was claimed
that the terms of licences for the use of computer programs were poorly defined.
The Committee notes,
however, that this may not be a universal problem. One
computer program supplier, Lotus Development Pty Ltd, commented that at least
relation to the terms of its licence agreements, it had not been made aware of
any particular difficulties encountered by educational
institutions.
12.11 It was also submitted that licences failed to address issues such as
the storage and loan of computer software by school libraries
and resource
centres. It was submitted by the Minister for Education (WA) that the use of
computer programs by libraries and resource
centres should be clarified in
legislation, so that standard rules applied to educational institutions to
provide both protection
and to ensure compliance on the part of users. It was
also suggested that specific provisions allow educational institutions to make
back-up and multiple copies of programs, including libraries and resource
centres. Autodesk Australia commented that if there were
to be legislative
provision made for the loan of computer programs, it should ensure that the
The basis of such a calculation
should have regard to the period of use of a particular program.
12.12 At a more general level, the comments received from educational
institutions by the Committee on the terms of existing licence
agreements were
critical of the uncertainty of the meaning of terms such as “site
licence” between individual agreements.
Particular problems perceived by
educational institutions, including tertiary institutions, relate to site
licences for the use of
computer software on network systems which in most cases
requires the making of ephemeral
multiple copies of computer programs
where the software is downloaded to a work station. It was also noted that the
concept of a site
licence was not particularly amenable to application where an
institution serviced external students. It was suggested by the Computers
Education Project (Ministry of Education, WA) that there is a need to develop
standard licences, which should be issued to State
or central educational
authorities. In this regard the Committee notes the comments in the submission
from the AEC that it intends
to negotiate a standard educational site licence
that would be acceptable to computer program suppliers across the country.
12.13 In its Issues Paper the Committee commented that the problems faced by
educational institutions could be approached in either
of these two ways
(pp. 22–23):
• by leaving the situation as it is and allowing educational
institutions and computer program manufacturers to negotiate a system
voluntary licences which cater for the specific needs of particular
institutions; or
• by introducing a statutory licence as has been done in respect of
works and broadcast audio–visual copyright materials.
12.14 These two options were canvassed at the public hearings held in Sydney
in 1990 and addressed in some of the submissions received
by the Committee
following those hearings. The majority of educational interests and computer
program suppliers expressed the view
strongly that a statutory licence scheme
was not the appropriate way of dealing with the issue of educational use of
computer programs.
The Australian Copyright Council submitted that a statutory
licence scheme is not an appropriate response to complaints about the
such a scheme is to meet circumstances where
the copyright owners are too
numerous and voluntary licences are impossible to negotiate, as indicated to the
Committee by the AVCC–ACDP,
in the case of computer programs, the relevant
12.15 The Committee also notes that, unlike other literary works, which may
be copied by educational institutions under the statutory
licence scheme
established in Part VB of the
(the Act), it would only
be in unusual circumstances that an educational institution would seek to copy
only part of a program. Usually
the whole program would be required. Special
circumstances would include those where part of a program may need to be copied
the purpose of teaching students about the function of the program rather
than using the program for the purpose for which it was
12.16 Having regard to the above considerations the Committee recommends, in
accordance with the expressed preference of most educational
institutions and
makers of computer programs, that a statutory licence scheme for the educational
use of computer programs should
not be established (Recommendation 2.40(a).
However, the Committee notes the past experiences of educational institutions in
to negotiate with copyright owners, and the efforts of both sides to
conclude mutually acceptable agreements. It therefore expresses
some doubt as to
the likelihood of both parties reaching agreement in the absence of a statutory
regime to fall back upon and recommends
that the Government should review the
situation in three years time to determine whether an optional statutory licence
scheme should
be introduced to encourage voluntary licensing (Recommendation
12.17 Educational institutions also raised three other issues namely backup
copying, liability for infringement and electrocopying
(in submissions following
the Draft Report). The issue of backup copying is discussed more fully at
paragraphs 10.13–10.17.
The issue of electrocopying is discussed more
fully from paragraph 14.09.
LIABILITY FOR
INFRINGEMENT—AUTHORISATION
12.18 Educational institutions raised a concern that they may be held liable
for authorising copyright infringement by making computing
facilities available
to staff and students and for failing to prevent copying by the use of those
facilities. Discussions at the
public hearings and submissions to the Committee
focussed on the need for a provision analogous to s.39A of the Act which
infringing copies of works made on machines installed in libraries and
12.19 Section 39A provides:
(a) a person makes an infringing copy of, or of part of, a work on a machine
for the making, by reprographic reproduction, of copies
of documents, being a
machine installed by or with the approval of the body administering a library or
archives, on the premises
of the library or archives or outside those premises
for the convenience of persons using the library or archives; and
(b) there is affixed to, or in close proximity to, the machine, in a place
readily visible to persons using the machine, a notice
of the prescribed
dimensions and in accordance with the prescribed form;
neither the body administering the library or archives nor the officer in
charge of the library or archives shall be taken to have

of the infringing copy by reason only that the copy was made on that
12.20 The Department of State Services (WA) submitted that the owner of a
computer program package should not be responsible for infringements
by other users of that package. The AVCC–ACDP also commented in its
submission that at many higher education institutions
there is multiple public
access to micro computer laboratories. It expressed concern that institutions
would be open to the possibility
of authorisation actions flowing from students
and staff copying programs using institutional equipment. Accordingly, it
recommended
an amendment of s.39A to include reference to the making of machine
readable copies by use of machines in libraries and higher education
institutions.
12.21 The Committee notes the view expressed in submissions that in some
cases educational institutions may have insufficient resources
to allocate to
the supervision of students to ensure that they do not infringe copyright in
computer programs. Nonetheless, it is
of the view that this in itself is not
sufficient justification to remove a copyright owners’ right to seek

recommends that no change be made to the Act in this respect (Recommendation
12.22 The Committee also notes the concerns expressed by educational
institutions about the uncertainty in the minds of educators
and administrators
of educational institutions as to what the law provides with respect to computer
programs. The Committee encourages
the various State Education Departments and
the Australian Vice-Chancellors Committee to embark upon and maintain a regular
of providing general advice to educational institutions as to their
rights and obligations under the Act. Given the complex nature
of the Act it
would be prudent for those bodies to seek appropriate legal advice in relation
to any matter that requires clarification.
Works Made with the Assistance of Computer Programs and
Computer-Generated Materials
13.01 In its Draft Report the Committee noted that the use of computers to
produce various materials is becoming more and more commonplace
and that the
technology involved is becoming increasingly sophisticated. Computer programs
are used to manipulate databases to create
valuable output often without the
expenditure of much identifiable human skill or effort. Examples of these
include programs for
desk top publishing, programs which facilitate the
presentation of financial, statistical, meteorological and seismic geological
information, computer aided design packages and expert systems which are
increasingly being used to solve problems in a wide range
of areas including:
the diagnosis of medical conditions, legal advice and problems with
13.02 With respect to the examples given above it is clear that some of the
materials are created only with the assistance of computer
programs. For example
a literary work written using a personal computer and a word processing program
is clearly created using the
computer program as a tool. However, while this
example is clear cut the Committee recognises that in some cases, because of the
interactive nature of the technology used to create materials or because
computer programs are operating equipment automatically,
it will be more
difficult to separate the contribution of the user (if any) from that of the
computer/computer program. Furthermore,
the contributions of each to the final
product are likely to be of varying degrees in different circumstances. It can
also be argued
that in many cases materials generated by a programmed computer
would constitute an original literary, musical or artistic work if
they had been
created solely by a human author.
13.03 In the light of submissions made to the Committee since the Draft
Report the Committee has come to the view that a distinction
can and should be
drawn between those materials created with the assistance of computer programs
and those created by computer programs
in circumstances where there is no
identifiable human author (so called “computer-generated works”). To
determine how
such materials should be protected requires a consideration of
first, whether what is created is in fact an original copyright work,
secondly, if it is, who should be regarded as the author or alternatively, the
first owner of copyright. The following discussion
examines these issues in
respect of each of these types of materials.
MATERIALS CREATED WITH THE ASSISTANCE OF COMPUTER
13.04 Materials created with the assistance of computer programs include, as
in the example already given, literary works written
on a computer using a word
processing package, musical scores composed using musical software, and artistic
works such as engineering
design drawings, logos and images used in
advertisements. In cases such as these the computer and the relevant software
used by the creator of the work as a tool which facilitates the
creation of the work. In its submission dated 31 August 1993, Oracle
(Australia) Pty Limited (Oracle) raised the issue of the use of CASE (computer
aided software engineering) programs and the
need for both clients and
consultants who use such programs to be sure of the ownership of resulting code.
The Committee takes the
view that this is another example of materials created
with the assistance of a computer program. The ownership and protection of
materials should be determined in each case having regard to the principles
discussed below.
13.05 The issue of who should be the author of materials produced with the
aid of a computer was considered in the UK by the Whitford
Committee in 1977
(Cmnd 6732). In its report the Whitford Committee stated (paragraphs
In this matter we feel the correct approach is to look on the computer as a
mere tool in much the same way as a slide rule or even,
in a simple sense, a
paint brush. A very sophisticated tool it may be, with considerable powers to
extend man’s capabilities
to create new works, but a tool
nevertheless.
On that basis it is clear that the author of the output can be none other
than the person or persons who devised the instructions
and originated the data
used to control and condition the computer to produce the particular result. In
many cases it will be a matter
of joint authorship. We realise that this in
itself can cause problems but no more than in some other fields, and we are not
there is a need for special treatment.
13.06 The issue of authorship of materials created with the assistance of a
computer program was also considered by the Commission
of the European
Communities. In its
Green Paper on Copyright and the Challenge of
Technology—Copyright Issues Requiring Immediate Action,
1988 (COM (88)
172–final ) the Commission took the view that (paragraph 5.6.26):
. . . it is those who use the programmed computer, which is essentially a
tool, who should be regarded as entitled to protection.
The view taken by the Commission appears to acknowledge that the more
appropriate analogy is to regard the programmed computer, rather
than a computer
by itself, as a tool.
13.07 In the Committee’s view the copyright protection of materials
created with the assistance of computer programs should
be based on the same
principles as apply to works created using traditional means. In other words, if
the materials produced with
the assistance of a computer program amount to an
original form of expression of an idea, and that expression comes within one of
the recognised categories of works protected under the
(the Act) the materials should also be protected as such, protection being
granted regardless of how much easier the author’s
task may have been made
by the use of the computer program.
Who should be the author of a work created with the
assistance of a computer program?
13.08 It follows from the discussion in paragraphs 13.05-13.07 that the
person who uses the programmed computer as a tool in order
to create a new
13.09 Under the Act “author” is not defined, except in relation
to photographs (s.10(1)). Section 32 provides, in effect,
that copyright
subsists in a work of which the author is a qualified person. Section 32(4)
defines “qualified person”
as meaning:
. . . an Australian citizen, an Australian protected person or a person
resident in Australia.
Accordingly, the author of a copyright work created with the assistance of a
computer program must be a natural person. Of course,
the copyright owner may be
a natural or legal person (s.35).
13.10 Matters such as the ownership and duration of copyright in such works
created with the assistance of a computer program should
be determined according
to the existing provisions of the Act.
MATERIALS CREATED BY COMPUTER PROGRAMS
(“COMPUTER-GENERATED WORKS”)
13.11 In contrast to the situation described already, where a work is clearly
the product of the labour of a human author and the
computer program is a mere
aid, is the circumstance where it is difficult, or not possible, to say that the
computer output is the
result of the labour of an individual or individuals.
Perhaps the simplest example to consider is that of satellite images of things
such as weather patterns, vegetation, and geological formations. In many cases
the data that makes up these images is collected automatically
by remote sensors
on satellites. The information is automatically processed by specialised
computer programs and the final image
down loaded or printed out in hard copy
form either automatically or at the press of a button.
13.12 Another example would be the report generated by an expert system.
There are two components to an expert system, a so called
knowledge base, and an
inference engine. The knowledge base is in effect a data base that contains the
knowledge of one or more experts
in a particular field. The inference engine
contains the problem solving knowledge that enables the expert system to decide
apply the specialist knowledge to the facts the user supplies in order to
solve the problem posed by the user. It is a computer program
that applies the
knowledge to the problems. The solution suggested, or the report produced,
depends on the data entered by the user.
In most cases the data entered will be
determined by the circumstances in which the expert system is being used rather
result of any selection on the part of the user.
The nature of computer-generated material
13.13 A number of submissions received by the Committee following the release
of the Draft Report did not support the Committee’s
classification of
“computer-generated works” as “works” under the Act. The
Australian Copyright Council (ACC)
was opposed to the notion that an
“author” could be something other than a human and therefore had
difficulty with the
Committee’s recommendation that
“computer-generated works” with no identifiable author could be
protected as “works”
under the Act, especially as the requirement
for originality in “works” may not be satisfied. The ACC appeared to
suggesting that “computer-generated works” should be protected by
the Act as subject matter other than works.
13.14 The Committee has considered the submission of the ACC to the effect
that “computer-generated works” are not properly
a category of
“works”, lacking as they do a human author. The Committee agrees
with the ACC’s submission. It notes
that while there are a variety of ways
in which copyright systems around the world provide protection to the various
subject matters
they encompass, those materials which are protected as
“works” have a human author. This would appear to be consistent
the principles embodied in the Berne Convention for the Protection of Literary
and Artistic Works. The other requirement for
of authorship must satisfy some notion of originality ie. be the product of some
skill and labour
on the part of the author.
13.15 As already noted, one of the features of computer-generated material is
that it has no identifiable human author. The examples
given in paragraphs 13.11
and 13.12 are, in the Committee’s view, clear examples of
computer-generated materials that, but
for the lack of a human author, could be
classified as original copyright works. A satellite image is not the product of
of any one author—in this respect it differs from a
photograph—nor can it, in the Committee’s view, be properly
considered to be the result of the combined efforts of a number of joint
authors. A report generated by an expert system is also,
on closer examination,
one that lacks a human author. The compiler of the knowledge base, and the
author of the inference engine
computer program cannot be regarded as authors of
the output of the expert system as that output will be determined in each case
by the data that is entered. It is also difficult to attribute authorship to the
person entering the data (the user) as the data
entered will necessarily be
determined by the circumstances that have given rise to the need to use the
expert system rather than
any process of selection involving the exercise of
skill and judgment by the user.
13.16 In so far as these types of computer-generated materials are deserving
of protection it seems to the Committee that the protection
that they should
attract is more akin to that extended to neighbouring rights. In other words the
protection that is extended to
performers, producers of phonograms and
broadcasting organisations. That protection emerged in response to the
development of new
techniques of disseminating cultural materials, and the
development of technology that enabled the making of accurate and cheap copies.
One of the characteristics of this type of protection is that there is not the
same need for originality. The term of protection
is also shorter which, as is
the case with industrial property protection, reflects the fact that the object
of protection is investment,
rather than creativity.
13.17 Under the Act the neighbouring rights of producers of phonograms (sound
recordings) and broadcasters are protected as subject
matter other than works.
Having regard to this and the reasons given above, the Committee considers that
it is appropriate to afford
protection, as subject matter other than works, to a
new class of subject matter to be described as “computer-generated
(Recommendation 2.42(a)).
13.18 In the Draft Report the Committee recommended the adoption of a
definition of “computer-generated work” along the
lines of the one
used in s.178 of the UK
(paragraph 13.08). Since the Committee has reconsidered its recommendation
regarding computer-generated material it now
recommends that the following
definition be added to the Act (Recommendation 2.42(b)):
“computer-generated”, in relation to computer-generated material,
means that the material is generated by computer in
circumstances such that
there is no human author of the material.
Ownership of copyright in computer-generated
13.19 In the Draft Report the Committee also considered the question of
identifying the author of a “computer-generated work”.
It observed
that the author could be one of the following four parties (paragraph
(a) the programmer or owner of the copyright in the programs that assisted in
creation of the work;
(b)the provider of the data;
(c)the user of the computer/computer program; or
(d)the investor or owner of the computer/computer program.
13.20 In light of the Committee’s recommendation above, regarding the
classification of computer-generated material, it is no
longer necessary to
consider these parties in relation to the question of authorship. However, this
division of interests is helpful
in determining who should be the first owner of
of computer-generated material as subject matter other than works it is the
investor or owner of the computer/computer program who
should be the owner of
the copyright in any material generated by its use. It will be that person who
makes the necessary arrangements
for the creation of the material.
13.21 In the Draft Report the Committee also referred to s.9(3) of the UK CDP
Act which defines who the author of a “computer-generated
and recommended that a similar definition be adopted by the Act. The Committee
noted that the treatment of authorship
in relation to “computer-generated
works” equated with that for films and sound recordings. As a result of
in its recommendation above, namely, that computer-generated material
be protected by the Act as subject matter other than works,
the Committee now
recommends that the Act be amended to provide with respect to authorship of
computer-generated material that (Recommendation
In the case of computer-generated material, the author shall be taken to be
the person by whom the arrangements necessary for the
creation of the material
are undertaken.
13.22 In its submission the Australian Information Industry Association
Limited (AIIA) did not believe that “computer-generated
material) should receive special protection. It identified a number of practical
difficulties if such “works”
were protected, such as, defining if
and when a “work” is computer-generated; defining who
“arranges” for
the creation of the work; distinguishing which parts
of the expression in an application are computer-generated, which are generic
industry standards and which are uniquely “authored” by humans. The
Committee notes these concerns but takes the view
that the drawing of a
distinction between materials created with the assistance of a computer program
and those that are computer-generated
is one that will be able to be determined
in each case having regard to the circumstances. Similarly the determination of
of who is the person that arranges for the creation of the
work/materials should not be any more difficult than determining the identity
the maker of a cinematograph film for the purposes of the Act.
Duration of protection of computer-generated
13.23 The Committee has also considered the issue of the appropriate period
for the duration of copyright in computer-generated material.
Having regard to
its recommendation that computer-generated material be protected as subject
matter other than works the Committee
is of the view that copyright should
subsist in such material for 25 years from the end of the year in which the
computer-generated
material is made (Recommendation 2.42(d)).
Works Stored in Computer Memory: Databases
INTRODUCTORY COMMENTS
14.01 Storage of works in computer memory is now a commonplace occurrence.
Scanning equipment has made it possible for private individuals
to transfer
printed works to computer storage almost as easily as making a photocopy. Data
can be “downloaded” from a
and freely manipulated to suit the user’s wishes. Commercial interests
make works available
either through connection to a remote database or through
the supply of a copy in various machine readable formats. Multi-media works
produced containing text, sound and graphics. Interactive multi-media works
permit the user to move about freely within the work
and create new combinations
of sounds and images to match the user’s requirements. Word processing
programs are so pervasive,
being found in computers down to the cheapest and
simplest home computer, that a substantial and rapidly increasing proportion of
all words in print have reached that state via a word processor. Every word
processor has a computer memory, however rudimentary
or sophisticated, and
printing businesses of any size print from manuscript in electronic form or
otherwise employ computerised typesetting
or formatting. The Committee is under
no illusion about the universality of use of storage on computer memory in the
production of
the printed word.
14.02 Where works are stored in computer memory for commercial purposes,
access to those works and their use are in most cases covered
agreements. The Committee does not propose to consider the adequacy or
appropriateness of these contractual agreements
for the protection of the
authors of works stored in computer memory insofar as they control use by the
contracted user. Whether
use by others is legitimate will be determined by the
terms of the licence and the circumstances of that use.
14.03 In its Draft Report, the Committee commented that the facilities for
and printing material
stored in computer memory were still new enough to dazzle the average person.
The Committee noted, however,
that the electronically stored databases
themselves were not fundamentally different from databases that have been
compiled through
vastly slower, non-electronic means in times past. There was no
shortage of examples of large non-electronic databases which raised
issues in relation to authorship, ownership and duration of copyright
protection. Such databases, comprising cards or files,
might be constantly
updated, be created by numerous people and be accessible to many others who were
jointly working on them. The
information that they contained might be selected
and combined in new, previously unthought of ways. The Committee said that it
to draw attention to this at the outset of its discussion of databases to
indicate what had guided its approach in seeking to isolate
the fundamental
differences between storage and manipulation of material in computer memory and
such treatment of material in non-electronic
form. The Committee did not regard
electronic storage as necessarily raising a whole set of unprecedented copyright
14.04 In his comments on the Draft Report, Mr David Lindsay, Research
Associate at the Centre for Media and Telecommunications Law
and Policy, Law
School, University of Melbourne (who presented his paper at the CIRCIT seminar
on the Draft Report in September 1993)
felt that the capacity of the user to
select information from a computerised database was so powerful, that making
protection of
such databases depend on their originality by reason of the
selection and arrangement of the data ignored the reality that the user,
needs. All the operator had to do was to ensure
that the database was
comprehensive and up to date. Similar views were expressed in the submission by
Messrs John Houghton and Robert
Reid–Smith from the Centre for
International Research on Communication and Information Technologies (CIRCIT).
14.05 The Committee acknowledges that the power put in the hands of computer
data should not be
understated. However, in so saying, it does not think that this should
overshadow the creativity of conceiving
the idea of the contents of a
contents—and, for that matter,
choosing (or even creating) the software by
which users may search it for their individual needs. On the other hand, the
acknowledges that some computer databases, if easily established or
comprising a certain kind of data, may lack sufficient originality
entitled to copyright protection. The Committee has, later in this chapter,
drawn attention to the EC
Amended Proposal for a Council Directive on the
legal Protection of Databases
(COM(93) 464 final—SYN 393, October
1993) (Directive on Databases) which proposes a form of
sui generis
protection for non-original databases (see paragraph 14.75 below).
14.06 From a copyright perspective the protection of works stored in computer
memory raises several issues:
whether the scope of the rights afforded to authors of works
aggregated in databases is adequate, having regard to any new uses available
works stored on computer;
whether the authors of computerised databases, ie, collections
of works and other materials stored in computer memory, need protection
additional to that afforded by present legislation;
whether the authors of interactive multi-media materials
need protection additional to that afforded by present legislation; and
whether there are now new kinds of works not covered by
present copyright legislation.
14.07 Submissions received by the Committee also raised questions concerning
the need for a statutory licence to govern the input
and retrieval of works into
and from computer memory (see paragraphs 14.09–14.11), the rights of
authors whose works are stored
in computer memory (discussed from paragraph
14.09), as well as the need for rights in respect of screen displays (paragraph
Submissions from the Australian Copyright Council (ACC), the Australian
Information Industry Association (AIIA), the Copyright Agency
Limited (CAL), IBM
Australia Limited, the Western Australian Government Department of Computing and
Information Technology (WADCIT)
and the Western Australian Government Department
of State Services (WADSS) all supported the principle of protection for
CURRENT PROTECTION OF WORKS STORED IN COMPUTER
(the Act) currently extends protection to
all forms of original works stored in computer memory. An original work created
use of a computer and therefore first fixed in storage in its memory
will be protected under the Act. Section 22 provides that a
work is made when it
is first reduced to writing or to some other material form and, as a result of
the 1984 amendments, s.10(1)
provides that material form:
. . . includes any form (whether visible or not) of storage from which the
work or adaptation . . . can be reproduced.
As discussed in Chapters 5 and 6, it is clear that these provisions extend
stored in a
form which is not perceptible to the human senses.
RIGHTS IN WORKS STORED IN COMPUTER MEMORY
A USE RIGHT
14.09 In its submission to the Committee CAL noted that the use of analogue
and optical character recognition (OCR) scanners enable
users to “capture,
store, view, print and modify documents”. CAL referred to the use of this
technology as “electrocopying”.
Electrocopying enables users to
transfer copyright and other material from hard copy form to machine-readable
form, from one machine-readable
form to another and from machine-readable form
to hard-copy form. The Committee understands that scanning technology is
rapidly and, indeed, that the latest generation of photocopying
machine in fact scans the material and stores it on a database prior
to printing
the required copy, with the obvious capacity thereby to retain what is scanned
in electronic memory.
14.10 The Draft Report noted (paragraph 14.08) CAL’s submission that,
to control these activities, copyright owners should be
granted a “use
right” which would entitle them to regulate the input and retrieval of
works into and from computer memory.
In the Draft Report, the Committee
commented that, having regard to the scope of the copyright owner’s right
under the Act
to reproduce his or her works in a material form, the Committee
was not persuaded that a “use right” such as that proposed
was justified or necessary. The Committee’s view was that both the acts of
storage and retrieval in hard-copy would
result in the work being reproduced in
a material form within the meaning of the Act. The Committee’s
recommendation in paragraph
6.55 and its comments in 6.56 that an inclusive
definition of “reproduction” be added to the Act puts it beyond
that digitisation of a work and hard-copy reproduction of a work existing
only in digital form would both be regarded as reproductions.
14.11 In commenting on the Draft Report, the Australian Council of Libraries
and Information Services (ACLIS) supported the Committee's
opposition to a new
use right, while in their submission Messrs Houghton and Reid–Smith
submitted that the fundamental difference
that they considered existed between
electronic databases and traditional hard-copy databases (see paragraph 14.04
above) justified
a use right for the former. CAL asked the Committee (p.1):
. . . to clarify the extent to which, under the existing provisions of the
for free use and remunerable statutory licences, works can be
stored, supplied or manipulated electronically.
CAL challenged the notion, which it attributed to the Committee, that
photocopying and electrocopying were “functionally equivalent”.
Committee is of the opinion that, having regard to its conclusions and
recommendations, made later in this chapter, regarding
rights of owners of
need to further consider an
additional right to use a work stored
electronically, such as has been proposed (see from paragraph 14.31)
(Recommendation 2.43).
THE REPRODUCTION RIGHT
Electrocopying
14.12 In the Draft Report, the Committee noted that CAL had submitted that
the reproduction of works by electrocopying should be the
subject of a statutory
licence to ensure that copyright owners received appropriate compensation for
the reproduction of their works.
In their submissions to the Committee the WADSS
and the WADCIT had suggested that the storage, retrieval of and access to works
computer memory should be left to contract and not controlled by legislation.
The Committee was of the view that the case for a statutory
licence had not been
made out in CAL’s submission. The
which responded to the rapid spread of photocopying, did not introduce any
general statutory licence for photocopying, although
it did introduce guidelines
on what copying would be a fair dealing, which is a provision of general
application. The Committee said
that it had seen nothing to suggest that the
nature or use of electrocopying was such as to warrant subjecting it to a
general licence
by virtue of its being an act of reproduction. It noted that it
was always open to copyright owners to enter into voluntary licensing
with people or organisations seeking to copy their material and it is therefore
with interest that the Committee notes
a press report late in 1994 that CAL has
concluded an agreement with the BHP Company voluntarily licensing it to
photocopy works
in the course of business. In commenting on the Draft Report, Mr
Lindsay thought that in view of the rapid growth in databases the
should reconsider its opposition to a statutory licence. Without more detailed
supporting argument, the Committee is not
convinced that it should do so. It is
also mindful that a general licence such as proposed by CAL might contravene
Australia’s
international obligations under the Berne Convention for the
Protection of Literary and Artistic Works (Berne Convention) (Recommendation
14.13 The Committee agrees with Mr Lindsay and Messrs Houghton and
Reid–Smith (paragraph 14.04 above) that the distinguishing
feature of a
computer database is the facility whereby it can be connected to a network of
terminals from any of which the database
can be accessed using computer program
(whether screen
display is a reproduction or a public performance is dealt with
below). This assessment was strongly endorsed by the Hydrographic
Service, Royal
Australian Navy (RAN). In the Draft Report, the Committee made comments to the
effect that the inclusion of a work
in an electronic database increased the
value of that work to both the database operator and the author because of the
viewing facilities of electronic databases, which conventional hard
copies of works lacked. The Committee also said that the increased
work in that form would give cause to a copyright owner to seek from the
reflected the value of the work
once included in the database. The Committee endorses the comments it made in
the Draft Report noting
that the RAN submitted that the value of the database in
its entirety would give cause to the maker of the database (or other person
might be the owner of copyright in the database) to seek protection against
unauthorised access to the database.
14.14 In its Draft Report (paragraph 14.10), the Committee commented that
networking of a database was akin to broadcasting or, more
precisely, cable
diffusion, where the copyright owner sought a licence fee calculated in
accordance with the expected audience that
would receive the broadcast or
diffusion. In both cases, the copyright owner had to collect whatever licence
fee could be successfully
negotiated “up front” from the broadcaster
or operator of the diffusion service, as the act of receiving the broadcast
diffusion was not a use of copyright for which the copyright owner could seek
remuneration, let alone do so practically. Since
the Draft Report, the
increasing focus on the “convergence” of means of transmission of
information and entertainment—over-the-air
broadcasting, cable
transmission of program material, telephony and data transmission—in the
“information superhighway”
has served to confirm the aptness of the
Committee’s comparison in the Draft Report.
14.15 Returning to computer database networking, if screen display is not an
exercise of the copyright owner’s rights or, if
it is, assuming that it
would be practically impossible for individual copyright owners to seek
remuneration from individual network
users, it is all the more important for the
operator, given that a network user can repeatedly access the database
(cf broadcasting and cable diffusion which are at present
non-“interactive”—although interactive opportunities for
receivers are said to be on the way). In commenting on
the Draft Report, Oracle
(Systems) Australia Pty Ltd (Oracle) referred to the practical difficulties of
monitoring access to databases
linked to a worldwide network, and recommended
consultation with copyright owners on the technical possibilities for effective
of remuneration.
14.16 The Committee acknowledges that with the increasing growth in numbers,
and ongoing development, of databases consisting of large
collections of
become ever more concerned at the
likelihood of unauthorised, and
non-remunerated, uses of their materials. It is also quite likely that this
concern will lead to
the establishment of a collecting society to administer the
rights of those copyright owners. It could be expected that in Australia
role might be taken over by an existing collecting society, such as the
society. Such a
development would have clear advantages for both database makers and copyright
owners. It would simplify the process
of establishing databases by providing a
single body to authorise the use of copyright materials and negotiate licence
fees on behalf
of copyright owners. In the absence of such a collecting society,
the law, be
required to negotiate permission to use materials separately with each copyright
owner. Recognising that such a collecting
society would have a virtual monopoly
over the use of many copyright materials the Committee said in its Draft Report
that it saw
considerable merit in any such society being brought under
of the Act by appropriate amendment to the Act, thereby extending the
confined in its
reaffirms that view, which it notes
is supported by comments received on the
Draft Report from CAL and the ACC (Recommendation 2.45 ).
Liability of database operators
14.17 In the Draft Report, the Committee considered the possibility that, in
the absence of a licence from the owner of copyright
in a work included in a
computer database, the database operator might, by networking it to subscribers,
be liable for authorising
infringement constituted by the printing of a
hard-copy of the work by a subscriber without the copyright owner’s
permission.
In other words, the database operator might be found to be in the
same position as was the library of the University of New South
Wales, which was
University of New South Wales
(1975) 133 CLR 1
to have authorised the copying of a work by an individual from a library book on
the photocopying machine provided by the library
for library users (which led to
the inclusion of s.39A in the Act).
14.18 In the Draft Report the Committee indicated that it was of the view
that the Act should include a provision to the effect that
where the licence to
the database operator did not extend to the making of hard-copies by database
users, the networking of the database
to subscribers would not, of itself,
amount to authorisation of the making of any hard-copies by users if accessing
the database
caused a message to appear on the screen containing relevant
information about copyright in the works in the database. This recommendation
was supported by ACLIS but thought to be unnecessary by the ACC.
14.19 The Committee considers that, particularly if a database operator will,
under the proposed new right of transmission to the
public accepted in principle
by the Government, be liable for acts of subscribers of calling up a work on
screen from the database
(see paragraph 14.40), it should reaffirm its
recommendation that the operator be able to avoid liability for hard-copy
reproduction
of the work by the subscriber if the operator arranges for a
message to advise subscribers of their copyright liability for such
reproduction
(Recommendation 2.46). The Committee has also considered whether the Act should
provide that the operator of a database
might avoid liability for copyright
infringement if he or she employed some technical means to prevent or control
downloading of
that while such technical means might be developed their implementation
not be easy and their effectiveness uncertain.
14.20 Finally, it should be noted that the Committee has discussed in Chapter
16 bulletin board systems and the activities of bulletin
board operators and
users, without reaching any conclusions on the level of liability that should be
attributed to bulletin board
operators for the activities of users of such
systems. In the Committee’s opinion the position of bulletin board
and database operators with respect to reproduction of copyright
material by users is somewhat different and for this reason the
Committee does
not consider that its recommendations in relation to database operators should
necessarily apply to bulletin board
operators. Without giving complete
consideration to the extent of the differences between such operators and
without having received
any submissions in relation to this issue, it is readily
apparent to the Committee that bulletin board operators are not in the primary
business of providing copyright materials to their users as are database
operators. Rather bulletin board operators provide the means
by which users may
choose to deposit and access copyright materials and for this reason, unlike
in a position to have negotiated with copyright
owners for the use of their materials. As the Committee has recommended in
16, the issues raised by bulletin board systems should be further
considered.
Copying by libraries and educational
institutions
14.21 In the Draft report, the Committee expressed the view that the
provisions of the Act concerning fair dealing, and the copying
of works in
libraries, would of course, apply to copying of works as stored on computer
more traditional formats. The
Committee also believed that, in the public interest, the provisions concerning
copying by libraries
and educational institutions should apply to storage of
works in computer memory. In particular, the Committee expressed the view
Part VB of the Act should allow educational institutions to make copies onto
computer memory for use by students, within the
same limits and subject to
payment of royalties under licensing agreements made with the appropriate
collecting society or as set
by the Copyright Tribunal. (As there is no similar
provision for subject matter other than works, any arrangements for copying of
such materials would remain subject to private licensing). The Committee
considered that it was doubtful that the licence under Part
VB to educational
institutions to make “one or more copies of . . . [a] work”
(s.135ZL) applied to scanning a work onto
a database, and considered that an
amendment to the Act should be made to confirm that the licence did so apply.
14.22 In commenting on the Draft Report, ACLIS supported the Committee's view
that the library copying provisions should extend to
electrocopying. In its
submission, CAL asked whether the Committee intended by its proposal regarding
library copying to allow libraries
to establish databases; if so, CAL suggested
that a “remuneratory statutory licence” to libraries for that
recommended. The ACC appeared to support CAL. CAL also challenged the
view of the Committee, which it inferred from the Draft Report,
electrocopying should be possible by way of fair dealing under s.40 to the same
extent as photocopying. Each of these proposals
is discussed in turn below.
Sections 49 and 50—copying by
14.23 In the Draft Report (paragraph 14.12), the Committee, albeit very
broadly and briefly, intended to convey the view that the
existing provisions
for libraries to make copies for library users (found in s.49) applied to the
making of electronic copies. The
Committee acknowledges that, in expressing this
view, it did not make it clear whether it also had in mind the provisions in
for inter-library copying. With regard to CAL's query, the Committee did
not intend that its proposal for extension of s.49 to supplying
copies could be used by libraries as a means of digitising their holdings, which
activity it agrees should be a matter
for negotiation with copyright owners or,
preferably and more practically, with an authorised collecting agency.
14.24 Section 49 has elaborate conditions to be satisfied before a copy can
be made and supplied by a library to a user, including
the requirement that the
user have the purpose of research or study in requesting a copy and that where a
request is made for more
than a reasonable portion of a work the relevant
library officer be satisfied that a copy of the work cannot be obtained within a
reasonable time at an ordinary commercial price (s.49(5)(b)). While the
Committee does not necessarily endorse the elaborate nature
of these conditions,
the Committee believes that they would prevent libraries—if indeed they
were so minded—from using
s.49, if amended as proposed, to systematically
digitise their collections.
14.25 In addition, the Committee does not consider that s.49, would apply so
as to enable works to be placed onto a database simply
for the purpose of
enabling a library user to browse through the works in the database on screen.
Section 49 is directed to the supply
of a copy, subject to the conditions
prescribed, and the Committee is proposing simply that that act include
electronic transmission
of a copy stored in digital form or the lending of an
electronic copy on a carrier such as a floppy disc. With more hesitation,
noting that the restriction on supply of a copy of more than a
reasonable portion of a work also applies to inter-library copying,
Committee extends its proposal to s.50 and affirms its recommendation in
relation to s.49 made in the Draft Report (Recommendation
14.26 In response to CAL’s other suggestion, that the licence to
libraries be subject to remuneration, the Committee considers
that given the
restricted circumstances in which copying can be undertaken by libraries under
ss.49 and 50, this suggestion cannot
be supported. As to CAL's further
suggestion that a separate review of library copying be recommended, the
Committee considers that
a review of the complexity of ss.49 and 50 could be
included in the Government's proposed examination of the whole Act. The
notes that the Government recently reaffirmed its intention to refer
such an examination to the Committee in its statement released
in October 1994
on cultural policy, entitled
Creative Nation
Section 39A—copying by users on library
facilities.
14.27 The Committee notes that s.39A protects libraries and archives from
liability for the making by users of infringing copies on
photocopying machines
provided for the convenience of users, if a prescribed notice is put up by the
library near the machine warning
of the limits of the fair dealing defence to
reproduction, especially
in the form of electrocopying as already noted, the
Committee believes that the section should be amended to better recognise the
access by users to libraries and archives holdings on databases. The Committee
accordingly recommends that the reference to making

terminal made available to

by printing out the work (Recommendation 2.48(b)). The Committee
considers that such amendments, which would be in line with its

in paragraph 14.19 in regard to database operators in general, and would be
consistent with the operation of ss.49
and 50 it has proposed in paragraphs
14.23–14.26.
Part VB—copying by educational

14.28 The Committee's recommendation in the Draft Report that the educational

apparently by the AVCC which noted that the remuneration payable might be

prescribed by the licence. The Committee commends for consideration another

Section 40—fair dealing
14.29 The Committee acknowledges that, as noted in the Draft Report and as
already mentioned at paragraph 14.13, a copy of a work
held in an electronic
“copying”, deemed to be a fair
dealing under s.40(2) and (3),
extends to acts of storage on computer database, the Committee draws attention
to the threshold requirement
of any fair dealing under s.40(2) and (3), namely,
that it be “for the purpose of research or study”. The Committee has
noted in Chapter 10 (from paragraph 10.29) the effect of legal opinion on the
limits of “research or study”. The Committee
also refers further to
the interpretation of s.40 by the full Federal Court in
(1982) 42 ALR 549
Haines case)
in which the Court held
that s.40 could not be relied on to exempt from infringement an activity for
which another provision (in the
particular circumstances, the statutory
educational copying licence) was evidently enacted. Further, in the
determination of whether
the copying of the whole or substantial part of a work
under s.40(2) is a fair dealing, the Committee notes that regard is required
be had to, amongst other things, “the purpose and character of the
dealing”. The Committee believes that given this
requirement, the greater
value of a copy in a database compared to a hard copy could be raised as a
consideration weighing against
regarding the making of the former as being a
fair dealing. In that context, the Committee imagines that other relevant
considerations
might be whether the database in which the work was stored was a
stand-alone PC or a photocopier or one accessible to a network of
Given the foregoing, the Committee is not satisfied that there is a strong
enough case for recommending explicit exclusion
of electrocopying from
“copying” as referred to in s.40 and accordingly does not recommend
any change to the section
(Recommendation 2.50).
14.30 In the event that the Committee declined to recommend exclusion of
electrocopying from the scope of s.40, CAL invited the Committee
to recommend
that attribution of the copyright owner be a condition of the availability of
the fair dealing defence. The Committee
sees no reason for singling out
electrocopies for such an additional requirement, and none was given by CAL. CAL
also invited the
Committee to consider recommending an addition to s.40, to the
effect that the subsequent commercial dealing with a copy made by
way of fair
dealing would retrospectively disentitle the maker of the copy to the fair
dealing defence. As to the second suggestion,
the Committee doubts the need for
the amendments suggested by CAL. The determination of whether a particular
dealing in copyright
material is fair within the meaning of the Act should be
determined based on the existing provisions in the Act and in light of the
particular circumstances of each case.
A DISPLAY RIGHT
14.31 It was submitted to the Committee that owners of copyright in works
stored in computer memory should have a right to control
the visual display of
those works. The Draft Report referred to the concern of CAL that access to
works stored in computer memory
on many occasions substituted for the purchase
of those works. For example, database users were able to read articles without
the journals from which they are extracted. Later comments on the
Draft Report from the Hydrographic Service RAN gave other examples
the contention that screen display alone might often be the limit of the use
that users might want to make of a database
and that copyright owners should be
remunerated for that use.
14.32 While the Committee adheres to the view it expressed in the Draft
Report, that the concerns expressed by CAL and the Hydrographic
Service RAN can
be addressed by contractual means, it nonetheless proceeds to consider the
recommendations of the Copyright Convergence
Group (CCG) in relation to a new
transmission right and whether the display of a work, or a substantial portion
of a work, stored
in computer memory amounts to a reproduction of the work, a
public performance of a work or a diffusion service within the meaning
Act (in which case the owners of copyright in the works comprising the database
would have an exclusive right to control the
transmission of their works to
“subscribers” to the database).
A right of transmission to the public
14.33 The Committee notes that the concerns raised by copyright owners of
works included in databases are addressed by at least two
recent reports,
namely, the Copyright Convergence Group’s (CCG) report,
Highways to
Change—Copyright in the New Communications Environment
and the US Green Paper (Draft Report) entitled
Intellectual
Property and the National Information Infrastructure
by the US Assistant
Secretary of Commerce (July 1994). These reports suggest a plurality of ways in
which screen display might be
made subject to the control of the owner of
14.34 The CCG says in its report (paragraph 1.3.4, p.29) that:
There is a need to ensure that certain uses of copyright material which are
provided on a point-to-point basis and which may therefore
not be “to the
public”, such as on-demand services, are nevertheless licensable by
to artificially extend the concept of
the public by means of a definition, the CCG considers that the unauthorised
transmissions
made for a commercial purpose should also infringe a copyright
owner's rights. It therefore recommends that a provision be inserted
Act which deems transmissions of copyright material which are made for a
commercial purpose to be transmissions to the public.
The Government committed itself to implementing the new right of transmission
to the public recommended by the CCG in its cultural
policy statement,
Creative Nation
. The ACC also supported the introduction of such a right,
in its submission on the Draft Report.
14.35 The US Green Paper suggests that the distribution right that copyright
owners have under the US
should be modified to cover
“electronic distribution” of works on database to users of terminals
linked to the database.
There is no right of distribution amongst the rights
presently accorded copyright owners under the Australian Act, although the right
of publication that is among the rights comprising copyright in works, and the
right of owners of copyright in works and other subject
matter to prevent
importation of copies of their materials, which was the subject of an earlier
report by the Committee, effectively
amount to a measure of control over
distribution. In its
Creative Nation
statement, the Government has also
committed itself to consideration of a distribution right at the international
level, and to asking
the Committee, as part of the proposed reference on
simplification of the Act, to consider such a right. In its submission, the ACC
also expressed support for such a right.
14.36 From the foregoing it can be seen that there are at least three ways in
which control by copyright owners can possibly be extended
to screen displays of
a copy of their work stored on database, namely, the public performance right,
the proposed new right of transmission
to the public and a right of distribution
to the public. The Committee notes that each possibility would have different
implications
for the operator of the database and the person causing the screen
display of works stored in the database, if they are two different
14.37 For instance, if screen display were to be made subject to the
distribution right, it would seem to be more appropriate to regard
the database
operator as having exercised that right in allowing the terminal user access to
the database, rather than regard the
latter as having engaged in an act of
distribution. Likewise, if screen display is to be regarded as an act of
transmission, it would
seem more logical to regard the database operator as
responsible, by reason of having put in place the arrangements for screen
by database subscribers, rather than the terminal user having
transmitted the work to himself or herself.
14.38 A modified or expanded performance right may have a different
operation. As noted later by the Committee at paragraph 14.45,
under the present
law, the user of a terminal on which he or she causes a work to be displayed may
be performing the work in public,
depending on who is present at the time. The
possibility of fixing the database operator with liability for publicly
performing a
work which is displayed on a user's terminal by action of the user
is more problematical, particularly if the users of the database
regarded as subscribers to a diffusion service and s.27(2) of the Act applied.
(Section 27(2) deems that causing a work
to be transmitted to subscribers to a
diffusion service is not a performance).
14.39 The Committee has not had the opportunity to give proper consideration
to either the CCG report or the US Government report,
in the time left to it to
finalise and present this report. However, to the limited extent that it has
been able to consider them,
it is reinforced in its view (discussed in full from
paragraph 14.44) that the act of displaying a work on a screen from computer
notes that this view appears to be shared by the ACC.
14.40 As the Government has provisionally committed itself to implementing
the right of transmission to the public recommended by
the CCG, this may offer
the appropriate avenue for enabling owners of copyright in works stored on
over, or at least obtain remuneration for, screen
displays of the works from the databases. Assuming that the database operator,
rather than the individual terminal user, would, by virtue of such a right, be
fixed with responsibility for screen displays by users,
this would appear to be
consistent with the view of the Committee that the practical way for copyright
owners to effectively secure
remuneration for use of their work when stored on
the Committee makes no recommendation regarding a transmission right, it
supports, in principle, its introduction and application
to screen displays.
(Recommendation 2.51).
Is a display on screen a reproduction in a material
14.41 The Committee has considered whether or not a screen display is a
reproduction in a material form of the work or other material
displayed. In this
regard it notes that the definition of material form in s.10(1) provides that
material form:
any form (whether visible or not) of storage from which
the work . . . or a substantial part of the work can be reproduced (emphasis
In its Draft Report, the Committee commented that screen displays were
ephemeral in nature and, for this reason, the Committee did
not regard them as a
form of storage. However, the Committee added that because the definition of
material form is an inclusive and
not an exhaustive one its conclusion in
relation to the definition could not be regarded as conclusive. The Committee
received two
submissions (one from Mr Russell K Evans and the other from Oracle)
commenting on whether or not works displayed on screen would
have first been
stored in computer memory. However, the Committee is concerned here with whether
the display itself on the screen
is a reproduction of the work stored in
computer memory, not whether the work is in a “form of storage”.
14.42 It is clear to the Committee that there remains some doubt whether any
or all screen displays involve a reproduction in material
form. In the Draft
Report the Committee said that while it was arguable that a screen display could
be a reproduction in a material
form of a work stored in computer memory, the
better view, from a policy perspective, was that it should not be regarded as
The AVCC fully supported the Committee in its view. The Committee remains
of this view and affirms the recommendation it made in
the Draft Report that the
Act be amended to make it clear that screen displays do not constitute a
reproduction in a material form
of works stored in computer memory
(Recommendation 2.52). The Committee believes that this recommendation
recognises that the “electronic
browsing” through works stored in an
electronic database is part of the normal use of a such a database and should be
as such, just as the reader of a book in hardcopy is able to browse the
pages without exercising any of the copyright owners’
rights. The
Committee also is of the view that the concerns of copyright owners will be
adequately addressed with the introduction
of a transmission right to the public
which the Government is committed to introducing (see paragraph 14.40).
14.43 In the Draft Report, the Committee also expressed the view that it
should not recommend the introduction of a “display
right” for works
stored in computer memory. ACLIS strongly supported this view and the Committee
sees no reason to change its
view (Recommendation 2.53). The Committee further
recommended that, if its recommendation to extend the jurisdiction of the
Tribunal to licenses for the inclusion of materials on databases was
accepted, then an amendment to the Act should be made requiring
the Copyright
Tribunal to take into account a proposal by a database operator to grant users
screen display access when arbitrating
a dispute under Part VI of the Act. The
Committee reaffirms this recommendation (Recommendation 2.54).
Is a display on screen a public
performance?
14.44 The Committee has also considered the question whether the screen
display of materials contained in a database, or of a work
stored in computer
memory, can be regarded as a public performance of that material. Section
31(1)(a) of the Act provides that, in
the case of literary, dramatic or musical
works, the rights of the copyright owner include the right to perform the work
The owners of copyright in artistic works, however, do not have a
performance right (s.31(1)(b)).
14.45 Section 27(1) of the Act has the effect that a performance includes any
mode of visual or aural presentation, whether by the
operation of wireless
telegraphy apparatus or by any other means, but does not include broadcasting or
cable transmission. The Act
is silent as to the meaning of “in
public”. The Courts have, however, interpreted “public” by
reference to
whether the persons present at the performance were there in a
private, domestic capacity or whether the performance formed part
public, “external” lives. Recently, in
Australasian Performing
Right Association
Commonwealth Bank of Australia
[1992] FCA 609
(1992) 40 FCR 59
Gummow J said
[1992] FCA 609
[1992] FCA 609
The occasion of the performance was the imparting of information by the
employer to its employees and the musical work was used to
facilitate that
process. The audience was brought together by the commercial purpose of the
respondent and their public lives as
employees. These are the most significant
