facts which, consistently with the pre 1968 case law, support the
characterisation of the
performance of the work as one ‘in public’
within the meaning of paragraph 31(1)(a)(iii) of the 1968 Act.
By applying the definition of performance and the meaning given to
“public” by the courts, it appears that the screen
materials stored in computer memory can be considered to constitute a public
performance where such display takes place
in a non-domestic context such as at
14.46 Section 27(3) provides that where visual images or sounds are displayed
or emitted by any receiving apparatus to which they
are conveyed (whether or not
by a path made of a material substance) the performance is deemed to be effected
by the operation of
the receiving apparatus (in other words the visual display
unit or monitor). It follows from this that in certain circumstances the
of works stored in computer memory may constitute a public performance by the
person operating the terminal by which the
works accessed in the database are
14.47 In the Draft Report, the Committee recommended that the Act be amended
(paragraph 14.22):
. . . to make it clear that the screen display of work stored in computer
memory does not per se constitute a public performance of
that work. By reason
of the definition of a literary work in the Act this would also apply to
This recommendation was strongly supported by ACLIS, but was queried as being
unnecessary by the ACC and opposed by Mr Lindsay, who
had the impression that
the Committee was recommending that screen displays of works should
amount to a public performance. It was also opposed by CAL, which was
. . . strongly of the opinion that as a matter of policy, screen displays
should be considered to be a public performance”.
CAL argued that (p.7):
With the advent of multi-media works the public performance right may become
most relevant in assisting copyright owners to control
the use of their works.
This view was essentially supported by the Hydrographic Service RAN.
14.48 The Committee acknowledges that there may have been ambiguity in the
way the recommendation was expressed. What was intended—and
what is now
affirmed by the Committee—is that the mere act of calling up onto a
computer terminal screen a work, or portion
of it, from a computer database
should not,
, constitute a public performance of the work and
the Act should be amended to reflect this (Recommendation 2.55). What should
whether that act is a public performance is whether or not it is
carried out “in public”. This is consistent with—indeed
reflects the operation of—s.27, as already noted in paragraph 14.45. The
Committee recognises the seriousness of the possibility
that displays on screen
will become an increasingly frequent form of use of works. However it does not
believe that the existing
right of public performance can be adapted to give a
right to control all acts of displays on screen without distorting the notion
public performance as presently existing and understood in the Act. At most,
some acts of displays on screen of a work, by reason
of the presence of persons
when the act is carried out, will be a public performance of the work and should
be treated in this way
by the Act.
Is a subscription database a diffusion
14.49 Transmission to subscribers to a diffusion service is the exclusive
right of owners of copyright in literary, artistic and musical
cinematograph films, broadcasts and, to a limited extent, artistic works.
Section 26(1) defines such transmission as a transmission
of those copyright
materials, “. . . over wires, or over other paths provided by a material
substance. . .” (which would
appear to include fibre optic cable) “.
. . in the course of a service of distributing broadcast or other matter . . .
premises of subscribers to the service”. While the references to
“broadcast matter” and “premises of subscribers”
suggest that diffusion services contemplate typically cable TV and radio
networks, it may be that some databases serving
subscribers at their offices
would come within the definition. In its Draft Report, the Committee expressed
the view that as many
diffusion service, eg, those in libraries or confined to one business or other
organisation, it would not be appropriate to extend “diffusion
service” beyond its evident focus, ie, cable TV and radio
services. The
Committee notes that the decision of the Federal Court in
[1993] FCA 542
(1993) 27 IPR 357
, referred to the limits on the scope of the
diffusion right. The Committee understands that the decision is being
14.50 In comments on the Draft Report, ACLIS supported the Committee's view
that the diffusion right not be extended beyond its present
effective focus on
cable TV and cable radio services, while CAL and the ACC opposed the Committee's
view. In its report the CCG recommended
that the diffusion right be omitted from
the Act, as a corollary to introduction of a right of transmission to the public
which would
enable copyright owners to control whatever constitutes a diffusion.
The ACC and Mr Lindsay made similar suggestions to those of
the CCG to the
Committee, the ACC adding that artistic works should have the full benefit of
such a right instead of the limited,
indirect right currently enjoyed over
diffusion. In the light of the commitment of the Government to implement the
proposed right
of transmission to the public and the CCG's intention that it
cover “transmissions of copyright material which are made for
a commercial
purpose”, the Committee sees no purpose in considering whether it would be
appropriate, in order to address CAL's
concerns, to review its opposition to
extension of the diffusion right, and it accordingly makes no change to its
recommendation,
while recognising that the CCG’s recommendations, when
implemented, will impact on the diffusion right. (Recommendation 2.56).
THE PUBLICATION RIGHT
14.51 In its comments on the Draft Report, the AVCC suggested that
unpublished works stored on a database that is accessible to a
wide range of
subscribers, such as a bulletin board, might thereby be considered published.
The Committee has elsewhere noted that
what constitutes publication, such as to
amount to an exercise of the publication right comprised in the copyright in
considered by the High Court in
[1990] HCA 58
, in which the Court interpreted s.29 as fixing the time
of publication, rather than defining publication. In its report the CCG
similar expressions of concern, and recommended careful consideration
of the consequences of extending publication to cover transmission
public, in addition to making available copies to the public.
14.52 Given the Government commitment to the principle of a broadly based
right of transmission to the public, this would seem a more
appropriate vehicle
for copyright control over networking of a work from a database, rather than an
extension of the publication
right. However, the Committee agrees with the CCG's
suggestion insofar as the status of a work or other subject matter as either
published or unpublished has significance for the purposes of determining
duration of protection and, in the case of, eg, sound recordings
made in some
overseas countries, the extent of protection.
A RENTAL RIGHT
14.53 In its comments on the Draft Report, RAN submitted that a rental right
should be extended to owners of copyright in all works—not
just computer
programs—and cited an instance of a yacht club purchasing a limited number
of copies of charts in electronic
format and renting them to members. This it
submitted was a valuable exploitation of the database. In recommending in its
Draft Report
that a rental right should be introduced for owners of copyright in
computer programs, the Committee said that its recommendation
. . . should not automatically carry over to other categories of copyright
works or subject matter and that the case for a similar
right in respect of
those other works or subject matter would have to be separately made out.
While the submission just referred to raises the issue, and gives a practical
illustration in support of the case for a rental right
for works, the Committee
feels that in the absence of other submissions this is too narrow a basis for
reaching any firm conclusion.
Accordingly, it draws attention to the matter
without making a recommendation.
THE MEANING OF THE TERM
14.54 The protection of databases was among the issues considered at the
first meeting of the Committee of Experts on a Possible Protocol
to the Berne
Convention held in Geneva from 4 to 8 November 1991. In the memorandum prepared
by the World Intellectual Property Organisation
(WIPO) for that meeting,
consideration was given to the meaning of the term “database”. The
memorandum, at paragraph
40, noted that the term was first used in a narrower
sense, to mean only an “electronic database”. In other words an
aggregate of information systematically arranged and stored in a computer
system. The memorandum commented that it was now clear
that the term should no
longer be connected solely to the element of computer storage. It suggested that
all compilations of information
should be considered databases irrespective of
whether they exist in print, in computer storage, or in some other form.
14.55 Since the publication of the Committee’s Draft Report, the TRIPS
Agreement has been concluded. Article 10(2) of TRIPS
provides that:
Compilations of data or other material, whether in machine readable or other
form, which by reason of the selection or arrangement
of their contents
constitute intellectual creations shall be protected as such. Such protection,
which shall not extend to the data
or material itself, shall be without
prejudice to any copyright subsisting in the data or material itself.
The Committee notes that the scope of application of art.10(2) is wider than
art.2(5) of the Berne Convention, which is directed to
“collections of
literary or artistic works”. It has been pointed out, however, that it is
arguable that the databases
covered by TRIPS art.10(2) may instead be covered by
art.2(1) of the Berne Convention insofar as it refers to “literary and
artistic works” as including:
. . . every production in the literary, scientific and artistic domain,
whatever may be the mode or form of its expression, such as
books, pamphlets and
(emphasis added).
14.56 The Committee also notes that on 29 January 1992 the EC Commission (as
it then was) announced its proposal for a directive to
harmonise database
protection throughout the European Union—the EC Directive on Databases. An
amended proposal was submitted
on 4 October 1993. The preamble to the current
amended Directive on Databases provides that the term “database”
be understood to include (paragraph 16):
. . . collections of works, whether literary, artistic, musical or other, or
of other material such as texts, sounds, images, numbers,
facts, data or
combinations of any of these.
However, the scope of protection under the amended Directive is limited to
collections of materials that are arranged, stored and
accessed by electronic
means. The definition of “database” in art.1 includes the materials
necessary for the operation
of the database but excludes any computer program
used in the making or operation of the database. The amended Directive is
to operate without prejudice to the rights subsisting in such
collections as compilations within the meaning of art.2(5) of the Berne
Convention.
14.57 In the light of, particularly, art.10(2) of TRIPS, the
Committee’s view is that the meaning of the term “database”
should not be confined to collections of works and materials stored
electronically. There is no definition of the term in the Act
and the Committee
has not received any submissions on the scope of the term. However, it does not
consider that there is any reason
why, from a copyright perspective, different
principles should apply to either the materials collected or the collection of
as a whole, merely because of the particular material form in which
those materials are stored. Accordingly, for the purpose of the
discussion in
this report the Committee has not drawn any distinction between different types
of databases. The Committee acknowledges
this means its conclusions will apply
to traditional hard-copy databases, databases stored in computer memory and
in other forms of storage such as floppy discs, tape streams
and CD–ROM which may not be considered to be true forms of computer
CURRENT PROTECTION OF DATABASES STORED IN COMPUTER
Act protects compilations as literary
14.58 As mentioned in paragraph 14.06, one of the issues for consideration is
the protection of collections of works stored in computer
memory (databases).
The definition of a literary work in s.10(1) of the Act provides, in part, that
the term literary work includes:
(a) a table or compilation, expressed in words, figures or symbols (whether
or not in a visible form).
Prior to the
, the definition read
“literary work” includes a written table or compilation.
The explanatory memorandum for the 1984 amendments to the Act makes it clear
that the definition was amended to ensure that tables
or compilations should not
be denied protection simply because they were stored in an invisible form in
computer memory.
14.59 In its submission CAL suggested that the definition of a literary work
should be amended to include a specific reference to
computer databases. The
AIIA also suggested that the definition should be clarified to specify the
elements necessary for a database
to be a literary work. It submitted that the
definition of a compilation in the US
could be used.
That definition provides that a compilation is:
a work formed by the collection and assembling of pre-existing materials or
of data that are selected, co-ordinated or arranged in
such a way that the
resultant work as a whole constitutes an original work of authorship.
14.60 The Committee considers that the meaning of the word compilation in the
Australian Act, as used in paragraph (a) of the definition
of literary work,
would ordinarily include a literary work constructed of materials collected from
different sources. In his book
The Law of Intellectual Property
Law Book Company, Sydney) Professor Ricketson notes that the term compilation is
very broad and might include (p.98, paragraph
. . . a dictionary, an almanac, a gazette, an anthology or selection of other
non-copyright works or factual information, a catalogue
or a news report.
Accordingly, the Committee is of the opinion that the ordinary meaning of
compilation is broad enough to include databases which are
collections of works or other materials gathered from different sources. The
Committee notes that the Copyright (World
Trade Organisation Amendments) Bill
1994, which was introduced in Parliament in late 1994 to implement
Australia’s obligations
under the TRIPS Agreement, does not include any
amendment to implement art.10(2) (referred to at paragraph 14.55), inferring
the Government has taken the view that the obligations under art.10(2) are
already effected by the Act as it stands.
14.61 The scope of copyright protection for compilations was considered in
the US by the Supreme Court in the case of
Feist Publications Inc
Rural Telephone Service Co Inc
(1991) 20 IPR 129
Feist case)
that case Rural Telephone (Rural), a public utility, provided telephone services
to north–west Kansas. Each year, as required
under State regulations,
Rural issued an updated telephone directory to its subscribers. Feist
Publications (Feist) did not provide
telephone services. It published a
telephone directory for a geographical area which included some of the area
serviced by Rural.
To compile its directory, Feist’s practice was to
obtain a licence from the telephone companies operating in the area covered
its directory. Rural refused to grant Feist a licence, so Feist used
Rural’s listing without its consent. (Of the 46,878
listings in
Feist’s directory 1309 were identical to those in Rural’s
directory).
14.62 Feist conceded that Rural’s directory was subject to copyright.
However, it denied infringement, contending that it did
not copy anything that
was original to Rural but only copied facts reported by Rural. These it
submitted existed regardless of their
being recorded in Rural’s directory.
The Court considered only whether Rural had selected, co-ordinated or arranged
the uncopyrightable
facts in an original way. The Court held (p.142):
This is “selection” of a sort, but it lacks the modicum of
creativity necessary to transform mere selection into copyrightable
expression.
In other words, the Court found that Rural had not satisfied the test for the
minimum standard for originality. Accordingly, copyright
protection was denied
to its directory.
14.63 In order to be protected in Australia under the Act, a work must have
entailed expenditure of a sufficient degree of labour
and skill in the selection
and arrangement of the material comprising the work before it will be considered
to be an original work.
It is the combination of the labour and skill expended
in compiling the database that is protected, not the underlying material or
information which, depending on its nature, may or may not attract copyright
protection. The Committee notes that the standard for
originality appears to be
quite low under Australian law. This was also noted by Mr Lindsay. For example,
with lists and tables of
factual material there can often be little if any
element of original composition by the compiler in setting out the information.
What originality may subsist in the work will be the skill and labour found
solely in the way the compiler has presented and arranged
the information. The
test is whether the compiler has imposed some sort of order upon the material,
ie, whether an element of coherence
has been introduced which is not otherwise
found in the mass of materials collected.
14.64 In its submission, IBM commented that the present Act provides adequate
protection for databases and that the protection to
be afforded in each case
should be determined on general principles. In its Draft Report the Committee
expressed the opinion that
the definition of a literary work, as amended in
1984, was broad enough to include computer databases, ie, tables or compilations
of works or information stored in computer memory. For the reasons given above,
the Committee agreed with IBM and was not persuaded
that there was any need to
amend the definition of literary work to specifically include a reference to
computer databases or to
define compilations ACLIS and the ACC agreed with that
conclusion. Accordingly the Committee adheres to the opinion it expressed
Draft Report that no amendment is necessary to the definition of literary work
to include a reference to computer databases
or to define compilation
(Recommendation 2.57).
OTHER ISSUES CONCERNING DATABASES
Term of protection
14.65 The Committee is of the view that databases should be protected for the
same period as other literary works. As art.2(5) of
the Berne Convention
specifically extends protection, as literary and artistic works, to at least
some compilations coming within
the definition in the Australian Act, it is not
consistent with its international obligations for Australia to consider varying
term of protection from life of the author plus fifty years. The Committee
notes that TRIPS art.10(2) does not require databases,
to which it applies, to
be protected as literary works—as art 10(1) requires in the case of
computer programs—and art.12
prescribes a minimum term of fifty years for
protection of a work (other than a photograph or work of applied art) for which
life of the author is not the prescribed duration. While it might be
possible to prescribe a fifty year term for some databases and
compliance with the Berne Convention and the TRIPS Agreement, the Committee is
certainly not persuaded that it should seriously
entertain the idea of reducing
the existing duration of protection of any class of databases already protected
under the Act and
does not recommend any change to the Act with respect to the
term of protection (Recommendation 2.58).
Dynamic databases
14.66 It has been suggested that the duration of copyright protection for
electronic databases which are being constantly updated
is potentially eternal.
The Committee notes that for any published database there will be a time when
the first edition of the database
is made. This will be the time from which the
period of copyright protection will commence. Determining that time will be a
of fact which can only be answered having regard to the circumstances
in each case. The addition of information to a database would
not, in the
Committee’s view, automatically give rise to the creation of a new edition
of the database unless there was a sufficiently
substantial change to the
protection should subsist. Insubstantial
additions would not give rise to a new
edition. In order to determine whether or not a new work has been created,
consideration must
be given to the quality and quantity of the material added to
the database and how the author of the database has incorporated that
The Committee notes that in some cases the material added to a database may
itself qualify for separate copyright protection.
14.67 In its Draft Report, the Committee was of the view that the questions
when copyright in a database commenced and the identification
of the period for
which it subsisted in a database were questions to be determined on the basis of
the evidence in individual cases
and that, accordingly, no legislative action
was required. In his comments on the Draft Report, Mr Lindsay expressed concern
difficulty of determining the duration of protection of a
“dynamic” database that is being constantly updated, and asked
producer “is to be penalised for supplying a database that is continually
being upgraded”.
14.68 The Committee acknowledges the potential difficulty in determining the
term of protection of a “dynamic database”,
but believes that, as
noted in paragraph 14.66 above, at some stage the cumulative effect of the
constant updating will result in
the view being taken that a new edition, ie, a
new work, had come into being at that stage, with duration being calculated
accordingly.
This problem is not confined to databases, although it may manifest
itself more in their case. The Committee notes that a new play
being performed
for the first time may be repeatedly adjusted as rehearsals progress—not
just lines, but whole scenes may be
written in or out—with the result that
it may be difficult to determine when the work was completed and, thus, from
when duration
of protection commences.
14.69 The Committee notes that the Federal Court addressed this issue in
relation to the copyright in working drawings for toy building
Interlego AG
Croner Trading Pty Limited
[1992] FCA 624
(1992) 39 FCR 348
A number of amendments were made to drawings in which copyright
subsisted. The court held that a new copyright in the altered drawings
with the effect that a new copyright was conferred on the drawings, from the
period commencing on the date the new drawings
came into existence ie, were
altered. The court disagreed with the decision of the Privy Council in
Tyco Industries Inc
[1988] UKPC 3
[1989] AC 217
where the Privy
Council had, on precisely the same facts, reached the opposite conclusion.
14.70 The duration of copyright in databases has always depended on their
authorship. Accordingly, the need to determine their authorship
is not new. In
this context there are two main issues. First, where the database is a
compilation of works, is there sufficient originality
in the compilation to
found authorship in it as a work, independently of the copyright in the works
comprising the compilation? Or,
where the database is a collection of facts or
figures, such as a directory or timetable, is there sufficient originality to
authorship in it as an original literary work—or would originality
be denied as in the
? Secondly, whatever the nature of the
compilation, is the work of a person who has contributed to the compilation such
as to entitle
that person to be treated as the author or a joint author of the
compilation?
14.71 The spectrum of compilations from encyclopedias at one extreme to
directories of factual information at the other has long existed
form. Where the component parts are of sufficient import for recognition as
independent literary works, compilations will
usually have required a contract
between the authors and the person organising the compilation. At the other
extreme, individuals
will be employed to gather and organise factual information
which requires no originality. Large databases may be compiled by a number
individuals. As already mentioned, a sufficient degree of labour and skill must
have been expended in the selection and arrangement
of the materials that make
up a database before it will be considered to be an original work. It follows
that the person or persons
who contribute this skill and labour should be
regarded as the author or authors of the database. In the Committee’s view
mere contribution of labour to the creation of a database should not be
sufficient to entitle a person to claim authorship, just
as the work of a
stenographer in typing dictation of a novel does not entitle the stenographer to
co-authorship of the novel. If
this principle is applied, people who merely
enter data collected by others into computer memory would not be regarded as
14.72 The determination of the authorship of large databases stored on
computer memory, difficult or practically impossible as it
may be, is not a
problem peculiar to computer databases or other large works stored in that
medium to which many have contributed
in varying degrees. Indeed, in Chapter 8,
paragraph 8.06, the Committee noted that the same problem occurred with the
of computer programs. That being so, and as the reference to the
Committee does not extend to a general review of the law governing
authorship, the Committee does not feel that it should recommend a change in the
law regarding the authorship of works because
they happen to be created and
stored in computer memory (Recommendation 2.59). In addition, because the Berne
Convention requires
protection to extend to all those who are joint authors of
literary and artistic works, there may be limits on seeking to vary the
law, if that were thought desirable.
14.73 The Committee notes that determining ownership of copyright in a work
of many joint authors also has the potential to be difficult
to the point of
being practically impossible. However, the Committee did not receive any
submissions concerning the ownership of
because the Act provides the opportunity for a person controlling or
coordinating the preparation
or maintenance of a database to secure ownership of
and further provides that, in the absence of contractual arrangements to the
contrary, such person has copyright in all inputs by
his or her employees. Given
the confines of the Committee’s reference to computerised databases, and
in the absence of submissions
critical of the present law, the Committee
reaffirms its view that the mere fact of the storage on computer memory of a
does not, of itself, warrant or require any change to provisions
regarding copyright ownership (Recommendation 2.60).
PROTECTION OF NON-ORIGINAL DATABASES
14.74 As the Committee mentioned in paragraph 14.04, there may be situations
where a considerable amount of time, money and effort
has been expended in
creating a comprehensive database which, under the present copyright regime, may
not be protectable because
the selection or arrangement of its contents are
insufficiently original. Nonetheless, in such cases those who created the
will have an understandably strong desire to protect the considerable
economic investment expended in creating and maintaining the
also be concerned to protect the database from unauthorised copying and
manipulation of the material contained
in it. Ensuring such protection would be
considerably more difficult if the database were not protected as an original
literary work.
If the decision in the
(paragraphs
14.61–14.62) were followed by Australian courts there would be some cases
in which it would be difficult to maintain
that publicly available facts as
stored in computer databases would have sufficient originality to attract
This could be expected to have significant implications
for businesses providing information stored in computer memory.
The EC Amended Directive on Databases
14.75 As mentioned in paragraph 14.56 the EC Commission announced its
proposal for a directive on database protection in January 1992
and released an
amended proposal for the Directive in October 1993. In essence the amended
Directive on Databases created a new right
of “unfair extraction” in
both non-original databases (ie, those not protected by copyright) and databases
by copyright. The amended Directive did not affect copyright
protection for original databases. Under the amended Directive the maker
from that database, of its contents, in whole
or in substantial part, for
commercial purposes irrespective of the eligibility of the database for
protection under copyright. The
right to prevent “unfair extraction”
runs from the date of creation and expires 10 years from the date when the
is first lawfully made available to the public. It proposed that
insubstantial changes to the contents of a database would not extend
original period of protection of the database.
14.76 The need for a right to prevent unfair extraction from a database was
not raised in the Committee’s Issues Paper and the
Committee had not
received any submissions on it prior to releasing its Draft Report. This being
so, and given the Committee’s
conclusion as to the wide scope of existing
protection under the Act for databases the Committee did not, in its Draft
Report, make
a recommendation regarding possible adoption of the approach in the
EC Directive on Databases, stating that it would consider this
issue further in
the light of any comments it received in response to the Draft Report.
14.77 The Committee received several submissions commenting on the possible
adoption of an unfair extraction right applicable to non-original
Evans supported adoption of such a right, if it was stated not to attract the
exemption provided for in
Trade Practices Act 1974
Believing that the “effort involved in converting a non-original database
into electronic form is worth protecting”,
the Law Council thought that
electronic non-original databases should be protected against electronic, but
not hardcopy, reproduction,
for a considerably shorter duration than protection
for literary works. Mr Lindsay, while commending a
sui generis
protection because it could be made to fit the circumstances of such databases,
noted constitutional difficulties with such
an approach and suggested that, as
the EC Directive on Databases had not been finalised, an amendment to the Act in
Australia might
be premature at this stage. The ACC queried whether the
extension of protection of published editions recommended in Chapter 15 might
protect non-original databases. Messrs Houghton and Reid–Smith favoured
consideration of following the EC model.
14.78 The Committee believes that there is merit in Mr Evans’
suggestion, both as to adoption of an unfair extraction right
and as to the
application of Part IV of the
Trade Practices Act
, since what would be
protected under such a right is a mass of data, not a form of expression. The
Committee queries the limitation
proposed by the Law Council. It seems to the
Committee that extraction from the database whether in hardcopy or in electronic
would be equally injurious to the creator of the database. The Committee
endorses Mr Lindsay's concerns regarding the constitutional
powers available to
make provision for non-original databases, while noting that the
Layouts Act 1989
was enacted, notwithstanding that Australia had not adhered
to the Washington Treaty, which the Act was intended to implement. Of
most of that Treaty plus some additional obligations have now been incorporated
in the TRIPS Agreement.
14.79 Lastly in relation to published edition copyright, the Committee
believes that while the published edition copyright, amended
as recommended in
Chapter 15, would effectively protect published databases, it would not do so
where the database had not been published.
While not having been informed on the
subject, the Committee is prepared to accept that many commercially valuable
not be “published” in the copyright sense or, if
intended to be published, would not have the protection of the published
14.80 Notwithstanding the support evident in the submissions for the
introduction of an unfair extraction right, the Committee is
of the view that a
decision on adoption of such a right should be deferred until consideration has
been given to the final form of
the EC Directive on Databases and accordingly it
recommends further review of this issue bearing in mind developments in relation
to this Directive (Recommendation 2.61).
NEW KINDS OF COMPUTERISED WORKS
14.81 In its Draft Report, the Committee referred to a United States Office
of Technology Assessment 1992 publication entitled
Finding a balance:
computer software, intellectual property and the challenge of intellectual
. The Committee noted several new issues: that the report raised for
consideration:
• First, given that the law recognised several different kinds of works
and some multi-media “subject matter other than
works”, eg, films,
could computer-based multi-media productions be accommodated in any of the
existing definitions, or was
there a need for a new “subject matter other
than a work”?;
• Next, was a new licensing regime required to enable makers of
multi-media productions to use, for example, small parts of hundreds
• Additionally, was the definition of “public performance”
clear in relation to the use of a multi-media production
in a public place?;
• Finally, were hypertexts, consisting of objects—words,
paragraphs, pictures, sounds—interconnected by “links”
protected by copyright? If another person created a different set of links using
the same objects, was this a new work, or a work
which infringed copyright in
the previous hypertext?
14.82 The Committee did not receive any submissions prior to the Draft Report
concerning new kinds of works, and therefore invited
comments on these and other
issues relating to new forms of works. In response to this invitation the Law
Council opposed any additional
protection for hypertexts and, in the absence of
any other submissions, the Committee makes no recommendation on additional or
protection for hypertexts (Recommendation 2.62).
Multi-media
14.83 In its submission on the Draft Report, Australian Pay TV Pty Ltd (APTV)
so defined the nature of multi-media as to leave the
Committee with the strong
impression that a multi-media production is essentially a collective production
combining a number of items
of copyright material comprising literary and
artistic works, sound recordings and cinematographic films, in a way comparable
books comprising both literary and artistic works. (Perhaps the novelty
musical greeting cards comprising artistic and literary works
and a sound
recording are a form of multi-media that preceded the CD–ROM, silent films
that included text interposed between
sequences of moving pictures are an even
earlier example, and of course films and videos combining moving pictures and
the most familiar form.) However, following its definition of
multi-media, APTV proceeded to list examples of multi-media that included
“interactive broadcast”, “on-line video libraries” and
“electronic publishing of newspapers”,
which the Committee
understands might not contain all the elements included in the suggested
definition.
14.84 Moving pictures were in the course of time accorded protection as a new
form of copyright subject matter distinct from the individual
photographic works
and, in the case of sound films, the sound track of the film. Is a multi-media
production of such a distinctive
nature that it warrants being given the status
of a new form of copyright subject matter? Insofar as a multi-media production
of moving pictures and accompanying recorded sounds, it seems to come
within the definition, in s.10(1) of the Act, of cinematograph
film which is
defined as:
. . . the aggregate of the visual images embodied in an article or thing so
as to be capable by the use of the article or thing:
(a) of being shown as a moving picture; or
(b) of being embodied in another article or thing by the use of which it can
and includes the aggregate of sounds embodied in a sound-track associated
with such visual images, bearing in mind that “sound-track”
turn defined, in part, as:
in relation to visual images forming part of a cinematograph film, means:
(a) the part of any article or thing, being an article or thing in which
those visual images are embodied, in which sounds are embodied
It does not seem to the Committee that the inclusion of text and still images
in a multi-media production serves to deprive it of
protection, as a
cinematograph film, any more than does the inclusion of credits or other
narrative, text or still images sometimes
seen in movies.
14.85 It seems to the Committee that what might be regarded as setting
multi-media productions apart from traditional cinematograph
film are two
features. First, there is the much talked-of facility, via the computer program
with which the moving pictures, text,
sound and images are associated on the
CD–ROM or other medium, for the user to manipulate these works and subject
interact with them. Second, there is the collective nature of the
multi-media production—the very combining of moving and still
text and sound—in the one composite production.
14.86 As to the interactive nature of multi-media, does this give rise to an
unprecedented copyright issue? The possibility and practice
of editing and
dubbing films is as old as silent and sound cinematography, and it does not seem
that interacting with a multi-media
production is different in essence from
editing a celluloid film, so much as making it infinitely more possible to
modify the multi-media
production in an infinite variety of ways. The question
whether a new work or other subject matter has been created by the modifier
the consequences of adapting the production modified are not new issues. As to
the collective nature of multi-media, the Committee
recalls that there are
plenty of examples of films and TV programs that have consisted of collages of
moving and still pictures with
accompanying sound (a well-known recent example
is the TV series on the US Civil War based substantially on contemporary
photographs,
supplemented by moving picture sequences shot for the series or
otherwise in recent times).
14.87 The APTV’s submission also described the technique for creating
multi-media which uses a computer to assemble the component
elements comprising
a multi-media production. The Committee does not believe—if indeed it was
suggested—that the use
of a computer as described to assemble a
multi-media production is a reason for treating the result as a new form of work
subject matter, any more than the use of a word processor to write a
novel or a computer to compose a musical piece is a cause for
regarding the
result as other than a literary or musical work, as the case may be. In general,
the foregoing analysis leads the Committee
to the conclusion that the case for
new or additional protection of multi-media as a new form of copyright material
has not been
made out. In its submission on the Draft Report, the Law Council
came to the same conclusion. The Committee also notes that the CCG
briefly considered the position of multi-media expressed the opinion that it was
not immediately apparent why a new category
of subject matter other than works
was necessary to extend copyright protection to such materials. Having also
considered this matter,
the Committee does not recommend the creation of a new
category of protection for multi-media (Recommendation 2.63(a)). That said,
Committee does accept that it is stretching the generally understood meaning of
“cinematograph film” to apply that
term to a multi-media production,
and recommends consideration as did the CCG of the adoption of a more
appropriate alternative,
such as “audiovisual work” which could
encompass cinematograph films and multi-media materials (Recommendation
14.88 The APTV submission noted, without criticism, that the computer program
by which a multi-media production is used is protected
as a literary work. Nor
did it appear to have a problem with the protection of component elements as
films, sound recordings and
works, as the case may be. However, APTV felt that
was uncertain, giving the impression that the multi-media production consisted
of the very act of displaying or transmitting the
component parts to receivers.
The Committee believes that there is some confusion here between the multi-media
production as a “film”,
and the use of that production. There seems
no reason to depart from the consistent policy of the Act of withholding
protection from
works that are not reduced to a material form. As to display on
a TV receiver of a broadcast of a multi-media production, the Committee
that just as a prerecorded TV broadcast is a film, although made purely for
putting to air, rather than cinema release,
so the assembly of film, text,
pictures and sounds prior to their transmission as a composite entity would
constitute the creation
of a film. If the act of transmission amounted to a
broadcast, that would give rise to broadcast copyright, and the multi-media
production,
as a copyright film, would be protected against unauthorised
broadcasting. As to transmission of multi-media that does not amount
broadcast and other screen displays, these are covered by the discussion of
screen display (see paragraph 14.31 above).
SECTION 35(4)—OWNERSHIP OF COPYRIGHT IN THE
PRINT MEDIA
14.89 In its Draft Report, the Committee summarised the submissions it had
received concerning the operation of s.35(4) as it applies
to the use of
journalists’ articles in computerised databases and by media monitoring
organisations. It also noted that the
then Attorney-General had made a separate
reference to the Committee in 1992 asking it to inquire into and report on
whether or not
s.35(4) should be modified or repealed. The Committee left
resolution of whether there should be any change in s.35(4) in relation
electronic news databases to its consideration of the Attorney-General’s
separate reference on s.35(4). On 22 April 1994
the Committee presented tits
report on that reference entitled
Journalists' Copyright
to the Minister for Justice, the Hon. Duncan Kerr. As that report deals fully
with the views of the Committee on s.35(4) in relation
to news databases, the
Committee refers readers to that report, rather than repeating its views
SECTION 183—GOVERNMENT USE OF COPYRIGHT
MATERIALS HELD ON DATABASE
14.90 In its submission on the Draft Report, CAL advocated the placing of
“quantitative limits on the proportion of a work that
can be included in a
report (paragraphs 10.119 and
10.122), the Committee has recommended limiting
the circumstances in which computer programs can be copied under s.183. The
feels that a distinction can be drawn between computer programs and
other works in this regard. As the Committee has noted several
times, computer
programs are a utilitarian item, used to run machines and not to be read for
information or entertainment. Because
custom software is a readily obtainable
tool, the Committee thought that the right of governments to copy programs under
s.183 should
be limited to circumstances in which the particular program needed
is not readily available. The variety of other copyright materials
governments might want to make use of—in particular, to scan onto database
for diffusion to those who need to know or
to assemble with other
materials—is almost infinite.
14.91 The Committee does not feel that the context of this reference is the
one in which it should make what would amount to a broad
recommendation for
cutting down the scope of s.183. Having acknowledged the greater value to the
user of holding a copy of a work
on database compared to holding a hard copy,
the Committee feels that this can be taken into account in the discharge of the
that s.183 imposes on governments to agree on payment for use of
the rate of
payment. CAL also suggested reform of the method of payment for use under s.183.
The Committee understands that the Government
has made a decision to enact
reforms to s.183, which are currently being drafted for presentation to
Parliament. Accordingly it is
unnecessary for the Committee to make any
recommendation in relation to s.183 as it applies to government use of copyright
held on database (Recommendation 2.64).
Published Edition Copyright
15.01 As mentioned in paragraph 1.03, the Committee was given an additional
term of reference in January 1991 to inquire whether there
is any need to amend
(the Act) to take account of electronic
and mechanical means of entering print material onto a database with capacity to
copies of that material.
15.02 Section 88 of the Act provides:
For the purposes of this Act, unless the contrary intention appears,
dramatic, musical
or artistic work or of two or more literary, dramatic, musical or artistic
works, is the exclusive right to make,
by a means that includes a photographic
process, a reproduction of the edition.
“Photograph” is defined in s.10(1)as:
. . . a product of photography or of a process similar to photography, other
than an article or thing in which visual images forming
part of a cinematograph
film have been embodied, and includes a product of xerography, and
“photographic” has a corresponding
15.03 The need for a published edition copyright was considered in the UK in
Report of the Departmental Committee on International Copyright
and also in the 1952
Report of the Copyright Committee
(Gregory Report,
Cmnd. 8662). In Australia the matter was considered by the Copyright Law Review
Committee in 1959 (the Spicer Committee).
The case for the enactment of a
provision protecting published edition copyright is set out in the 1952 Gregory
Report (UK) which
states that the reason for seeking protection for
typographical arrangements was (paragraph 3.06 ):
. . . so that a particular edition of a literary or musical work printed by
or for a publisher could not be directly and exactly copied
by an unscrupulous
competitor by photo–lithography or similar means. . . once a book or an
orchestral score has been printed,
it is now possible by photographic processes
to produce reprints quickly and, compared with the costs involved in
cheaply. If the literary or musical work printed is in
owner, and this would also be the case if the edition includes original artistic
works in copyright. But if the work printed is itself
out of copyright, there is
nothing to prevent the unscrupulous competitor from copying the work
photographically and so benefiting
unfairly from the work of the original
15.04 The report goes on to note, in paragraph 3.07, that the work done by a
publisher in bringing out an edition of a literary or
musical work is analogous
to the work which is done by the manufacturers of sound recordings. Both spend
time and effort making the
original work available in a convenient and
attractive form to the public irrespective of whether the underlying work is in
or not. The 1959 Report of the Spicer Committee (
Report of the
Committee appointed by the Attorney-General of the Commonwealth to Consider What
Alterations Are Desirable In The Copyright
Law Of The Commonwealth
Commonwealth Government Printer, Canberra, 1959) recommended that a copyright in
typographical arrangements should be enacted. Section
88 of the Act gives effect
to this recommendation.
15.05 The Committee received three submissions in relation to this issue, one
from Apple Computer Australia Pty Limited (Apple), another
from the Australian
Book Publishers Association (ABPA) and the third from Davies Ryan De Boos
Solicitors (DRD). The Apple submission
(6 June 1989) outlined some of the
processes made available by technological advances that could impact on
published edition copyright.
15.06 Apple’s submission distinguished two processes by which text and
images can be transferred to computer memory. One such
process identified was
that of scanning, referred to in paragraph 14.09, in connection with computer
similar to conventional photocopying, whereby
digital information is created and stored by means of reflected and absorbed
from the printed page that is scanned. The result is that a pictorial
image of the scanned page (like a digital photograph) can be
reproduced from the
stored information but the information/data itself cannot be used, for example,
in word processing. The other
more advanced process is known as “optical
character recognition” (OCR). OCR uses a similar process to that of
but a computer program analyses the scanned information from the page,
converts the letters to digital form and stores them in computer
memory from
which they can be displayed on a screen or printed out in hard copy form.
15.07 The ABPA submitted that there is some ambiguity whether s.88 only
applies where the reproduction involves a photographic process
or whether a
photographic process is but one of the processes by which the edition may be
reproduced. The ABPA submitted that the
section should be amended to clarify
that it is not limited to reproductions by photographic processes and suggested
that this would
most simply be achieved by deleting the words “by a means
that includes a photographic process” from the section.
15.08 It is not clear to the Committee whether either scanning or OCR include
a “photographic process”. Both appear to
rely on information being
created through the reflection and absorption of light which, at a basic level
of abstraction, is a process
similar to photography. However, the information
gained by scanning, for example, a page of a book, is stored in digital form. It
is only when this digital information is converted and printed out in hard copy
that a reproduction of the published edition can
be considered to have been
made, and then only if the material has not been reformatted, eg, by using a
different type face. The
storing of the information gained by scanning and OCR
and the display of that information on a screen would not, it appears, amount
a reproduction of the published edition.
15.09 In considering the implications for published edition copyright of the
possibilities offered by scanning onto a computer memory
and printing out from
that memory, the Committee believes that there are two issues to be addressed.
First, what is encompassed by
the word ”reproduce” in s.88 and,
second, whether the immediately succeeding words “by a means that includes
photographic process” limit the means of reproduction covered by the
published edition copyright to such process.
THE MEANING OF “REPRODUCE” IN SECTION
15.10 “Reproduction” has a broad meaning under the Act in
relation to works. For example, s.21(1) deems a literary, dramatic
work to have been “reproduced” in a material form if a record or
film is made of the work. The Committee notes,
by way of contrast, that where
“copy” is used in the Act it has a more specific meaning of a
duplicate. For example one
of the exclusive rights of owners of copyright in
sound recordings, films and broadcasts in ss.85–87 is to make
of their materials.
15.11 However, when applying “reproduction” to a published
edition, which is a subject matter other than a work, regard
must be had to its
different nature from that of “works”. Since a literary work
consists of a form of expression of ideas
and can be reduced to a material form
in a variety of ways —in manuscript, print, sound recording and computer
is understandable that under the Act it can be
“reproduced” in a variety of ways. On the other hand an edition of a
or works seems necessarily confined to one format. Under s.29, an edition
becomes a published edition when “reproductions”
of the edition have
been supplied to the public. This seems to lend weight to the inference that
“reproduction” such
as is the exclusive right comprising published
edition copyright is intended to be confined to exact copies (Professor
The Law of Intellectual Property
, 1984 at paragraph 9.151
suggests as well that the right is to make “near exact” copies. The
Committee also notes that
the Gregory Report (UK) at paragraph 3.08 stated that
the protection they recommended should relate only to exact copying by
photo–lithographic
or similar means).
“BY A MEANS THAT INCLUDES A PHOTOGRAPHIC
15.12 As regards the second issue, the Committee indicated in the Draft
Report that it is inclined to the view that the words “by
a means that
includes a photographic process” are words of limitation on the manner of
making a reproduction of an edition.
If the intention of s.88 was to stop unfair
competition with a published edition occasioned by free-riding on the labour and
outlaid by the publisher in typesetting the edition, the Committee
presumes that published edition copyright was not intended to
production of an exact copy of a published edition (a “reproduction”
of it) ie, by applying the same labour
and skill as that employed by the
publisher of the original edition thus reproduced. The second publisher would
have incurred similar
production costs to the first, and there would be fair
competition between two versions of the same work. (Of course, if the later
edition extended to copying all aspects of the original edition, including the
original publisher’s imprint, that would give
rise to actions against the
publisher of the later edition for passing off and/or misleading and deceptive
conduct). If a contrary
view were taken, that is, that the words mean in effect
“by any means, including a photographic process”, the Committee
questions why the words were included at all.
15.13 Proceeding on that basis, the bare act of scanning onto database, while
it may include a photographic process as defined in
the Act, ought not of itself
constitute a reproduction of the published edition. The work and edition as
captured on the database
look nothing like the published edition. However, if
scanning onto a database is but an intermediate step to printing hard copy from
the database which reproduces, ie, identical to or closely resembles, the
edition scanned, then that seems to achieve the same result
as the copying of an
edition by a means that includes a photographic process, and would appear to be
the sort of activity intended
to be made the subject of control by the owner of
the published edition copyright.
15.14 Having regard to the limited nature of the published edition right ie.
the protection of the published arrangement or layout
of the work the Committee
can see no reason for constraining the copyright owners’ right in the
manner the interpretation of
s.88 in paragraph 15.12 would suggest. Copyright
owners should have the right to protect the published arrangement of the work
unauthorised copying regardless of the process used to make the copy.
Therefore s.88 should unambiguously provide that the copyright
in a published
edition is the exclusive right to make a facsimile copy of that arrangement.
Accordingly, the Committee recommends
that the words “by a means that
includes a photographic process, a reproduction” in s.88 be deleted and
replaced with
the words “a facsimile copy” (Recommendation 2.65(a))
OTHER ISSUES
Reformatted editions
15.15 A further question which arises is whether the printing of a hard copy
of the scanned edition from the database should be subject
to the control of the
edition copyright owner if the edition was reformatted before being printed out
from the database? If a different
adopted, so that the print-out of the scanned edition did not look the same as
original edition should this be regarded as a reproduction? The Committee
takes the view that if the resulting edition is not a
“reproduction”,
ie, is not an exact or near exact copy, of the
original edition then it should not be subject to the copyright in the original
To have so altered the original edition that the new edition is not a
“reproduction” in the sense just used, the publisher
edition would have exercised labour and skill to effect those alterations. While
this might not be a very demanding task,
because of the capacity of facilities
for manipulating databases, the Committee is mindful that such technology is
available to all
publishers now. Thus it is open to any publisher of an original
edition to use computer technology to scan an old edition of, eg,
that s.92(2) denies copyright to an edition
that “reproduces a previous
edition”. So if another publisher did the same to the edition thus
created, that second publisher
would likely expend no less skill and labour and
effort making his edition sufficiently different from the firstmentioned edition
as did the publisher of that edition in deriving it from the old edition.
Possible repeal or amendment of published edition
15.16 The submission by DRD was made on behalf of one of its clients who
operates a media monitoring service which provides copies
of clippings from
newspapers, magazines and other periodicals to its customers. That service
operates pursuant to a licence it has
negotiated with the Copyright Agency
Limited (CAL). DRD claimed that its client was experiencing difficulties
continuing its service
because of the overlap of the published edition copyright
with that subsisting in the underlying works it copies. Certain publishers
threatening to assert their published edition copyright against the service
notwithstanding the licence to reproduce given by
15.17 Accordingly, DRD asked, on behalf of its client, that the Committee
• whether the grant of published edition copyright can now be justified
given the passage of time and the technological developments
since the 1952
Gregory Report (UK) recommended the grant of such protection;
• whether published edition copyright should be limited to the case
where the work or works comprised in such edition do not
enjoy copyright
protection; and
• whether provision should be made for the grant to media monitoring
services of compulsory licences to reproduce published editions
on payment of
equitable remuneration
DRD also suggested as an alternative that a statutory licence scheme could be
introduced for the reproduction of literary, dramatic
and artistic works for the
purpose of dissemination through a media monitoring service.
15.18 The Committee does not feel that its terms of reference in relation to
the issue of published edition copyright are sufficiently
broad to justify its
consideration of the desirability or otherwise of retaining s.88 in the Act.
Indeed the terms of reference of
the Committee in relation to s.88 are premised
on the need to ensure the continuing adequacy of the provision. For this reason
Committee does not make any specific recommendation concerning the retention
15.19 In relation to the second point that is raised in the DRD submission
the Committee has some sympathy for the position that the
media monitoring
service finds itself in and understands the reasoning behind the suggestion.
However, it seems to the Committee
that the rationale for the granting of a
published edition copyright applies equally whether or not the works comprising
the edition
are in copyright. The aim of the provision is to protect the
investment of the publisher in the typeset and layout of the publication.
will be the same regardless of whether or not copyright subsists in the
materials used. The Committee also notes that in the
case of media monitoring
services there must be a real question to be determined of whether or not the
reproduction of a single article
would constitute a reproduction of the
published edition or a substantial part of that edition.
15.20 The Committee does not consider that the issue of compulsory or
statutory licences to enable the use of published news materials
monitoring organisations comes within its terms of reference and accordingly
makes no recommendation on this issue.
15.21 However, there is another, separate issue, that the Committee feels is
raised by the reference to it concerning s.88, namely,
whether the
“editions” which are protected by s.88 are confined to printed
editions or whether they do, or should, extend
to editions of works in machine
readable database format. The Committee understands that a recent development in
publishing has been
the publication of works on CD ROM, ie, on compact disc
which, when inserted in a CD ROM reader linked to a computer, can afford
to search the material and read on screen or print out in hard copy all or
selected parts of the material. Given that a published
CD ROM consisting of
public domain works could not depend on copyright in the underlying material for
protection against unauthorised
copying, the question that has been raised in
the Committee is: should original publication in that format be protected
against copying?
15.22 The Committee believes that because of the usefulness of database
facilities to search large works there may well be an increase
in publication in
this format by publishers other than the publishers of the print editions.
Unauthorised copying may in some cases
also include unauthorised copying of the
computer program for operating the database. That issue aside, the Committee
does not see
why the person who prepared the CD ROM publication should not be
able to take action on their own behalf to prevent copying of the
publication in
that format. On the other hand, a non-print format that is another form of
book, which is a sound recording, does
not need such additional protection. Accordingly, the Committee (with one
exception) recommends
that the Act be amended to provide that published edition
(Recommendation 2.65(b)). It notes that the right would be subject to the same
limitations that apply in respect of the published
edition copyright of
traditional literary works. In other words it would be confined to facsimile
copying of the original edition
machine readable format.
15.23 To sum up, the Committee is of the view that the published edition
exclusive right to make
a reproduction of the edition, with “reproduction” being understood
to be an exact copy of the
edition. The Committee (with one exception)
recommends that the means of manufacture of the reproductions that is the
the exclusive right should include scanning onto computer memory and
printing out from the database thus created. Finally, the types
of editions that
can benefit from the protection of copyright under s.88 should not be confined
to printed editions and, specifically,
should extend to editions in computer or
machine readable format.
EXPANSION OF PUBLISHERS’ COPYRIGHT
15.24 During the course of the Committee’s deliberations with respect
to published edition copyright the Committee discussed,
among other matters,
whether published edition copyright was appropriate given changes in technology
directly affecting the publishing
industry and whether the existing
“publishers’ copyright” should be supplemented by any
additional rights. The
Committee has recently become aware that various
publishers’ associations in the European Union have been advocating that
should enjoy a right of exclusive ownership of copyright in the
electronic products they produce. Though the rights comprised in
publishers’ copyright have not been clearly defined by those advocating
their introduction, the Committee assumes that
the rights of publishers would be
similar to those enjoyed by copyright owners of sound recordings, ie. to control
reproduction and
broadcast. Such rights would be clearly “neighbouring
rights”, rewarding the effort and investment of the publisher in
work into a published form.
15.25 Although the Committee considered these matters, it did not seek or
receive any submissions concerning the extension of the
right presently enjoyed
by publishers and it did not reach any conclusions on the future of
publisher’s copyright. However,
the Committee is of the view that further
thought should be given to this issue and in particular any relevant
developments internationally.
The Committee expects that a review of
publishers’ rights could be conducted as part of the Committee’s
future inquiry
into simplification of the Act.
Other Issues
DELIVERY OF MATERIAL TO THE NATIONAL LIBRARY OF
16.01 The Committee sought the views of the National Library of Australia
(NLA) on a submission from the Department of State Services
(WA) (WADSS) that
s.201 of the
(the Act), reproduced in Appendix H,
should be amended to require the deposit of computer programs with the NLA and
should also make
special provision for programs which can only be run on
particular computers.
16.02 In its response to the Committee, the NLA indicated that the means of
ensuring the development and preservation of collections
of electronic materials
in libraries has received extensive consideration both in Australia and
overseas. Consideration of these
issues has extended to materials such as
CD–ROM discs, optical discs, electronic databases and electronically
published journals.
16.03 The NLA drew to the Committee’s attention developments in Norway,
where an attempt has been made to ensure that the requirements
legal deposit legislation will facilitate collections of library materials
that truly represent the country’s recorded documentary
heritage by
covering all or most formats in which material is published. Implementation of
the Norwegian scheme is being achieved
through subordinate legislation. Among
the problems identified when considering the form of that legislation have been
(a) for electronic material to be deposited in a standard internal format
(preferred physical formats to ensure accessibility and
long term preservation);
(b) to ensure that there is a regular deposit of material from dynamic
The NLA also advised the Committee that in Norway and Germany (which is
considering legal deposit legislation for electronic publications),
the focus of
the national deposit legislation was not on computer programs but on electronic
publications generally. Accordingly,
there is nothing in their schemes to
suggest that computer programs would necessarily be collected.
16.04 The NLA submitted that for resource reasons it would prefer to have the
power to claim certain categories (or even all categories)
of material on a
selective rather than comprehensive basis. The NLA expressly indicated that
electronic materials, with the possible
inclusion of some types of computer
programs, would be among the formats in respect of which it would like such a
discretionary power.
16.05 The Committee notes that as it presently stands, the definition of
“library material” in s.201(5) of the Act does
not include computer
programs or other materials in machine readable form. The most convenient way to
ensure that the NLA had access
to any machine readable materials (including
computer programs), that it thought appropriate for its purposes, would be to
the scope of that definition.
16.06 In its submission the Australian Copyright Council (ACC) stated that
the initial justification for s.201 was to facilitate the
establishment of a
complete collection of Australian literature. The ACC noted, however, that s.201
is drafted to apply to all library
material published in Australia, and is not
limited to material that is Australian in character. The ACC submitted that it
is difficult
to justify the continued existence of s.201, let alone provide for
its expansion.
16.07 The policy behind the introduction of s.201 is not a matter which needs
to be considered by the Committee given its terms of
reference. For this reason
no recommendation is made with respect to this aspect of the section’s
operation. Nonetheless, the
Committee is concerned to ensure that while a
general provision such as s.201 is considered desirable, it should operate in a
that is up to date.
16.08 Oracle Systems (Australia) Pty Limited (Oracle) was not in favour of a
requirement that computer programs and other materials
in machine readable form
be provided to the NLA. However, it suggested that if the scope of s.201 were to
be broadened the following
restrictions should be imposed:
(a) programs should be deposited in object form only;
(b) mandatory updates should only be provided annually;
(c) programs deposited with the NLA should only be used for fair dealing
(d) programs should only be provided on one port in one language.
16.09 The Committee notes Oracle’s concerns and the comments by the NLA
considered it unlikely that it would be given the considerable
resources which would need to be available if it were to acquire, preserve
continue to make available all materials in all electronic formats. For this
reason the Committee accepts that the NLA should
have a discretionary power in
this area. This would ensure that those classes of material that need to be
available in the long term,
and therefore justify preservation, would be
16.10 The Committee (with one exception) recommends that the definition in
s.201 of the Act be amended to include computer programs
and works stored in
computer memory. It also recommends that in relation to these classes of
materials, the NLA should be given a
discretion to determine the particular
materials for inclusion in its collection. The operation of the scheme should be
by the NLA in consultation with copyright owners (Recommendation
ARCHIVAL COPYING OF MATERIALS INTO MACHINE READABLE
16.11 The Australian Council of Archives (ACA) submitted that the Act does
not make adequate provision for the preservation of material
in machine readable
form. In its submission the ACA noted that both databases and other records in
machine readable form are already
held in archival custody and that a proportion
of such holdings can be expected to require regular copying onto new or
physical media. In some cases this will not be possible without the
use of associated computer programs. Also, unless the associated
or necessary
computer program is available, access to deposited material may be impaired or
impossible. The ACA suggested that, in
circumstances where archives do not have
a copy of the appropriate operating program, depositors should be allowed to
provide a copy
of the operating program to the archives. Where a copy of the
program is provided in such circumstances, the archives should have
the right to
use, and make a back-up copy of, that program without infringing copyright.
16.12 In its submission the Australian Council of Libraries and Information
Services (ACLIS) supported the ACA’s suggestion.
ACLIS also submitted that
any copying should only be allowed for the limited purposes of preserving and
accessing archival material
deposited in machine readable form.
16.13 The Committee has given
consideration to the ACA’s and
submission. It also notes that the protection of
computer programs as literary works under the Berne Convention for the
of Literary and Artistic Works (Berne Convention) is required by
Article 10(1) of the Agreement on Trade-Related Aspects of Intellectual
Rights (TRIPS) (See explanation of TRIPS in Chapter 3). Accordingly, Article
9(2) of the Berne Convention will apply. Article
9(2) allows member countries to
provide for the reproduction of works in special cases, where that reproduction
does not conflict
with the normal exploitation of the work and does not
unreasonably prejudice the legitimate interests of the author.
16.14 In the World Intellectual Property Organization (WIPO)
Guide to the
Berne Convention
for the protection of Literary and Artistic Works
(Geneva, 1978) (the WIPO Guide) the issue of photocopying and its impact on
authors is discussed (p. 57, paragraph 9.12). The WIPO
Guide notes that on the
one hand, large scale photocopying can seriously damage the interests of
hand, it greatly facilitates the diffusion of
knowledge. It goes on to state that the interests of copyright owners must be
with the needs of users, and that it rests with each country to take
appropriate measures best adapted to its educational, cultural,
social, and
economic development. The Committee notes that the Berne Convention should not
be treated as a statute, and that it is
entitled to be given a broader
interpretation to ensure full effect is given to its intention. In this regard
the Committee notes
that this approach appears to have been adopted by the
Australian Government when it first introduced a statutory licence in respect
photocopying for educational purposes.
16.15 In its Draft Report the Committee sought further submissions on this
issue (paragraph 16.10). In preparing the final report
the Committee has noted
the views of ACLIS and the fact that there were no submissions which opposed the
recommendation formulated
in the Draft Report. Accordingly, the Committee
reaffirms the position it adopted in the Draft Report—that to ensure the
presentation and availability of archival material, archives should be
allowed to make copies of computer programs for the limited
purposes of
preserving and accessing archival material deposited in machine readable form
(Recommendation 2.67).
PROOF OF OWNERSHIP OF COPYRIGHT IN COMPUTER
16.16 Lotus Development Pty Ltd (Lotus) and the Business Software Association
of Australia Limited (BSAA) submitted that proving ownership
of copyright in a
computer program, particularly where it was written by a number of people over a
period of time, can be a difficult
task. Lotus noted that proof of ownership
required the copyright owner to show that each programmer was an employee of the
owner at the time he or she wrote the program or that an assignment of
the copyright was executed in favour of the owner. Lotus added
that where a
program was written overseas the problem is compounded.
16.17 Lotus and the BSAA also noted that both US and UK copyright legislation
assists copyright owners. In the US there is a registration
system—even
though it is no longer compulsory to register for copyright protection as a
consequence of the US becoming a party
to the Berne Convention. In the UK s.104
and s.105 of the UK
contain provisions to the effect that where works or sound recordings and
films are issued to the public bearing a statement that
a named person is the
owner of copyright in the work that fact is presumed to be correct unless the
contrary is proved by the defendant.
16.18 Microsoft Pty Ltd (Microsoft) suggested that the issues of subsistence
and proof of ownership of copyright in computer programs
should be addressed by
the insertion of a new s.130A into the Act drafted in similar terms to the
current s.130, which allows subsistence
and ownership of copyright in sound
recordings to be presumed by reference to labels or marks on such recordings.
The Committee notes
that s.130 provides a rebuttable presumption in relation to
sound recordings similar to that provided under s.105 of the UK
(see Appendix M). The Committee also notes the submission by Lotus
seeking to justify such a provision is on the basis that ownership
disputed by a defendant as a means of discouraging a plaintiff to pursue
proceedings (because of the difficulty and cost).
Microsoft claimed that any
costs that a defendant incurs by doing so are not a sufficient deterrent because
a plaintiff only recovers
a portion of his costs.
16.19 Microsoft submitted that, in addition to, or as an alternative to its
proposal, a registration system recording details of the
ownership of copyright
should be established. It suggested that a register could be established under
the auspices of what is now
the Department of Industry, Science and Technology.
The register would constitute:
(a) a rebuttable presumption of the existence and ownership of copyright in a
computer program; and
(b) imputed knowledge of any importer or pirate of the existence and
ownership of copyright for the purpose of sections 37 and 38
of the Act.
Microsoft acknowledged that the register could not be the basis upon which
because of the
prohibition in Article 5(2) of the Berne Convention on making any formal
registration procedure a precondition to
recognition of copyright .
16.20 The Committee is not persuaded that a registration system such as that
proposed by Microsoft is warranted. It is not clear what
public benefit would
accrue from the expenditure of public moneys to establish a system that would
only serve to facilitate the copyright
owner’s capacity to assert his or
her rights. The Committee is also not convinced that a voluntary registration
system would
be of much practical value, given the likely limits on its
reliability. It would not be comprehensive and, where ownership of copyright
a particular program or other work changed hands, either the new or old owner
might fail to register the change.
16.21 As an alternative to its proposal concerning the proof of ownership of
to require a defendant
to give security for costs or pay the plaintiff’s costs on an indemnity
basis, where he or she seeks
to challenge ownership of copyright without having
any reasonable grounds for believing that the plaintiff is not the owner of the
Patents Act 1952,
the Court should have power to make a
solicitor–client costs order in favour of the plaintiff where the
defendant has put the
plaintiff to proof of ownership and/or subsistence of
determination and a declaration of ownership and/or subsistence has been made.
The Committee notes that the
Patents Act 1952
has been repealed and
replaced by the
Patents Act 1990
of that Act now provides for
the issue of a certificate of validity on the same basis as previously provided
16.22 The Committee favours an amendment to the Act to facilitate the proof

programs. In this
regard the Committee notes that the

16.23 The Explanatory Memorandum continues its explanation of the proposed
