Clause 11 inserts the new s.126A into the Act which will provide, in an
action brought under Part V of the Act in relation to a sound
recording, that if
the plaintiff files an affidavit stating that the date and place of first
publication are specified on the sound
recording label or container and
specifying that date and that place of first publication, copyright is presumed
to subsist in the
sound recording (s.126A(1)).
New s.126A(2) will provide that, if the subsistence of copyright is presumed
under subsection (1), the plaintiff is presumed to be
entitled to bring the
action under Part V in relation to the sound recording if the affidavit filed by
the plaintiff asserts that
the plaintiff is the maker of the sound recording and
is specified as such on the sound recording label or container and that he
she has not assigned the copyright in the recording.
New s.126A(3) provides that, if the plaintiff is not the maker of the sound
recording, the plaintiff is presumed to be entitled to
bring the action under
Part V in relation to the sound recording if the affidavit filed by the
plaintiff asserts that the plaintiff
is entitled to bring the action as the
assignee or exclusive licensee of the copyright in the sound recording:
(i)from the maker; or
(ii)from a person other than the maker,
and lists all the persons through whom the plaintiff claims to have the
assignment or licence of the copyright in the sound recording.
New s.126A(4) provides that the presumptions under subsections (1), (2) and
(3) apply unless the contrary is established . . .
In effect s.126 of the Act was to be read subject to the proposed s.126A. The
Committee notes that the Bill has not as yet been reintroduced.

the proposed s.126A of the Bill be introduced in respect
of computer programs.
The view of the Committee in respect of this matter has been widely supported

PRIVILEGE AGAINST SELF-INCRIMINATION
Lotus’s submission
16.25 In its 1990 submission to the Committee Lotus raised as a concern for
self-incrimination,
submitting that its operation may “severely hamper” the prosecution
of an infringement action. At
common law, a person is not obliged to answer
questions or to produce documents where the giving of answers or the production
documents would tend to expose him or her to a criminal charge. Lotus
envisaged a situation where the privilege may be used to withhold
containing evidence about the number of infringing copies of a computer program
made and sold or the state of knowledge
of an infringer. Lotus referred to the
decision of Waddell CJ in
BPA Industries Limited
where his Honour expressed the view that the privilege could be used
to defeat an Anton Piller order and that a similar provision
to that of s.72 of
Supreme Court Act 1981
(UK) should be adopted in NSW. Lotus also
submitted that such a provision should be adopted. It should be noted that the
has held that the privilege does not to apply to corporations
Environment Protection Authority
Caltex Refining Co Pty Ltd
[1993] HCA 74
(1993) 118 ALR 392)
and the Full Court of the Federal Court has held
similarly, in a majority decision, that the privilege against self-exposure to a
penalty does not apply to corporations (
Trade Practices Commission
Abbco Ice Works Pty Ltd and Others
[1994] FCA 1279
(1994) 123 ALR 503.
Court’s decision is now reflected in the Commonwealth
Evidence Bill 1993
(discussed further below from paragraph 16.28).
Position in the UK
16.26 The privilege against self-incrimination was considered in the UK in
the early 1980s and led to the inclusion of s.72 in the
Supreme Court Act
(see Appendix K). The effect of the provision is to remove the
privilege against self-incrimination in proceedings relating to the
infringement
of intellectual property rights. However, s.72 also provides that no statement
or admission made by a person in answering
a question or otherwise complying
with an order in such proceedings is to be admissible evidence against that
person in criminal
proceedings. Lotus submitted that such a provision should be
adopted in New South Wales.
Review of Commonwealth and States evidence
16.27 Reform of the law of evidence was considered by the Standing Committee
of Attorneys-General (SCAG) and on 25 October 1991 SCAG
agreed to support, in
principle, the adoption of substantially uniform evidence laws based on
Commonwealth and New South Wales Evidence
Bills introduced in that year
(Evidence Bill 1991 (Cth), Evidence Bill (NSW) introduced into Legislative
Assembly on 23 March 1993).
A common text for New South Wales and Commonwealth
Evidence Bills was then
prepared. The Commonwealth’s Evidence Bill
1993 (the Bill) was passed by the House of Representatives on 19 October 1994.
Committee understands that the Bill is likely to be debated in the Winter
1995 sittings of Parliament.
16.28 The Bill includes a provision similar to clause 120 of the Commonwealth
Evidence Bill 1991
, concerning the privilege in respect of self-incrimination.
The Explanatory Memorandum to the
Evidence Bill 1993
explains the operation of
clause 128 (pp. 36–37):
This clause provides a general rule that a court must not require a witness
to give particular evidence if the witness objects that
the evidence may tend to
prove he or she has committed an offence or is liable to a civil penalty, and
the court is satisfied there
are reasonable grounds for the objection.
The court may require the witness to give the evidence . . . if the interest
of justice require the witness to give the evidence.
The certificate has the effect that evidence given by the witness to which it
relates, and evidence of any information or document
or thing obtained as a
direct or indirect consequence of the person having given evidence, is not
admissible in any Australian proceeding,
except a criminal proceeding in respect
of the falsity of the evidence.
16.29 As mentioned in paragraph 16.25 the privilege against self
incrimination has been abolished as it applies to corporations and
representatives. Clause 187 of the
Evidence Bill 1993
has the effect that if a
body corporate is required to answer questions or give information, produce
documents or other thing or
do any other act it is not entitled to refuse or
fail to comply with the requirement on the grounds that complying with the
might tend to incriminate the body or make the body liable to a penalty.
The Committee understands that where an individual is representing
a corporation
clause 187 will apply. Additionally an individual representing a corporation
would be bound by clause 187 even where
in the process of complying with a
request to a body corporate that individual may be incriminated.
16.30 The Committee agrees with Lotus’s submission that the privilege
against self-incrimination should be removed in respect
of civil proceedings
relating to the infringement of copyright, provided that an indemnity is also
given in relation to other proceedings.
Submissions to the Committee following
the Draft Report indicated that there is broad based support for this
proposal.The Committee
understands that the provisions of the
Evidence Bill
apply to civil proceedings relating to copyright infringement and will have the
effect that the privilege will not be available
to a witness where that witness
has no reasonable grounds for not giving particular evidence or where the
interests of justice require
that the witness give evidence. The Committee
expects that in most cases these qualifications on the privilege and the fact
the privilege does not apply to corporations will assist litigants in
makes no further
recommendation in relation to the privilege against self-incrimination
(Recommendation 2.70).
POSSESSION IN THE COURSE OF A BUSINESS
16.31 Lotus submitted that an additional infringing act of “possession
in the course of a business” should be added to
s.38 of the Act. Currently
s.38 prohibits certain commercial dealings in infringing copies or in copies
which have been imported
without the consent of the copyright owner. Lotus
suggested that s.38 should be extended to include possession by a person in the
course of business of an article which he or she knows or has reason to believe
is an infringing copy of a work. In this regard Lotus
pointed out that s.23 of
the UK CDP Act (see Appendix L) provides, in part:
The copyright in a work is infringed by a person who, without the licence of
the copyright owner—
(a) possesses in the course of a business . . .
an article which is, and which he knows or has reason to believe is an
infringing copy of the work.
16.32 Lotus submitted that a provision of this sort would assist copyright
owners in situations where infringing copies of a computer
program were found at
the premises of a commercial organisation, but it could not be proved that the
organisation had made the copies
or was using them. The Committee notes that
s.132(2A) of the Act in effect makes it an offence for a person to have in his
possession an article for various commercial purposes:
. . . or for any other purpose to an extent that will affect prejudicially
the owner of the copyright in the work . . .
if the person knows or ought reasonably to know the article is an infringing
copy. This provision applies to both works and subject
matter other than works
(s.132(4)). Section 132(2A) was introduced into the Act by the
Amendment Act 1986
. In his second reading speech the then Attorney-General,
Lionel Bowen MP, commented (House of Representatives 1986,
vol. HR149, p. 3668):
The Government has been concerned that the
offences do not
afford owners protection at each point at which they are vulnerable to
commercial piracy. The new offence of possession
of an infringing copy for
commercial purposes, and the new and restructured offences of causing infringing
public performance of
(emphasis added)
16.33 The Committee received numerous responses to its draft proposal in
respect of this matter. Oracle was in support of the adoption
of legislation
similar to that of the UK CDP Act
relating to the infringing activity of
“possession in the course of business”. Oracle’s submission
suggested that
corporate overuse of software by individuals fell into two broad
categories:
(a) overuse by those who were indifferent about copyright protection; and
(b) overuse by those who mistakenly make copies.
16.34 Oracle submitted that the instances of copyright abuse would be
significantly reduced by the enactment of appropriate legislation
making it an
offence to illegally possess copyright material in the course of business. The
ACC also supported the enactment of a
provision similar to the UK Act, whereby
the possession of infringing copies in the course of business would infringe
16.35 In its submission the Law Council of Australia (LCA) recommended the
implementation of provisions like those in the UK but emphasised
the need for
the law to be drafted so that it would not lead to harsh results. The example
given was that in certain situations,
over zealous employees illegally copy
programs to help them with their work outside of hours. The LCA submission
warned of the dangers
in enforcing vicarious liability on employers in such
circumstances. The LCA’s submission suggested that the only basis for
liability for employers in any such legislation should be if the employer had
actual, rather than imputed, notice that illegal copying
had occurred.
16.36 The Committee notes that s.132(2A) is narrower in scope than s.23 of
the UK CDP Act in that an element of the offence is having
a purpose, viz, trade
or other prejudicial distribution, whereas the UK provision refers merely to
possession in the course of business
without requiring proof of any purpose. In
the Committee’s view it would be consistent with the policy behind the
introduction
of s.132(2A) if copyright owners were to be granted a counterpart
right to bring civil actions under Parts III and IV of the Act
for possession,
for commercial purposes, of infringing copies known to be such.
16.37 The Committee believes that the best way to ensure that copyright
breaches are kept to a minimum is by imposing higher standards
of care on all
Committee, therefore, recommends that the
Act be amended so that an employer
will be guilty of an offence where he or she is found in possession of an
article which he or
she knew, or ought to have reasonably known, is an
infringing copy of a work (Recommendation 2.71(a)). Also consistently with the
Committee’s recommendation that software penalties be brought into line
with those applying in respect of unauthorised commercial
cinematographic films, it recommends that (Recommendation 2.71(b)):
(a) a first offence be punishable with a fine not exceeding $1,500 for the
article, or for each article, to which the offence relates
or imprisonment for a
period not exceeding 2 years, or both; or if the person is a body
corporate—a fine not exceeding $7,500
for the article, or for each
article, to which the offence relates; and
(b) for any subsequent offence a penalty not exceeding $50,000 for an
individual, or 5 years imprisonment, or both; or if the person
corporate—a fine not exceeding $250,000 .
ANTON PILLER ORDERS
16.38 Microsoft submitted that the common law relating to Anton Piller orders
should be codified such that subject to:
(a) an arguable case of infringement by a pirate or grey marketeer being
produced to the Court by the plaintiff;
(b) an arguable case of ownership being produced, ie, by reliance on the
proposed s.130A (see paragraph 16.18) or a declaration of
the court (see
paragraph 16.21);
(c) the usual undertakings being given by the plaintiff to the Court as to
(d) payment into court of a bond by the plaintiff in respect of such damages;
(e) the imposition of special penalties for misleading or withholding
relevant evidence from the Court by the plaintiff
then the plaintiff should be entitled to an Anton Piller order.
16.39 The Committee is of the view that the proposal by Microsoft merits
serious consideration, concerning as it does a procedural
issue that is very
important to the enforcement of copyright. However, for the very reason that it
affects copyright generally the
Committee feels that it falls outside the
Committee’s terms of reference, which are limited to computer programs,
and published editions. Accordingly, the Committee feels unable to
make a specific recommendation in respect of it, other than to
suggest serious
consideration of this proposal for the codification of the law on Anton Piller
orders (see paragraph 2.72).
PROGRAM LOCKS
16.40 The Committee received a number of submissions concerning the use of
locking devices on computer programs (already discussed
in paragraphs
10.90–10.94). Autodesk Australia Pty Ltd and Gilbert and Tobin Solicitors
noted that Article 6 of the draft EC
Directive on the legal protection of
computer programs (as it stood in 1990) provided that it would be an
infringement to:
. . . make, import, possess or deal with articles intended specifically to
facilitate the removal or circumvention of any technical
means which may have
been applied to protect a program.
Article 7(1)(c) of the Directive, as adopted, provides that Member States
shall provide appropriate remedies against a person committing:
any act of putting into circulation, or the possession for commercial
purposes of, any means the sole intended purpose of which is
to facilitate the
unauthorised removal or circumvention of any technical device which may have
been applied to protect a computer
Sun Microsystems Australia Pty Ltd also submitted that the elimination,
deletion or by-passing of copyright protection devices should
be an offence
punishable by a fine. The WADSS submitted that copy protection should be limited
to computer programs licensed for
use in a commercial environment.
Interestingly, in its submission the Queensland Department of Education urged
that the production
of locked computer programs should be prohibited by
legislation.
16.41 At the Public Hearings held in July 1990, IBM Australia Ltd drew to the
Committee’s attention s.296 of the UK CDP Act.
Section 296 deals with
devices designed to circumvent copy protection. The section applies to works
issued in electronic form that
are copy protected. It provides that the person
issuing copies to the public has the same rights against a person who:
(a) makes, imports, sells or lets for hire, offers or exposes for sale or
hire, or advertises for sale or hire, any device or means
specifically designed
or adapted to circumvent the form of copy-protection employed, or
(b) publishes information intended to enable or assist persons to circumvent
that form of copy-protection,
as a copyright owner has in respect of an infringement of copyright.
A person will be caught by the section if that person knows, or has reason to
believe, that the device will be used to make infringing
copies. The section
also extends to such devices the rights under that Act to delivery up or seizure
of certain articles.
16.42 The Committee is of the view that the unauthorised circumvention of
locking devices should be actionable at the suit of copyright
owners and their
exclusive licensees. In other words the use of any device which enables the
unauthorised use of a program should
be prohibited. Accordingly, the Committee
recommends that the Act be ammended to provide that copyright owners and their
licensees should have the right to prevent the commercial manufacture,
importation, distribution and possession for commercial purposes
which are designed to facilitate the unauthorised circumvention of locks or
other devices applied to protect computer
programs from unauthorised copying
(Recommendation 2.73).
16.43 The Committee would like to emphasise that there will be instances
where the users of copyright material will have cause to
legitimately circumvent
locking devices, such as for the purpose of decompilation for error correction
or to create a new interoperable
program. The Committee is of the view that the
onus should lie with users of computer programs to clearly understand the
circumstances
in which the circumvention of a program locking device will be
permissible.
PENALTIES FOR CRIMINAL OFFENCES UNDER THE ACT
16.44The BSAA submitted that
29 January 1993, p.4):
. . . the penalties for software piracy should be substantially greater and
not less than those for films and videos.
16.45 In its Draft Report the Committee noted that the
had the effect that the penalties for piracy of sound recording
offences under the Act would be brought into line with those for
cinematograph
films. The Committee also recommended in the Draft Report that, having regard to
the size and importance of the computer
program industry and the potential
damage that unauthorised commercial copying of programs would cause to copyright
owners (as well
as the ease with which it can be accomplished,) and in order to
discourage computer software piracy, the criminal penalties in relation
computer programs should be brought into line with those currently applying to
films and proposed for sound recordings (Draft
Recommendation 2.54).
16.46 In a supplementary submission to the Committee in response to the Draft
Report, the BSAA supported the recommendation made by
the Committee in the Draft
Report. The BSAA also emphasised that increased penalties for software copyright
infringement could only
be effective if:
(a) the Australian Federal Police are given the resources and show a
commitment to bringing criminal prosecutions for software copyright
infringement; and
(b) the courts actually impose substantial penalties for offences of
software copyright infringement.
16.47 As part of its submission the BSAA referred to the current criminal
sanctions for software copyright breaches in the United
Kingdom, Taiwan and the
United States. The Committee notes that the penalties in each of these countries
are higher than those currently
provided for in the Act. For instance, the UK
CDP Act provides in s.107(4), in relation to criminal liability for the making
with infringing articles, that where a person is found liable on
summary conviction, the penalty can be up to six months imprisonment
up to £5,000 (s.107(4)(a)). Alternatively, where a person is convicted on
indictment, they may face a fine or imprisonment
for up to two years or both
(s.107(4)(b)). The Committee understands that the amount of a fine that can be
imposed is not specifically
limited by any statute.
16.48 In addition, unlike the current provisions in the Australian Act, the
UK CDP Act does not distinguish between first and subsequent
offences and it is
therefore possible in the UK, for a person found liable of an offence for the
first time to be imprisoned. It
should be noted that the penalties stated in the
UK CDP Act are maximum penalties and that courts in the UK, as in Australia,
discretion in imposing penalties. Consequently, not all offences would
attract prison sentences or the highest fine. The Committee
understands that the
Government’s current policy in relation to Commonwealth offences is to set
down maximum penalties for
offences, not to distinguish between first and
subsequent offences and, as mentioned above, to allow discretion to courts to
up to the maximum penalty as they see fit in the circumstances. Future
amendments to the Act may therefore result in a change to
the present
distinction between first and subsequent offences.
16.49 If the Committee’s recommendation in relation to computer
programs is implemented, the maximum penalties that could be
imposed for a first
offence for commercial dealing or possession of infringing copies would be
$1,500 or two years imprisonment or
both. For a subsequent offence a five year
prison term could be imposed. The Committee believes that an amendment to this
would be an effective deterrent to address the issue of unauthorised
dealings in computer programs and therefore endorses its previous
recommendation
(Recommendation 2.74).
DISTRIBUTION OF COPYRIGHT MATERIALS VIA COMPUTER
BULLETIN BOARD SYSTEMS
16.50 At a meeting in March 1994 the Committee considered the issue of the
unauthorised copying and distribution of copyright materials
via computer
bulletin board systems (BBS). However, it should be noted that the Committee
received no submissions regarding copyright
infringement through use of BBS and
was not in a position to seek submissions on this issue during 1994.
Accordingly, the Committee
has not made any recommendations on the most
appropriate way in which to respond to the copyright issues raised by BBS (and
the international
network, Internet).
16.51 The technology of computer bulletin boards systems (BBS) can be
described as follows (Bulletin Board Task Force,
Regulation of Computer
Bulletin Board Systems
(August 1994),Appendix C, p.54):
Generally speaking, a ‘computer bulletin board’ is a computer
program that simulates the functions of the physical bulletin
boards found in
supermarkets, universities and other public gathering places.
To create a BBS, an individual needs a computer, the necessary software
(i.e., the computer program), and the means to communicate
with other computers,
usually through a modem (a device that enables the user to send and receive
digital data over a telephone line).
BBS can be configured to be accessible to
select groups or the public at large, freely or on a charged basis. Little
specialist knowledge,
equipment or capital outlay is needed to establish a
simple BBS. The computer hardware required and the software necessary to create
a BBS are affordable, with the latter often being freely available.
BBS are typically accessed via modems and the public telephone network. Users
simply dial the number of the board through their computer
in order to gain
access to a central or ‘host’ computer system. They generally
register their name and other details
to gain BBS access rights. They are then
given a password and often charged annual access fees.
16.52 The Committee understands that BBS not only allow callers to send
messages to the BBS, read and delete existing messages but
also copy information
already on the BBS into their own computer. It is the ability to copy
information, such as computer programs
and other copyright materials, by
downloading them from the BBS that is a concern for copyright owners. The
anonymity with which
users can access BBS and post messages makes detection of
such practices extremely difficult. Equally importantly, BBS operators
participate in the exchange of information between users, nor do they usually
know who the users actually are as most use
code names. The fact that the
posting of messages can involve no positive act by the operator together with
the large volume of messages
that may be on a board at any one time, means that
no single operator could be aware of everything being posted on to and taken off
the board at any particular time. Determining who should be held responsible for
a BBS is an issue that requires
further consideration and without the benefit of submissions on this issue the
Committee has not
made any recommendations on who should be held responsible.
This issue is discussed further in paragraphs 16.62 to 16.65.
Bulletin Board Task Force
16.53 In February 1994 the Attorney-General, Michael Lavarch, and the
Minister for Communications and the Arts, Michael Lee, jointly
established a
Task Force to consider options for the regulation of bulletin boards. The focus
of the Task Force’s inquiry was
censorship and content issues. However, it
was also required to examine the adequacy of existing laws to deal with the
technology. In its August 1994 report the Task Force has made
recommendations with respect to the regulation of the content of material
available on BBS, especially “adult” material, whether it be
pornographic, violent or sexually explicit material. While
the placing and use
of such material may involve copyright issues, these are not addressed in the
Task Force’s report. The
Task Force recommended, as its preferred option,
that the BBS community be self-regulating ie. that BBS operators and users
and adopt guidelines for their industry. However, the question which
arises is whether such self-regulation is appropriate with respect
to the use of
US legal developments
16.54 Actions for copyright infringement relating to the distribution of
of cases in the
US. These cases highlight how the use of BBS may result in infringement of
the issue of who should be held
responsible for such infringements. In
Playboy Enterprises Inc. v
George Frena d/b/a Techs Warehouse BBS Systems and Consulting, and Mark
839 F. Supp. 1552 (M.
D. Fla. 1993) (
Playboy case
) the Florida
District Court considered a copyright infringement suit brought by the magazine,
Playboy, against the operator of a
subscription computer bulletin board for
unauthorised distribution of its copyrighted photographs. Schlesinger J held
that two copyrights
of the plaintiff had been infringed, namely the right to
distribute copies of a work to the public and the right to display pictorial
works publicly.
16.55 In another unreported case 140 music publishers sued CompuServe
(database operators) for copyright infringement of 500 musical
works which had
been uploaded onto one of CompuServe’s bulletin boards, MIDI/Music Forum.
The Committee is aware that in the
Intellectual Property Law Bulletin
(Vol 7, No. 6, July 1994, p.80) it was reported that subscribers to this
particular CompuServe bulletin board could download to their
own personal
computers, musical material placed there by other subscribers. The music
publishers charged CompuServe with wilful acts
of copyright infringement by
encouraging subscribers to commit unauthorised reproduction of musical works by
providing the bulletin
board service (
Frank Music Corp.
Incorporated
Unreported, US District Court, Southern District of New York,
93 Cir 8.53). The Committee is not aware of the outcome of this action.
filed 29 Nov 1993)
16.56 In a more recent decision
Sega Enterprises
(No. C93–4262 CW, 1994 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 5266 (N. D. Cal.
Mar. 28. 1994) (
) the court granted a preliminary injunction
finding that facilitating unauthorised copying by providing facilities,
direction, knowledge
and encouragement amounted to contributory infringement on
the part of the BBS operator. With the knowledge of the defendant, users
bulletin board, Maphia, were uploading and downloading unauthorised copies of
Sega’s video games through use of devices
provided by the defendant.
16.57 Finally, the Committee is aware of further copyright infringement
actions being taken against bulletin board operators. In the
(Vol 2, No. 9, p.11) it is reported that David LaMacchia is charged
with criminal fraud for making $1 million worth of computer software
on the Internet. Similarly the
Wall Street Journal
reported on 27
September 1994 that an operator of an internationally used bulletin board was
charged with criminal violation of copyright
for the unauthorised copying of
computer software. The newspaper article reported that Lotus Development
Corporation’s Lotus
1–2–3 and Symantec Corp.’s Norton
Utilities were available to subscribers to the service for US$99 per year.
US Green Paper on Intellectual Property and the
National Information Infrastructure
16.58 In July 1994 the US Working Group on Intellectual Property Rights,
released a preliminary draft Green Paper that considers the
intellectual
property implications of the National Information Infrastructure. The Green
Paper discusses the range of rights presently
enjoyed by copyright owners of
works and how these may be adapted to address technological advancements such as
distribution of works
electronically, for instance by BBS. To address possible
deficiencies in the US
, not only arising from BBS, the
Working Group recommended that the distribution right be amended to include
transmission of copies
Legal position in Australia
16.59 The relevant provision of the Act is s.132 which is concerned with
offences arising from the making of and dealing in articles
that are infringing
copies of copyright works. The making of articles for sale or hire, their sale,
hire or exposition for trade
and the importation, distribution and possession of
them are all activities which are prohibited by this section. By virtue of
s.132 applies to both copyright “works” and “subject
matter other than works”.
16.60 The Committee notes that s.132 of the Act may not apply where
electronic reproductions of works are made, even where they may
be infringing
copies and/or where this was facilitated by the use of a BBS. This view is based
on two related issues of interpretation
of the section. These are as
(i) As the Act refers to dealing in “
infringing copies of works, for electronic copies or reproductions of works to
be covered by s.132 they would have
to fall within the meaning of
“article”. It is arguable that “article” means a
physical or tangible article
and does not extend to electronic infringing copies
of copyright materials.
(ii) Although, s.132(5A) of the Act, introduced by the
provides as follows:
For the purposes of this section, a
transmission
by a person of
a computer program that is received and recorded so as to result in the creation
of an infringing copy of the computer
program shall be deemed to be a
distribution
by the person of that infringing copy (emphasis added).
and would appear to apply to cases where works are distributed by electronic
methods, the Committee notes that the use of bulletin
boards may not involve any
transmission
distribution
by either the BBS operator or
any users with the result that the use of this technology to provide access to
not be caught by the offence provisions in s.132. For
• The person who places a copyright work or film onto a BBS would not
appear to be distributing it, since this act would not
appear to involve
delivery to known persons or known subscribers and could not therefore be
construed as an act of distribution.
For example, it is possible that a BBS
message may not be accessed and therefore the chain of distribution from
distributor to distributee
would be broken.
• Those having access to a BBS can if they choose retrieve a work or
film from the BBS and copy it onto their hard disc. This
act can occur without
the involvement of the operator of the BBS and so there will not be a
“transmission by a person”
as required by the section.
• In addition, the person who receives and records any copyright
material accessed from the BBS would not be guilty of an offence
of distribution
(or transmission), unless the meaning of distribution was made wide enough to
encompass an act of acquiring a copy
for oneself. It should be noted that the
section does not create an offence of receiving and recording the transmission
for the act of transmission.
In reaching this conclusion in respect to s. 132(5A), the Committee notes
that the Explanatory Memorandum (EM) regarding the s.132(5A)
amendment says
This provision will thus extend the existing prohibition on commercial
distribution of ‘pirate’ copies of programs to
cover the situation
where the program is supplied to the purchaser not as a physical copy but by way
of a transmission which he can
It appears to the Committee that although the intention expressed in the EM
was to expand the concept of distribution in s.132(2)
to include distribution by
electronic means, the amendment does not address the issue discussed in (i) and
also, does not go far
enough to cover the special case of
“transmission” by BBS.
16.61 The Committee also notes that the Copyright Convergence Group has
recommended the introduction of a new transmission right for
and that the Government has announced its intention to adopt the CCG’s
recommendations. The Committee recommends
that when such a right is introduced
consideration should be given to the amendment of s.132 and more specifically
s.132(5A) which
also concerns offences arising from the transmission of computer
programs (Recommendation 2..75).
16.62 Although there is doubt as to whether the offence provisions in the Act
apply to BBS, copyright will be infringed by the unauthorised
distribution of
materials will result in their reproduction.
The difficulty for copyright owners
is that the individuals concerned may be hard to trace. This raises the question
of whether the
bulletin board operator should be deemed to be responsible for
such infringements, (this appears to be the approach that US courts
are taking).
The Committee notes that the position of copyright owners is made even more
difficult by the fact that some BBS are
international in their operation. Thus
it is quite possible that copyright materials may be loaded into the BBS by a
user in a different
country to the operator of the BBS and that another user in
a third country might download that material. Any or all of the parties
a case may be in a different country to the copyright owner.
16.63 In relation to the liability of BBS operators in Australia, mention
should be made of s.13(2) of the Act which provides:
For the purposes of this Act, the exclusive right to do an act in relation to
a work, an adaptation of a work or any other subject-matter
includes the
exclusive right to authorize a person to do that act in relation to that work,
adaptation or other subject-matter.
The effect of this section, as far as copyright infringement is concerned, is
that a person may be liable for indirect, as distinct
from direct, infringement,
by in some way authorising another person to commit an infringement. Australian
courts, as well as UK
courts, have held that authorise should be understood in
its ordinary dictionary sense of “sanction, approve and
countenance”.
The leading Australian decision in this area,
of New South Wales
(1975) 133 CLR 1
endorsed this view
and also discussed what types of actions or inactions may constitute
authorisation. In his text,
The Law of Intellectual Property
Company, Sydney, 1984, pp. 228–234), Professor Ricketson provides a guide
to what may be elucidated from the cases
in this area and gives some general
propositions on authorisation. First, he suggests that before a person can be
held to have authorised
an infringement of copyright he/she must have had some
power to prevent it or had some control over the person committing the
act. Next, there must be a specific act/s of infringement linked to
the actions of the alleged authoriser; it is not sufficient to
infringement was encouraged, there must actually be infringement. Finally, there
is no requirement that the alleged authoriser
have knowledge that a specific
infringement has occurred. Professor Ricketson concludes his discussion by
noting that liability for
steps are taken to avoid infringing acts occurring, depending on the
circumstances
of the case.
16.64 In paragraph 14.20 the Committee acknowledged that the position of
operators of databases and bulletin board operators in respect
reproduction of copyright materials by the users of their services was somewhat
different. Although not offering any concluded
view on whether bulletin board
operators would generally be found to have authorised copyright infringements,
it appears to the Committee
that the issue of whether bulletin board operators
have effective control over the BBS they provide would be crucial to any
on this matter. As argued by defendants in the US, bulletin board
operators claim not to have knowledge of or control over the activities
subscribers. Though this argument has not been successful to date in the US, it
cannot be assumed that it would be equally
unsuccessful in Australia.
16.65 If bulletin board operators are not regarded as having authorised
is should they
nonetheless be deemed to be liable? Additionally, should such bulletin board
operators be able to avoid liability
by, for instance, posting warning notices
and their content may have a bearing on any avoidance of liability if,
for instance, they were not to be taken seriously and were
merely token
gestures. As these issues were not raised in submissions to the Committee, nor
mentioned in the Draft Report, the Committee
does not feel it has been able to
give these issues the consideration which they require. Accordingly, it makes no
specific recommendation
on any of the issues if has raised. The Committee does,
however, recommend that the issues raised by BBS be considered in the context
the proposed review of the Act (Recommendation 2.76).
solutions to the copyright problems raised by BBS will, in the Committee’s
view, have to acknowledge that the particular
form of distribution of copyright
materials using BBS, sometimes involving both piracy and own-use copying,
appears to be extremely
difficult to detect and potentially uncontrollable and
for this reason may have quite severe implications for all copyright owners,
just owners of computer programs.

Article 2(1) of the Berne Convention for the Protection of Literary and
Artistic Works
(1) The expression “literary and artistic works” shall include
every production in the literary, scientific and artistic
domain, whatever may
be the mode or form of its expression, such as books, pamphlets and other
writings; lectures, addresses, sermons
and other works of the same nature;
dramatic or dramatico-musical works; choreographic works and entertainments in
dumb show; musical
compositions with or without words; cinematographic works to
which are assimilated works expressed by a process analogous to cinematography;
works of drawing, painting, architecture, sculpture, engraving and lithography;
photographic works to which are assimilated works
expressed by a process
analogous to photography, works of applied art; illustrations, maps, plans,
sketches and three-dimensional
works relative to geography, topography,
architecture or science.
The Council of the European Communities Directive on the Legal
Protection of Computer Programs — 14 May 1991 (91/250/EEC)
THE COUNCIL OF THE EUROPEAN COMMUNITIES,
Having regard to the Treaty establishing the European Economic Community and
in particular Article 100a thereof,
Having regard to the proposal from the Commission,
In cooperation with the European Parliament,
Having regard to the opinion of the Economic and Social Committee,
Whereas computer programs are at present not clearly protected in all Member
States by existing legislation and such protection, where
it exists, has
different attributes;
Whereas the development of computer programs requires the investment of
considerable human, technical and financial resources while
computer programs
can be copied at a fraction of the cost needed to develop them
independently;
Whereas computer programs are playing an increasingly important role in a
broad range of industries and computer program technology
can accordingly be
considered as being of fundamental importance for the Community’s
industrial development;
Whereas certain differences in the legal protection of computer programs
offered by the laws of the Member States have direct and
negative effects on the
functioning of the common market as regards computer programs and such
differences could well become greater
as Member States introduce new legislation
on this subject;
Whereas existing difference having such effects need to be removed and new
ones prevented from arising, while difference not adversely
affecting the
functioning of the common market to a substantial degree need not be removed or
prevented from arising;
Whereas the Community’s legal framework on the protection of computer
programs can accordingly in the first instance be limited
to establishing that
Member States should accord protection to computer program under copyright law
as literary works and, further,
to establishing who and what should be
protected, the exclusive rights on which protected persons should be able to
rely in order
to authorise or prohibit certain acts and for how long the
protection should apply;
Whereas, for the purpose of this Directive, the term “computer
program” shall include programs in any form, including
those which are
incorporated into hardware, whereas this term also includes preparatory design
work leading to the development of
a computer program provided that the nature
of the preparatory work is such that a computer program can result from it at a
Whereas, in respect of the criteria to be applied in determining whether or
not a computer program is an original work, no tests as
to the qualitative or
aesthetic merits or the program should be applied;
Whereas the Community is fully committed to the promotion of international
standardisation;
Whereas the function of a computer program is to communicate and work
together with other components of a computer system and with
users and, for this
purpose, a logical and, where appropriate, physical interconnection and
interaction is required to permit all
elements of software and hardware to work
with other software and hardware and with users in all the ways in which they
are intended
to function;
Whereas the parts of the program which provide for such interconnection and
interaction between elements of software and hardware
are generally known as
“interfaces”;
Whereas this functional interconnection and interaction is generally known as
“interoperability”; whereas such interoperability
can be defined as
the ability to exchange information and mutually to use the information which
has been exchanged;
Whereas, for the avoidance of doubt, it has to be made clear that only the
expression of a computer program is protected and that
ideas and principles
which underlie any element of a program, including those which underlie its
interfaces, are not protected by
Whereas, in accordance with principle of copyright, to the extent that logic,
algorithms and programming languages comprise ideas
and principles, those ideas
and principles are not protected under this Directive;
Whereas, in accordance with the legislation and jurisprudence of the Member
States and the international copyright conventions, the
expression of those
ideas and principles is to be protected by copyright;
Whereas, for the purposes of this Directive, the term “rental”
means the making available for use, for a limited period
of time and for
profit-making purposes, of a computer program or a copy thereof; whereas this
term does not include public lending,
which, accordingly, remains outside the
scope of this Directive;
Whereas the exclusive rights of the author to prevent the unauthorised
reproduction of his work have to be subject to a limited exception
in the case
of a computer program to allow the reproduction technically necessary for the
use of that program by the lawful acquirer;
Whereas this means that the acts of loading and running necessary for the use
of a copy of a program which has been lawfully acquired,
and the act of
correction of its errors, may not be prohibited by contract; whereas, in the
absence of specific contractual provisions,
including when a copy of the program
has been sold, any other act necessary for the use of the copy of a program may
be performed
in accordance with its intended purpose by a lawful acquirer of
Whereas a person having a right to use a computer program should not be
prevented from performing acts necessary to observe, study
or test the
functioning of the program, provided that these acts do not infringe the
Whereas the unauthorised reproduction, translation, adaptation or
transformation of the form of the code in which a copy of a computer
program has
been made available constitutes an infringement of the exclusive right of the
Whereas, nevertheless, circumstances may exist when such a reproduction of
the code and translation of its form within the meaning
of Article 4(a) and (b)
are indispensable to obtain the necessary information to achieve the
interoperability of an independently
created program with other programs;
Whereas it has therefore to be considered that in these limited circumstances
only, performance of the acts of reproduction and translation
by or on behalf of
a person having a right to use a copy of the program is legitimate and
compatible with fair practice and must
therefore be deemed not to require the
authorisation of the right holder;
Whereas an objective of this exception is to make it possible to connect all
components of a computer system, including those of different
manufacturers, so
that they can work together;
Whereas such an exception to the author’s exclusive rights may not be
used in a way which prejudices the legitimate interests
of the rightholder or
which conflicts with a normal exploitation of the program;
Whereas, in order to remain in accordance with the provisions of the Berne
Convention for the Protection of Literary and Artistic
Works, the term of
protection should be the life of the author and fifty years from the first of
January of the year following the
year of his death or, in the case of an
anonymous or pseudonymous work, 50 years from the first of January of the year
the year in which the work is first published;
Whereas protection of computer programs under copyright laws should be
without prejudice to the application, in appropriate cases,
of other forms of
protection; whereas, however, any contractual provisions contrary to Article 6
or to the exceptions provided for
in Article 5(2) and (3) should be null and
Whereas the provisions of this Directive are without prejudice to the
application of the competition rules under Article 85 and 86
of the Treaty if a
dominant supplier refuses to make information available which is necessary for
interoperability as defined in
this Directive;
Whereas the provisions of this Directive should be without prejudice to
specific requirements of Community law already enacted in
respect of the
publication of interfaces in the telecommunications sector or Council Decisions
relating to standardisation in the
field of information technology and
telecommunication;
Whereas this Directive does not affect derogations provided for under
national legislation in accordance with the Berne Convention
on points not
covered by this Directive.
HAS ADOPTED THIS DIRECTIVE:
Object of protection
1. In accordance with the provisions of this Directive, Member States shall
protect computer programs, by copyright, as literary works
within the meaning of
the Berne Convention for the Protection of Literary and Artistic Works. For the
purposes of this Directive,
the term “computer program” shall
include their preparatory design material.
2. Protection in accordance with this Directive shall apply to the expression
in any form of a computer program. Ideas and principles
which underlie any
element of a computer program, including those which underlie its interfaces,
are not protected by copyright under
this Directive.
3. A computer program shall be protected if it is original in the sense that
it is the author’s own intellectual creation.
No other criteria shall be
applied to determine its eligibility for protection.
Authorship of computer programs
1. The author of a computer program shall be the natural person or group of
natural persons who has created the program or, where
the legislation of the
Member State permits, the legal person designated as the rightholder by that
legislation. Where collective
works are recognised by the legislation of a
Member State, the person considered by the legislation of the Member State to
have created
the work shall be deemed to be its author.
2. In respect of a computer program created by a group of natural persons
jointly, the exclusive rights shall be owned jointly.
3. Where a computer program is created by an employee in the execution of his
duties or following the instructions given by his employer,
the employer
exclusively shall be entitled to exercise all economic rights in the program so
created, unless otherwise provided by
Beneficiaries of protection
Protection shall be granted to all natural or legal persons eligible under
national copyright legislation as applied to literary works.
Restricted Acts
Subject to the provisions of Articles 5 and 6, the exclusive rights of the
rightholder within the meaning of Article 2, shall include
the right to do or to
(a) the permanent or temporary reproduction of a computer program by any
means and in any form, in part or in whole. Insofar as loading,
displaying,
running, transmission or storage of the computer program necessitate such
reproduction, such acts shall be subject to
authorisation by the
rightholder;
(b) the translation, adaptation, arrangement and any other alteration of a
computer program and the reproduction of the results thereof,
without prejudice
to the rights of the person who alters the program;
(c) any form of distribution to the public, including the rental, of the
original computer program or of copies thereof. The first
sale in the Community
of a copy of a program by the rightholder or with his consent shall exhaust the
distribution right within the
Community of that copy, with the exception of the
right to control further rental of the program or a copy thereof.
Exceptions to the restricted acts
1. In the absence of specific contractual provisions, the acts referred to in
Article 4(a) and (b) shall not require authorisation
by the rightholder where
they are necessary for the use of the computer program by the lawful acquirer in
accordance with its intended
purpose, including for error correction.
2. The making of a back-up copy by a person having a right to use the
computer program may not be prevented by contract insofar as
it is necessary for
3. The person having a right to use a copy of a computer program shall be
entitled, without the authorisation of the rightholder,
to observe, study or
test the functioning of the program in order to determine the ideas and
principles which underlie any element
of the program if he does so while
performing any of the acts of loading, displaying, running, transmitting or
storing the program
which he is entitled to do.
Decompilation
1. The authorisation of the rightholder shall not be required where
reproduction of the code and translation of its form within the
Article 4(a) and (b) are indispensable to obtain the information necessary to
achieve the interoperability of an independently
created computer program with
other programs, provided that the following conditions are met:
(a) these acts are performed by the licensee or by another person having a
right to use a copy of a program, or on their behalf by
a person authorised to
(b) the information necessary to achieve interoperability has not previously
been readily available to the persons referred to in
subparagraph (a); and
(c) these acts are confined to the parts of the original program which are
necessary to achieve interoperability.
2. The provisions of paragraph 1 shall not permit the information obtained
through its application:
(a) to be used for goals other than to achieve the interoperability of the
independently created computer program;
(b) to be given to others, except when necessary for the interoperability of
the independently created computer program; or
(c) to be used for the development, production or marketing of a computer
program substantially similar in its expression, or for
any other act which
infringes copyright.
3. In accordance with the provisions of the Berne Convention for the
protection of Literary and Artistic Works, the provisions of
this Article may
not be interpreted in such a way as to allow its application to be used in a
manner which unreasonably prejudices
the right holder’s legitimate
interests or conflicts with a normal exploitation of the computer program.
Special measures of protection
1. Without prejudice to the provisions of Articles 4, 5 and 6, Member States
shall provide, in accordance with their national legislation,
appropriate
remedies against a person committing any of the acts listed in subparagraphs
(a), (b), and (c) below:
(a) any act of putting into circulation a copy of a computer program knowing,
or having reason to believe, that it is an infringing
(b) the possession, for commercial purposes, of a copy of a computer program
knowing, or having reason to believe, that it is an infringing
(c) any act of putting into circulation, or the possession for commercial
purposes of, any means the sole intended purpose of which
is to facilitate the
unauthorised removal or circumvention of any technical device which may have
been applied to protect a computer
2. Any infringing copy of a computer program shall be liable to seizure in
accordance with the legislation of the Member State concerned.
3. Member States may provide for the seizure of any means referred to in
paragraph 1(c).
Term of protection
1. Protection shall be granted for the life of the author and for fifty years
after his death or after the death of the last surviving
author; where the
computer program is an anonymous or pseudonymous work, or where a legal person
is designated as the author by national
legislation in accordance with Article
2(1), the term of protection shall be fifty years from the time that the
computer program
is first lawfully made available to the public. The term of
protection shall be deemed to begin on the first of January of the year
following the abovementioned events.
2. Member States which already have a term of protection longer than that
provided for in paragraph 1 are allowed to maintain their
present term until
such time as the term of protection for copyright works is harmonised by
Community law in a more general way.
Continued application of other legal
1. The provisions of this Directive shall be without prejudice to any other
legal provisions such as those concerning patent rights,
trade-marks, unfair
competition, trade secrets, protection of semi-conductor products or the law of
contract. Any contractual provisions
contrary to Article 6 or to the exceptions
provided for in Article 5(2) and (3) shall be null and void.
2. The provisions of this Directive shall apply also to programs created
before 1 January 1993 without prejudice to any acts concluded
acquired before that date.
Final provisions
1. Member States shall bring into force the laws, regulations and
administrative provisions necessary to comply with this Directive
January 1993.
When Member States adopt these measures, the latter shall contain a reference
to this Directive or shall be accompanied by such reference
on the occasion of
their official publication. The methods of making such a reference shall be
laid down by the Member States.
2. Member States shall communicate to the Commission the provisions of
national law which they adopt in the field governed by this
This Directive is addressed to the Member States.
Done at Brussels, 14 May 1991.
For the Council
The President
Mr J F Poos.

Section 23 of the Copyright, Designs and Patents Act 1988
23 SECONDARY INFRINGEMENT: POSSESSING OR DEALING
WITH INFRINGING COPY
The copyright in a work is infringed by a person who, without the licence of
the copyright owner—
(a) possesses in the course of a business,
(b) sells or lets for hire, or offers or exposes for sale or hire,
(c) in the course of a business exhibits in public or distributes, or
(d) distributes otherwise than in the course of a business to such an extent
as to affect prejudicially the owner of the copyright,
an article which is, and which he knows or has reason to believe is, an
infringing copy of the work.
Section 105 of the Copyright, Designs and Patents Act 1988
105 PRESUMPTIONS RELEVANT TO SOUND RECORDINGS AND
(1) In proceedings brought by virtue of this Chapter with respect to a sound

the label or mark shall be admissible as evidence of the facts stated and

of issue of the copies, or
(c) that the film was first published in a specified year or in a specified
the statement shall be admissible as evidence of the facts stated and shall

computer program, where copies of the program are issued to
the public in
electronic form bearing a statement—

date of issue of the copies, or
(b) that the program was first published in a specified country or that
copies of it were first issued to the public in electronic
form in a specified
the statement shall be admissible as evidence of the facts stated and shall
be presumed to be correct until the contrary is proved.
(4) The above presumptions apply equally in proceedings relating to an
infringement alleged to have occurred before the date on which
the copies were
issued to the public.
(5) In proceedings brought by virtue of this Chapter with respect to a film,

in a cable programme
service bears a statement—
(a) that a named person was the author or director of the film, or
(b) that a named person was the owner of copyright in the film immediately
after it was made,
the statement shall be admissible as evidence of the facts stated and shall
be presumed to be correct until the contrary is proved.
This presumption applies equally in proceedings relating to an infringement
alleged to have occurred before the date on which the

broadcast or included in a cable programme service.
The classification of computer programs
N.01 In the Draft Report the content of this appendix was contained in
Chapter 6 in paragraphs 6.05–6.10. While this discussion
has now been
transferred to this appendix reference is made in Chapter 6 to the
recommendations of the Committee deriving from the
classification of computer
programs. As noted in the Draft Report the discussion that follows represents
the Committee’s understanding
of the general classification of computer
N.02 Computer programs can be classified, on the basis of function, into two
groups, namely, operating systems programs and applications
Operating systems programs
provide the interface between the user and the
computer. For this reason they are often written specifically for particular
The types of functions performed by these programs include input and
output routines, ie, controlling the flow of data into the applications
that is being run on the computer and the flow of the processed data out of the
computer. Another type of systems program
is a compiler program.
convert programs expressed in so-called high level languages, such
as C, Prolog, Ada or FORTRAN, into low level languages (machine
or object code).
Applications programs
, on the other hand, are those programs which direct
the computer to perform a task in a particular way.
Machine code and object code
N.03 The Committee notes the potential for confusion caused by the
interchangeable use of the terms machine code and object code.
Machine code
describes the class of languages which can be interpreted directly by
machines without a translation process.
Object code
describes programs
expressed in machine code (and usually containing information in addition to the
machine code necessary to ensure
the correct loading of the program). Since the
preference for the use of the term object code is entrenched in High Court
the Committee has used object code in all situations other than those
where its use would be misleading.
Programming languages
N.04 Programs may be written in any of a large range of programming
languages. These languages may be either specific for a particular
computer or independent of the computer type. Program languages are classified
at different levels; they may be either “high
level” or “low
High level languages
are so called because they are closer
to English in appearance or because they consist of recognisable mathematical or
logical statements.
They are powerful tools in computer programming because they
lend themselves to the precise and unambiguous statement of complex
instructions
in a form readily understood by humans. On the other hand,
, eg, assembler code, are closer to the machine language used by
the computer. However, they still allow the programmer to work with
instructions
expressed in alphabetic characters and decimal numbers rather than binary or
hexadecimal numbers.
Compilation and interpretation
N.05 The two techniques used to run computer programs written in languages
other than machine code are compilation and interpretation.
compilation
the high level language is converted into equivalent
instructions in machine code – the object code. This can be done by a
compiler program (paragraph N.03). A special class of compiler, called an
, generates object code from assembler code. An alternative way
to run a computer program is by using an interpreter program. Assembler
compiler programs operate by converting the entire source code program to object
code before it can run on the computer. An
interpreter program
converts the source code program “on the fly” one instruction at a
time. It does not produce a complete object
code version of the source code
program. On some computers, the object code or machine code is interpreted by
the machine itself
directly. On microprogrammed machines, however, the object
code is interpreted by microprograms, written in a code called
which is at a lower level than conventional machine code.
Microprograms
consist of a series of steps which go to make up an instruction, defining
exactly what each machine instruction does, eg, moving
or examining data. In
other words, microcode programs interpret higher level machine code
instructions.
Articles 9, 10, 11, 13 and 65 of the TRIPS Agreement
PART II: STANDARDS CONCERNING THE AVAILABILITY,
SCOPE AND USE OF INTELLECTUAL PROPERTY RIGHTS
Section I: Copyright and related rights
Article 9 Relation to Berne Convention
1. Members shall comply with Articles 1–21 and the Appendix of the
Berne Convention (1971). However, Members shall not have
rights or obligations
under this Agreement in respect of the rights conferred under Article
of that Convention or of the rights derived therefrom.
2. Copyright protection shall extend to expressions and not to ideas,
procedures, methods of operation or mathematical concepts as
Article 10 Computer Programs and Compilations of
1. Computer programs, whether in source or object code, shall be protected as
literary works under the Berne Convention (1971).
2. Compilations of data or other material, whether in machine readable or
other form, which by reason of the selection or arrangement
of their contents
constitute intellectual creations shall be protected as such. Such protection,
which shall not extend to the data
or material itself, shall be without
prejudice to any copyright subsisting in the data or material itself.
Article 11 Rental Rights
In respect of at least computer programs and cinematographic works, a Member
shall provide authors and their successors in title
the right to authorize or to
prohibit the commercial rental to the public of originals or copies of their
shall be excepted from this obligation in respect of
cinematographic works unless such rental has led to widespread copying of such
works which is materially impairing the exclusive right of reproduction
conferred in that Member on authors and their successors
in title. In respect of
computer programs, this obligation does not apply to rentals where the program
itself is not the essential
object of the rental.
Article 13 Limitations and Exceptions
Members shall confine limitations or exceptions to exclusive rights to
certain special cases which do not conflict with a normal
exploitation of the
work and do not unreasonably prejudice the legitimate interests of the right
PART VI: TRANSITIONAL ARRANGEMENTS
Article 65 Transitional Arrangements
1. Subject to the provisions of paragraphs 2, 3 and 4 below, no Member shall
be obliged to apply the provisions of this Agreement
before the expiry of a
general period of one year following the date of entry into force of the
Agreement Establishing the MTO.
2. Any developing country Member is entitled to delay for a further period of
four years the date of application, as defined in paragraph
1 above, of the
provisions of this Agreement other than Articles 3, 4 and 5 of Part I.
3. Any other Member which is in the process of transformation from a
centrally-planned into a market, free-enterprise economy and
undertaking structural reform of its intellectual property system and facing
special problems in the preparation and implementation
of intellectual property
laws, may also benefit from a period of delay as foreseen in paragraph 2
